 Explanation of the Presentation of Objects and Object-Possessors as well as Awarenesses and Knowers  by Pur-bu-jok (Phur-bu-lcog Byams-pa-rgya-mtsho) Translation by Elizabeth Napper   A Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness Clearly Revealing the Modes of Minds and Mental Factors  by Yeshe Gyeltsen Translated from the Tibetan by Toh Sze Gee    With oral commentary by  Geshe Tenzin Tenphel            © Istituto Lama Tzong Khapa Pomaia Italy 2005
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 12-16 September 2005 (01)  
1 Explanation of the Presentation of Objects and Object-Possessors as well as Awarenesses and Knowers by Pur-bu-jok (Phur-bu-lcog Byams-pa-rgya-mtsho)  Monday afternoon, 12 September 2005  [Note: All indented text is the translation Explanation of the Presentation of Objects and Object-Possessors as well as Awarenesses and Knowers by Elizabeth Napper.]  So here we are once again. I myself am very happy to be here and I would like to say Tashi Delek and welcome back to all of you.   We will now study the subject of lo-rig, that is, the functions of the mind. Perhaps we will not be able to cover the entire text in detail but we will at least do an overview. I would like to remind you that when you study it is important to do so with a long term view, that is, with plans to study over a long period. By thinking in this way you will reap the benefits of studying. Even if some of you have studied this particular text before, there is no harm in studying it again especially if you do so with a long-term view of its benefits. My teacher used to say that even if one were to study the same text a hundred times it would still not be too much study!   Some people start their studies w i t h  g r e a t  e n t h u s i a s m  a n d  e f f o r t  b u t  t h e n  a r e  u n a b l e  t o  maintain the pace and give it up entirely. This is a mistake, it is too extreme. You need to think about how to study and at the same time take care of your health. You should not have an over-exaggerated renunciation, that is, to give everything up and then later on regret it. This should be a gradual process. Of course, if you can maintain great effort during the course of your studies that would be wonderful, but if you cannot you should rest and in addition make prayers to be able to continue your studies free from any difficulties.   This particular subject of lo-rig, the mind and its functions, is very important and essential to understand. The reason is that when we talk about suffering, that which experiences suffering is the mind or the consciousness or the mental continuum. It is the mind that experiences suffering and creates the cause of suffering, and it is also the mind that creates the cause of freedom from suffering. Therefore, since the mind suffers, creates suffering, and can become free from suffering, it is essential to understand how it works. Although we may not be able to study this text in great detail we will try and cover this subject in as much detail as the time allotted allows.   While we could just discuss the assertions of a single system or school this would not lead to a firm understanding of the subject. For this reason it is important to examine the arguments of other systems so as to better understand the topic and to be able to decide for ourselves what is right and what is wrong. By studying the various arguments we will come to a clear understanding of the assertions of “our own system,” in this case the Sautrantika school, which is what is mainly set out in this text. Another reason to look at the various debates regarding this subject is that you should not just blindly accept whatever is said in a particular text. If you do this you will not come to a deep understanding of the subject. Secondly you should not accept what I, the teacher, say without examining it. Rather you should think about the subject and arrive at your own conclusions, which may or may not accord with what is set out in the text and with what I say.   We will begin by examining “our own system” and then look at some of the arguments against it. This is not how texts are usually studied in the monasteries but there the monks have already memorized the assertions of “one’s own system” prior to beginning to study the text. Therefore, all they need to be taught is the arguments against that position. However here, because we have not yet examined our own system, we will start by looking at the assertions of our own system or our own position,. Thus, in this text the words “our own system” refer t o  the Sautrantika school’s assertions regarding a particular topic. However, you do not have to accept this position as your own personal point of view, rather you should examine the presentation and then decide whether or not to accept it.   This text covers two main topics: objects and object-possessors. While object-possessors are generally consciousnesses this is not always the case. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 12-16 September 2005 (01)  
2 Part One: Objects In our own system, concerning the first from among the two, objects and object-possessors, the definition of an object is: that which is known by an awareness.  When objects are divided there are: (1) appearing objects,  (2) determined objects, and  (3) objects of engagement.  The appearing object of a particular awareness1 and apprehended object of that awareness are synonymous.   There are different kinds of objects: 1. appearing object, that which actually appears to a mind, 2. determined object, mainly an object of a conceptual mind, which is said to hold or seize its object,  3. object of engagement, the object that the mind actually engages. The appearing object of a particular mind and the a p p r e h e n d e d  o b j e c t  o f  a p a r t i c u l a r  m i n d  a r e  synonymous, that is, they are the same.   Whatever is an established base is necessarily an appearing object. This is because whatever is a thing is necessarily the appearing object of a direct perceiver, and whatever is permanent is necessarily the appearing object of a conceptual consciousness.  The first reason is established because appearing object of a direct perceiver, apprehended object [of a direct perceiver], and thing are synonymous. Furthermore, it follows that whatever is a thing must be the appearing object of a direct perceiver because whatever is a thing must be that which is realized in a manifest manner by a direct perceiver.  An “established base” i s  a ny e x i s t e n t ,  that is, any phenomena that exists. It is necessarily an appearing object because whatever is a thing or a functioning phenomenon is necessarily the appearing object of a direct perceiver and whatever is a permanent phenomenon is necessarily the appearing object of conceptual consciousness. That a functioning phenomenon is necessarily the appearing object of a direct perceiver is because appearing object of a direct p e r c e i v e r ,  apprehended object of a direct perceiver, and thing are synonymous. This is because a thing is a manifest phenomenon that is perceived by a direct perceiver.   The second basic reason [i.e., whatever is permanent is necessarily the appearing object of a conceptual consciousness] is established because appearing object of a conceptual consciousness, apprehended object [of a conceptual consciousness], and permanent phenomenon are synonymous.  Permanent phenomena are phenomena t h a t  d o  n o t  a r i s e  i n  d e p e n d e n c e  o n  c a u s e s .  T h e y  a p p e a r  only to conceptual consciousnesses. Why? Because appearing object of a conceptual consciousness, apprehended object of a conceptual consciousness, and permanent phenomenon are synonymous.  Also object of engagement of a direct perceiver, and object of the mode of apprehension of a direct perceiver are synonymous. Determined object of a conceptual consciousness, object of engagement [of a conceptual consciousness], and object of the mode of apprehension of a conceptual consciousness are synonymous.                                                          1 BP English translator: The word translated here as “awareness” refers to mind in the sense of it being that which knows its object.

3 Whatever is an established base is necessarily the object of the mode of apprehension of both a conceptual and a non-conceptual consciousness.   The object of engagement of a direct perceiver, for example, this microphone, and the object of the mode of apprehension of a direct perceiver are synonymous. Likewise, determined object of a conceptual consciousness, object of engagement of a conceptual consciousness, and object of the mode of apprehension of a conceptual consciousness are synonymous. In other words, in the case of a conceptual consciousness its determined object, its object of engagement, and its object of the mode of apprehension are one and the same.  STUDENT: I f  permanent phenomena are appearing objects of conceptual consciousnesses, it follows that emptiness, as the subject, cannot be realized directly b e c a u s e  i t  o n l y  a p p e a r s  t o  a  conceptual consciousness. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The Saut rant i kas do not  posit emptiness. However there is a lot of debate about this subject.   It is important to understand the structure of this text, which includes a lot of debate. In fact, this text is called a magical key because logical reasonings are said to be like a key that unlock the secrets of a particular subject. So do not worry if you do not understand everything right away. With time you will slowly come to understand the subject so do not get depressed and think to run away! When we monks studied t h i s  t e x t, i t  also i n i t i a l l y  a p p e a r e d  t o  u s t h a t  w e  d i d  n o t  u n d e r s t a n d  anything. However, later on when we studied Signs and Reasonings and then went back to help the younger monks with the subject of Awarenesses and Knowers, we came to see that we had in fact actually understood something!  There are three components to logical reasonings. The first is the subject, which is the actual topic under consideration. The second is that which is to be established or proved about that particular subject. The third is the sign or reason that establishes or proves it. When one is presented with a logical statement there are only four possible answers:  1. “I accept,” 2. “Why?” 3. “I don’t accept the sign (the reason),” 4. “There is no pervasion” (i.e., it is not that because it is A it is necessarily B).  An illustration is the statement: “Sound, it follows that it is impermanent.” Sound is the subject and that it is impermanent is what is to be established. What would you say to this statement? STUDENT: Why? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Th i s  answer i mp lies that you do not agree with the s t a t e me n t  t h a t  sound is impermanent. So to that I would respond: “Sound is impermanent because it is a product. Thus, the fact that you say “Why?” implies that you do not agree with the statement and a r e  asking for a reason. So then I reply with a reason. Then what do you say to my statement “Sound is impermanent because it is a product”? STUDENT: I accept. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: If I just say “Sound, it follows that it is impermanent,” you can either respond “I accept” or “Why?” You cannot say “The reason is not established” because I did not set out a reason, and you cannot say “There is no pervasion” because I did not set out a pervasion. To the question “Why?” I answered “Because it is a product.” At this point you can either say “The reason is not established” or “There is no pervasion.” If you say “The reason is not established,” meaning that sound is not a product, then I would say “Sound is a product because it is a thing or a functioning phenomenon.” Then what would you say?  STUDENT: Why does it follow that product and functioning thing pervade each other? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Sound is a thing because it is the appearing object of direct perceiver.  STUDENT: Sound can occur without the presence of a direct perceiver. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: In the context of debate you are not allowed to give such a response. What answer can you give to the statement “Sound is a thing because it is the appearing object of a direct perceiver”? You can say “Sound is not the appearing object of a direct perceiver” or “There is no pervasion that because it appears to a direct perceiver it is necessarily a thing.” STUDENT: There is no pervasion.

4 GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T hen I respond “That contradicts the text which says that a n  appearing object of a direct perceiver and thing are synonymous.”  STUDENT: I don’t accept what it says in the text.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: From the point of view of a Prasangika that would be fine, but here the text sets out the assertions of the Sautrantikas and this is the position you should take. You should not think that this is what they say but I do not accept it. This a Sautrantika scripture that sets out the a s s e r t i o n s o f  t h i s  p a r t i c u l a r  s c h o o l. It is important to understand the tenets of the particular school under study and not just say that you do not accept them. When you study this particular school, you should temporarily become a Sautrantika and seek to understand this school from within their assertions. Your own personal position is something else. Thus, you should try to understand the position and philosophy of the Sautrantikas by temporarily accepting it. In general outside the context of the Sautrantika system, it is not the case that appearing object of a direct perceiver is synonymous with thing, but inside this school this is the position that is to be taken.  STUDENT: Therefore, for the time being Geshe-la wants us to become a Sautrantika? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: F o r  t h e  t i m e  b e i n g  t h a t  i s  w h o  w e  a r e .  I t  i s  n o t  t o  s a y  t h a t  w e  a r e  Sautrantikas but that while we study their philosophy we should temporarily hold the Sautrantika tenets. In fact to understand t h e  P r a s a n g i k a s y s t e m it is necessary to first understand the Sautrantika system, otherwise later on you w i l l  h a v e  a  l o t  o f  d o u b t s  r e g a r d i n g  t h e  P r asangika philosophy.  Let’s examine the t e r m  “ s y n o n y m o u s ”  o r  “ m u t u a l l y  i n c l u s i v e.” There are d e f i nitions a n d  t h a t  which is defined or definienda. For example, in the case of the statement “The definition of object is that which is known by an awareness,” “object” is that which is defined or the definiendum, and “that which is known by an awareness” i s  t h e  d e f i n ition or defining characteristic. These two, definition and definiendum, are mutually inclusive by way of eight pervasions. The eight equal pervasions are: (1-2) pervasion of being: (whatever is A is B, and whatever is B is A) whatever is the definition is pervaded by being the definiendum, therefore whatever i s  k n o w n  b y  a n  a w a r e n e s s  m u s t  b e  a n  object; and whatever is the definiendum must be the definition, therefore whatever is an object must be that which is known by an awareness; (3-4) pervasion of  exi s t i ng: (whenever there is A there is B, and whenever there is B there is A) whenever an object exists, that which is known by an awareness exists; and whenever that which is known by an awareness exists, an object exists; (5-6) pervasion of not being: (whatever is not A is not B, and whatever is not B is not A) if it is not an object, it is not that which is known by an awareness, and if it is not that which is known by an awareness, it is not an object; (7-8) pervasion of not existing: (wherever there is not A there is not B, and wherever there is not B there is not A) if there is not an object, there is not that which is known by an awareness; and if there is not that which is known by an awareness, there is not an object.  “Synonymous” therefore has the meaning that there exist these eight pervasions. This relationship must exist between a definition and its definiendum. Therefore, with respect to object (the definiendum) and “that which is known by an awareness” (the definition), whatever is the definiendum is pervaded by being the definition, and whatever is the definition is pervaded by being the definiendum. Based on this, if I were to ask “If there is that which is known by an awareness is it pervaded by being the definition of object?” what would you respond?  STUDENT: Yes. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That which is known by an awareness is the definition of object. That is clear, but does it follow that whatever is known by an awareness is the definition of object? STUDENT: Yes, because they are synonymous. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It follows that vase, as the subject, is the definition of object.  STUDENT: It is not. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It follows that it is because you accepted that that which is known by an awareness is pervaded by being the definition of object. Therefore, vase i s  t h e  d e f i n i t i o n  o f  object because it is that which is known by an awareness. STUDENT: Vase is an example of that which is known by an awareness but it is not synonymous with it. Therefore, there is no pervasion. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Tsa! That is right but earlier on you accepted the statement “It follows that whatever is known by an awareness is the definition of object,” and vase i s  t h a t  w h i c h  i s

5 known by an awareness. The word “Tsa” in Tibetan indicates t h a t  y o u  have now contradicted yourself. Therefore, in the end you agree with me that whatever is known by an awareness is not necessarily the definition of object. Therefore, it follows that that which is known by an awareness is not the definition of object. Remember that you can only give one of the four standard responses. STUDENT: I accept. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Then I could say that that contradicts the Sautrantika assertion that that which is known by an awareness is the definition of object. In this case the solution is to quote scripture because we are temporarily Sautrantikas and therefore that which is known by an awareness is the definition of object because the text says so.   In short, the first sentence of the text “The definition of object is that which is known by an awareness” is not as simple as it initially appears. That which is known by a n  a w a r e n e s s  i s  a  definition, it is definition of object. However it does not meant that whatever is known by an awareness is the definition of object. F o r  e x a m p l e ,  o b j e c t  i t s e l f  i s  t h a t  w h i c h  i s  k n o w n  b y  a n  awareness, but object is not the definition of object because object is the definiendum. Therefore, whatever is known by an awareness is not the definition of object, for example, object. Object is that which is defined by a definition, it is not i t s e l f a  d e f i n i t i o n .  T h e r e f o r e ,  d o e s  i t  f o l l o w  t h a t  whatever is an object is a definiendum? STUDENT: No. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Give me an example of something that i s  a n  o b j e ct but is not a definiendum. STUDENT: That which is known by an awareness is a definition but not a definiendum. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Why is not it a definiendum? STUDENT: Object is a definiendum and that which is known by an awareness is the definition.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: You can say that it is not a definiendum because it is definition. If it is a definition does it follow that it is not a definiendum?  STUDENT: No. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Why? STUDENT: A definition can also be that which is to be defined. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That answer is not correct. Just because it is a definition does it follow that it is not a definiendum? Does it follow that whatever is a definition cannot be a definiendum? If you say that there is nothing that can be both, that would be correct. Thus it follows that whatever is a definition cannot be a definiendum since these two are mutually exclusive. STUDENT: So o b j e c t ,  the definiendum, and that which is to be known by an awareness, the definition, are mutually exclusive?  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: D e f i n i t i o n  a n d  o b j e c t  a r e  n o t  n e c e s s a r i l y  m u t u a l l y  e x c l u s i v e ,  f o r  example, that which is known by an awareness is both definition and object. This can be confusing but it is important to understand how logical reasoning functions as this will help you understand the coming debates. Definition and definiendum are mutually exclusive but it does not mean that that which is a definition and that which is a definiendum are mutually exclusive. For example, object and that which is known by an awareness are not mutually exclusive, they are synonymous. This type of argument is applied throughout the text so it is important to understand this reasoning. However we will look at definition and definiendum again tomorrow. This is just the beginning, it will get easier as we go ahead.  Tuesday morning, 13 September 2005  Sometimes when we study we may experience difficulties due to which we become unhappy and down. However, we should not allow this to happen as we can take control of our minds. Thus, we need to take care so as to not allow the difficulties to overcome us as this can lead to sickness and other problems. If we do not take charge of our own mind, something else will! However, at the moment we are not in control of our mind. When our mind suffers and becomes depressed, we follow these feelings. Although we say that we do not want to suffer, we continue to create our own suffering.  Yesterday we looked at the relationship b e t w e e n  d e f i n i t i o n  a n d  d e f i niendum. This subject may appear to be difficult but if you strive to understand it, gradually it will become clear. Of course, other difficulties will arise but if you can at least clarify the relationship between definition and definiendum that will be of great benefit during the study of this subject.

6  As I said yesterday there are three components of a logical reason. For example, in the statement “Sound is impermanent because it is a product,” sound i s  t h e  subject, that it is impermanent is what is to be established, and “because it is a product” is the reason that establishes it. On the other hand, if I make the statement “It follows that sound is impermanent,” I am throwing a consequence that is similar to asking you whether sound is impermanent. If you accept this consequence, you should s a y  “ I  a c c e p t . ”  I f  y o u  d o  n o t  a g r e e  t h a t  s o u n d  i s  i m p e r m a n e n t ,  y o u  should say “Why?”, which indicates that you do not agree with the statement.   If I a d d  t h e  r e a s o n  “ b ecause it is a product,” you can either answer “The reason is not established” which means that you do not accept the reason, or “There is no pervasion.” If it does not follow that because it is a product it is necessarily impermanent, you should say “There is no pervasion.” Thus, when you say that there is no pervasion it means that whatever is a product is not necessarily i m p e r m a n e n t ,  in which case t h e r e  m u s t  exist something t h a t  i s  a  p r o d u c t  b u t  i s  permanent.   The text says that the definition of “object” is that which is known by an awareness (or by a mind). In other words, that which is known by an awareness i s  t h e  d e f i n i t i o n  a n d  object i s  t h e  definiendum. These two, object and that which is known by an awareness, have the relationship of respectively being definiendum and definition. If an object is that which is known by an awareness, is it the case that whatever is an object follows as being the definiendum of t h e  d e f i n i t i o n  that which is known by an awareness? Similarly, if it is that which is known by an awareness does it follow that it is necessarily the definition of object? There are many things that are objects and many things that are that which is known by an awareness. That which is known by an awareness is a definition but it does not mean that every instant of that which is known by an awareness, for example, vase, pillar, and so forth, are a definition. Likewise, there are many objects, for example, vase, pillar, and so forth, b u t  i t  d o e s  n o t  f o l l o w  t h a t  b e c a u s e  o b j e c t  i s  t h e  d e f i n i e n d u m  o f that which is known by an awareness t h a t  everything t h a t  i s  a n  o b j e c t  i s  a l s o  t h e  d e f i n i e n d u m  o f  that which is known by an awareness. For example, vase is an object but it is not the definiendum of that which is known by an awareness because it has its own specific definition. Therefore, the relationship between definition and definiendum is that they are mutually exclusive and every instance of one of them is not necessarily the other. So here object is the definiendum, that which is to be defined, but is every instance of object a definiendum?  STUDENT: Yes. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: If that is the case, then what about that which is known by an awareness? That is an object. Therefore, it follows that whatever is a definition is not a definiendum because it is an object. Just because it is an object it does not have to be a definiendum. What is an object but is not a definiendum?  STUDENT: That which is known by an awareness is a definition and an object but it is not a definiendum. It is not a definiendum because it is the definition. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It follows that whatever is a definiendum cannot be a definition. STUDENT: I accept because it is a definiendum a n d  definiendum and definition a r e  m u t u a l l y  exclusive.  STUDENT: Why can a  d e f i n i t i o n  not be a definiendum? It cannot be the definiendum of that which it defines but it could be a definiendum. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That definition and definiendum are mutually exclusive means that there does not exist a c o m m o n  b a s i s  o r  l o c u s t h a t  i s  b o t h  a definition and a definiendum. You should not think that that which is known by an awareness is an object so it must have a definition. That which is known by an awareness is an object and a definition but it is not a definiendum.  STUDENT: Why cannot that which is a definition not have its own definition? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Definition and definiendum respectively mean that which shows and that which is shown. A  d e f i n i t i o n  s h o w s  s o m e t h i n g .  F o r  e x a m p l e ,  that which is known by an awareness shows object. One takes the definition into one’s mind and by understanding it one knows what an object is. Definiendum is that which is shown in the sense that that which is shown cannot be that which shows. Do you understand what is happening? Ha-ha!  That which is known by an awareness, that which is understood by an awareness, and that which is realized by an awareness are synonymous. You need to think about these points because later on they will be debated.

7 When objects are divided there are three: the appearing object, determined object, and object of engagement. For an e ye consciousness seeing vase, the appearing object is the vase b e c a u s e  t h e  vase a p p e a r s  d i r e c t l y  t o  the eye sense consciousness. An e y e  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  d o e s  n o t  h a v e  a  determined object because only conceptual consciousnesses have determined objects. The object of engagement of an eye consciousness seeing vase is vase. So in this case the appearing object and object of engagement are the same, while there is no determined object. The only type of mind that has all three objects is a conceptual consciousness. For example, when one thinks about vase, there is a conceptual consciousness apprehending vase. This conceptual consciousness apprehending vase h a s  a n  a p p e a r i n g  o b j e c t ,  a determined object, and an object of engagement. Its d e t e r mi n e d  object and object of engagement are the vase itself because the object of the mode of apprehension of that mind is vase. Vase is the determined object because the mind focuses on vase and it is the object of engagement because the mind refers to vase. However, t h e  a p p e a r i n g  o b j ect of a conceptual consciousness apprehending vase is not vase. The appearing object is a meaning generality or mental image of vase. Therefore, the appearing object is not vase, whereas the determined object and object of engagement of a conceptual consciousness apprehending vase is the vase i t s e l f .  S o  t h e  d e t e r m i n e d  o b j e c t  a n d  t h e  o b j e c t  o f  e n g a g e m e n t  o f  a  c o n c e p t u a l  consciousness apprehending vase are vase, but its appearing object is not vase. Thus, we can say that while a vase appears to the conceptual consciousness apprehending vase, the appearing object of the c o n c e p t u a l  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  a p p r e h e n d i n g  vase i s  n o t  vase, it is the mental image of vase. Thus, it can be said that the vase appears to it but it is not its appearing object. In this way, when this subject is debated it can become quite complex. We will talk about this again later on.  In short, all consciousnesses or awarenesses have an appearing object, but only conceptual consciousnesses have a determined object.   Appearing object of a direct perceiver and thing (or functioning phenomenon) are synonymous. This is because thing, that which performs a function, is necessarily the appearing object o f  a  d i r e c t  perceiver. On the other hand, permanent phenomenon (non-functioning phenomenon) and appearing object of a conceptual consciousness are synonymous. To be synonymous the eight pervasions must exist between the two. If thing and appearing object of direct perceiver are synonymous, whatever is an appearing object of a direct perceiver must be a thing and whatever is a thing must be an appearing object of a direct perceiver. Likewise, whatever is a permanent phenomenon must be the appearing object of a conceptual consciousness and whatever is the appearing object of a conceptual consciousness must be a permanent phenomenon.   We can take as an example the statement: “The no-self of persons, as the subject, it follows that it is a thing because it is the appearing object of a direct perceiver.” To this, the Sautrantika school would respond “The reason is not established” because for them the no-self of persons is not an appearing object of a direct perceiver. If we then say “It is the appearing object of a direct perceiver because it is the appearing object of a yogic direct perceiver (that is, of the path of seeing of an arya who directly realizes emptiness),” the Sautrantikas would again say “The reason is not established” because for them the no-self of persons is not the appearing object of a yogic direct perceiver. However, if they are asked “What directly realizes the no-self of persons?” they would say that it is a yogic direct perceiver. Thus, they say that a yogic direct perceiver directly realizes the no-self of persons, but t h a t  t h e n o-self of persons is not directly realized by a yogic direct perceiver in terms of being its appearing object. This is a specific assertion of the Sautrantikas.   Thus, the no-self of persons is not directly realized by a yogic direct perceiver but it is realized. It is realized because it is realized in the manner of having isolated the self, that is, by realizing the compositional factors free from t h e  s e l f  that is to be refuted. Because the no-self of persons i s  a  permanent phenomenon, it cannot appear directly to a direct perceiver. However, but since it must be realized, it must be realized indirectly. Thus, by realizing the compositional factors free from the self that is to be refuted, a y o g i c  d i r e c t  p e r c e i v e r  r e a l i z e s  t h e  n o-self of persons. This is an uncommon tenet of the Sautrantikas.   Hence, the no-self of persons cannot be directly perceived by a yogic direct perceiver because it is not the appearing object of a direct perceiver. The no-self of persons does not appear directly to a yogic direct perceiver because it is not an appearing object of a direct perceiver. It is not the appearing object of a direct perceiver because it is not a thing or a functioning phenomenon because it is a permanent phenomenon. If something is an appearing object of a yogic direct perceiver it must be an appearing object of a direct perceiver because a yogic direct perceiver is a non-conceptual direct perceiver. Therefore, whatever appears to a yogic direct perceiver must be a 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 12-16 September 2005 (01)  
8 thing or functioning phenomenon. However, a yogic direct perceiver can still realize the no-self of persons.  STUDENT. What is the difference between saying that (1) a yogic direct perceiver directly realizes the no-self of persons and (2) the no-self of persons, as the subject, is not directly realized by a yogic direct perceiver? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A yogic direct perceiver directly realizes the no-self of persons because it perceives the no-self of persons free from a meaning generality or mental image. However, the no-self of persons does not directly appear to a yogic direct perceiver because the no-self of persons is not the appearing object of a direct perceiver. For s o m e t h i n g  to be t h e  appearing object of a direct perceiver it must b e  a  m a n i f e s t  p h e n o m e n on. However, permanent phenomena such as the no-self of persons are not manifest phenomenon. Manifest phenomenon are things according to the Sautrantikas. STUDENT: Then can we summarize this by saying that a yogic direct perceiver directly realizes the no-self of persons but the no-self of persons is not the appearing object of a yogic direct perceiver? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right.  STUDENT: If the appearing object of a conceptual consciousness and permanent phenomenon are synonymous does it mean that every thought is a permanent phenomenon? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Thoughts or conceptions or conceptual consciousnesses are not permanent, it is there appearing object that is p e r m a n e n t .  T h e i r  a p p e a r i n g  o b j e c t  i s  a meaning generality or mental image. STUDENT: So the meaning generalities of all phenomena are permanent? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: They are permanent. STUDENT: A meaning generality is produced by my mind so it cannot be permanent. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is not produced by the mind, rather it i s  a p r o j e c t i o n  o r  superimposition (rgro btags) of the mind.  STUDENT: That moment of mind is also born or produced so it cannot be permanent. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Do not confuse the object of the conceptual consciousness and what appears to the conceptual consciousness. If you think “vase” there is a conception of vase, that conception is permanent. It is n o t  t h e  vase i t s e l f, but i s  a  m e n t a l  i m a g e  o f  vase. Thoughts themselves are impermanent but the appearing object of a conceptual consciousness, a me a ni ng generality or mental image, is p e r m a n e n t .  For e x a m p l e ,  f o r  m y s e l f ,  G e s h e  T e n p h e l , a s  a  p e r s o n  there exists a no-self of persons and that no-self of persons is permanent. However, if I cease to exist there would no longer be a no-self of persons of myself, yet that no-self of persons is still said to be permanent even though it can cease to exist. Likewise the fact that a vase can come to an end does not mean that the image of vase is also impermanent. Vase is impermanent or transitory, that is, it is subject to destruction. That is its nature and that is permanent, but still when the cup ceases to exist its impermanence also ceases to exist. T h e  vase i s  s u b j e c t  t o  d e s t r u c t i o n ,  t h a t  n a t u r e  o f  destruction comes into existence with the vase and that impermanent nature is destroyed when the cup is destroyed. However, the impermanent nature of the cup cannot be said to be impermanent because it is not that which is destroyed, rather it is the cup that is destroyed.   STUDENT: What are the definitions of permanent and impermanent? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: An impermanent phenomenon is that which changes momentarily or changes in each instant. A permanent phenomenon is that which does not change momentarily.  STUDENT: A meaning generality also changes, therefore it too is impermanent.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It does not change. Because you can think of something and in the first instant the image i s  c l e a r  but t h e n  it changes and loses its clarity d o e s  n o t  m e a n  t h a t  i t  i s  impermanent; STUDENT: For example, ten years ago I did not know what a mala is but now I know that it is a Buddhist rosary, s o  no w my  me a ni ng  g e ne r a l i t y  of mala is different than what it was ten years ago, so it does change. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: When you first heard the word “mala” you had a meaning generality based upon the name “mala.” Later on, when you came to know what a mala is, your meaning generality became based upon the meaning of what a mala is. 9 GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The mala of ten years ago is not the mala of today. Otherwise you too would still be the infant in your mother’s womb.  STUDENT: Perhaps it is important to say that a meaning generality is a negative phenomenon that comes about by eliminating other phenomenon and because of that is a permanent phenomenon? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is true that a meaning generality is an affirming negative phenomenon, that is, a phenomenon that is understood by negating something else, but that does not establish that it is a permanent phenomenon because there are also affirming negative phenomena that are impermanent phenomena.  STUDENT: The conceptual consciousness is not permanent but the mental image is a permanent phenomenon. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: You can say that. We have thoughts or concepts that follow each other so there are many meaning generalities that follow each other but it is not that the first meaning generality changes into the second and so forth. The mental image is a projection or superimposition of the mind, it does not change like a child who gradually grows into an adult. Otherwise a young mental image would gradually become an old mental image just like a young child becomes an old man! The projections from the side of the mind, the mental images, quickly follow each other, but it is not that the object of the mind changes. For example, if we take two instants of a cup, an earlier instant of cup changes into the successive instant of cup because it is a functioning thing. However, in the case of two instances of mental image of cup it is not that an earlier mental image changes into a later one. However, this subject requires a lot of thought in order to be understood and I too used to have a lot of doubt about this. In other words, a definition is also a definiendum because it too has a definition, due to which a definition is not a definition but a definiendum. A definition is that which highlights the main characteristics of the object being defined. Wednesday morning, 14 September 2005  First we will examine the assertions of our own system regarding valid cognizers, and after that we will go back and look at some of the arguments presented in the text. Our own system in this context is that of the Sautrantika school. There are differences between the Sautrantika school and the higher schools, the Chittamatra and Prasangika Madhyamaka, whereas the assertions of the Sautrantikas are quite similar to those of the Svatantrika Prasangika school. The differences are not very great but regarding some points there are slightly different opinions.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 12-16 September 2005 (01)  
13 (1) Valid cognizers In our own system, the definition of a valid cognizer is: a new incontrovertible knower.  There is a necessity for e x p r e s s i n g  t h e  t h r e e  – “ n e w ” ,  “ i n c o n t r o v e r t i b l e ” ,  a n d  “ k n o w e r ”  – a s  parts of the definition of valid cognizer, because: • “new” eliminates that subsequent cognizers are valid cognizers,  • “incontrovertible” eliminates that correctly assuming consciousnesses are valid cognizers, and  • “knower” eliminates that physical sense powers are valid cognizers.  There are three components to the definition of a valid cognizer: it is (1) new, (2) incontrovertible, and (3) a knower. The word “incontrovertible” means non-deceptive. According to the Sautrantikas “new” indicates the first instant of this mind. This first instant of new incontrovertible knower i s  a  v a l i d  c o g n i z e r, w h e r e a s  t h e  s e c o n d  i n s t a n t  o n w a r d  a r e  n ot c o n s i d e r e d  t o  b e  v a l i d  cognizers because they are not new. The second instant onward are considered to be subsequent cognizers. Hence, the word “new” is necessary in the definition because it eliminates that subsequent cognizers are valid cognizers. In addition, the word “incontrovertible” is necessary because it eliminates that correctly assuming consciousnesses are valid cognizers. A correct assumption is a mind that does not actually realize its object but is close to realizing it. Only when it becomes incontrovertible does a mind become a valid cognizer.   The word “knower” or “knowing” is necessary in the definition of valid cognizer because i t  eliminates that physical sense powers are valid cognizers. When an eye consciousness sees form that eye consciousness arises through the power of the eye sense power. T h e  w o r d  “ k n o w e r ”  eliminates that it is the eye sense power and so forth that knows form and so forth because some non-Buddhist schools assert that the eye sense power and so forth know their objects. An eye sense power is physical, a subtle form, and not consciousness.   STUDENT: Do the Vaibhashikas not also assert that? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The Vaibhashikas also assert that a physical sense power is a valid cognizer. Therefore, it would seem that for them a valid cognizer does not necessarily have to be an awareness. In that case, the definition of a valid cognizer as “a new incontrovertible knower” would not be acceptable to them since they assert that a valid cognizer is not necessarily a knower or consciousness.   When valid cognizers are divided there are two: (1) direct valid cognizers and  (2) inferential valid cognizers.  Their individual definitions, illustrations, and so forth will be explained later.  In the preceding pages of the Purbujog’s text the following arguments are set out:  With regard to the first of these, [that is, valid cognizers], someone might say that the definition of a “valid cognizer” is “an incontrovertible knower.”  [To that person we say,] “It [absurdly] follows that the subject, a subsequent cognizer, is a valid cognizer because of being an incontrovertible knower.”  If someone says that the reason is not established, “It follows that the subject [a subsequent cognizer] is [an incontrovertible knower] because of being a knower that gets at its object of analysis.”  If someone says that there is no pervasion, “It follows that the pervasion [that whatever is a knower that gets at its object of analysis is necessarily an incontrovertible knower] does exist, because the definition of something being an incontrovertible consciousness is “a knower that gets at its object of analysis.”  Here someone says that the definition of valid cognizer is “an incontrovertible knower,” omitting the word “new.” T h i s  d e f i n i t i o n  of valid cognizer is accepted by t h e  P r a s a n g i k a  s c h o o l  w h i c h  asserts that a valid cognizer does not need to be new. To this, the Sautrantikas, our own system, 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 12-16 September 2005 (01)  
14 reply: “It follows that a subsequent cognizer is a valid cognizer because it is an incontrovertible knower.” If someone responds that the reason is not established that a subsequent cognizer is not an incontrovertible knower , the Sautrantikas say that a subsequent cognizer is an incontrovertible knower because of being a knower that achieves its object of analysis, the object it is focusing on. However, according to them this is not sufficient to consider it a valid cognizer. If someone then says that there is no pervasion, the Sautrantikas respond that there is the pervasion that whatever is a knower that achieves its object of analysis is necessarily an incontrovertible knower because the definition of something being an incontrovertible consciousness is “a knower that achieves its object of analysis.” In other words, for the challenger it follows that whatever is a knower that achieves its object of analysis is necessarily an incontrovertible consciousness. Here we have not yet returned to the first argument we have gone off on a tangent but then the debate returns to the root consequence t hat a subsequent cognizer is a valid cognizer. First one goes off on a tangent when debating and then one returns to the root consequence.   If the root consequence [that a subsequent cognizer is a valid cognizer] is accepted, “It [absurdly] follows that the subject, a subsequent cognizer, is a knower that is a new realizer because of being a valid cognizer. The reason has been explicitly accepted.”  If the consequence [that a subsequent cognizer is a knower that is a new realizer] is accepted, “It follows that the subject, a subsequent cognizer, is not a knower that is a new realizer because of being a knower realizing that which has already been realized. This is because of being that subject.”  The challenger originally said that the definition of a valid cognizer is an incontrovertible knower to which we said that it follows that a subsequent cognizer is also a valid cognizer because it is an incontrovertible knower. Then the debate went off on a tangent. If the challenger accepts the root consequence that we set out that a subsequent cognizer is a valid cognizer then it follows that a subsequent cognizer is a knower that is a new realizer because it is a valid cognizer. The challenger has already explicitly accepted the reason that a subsequent cognizer is a  v a l i d  c o g n i z e r .  I f  t h e  challenger accepts that a subsequent cognizer is a knower that is a new realizer, we say that it is not a new realizer because a subsequent cognizer by its very definition is a knower that realizes that which has already been realized. Therefore, it cannot be a new realizer.   First one goes down through the debate until one arrives at a particular conclusion and then one retraces one’s steps backward up through the debate that sets out the previous points until one arrives at the beginning. “It follows that the pervasion [that whatever is a knower that gets at its object of analysis is necessarily an incontrovertible knower] does exist, because the definition of something being an incontrovertible consciousness i s  “ a  k n o we r  t h a t  g e t s  a t  i t s  o b j e c t  o f  a n a l y s i s . ”  That is accepted. Then one says “It follows that the subject, a subsequent cognizer, is an incontrovertible knower b e c a u s e  o f  b e i n g  a  k n o w e r  t h a t  g e t s  a t  i t s  o b j ect of analysis.” That is accepted. And then: “It absurdly follows that the subject, a subsequent cognizer, is a valid cognizer because of being an incontrovertible knower.” The response to this is that there is no pervasion. In this way one returns over the previous points. We do t h i s  b e c a u s e  t h i s  p e r s o n  b e l i e v e s  t h a t  a  subsequent cognizer is a valid cognizer. We say that it follows that a subsequent cognizer is a new realizer because it is a valid cognizer and then one returns backward through the debate. If the challenger accepts this, we say “A subsequent cognizer is not knower that is a new realizer because it is a knower that realizes that which has already been realized as that is the definition of subsequent cognizer.” If he accepts this, then we say that since a subsequent cognizer is a knower that realizes that which has already been realized, it follows that a subsequent cognizer it is not a new realizer. If he accepts this, we say that it is a new realizer because it is an incontrovertible knower, to which the challenger has to respond that there is no pervasion. We then say that it follows that whatever is an incontrovertible knower is not a valid cognizer, which he has to accept. Then we say that it follows that an incontrovertible knower is not the definition of valid cognizer. He has to accept this. That means that he has lost his root promise, that the definition of valid cognizer is an incontrovertible knower. We have gone down through the debate and then back up through it in order to show this. Then one says “Your root promise, tsa!” to show him that he has lost his root premise.   If you can follow this kind of forward and backward debate, i t  w i l l  b e  e a s y  for you to understand other debates as they follow a similar pattern.  If the challenger accepts the root consequence that a subsequent cognizer is a valid cognizer, the Sautrantikas say “It follows that a subsequent cognizer is a knower that is a new realizer because

Wednesday afternoon. 14 September 2005  The Sautrantika is not the final view or school of thought but, while we are studying their view, we should examine their assertions and then examine what others say about the subject with the purpose of arriving at a thorough understanding of the assertions of this school. We look at the assertions of the Sautrantikas and then move down through the debate and then up through i t  once again. This backwards and forwards style of debate is very important in order to achieve a good understanding of a particular subject. After having done that, you can then decide what is right and what is wrong.   There are two types of valid cognizers: (1) direct valid cognizers and (2) valid cognizers. However, first there is a discussion in the text of direct perceivers in general. (A) Direct perceivers The definition of an awareness that is a direct perceiver is: a knower that is free from conceptuality and non-mistaken.  When awarenesses that are direct perceivers are divided there are four:  (1) sense direct perceiver,  (2) mental direct perceiver,  (3) self-knowing direct perceiver, and  (4) yogic direct perceivers. (1) Sense direct perceivers With respect to the first of these [sense direct perceivers] there are two parts: (1) definition and (2) divisions. 1. Definition First, the definition of a sense direct perceiver is: • that which is produced in dependence on its own uncommon empowering condition, a physical sense power, and  • is a knower that is free from conceptuality and non-mistaken.  In the case of a sense direct perceiver the “empowering condition” is a sense power, this being what determines the type of consciousness that arises, such as an eye consciousness and so forth. “Uncommon” indicates that it is a particular or specific empowering condition.   Usually one would study the definitions of these various types of conditions in the context of Collected Topics prior to studying Awarenesses and Knowers ( l o-rig). There are immediate conditions, causal conditions, empowering conditions, and observed object conditions. They act to give rise to a particular consciousness. The empowering condition of an eye consciousness, the eye sense power, is so-called because it has the autonomy or power to produce an eye consciousness. The observed object condition of an eye consciousness apprehending vase i s the vase i t sel f .  The vase i s  a  c a u s e  f o r  t h e  a r i s a l  o f  t h e  e y e  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  a p p r e h e n d i n g  vase, w h i c h  h a s  t h e  a s p e c t  “vase.” Thus, the vase i t s e l f  i s  t h e  o b s e r v e d  o b j e c t  c o n d i t i o n  f o r  t h e  e y e  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  apprehending vase.

18  The immediate condition or immediately preceding consciousness is the mental consciousness that exists in the instant prior to the arisal of an eye consciousness apprehending vase. This mental consciousness is not necessarily non-conceptual since i t  i s  a  m e n t a l  e n g a g e m e n t  (yid la byed pa),which is what moves the mind toward vase.   STUDENT: Is it like intention? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is similar. Both intention (sems pa) and mental engagement (yid la byed pa) are included in the five omnipresent mental factors. The immediate condition for an eye consciousness seeing vase is the movement of the mind that occurs prior to the eye consciousness. Mental engagement, literally “to take to mind,” is what takes vase t o  m i n d . T h e  i m m e d i a t e  preceding condition that is mental engagement is not the same mental engagement that occurs simultaneous with the eye consciousness seeing vase. T h e  w o r d  “ i m m ediate” in immediately preceding condition shows that it occurs just before the eye consciousness apprehending vase. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: When we aspire (mos pa) for something, we then strive (‘dun pa) to obtain it. For example, we can aspire for or like our teacher. After that, comes the wish to obtain his qualities and an interest in or striving to attain them. For example, together with the mind of enlightenment, a main mind, there occur two aspirations: (1) the aspiration to attain enlightenment and (2) the aspiration to benefit others. These aspirations are based on liking and wanting something, which lead to an active interest in attaining i t .  T h i s  i s  t h e  m e a n i n g  o f  the word “aspiration.”  STUDENT: Then what happens when one sees an object for the first time? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I f  s o me o n e  h a s  n e v e r  s e e n  a  vase, is that eye consciousness seeing vase actually an eye consciousness seeing vase? There is some doubt regarding this point. When someone sees a vase f o r  t h e  v e r y  f i r s t  t i m e  i n  t h e  p r e c e d i n g  i n s t a n t  t h e r e  still exists a mental engagement.   These doubts are good. If you have no doubts you either know everything or know nothing!  2. Divisions Second, when [sense direct perceivers] are divided there are three: (1) valid cognizers that are sense direct perceivers,  (2) subsequent cognizers that are sense direct perceivers, and  (3) awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained that are sense direct perceivers.  The first is, for example, the first moment of a sense direct perceiver apprehending a form.   The second is, for example, the second moment of a sense direct perceiver apprehending a form.  The third is, for example, a sense direct perceiver apprehending a form in the continuum of a person whose mind is especially attracted to a pleasant sound.  A subsequent cognizer is the second instant onward of a valid cognizer therefore it belongs to the same continuum of consciousness. An awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained, on the other hand, is not included in the same continuum of consciousness as a valid cognizer. An example of such an awareness is that when we are engaged in an interesting conversation, although we hear the voice of another person interrupting the conversation, we do not ascertain what he or she said and cannot remember it later on.   When sense direct perceivers are divided in another way, there are five:  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 12-16 September 2005 (01)  
19 (1) sense direct perceivers apprehending forms,  (2) sense direct perceivers apprehending sounds,  (3) sense direct perceivers apprehending odors,  (4) sense direct perceivers apprehending tastes, and  (5) sense direct perceivers apprehending tangible objects.   Forms, sounds, odors, tastes, and tangible objects are all included in the category “form.” However, in the context of the five sense objects, the word form s p e c i f i c a l l y  r e f e r s  t o  c o l o r  a n d shape, that is, to the objects of the eye consciousness. Form in general is synonymous with matter. The definition of matter is that which is composed of particles. When form is divided there are five types: forms, sounds, odors, tastes, and tangible objects. The first of these five refers to color and shape.  The definition of a sense direct perceiver apprehending a form is: • that which is generated in dependence on its own uncommon empowering condition, the eye sense power, and its observed object condition, a form, and • is a knower that is free from conceptuality and non-mistaken.  Extend this format to the other [sense direct perceivers].  Thus, [the definition of a sense direct perceiver apprehending a sound], etc. is: (1) that which is generated in dependence on its own uncommon empowering condition, the ear sense power, and its observed object condition, a sound, [and (2) is a knower that is free from conceptuality and non-mistaken].  The observed object condition of a sense direct perceiver apprehending a form is the object itself, a form. This definition can be adapted to the other four sense direct perceivers by substituting the specific empowering condition and observed object condition. There are five physical sense powers or empowering conditions: the eye, ear, nose, tongue, and body sense powers. Their five respective objects or observed object conditions a r e: forms, sounds, odors, tastes, and tangible objects.  (2) Mental direct perceivers  With respect to the second, mental direct perceivers, there are two parts: (1) definition and (2) divisions. 1. Definition First, the definition of a mental direct perceiver is: • that which is generated in dependence on a mental sense power that is its own uncommon empowering condition and  • is a consciousness that is an other knower that is free from conceptuality and non-mistaken.  The uncommon empowering condition of a mental direct perceiver is the mental sense power. This school asserts other-knowers and self-knowers, that something is an “other knower” means that it knows something other than itself.  2. Divisions Second, when mental direct perceivers are divided there are three:  (1) valid cognizers that are mental direct perceivers,  (2) subsequent cognizers that are mental direct perceivers, and  (3) awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained that are mental direct perceivers.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 12-16 September 2005 (01)  
20 The first, [a valid cognizer that is a mental direct perceiver,] is, for example, the first moment of a clairvoyance that knows another’s mind.   The second, [a subsequent cognizer that is a mental direct perceiver,] is, for example, the second moment of a clairvoyance that knows another’s mind.   The third, [an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained that is a mental direct perceiver,] is, for example, a mental direct perceiver apprehending a sound in the continuum of a person whose mind is especially attracted to a beautiful form.  The third example indicates t h a t  “ t o  a p p r e h e n d ”  does not mean “to ascertain” since t h i s  i s  a n  awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained, whereas it does apprehend its object. For example, a consciousness can apprehend a truly existent self but it does not ascertain such a self because to ascertain means to realize. To apprehend (‘dzin pa) and to know (rig pa) are similar. All wrong consciousnesses, for example, apprehend their objects but they do not realize them.   Thursday morning, 15 September 2005 (3) Self-knowing direct perceivers  With respect to the third, self-knowing direct perceivers, there are two: (1) definitions and (2) divisions. 1. Definitions First, the definition of a self-knower is: that which has the aspect of an apprehender.  All consciousnesses have an aspect. Just as a mirror reflects an object so too does a consciousness take on the aspect of its object. In the case of a self-knower, this consciousness has the aspect of an apprehender, that is, of a consciousness.  Knowers are divided into self-knowers and other-knowers. An object other than consciousness is ascertained by other-knowing valid cognizers. What ascertains those valid cognizers? They are ascertained by a self-knower. Thus, by the power of a self-knower, a valid cognizer is ascertained to be a valid cognizer. By the power of the valid cognizer itself, an object is ascertained. These are respectively what are referred to as self-knowers and other-knowers.  An “apprehender” is an awareness, a consciousness, o r  a mind. Because a self-knower experiences an apprehender directly, it is said to have the aspect of an apprehender (that is, of a consciousness that apprehends).   The definition of a self-knowing direct perceiver is: that which has the aspect of an apprehender, is free from conceptuality, and is non-mistaken.  A self-knower that has the aspect of an apprehender and is also non-conceptual and non-mistaken is a self-knowing direct perceiver.  2. Divisions Second, when [self-knowing direct perceivers] are divided there are three:  (1) valid cognizers that are self-knowing direct perceivers,  (2) subsequent cognizers that are self-knowing direct perceivers, and  (3) awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained that are self-knowing direct perceivers.   The first [a self-knowing direct valid cognizer] is, for example, the first moment of a self-knowing direct perceiver that experiences an eye consciousness.   
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 12-16 September 2005 (01)  
21 The second [a self-knowing direct subsequent cognizer] is, for example, the second moment of a self-knowing direct perceiver that experiences an eye consciousness.   Examples of the third [an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained that is a self-knowing direct perceiver] are: • a self-knowing direct perceiver in the continuum of a Samkhya that experiences happiness as being a consciousness,  • a self-knowing direct perceiver in the continuum of a Vaisheshika that experiences happiness as being a consciousness, and  • a self-knower in the continuum of a Nihilist that experiences an inferential cognizer as being a valid cognizer.  The first instant of a self-knowing direct perceiver that experiences an eye consciousness is a self-knowing direct valid cognizer.2 T h e  s e c o n d  i n s t a n t  o n w a r d  i s  a  s e l f-knowing direct subsequent cognizer.  (  There are three examples of an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained that are s e l f-knowing direct perceivers. The first example is a self-knowing direct perceiver in the continuum of a Samkhya (an Enumerator) that experiences happiness as being a consciousness. This is so because the Samkyas do not accept that happiness feeling is a consciousness. Because happiness is a consciousness, it must be experienced by a self-knower to be a consciousness, but in the case of a Samkya it is not ascertained by it to be a consciousness. In other words, although the Samkyas do accept happiness, they do not accept that it is consciousness. Because of this their self-knowers do experience happiness a s  b e i n g  a  c o n s c i o u s n e s s , b u t  t h e y  d o  n ot ascertain it to be a consciousness because for them happiness is not a  c o n s c i o u s n e s s .  Thus, a self-knower in the continuum of a Samkyas experiences happiness but does not ascertain it as being consciousness. While a s e l f-knower necessarily ascertains consciousness as being consciousness or a valid cognizer as being a valid cognizer as t h a t  is i t s  f u n c t i o n ,  b e c a u s e  t h e  S a m k y a s  d o  n o t  a c c e p t  happiness as being consciousness a self-knower in their continua experiences the consciousness that is happiness but it does not ascertain it as being consciousness. The Sautrantikas say that it is possible to remember consciousnesses or minds only because there exist self-knowers t h a t  experience them. Therefore, while the Samkyas remember happiness, they do not remember happiness as being consciousness because they do not ascertain happiness as being consciousness. On the other hand, in the case of the Sautrantikas their self-knowers ascertain happiness as being consciousness and later on they can remember that happiness which is a consciousness.   The third example is a self-knower in the continuum of a Nihilist that experiences an inferential cognizer as being a valid cognizer. This school does not assert inferential cognizers as being valid cognizers because they accept that only direct perceivers are valid cognizers. However, inferential cognizers that are valid cognizers do exist in their continua since they exist in everyone’s continua. For example, the consciousness that d e d u c e s  t h e  e x i s t e n c e  o f  f i r e based on the reason of seeing smoke is an inferential cognizer. Thus, it is not necessary to be trained in reasoning in order to have an inferential cognizer. Therefore, for Nihilists and those who have not studied philosophy there exists a self-knower in their continua that experiences an inferential cognizer but that self-knower does not ascertain the inferential cognizer as being a valid cognizer.  (4) Yogic direct perceivers  With respect to the fourth, yogic direct perceivers, there are two: (1) definition and (2) divisions. 1. Definition First, the definition of a yogic direct perceiver is: • that which is generated in dependence on its own uncommon empowering condition, a meditative stabilization that is a union of calm abiding and special insight, and                                                         2 BP English translator: Note that in this particular context of a self-knower the word “experience” (nyams su myong) is used. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 12-16 September 2005 (01)  
22 • is an other-knowing exalted knower in the continuum of a Superior that is free from conceptuality and non-mistaken.  In order for a yogic direct perceiver to arise a particular empowering condition is necessary. Unlike the case of sense direct perceivers which take their respective s e n s e  p o w e r s  a s  t h e i r  empowering conditions, the empowering condition of a yogic direct perceiver is a meditative stabilization that is a union of calm abiding (samatha) and special insight (vipassana).   Although most of us tend to like the idea of achieving calm abiding and special insight, they by themselves are not particularly special since they exist in all those who have achieved the form and formless realms and in all those who have achieved a concentration or formless absorption. Only when calm abiding and special insight a r e  u s e d  t o  f o c u s  o n  e m p t i n e s s  d o e s  t h i s  u n i o n  o f  c a l m  abiding and special insight become special and meaningful.   While any intelligent person can gain an understanding of emptiness, initially it is not possible to remain focused on this understanding because the mind is dominated by afflictions that have existed in it since beginningless time. In order to overcome them, it is necessary to develop calm abiding. Then if, based on that calm abiding, one analyzes emptiness, one will be able to remain focused on it for a long time. Initially emptiness appears by way of a meaning generality or mental image. In other words, emptiness and the meaning generality of emptiness appear to be mixed since this is still a conceptual consciousness. Then gradually the meaning generality fades and at the same time emptiness or the no-self of persons gradually becomes clearer and clearer until all conceptual consciousnesses cease and there is a an appearance that is not mixed with a meaning generality.   Therefore, what is it that prevents the mind from abiding single pointedly on emptiness? It is the afflictions that have existed in the mind since beginningless time. There are different levels of afflictions, b u t f i r s t  o f  a l l  t h e  c o a r s e  ones have to be eliminated. These c o a r s e  a f f l i c t i o n s  a r e  controlled by controlling one’s actions of body and speech. On this basis, o n e t h e n e n g a g e s  i n  cultivating calm abiding in order to control the more subtle afflictions. After that, by focusing on emptiness one gradually becomes completely free from all the afflictions. In this way one progress successively through the three higher trainings of morality, concentration, and wisdom. Thus, the higher training in morality involves subduing or controlling one’s actions of body and speech, the higher training of concentration involves the attainment of calm abiding, and the higher training in wisdom involves the realization of emptiness. In this way the higher trainings follow one another. 2. Divisions  When [yogic direct perceivers] are divided there are two:  (1) valid cognizers that are yogic direct perceivers and  (2) subsequent cognizers that are yogic direct perceivers.  There are no awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained that are yogic direct perceivers because whatever is a yogic direct perceiver necessarily ascertains its object of comprehension. This is because Dharmakirti’s Commentary on (Dignaga’s) “Compendium on Valid Cognition” says, “From just seeing, the great intelligent ones ascertain all aspects.”  There is no yogic direct perceiver that is an awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained because a yogic direct perceiver necessarily ascertains its object of comprehension. This i s  supported by Dharmakirti who says “From just seeing, the great intelligent ones ascertain all aspects.”  Although subsequent cognizers that are yogic direct perceivers exist, the second moment and so forth of an omniscient exalted wisdom are not subsequent cognizers because whatever is an omniscient exalted wisdom is necessarily a valid cognizer. This is so because Gyel-tsap’s Explanation of (Dharmakirti’s) “Commentary on (Dignaga’s) ‘Compendium on Valid Cognition’”: Unmistaken Illumination of the Path to Liberation says, “No matter how much I turn inside and think about it, I do not feel that an omniscient exalted wisdom is not pervaded by being a new realizer,” [in other words, an omniscient exalted wisdom is necessarily a new realizer].

23  Also Kay-drup Rin-bo-chay’s Clearing Away Darkness of Mind with Respect to the Treatises on Valid cognition s a y s ,  “ I f  [ s o m e t h i n g ]  b e c a m e  a  s u b s e q u e n t  c o g n i z e r  m e r e l y  t h r o u g h  [ i t s  object’s] being apprehended by a former valid cognizer, it would follow that the second and subsequent moments of an omniscient exalted wisdom would be subsequent cognizers. There exist many such flaws as will be indicated below.”  There do exist subsequent cognizers that are yogic direct perceivers, but the second instant onward of an omniscient mind are not subsequent cognizers because all instants of omniscient mind are necessarily valid cognizers. Gyeltsab Je says that no matter how much he thinks about it, he does not feel that an omniscient exalted wisdom is not pervaded by being a new realizer.   Kedrub Je says that if something is a subsequent cognizer merely because it comes after an earlier valid cognizer, it would follow that the second instant onward of omniscient mind would be a subsequent cognizer. Since the definition of a subsequent cognizer is “a knower that realizes that which has already been realized,” it would seem that something is in fact a subsequent cognizer merely because it comes after an earlier valid cognizer. T h u s , if this were so, it would follow that t h e  s e c o n d  i n stant onward of omniscient mind would be a subsequent c o g n i z e r  because it comes after an earlier valid cognizer. If we take, for example, the meditative equipoise of the path of seeing directly realizing the no-self of persons, this path of seeing has a beginning and an end. The first instant of that path of seeing is a valid cognizer directly realizing the no-self of persons, its object. Because the second instant onward realize the same object, the mind is realizing that which has already been realized and is therefore a subsequent cognizer. However, in the case of omniscient mind it is not like this. The first instant of omniscient mind realizes all phenomena and the second instant of omniscient mind newly apprehends all phenomena. It is similar to the fact that our mental continuum of yesterday and our mental continuum of today are one but the our mental continuum of today is new.  An omni sci ent  consci ousness real i zes t he fi rst  i nst ant  of vase, then the second instant of that omniscient consciousness experiences the first instant of vase and in addition also experiences the second instant and is therefore not a subsequent cognizer. (B) Direct valid cognizers The definition of a direct valid cognizer is a new incontrovertible knower that is free of conceptuality.  The d e f i n i t i o n  o f  d i r e c t  v a l i d  c o g n i z e r  is based on t h e  d e f i n i t i o n  o f  v a l i d  c o g n i z e r  as “a new incontrovertible knower,” to which is added the words “that is free from conceptuality” because it is a direct perceiver.   STUDENT: The definition of direct perceiver also includes the word “non-mistaken.” Why is “non-mistaken” not included in the definition of direct valid cognizer? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is because whatever is a new incontrovertible knower that is free from conceptuality is necessarily non-mistaken. Because of this, it is not necessary to mention the word “non-mistaken” here, whereas it necessary to include it in the definition of a direct perceiver.  STUDENT: T h e r e f o r e ,  it would seem that if something i s  i n c o n t r o v e r t i b l e  i t  i s  n e c e s s a rily non-mistaken. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A n  i n f e r e ntial valid cognizer is incontrovertible but it is also mistaken because it is mistaken with respect to its appearing object (a meaning generality). The word “non-mistaken” must be included in the definition of a direct perceiver in addition to “free from conceptuality” because there exist non-conceptual wrong consciousnesses. STUDENT: Then the definition of direct valid cognizer should a l s o  i n c l u d e t h e  w o r d  “non-mistaken.” ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: That which is “a new incontrovertible knower that is free of conceptuality” is necessarily non-mistaken. STUDENT: Why is the word “non-mistaken” not included in the definition of direct valid cognizer since the fact that it is incontrovertible is not sufficient to make it non-mistaken? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is only when the two “ i n c o n t r o v e r t i b le” and “free from conceptuality” are mentioned together that there is no need to add the word “non-mistaken.” This is because that which is incontrovertible is not necessarily non-mistaken, for example, an inferential valid cognizer. Likewise, that which is free from conceptuality is not necessarily non-mistaken, for example, a wrong sense consciousness such as that perceiving a blue snow mountain. However, when “incontrovertible” and “free from conceptuality” are mentioned together, the word “non-mistaken” is not necessary. For the Sautrantika school, direct perceivers cannot be mistaken consciousnesses.  STUDENT: Why does the word “non-mistaken” need to be included in the definition of direct perceiver if it is only conceptual consciousnesses that are mistaken? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A l l  c o n c eptual consciousness perceive their objects by means of a meaning generality. They are mistaken because they are mistaken with respect to their appearing object (a meaning generality). Although a conceptual consciousness is mistaken with respect to its appearing object, it is not mistaken with respect to its object of engagement. For example, a conceptual consciousness apprehending vase i s  n o t  m i s t a k e n  w i t h  r e s p e c t  t o  i t s  o b j e c t  o f  engagement, vase, because vase exists. Nor is it mistaken with respect to its object of the mode of apprehension and with respect to its determined object. However, it is mistaken with respect to its appearing object, the meaning generality of vase.  STUDENT: Is a wrong consciousness necessarily mistaken? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is.
