25 (1) self-knowing direct valid cognizers,  (2) sense direct valid cognizers,  (3) mental direct valid cognizers, and  (4) yogic direct valid cognizers.   Thus, there are four divisions of direct valid cognizers.  (1) Self-knowing direct valid cognizers From among these, the definition of the first [a self-knowing direct valid cognizer] is: a new incontrovertible knower, free from conceptuality, that is directed only inward and is just an apprehender.  A self-knowing direct valid cognizer is directed only inward and does not focus on external objects such as forms but only focuses on an apprehender.   STUDENT: Are there as many self-knowers as there are consciousnesses, that is, one for the eye consciousness, one for the ear consciousness, and so forth, or is there just one self-knower? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: There are as many self-knowers as there are other-knowers since each other-knower has a self-knower that knows it.  STUDENT: So it is not that self-knowers know themselves as the name would imply, but rather they know another consciousness? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is not that a self-knower knows itself. It knows a consciousness or a knower. STUDENT: So a self-knower knows another consciousness? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Whatever is a consciousness, a knower, and so forth is said to have a corresponding self-knower that experiences it. For example, an eye consciousness apprehending a form has a self-knower that experiences it. However, since a self-knower that experiences an eye consciousness is itself a consciousness, if it were true that all consciousnesses are experienced by a self-knower, t h e n  t h a t s e l f-knower should have its respective self-knower, then that self-knower should have its respective self-knower, and on and on, without end. But it is probably not like that according to this school. Rather there is a self-knower that knows an eye consciousness but there is no self-knower that knows the self-knower.   Hence, when a particular assertion is examined in detail problems are found. Some years ago there was a meeting of scholars, b o t h Indian and Tibetan, in Varanasi. One well-known I n d i a n  scholar of t h e  C h i t t a m a t r a  school asserted that the s e l f-knower that experiences an e y e  consciousness is itself e y e  c o n s c i o u s n e s s. However, that cannot be the case because if that s e l f-knower were eye consciousness it would have to experience form, that is, shape and color, since that is what an eye consciousness experiences. While there are those of the Chittamatra school who assert self-knower, the Vaibhashikas do not assert self-knowers but assert truly existent external phenomena. The Prasangikas also do not assert self-knowers. STUDENT: If the Sanskri t term for self-knower corresponds exactly with the English translation “self-knower,” the very name implies that it is a consciousness that knows itself, for example, an eye consciousness that knows itself, just as the Indian pandit asserted. However, if that part of the eye consciousness is an eye consciousness it must apprehend form, whereas if it apprehends itself it apprehends eye consciousness. It is like cause and effect which are separate yet one can still say that a vase i s a cause and a vase i s an ef f ect .  Cause and effect are successive, so a vase is not cause and effect. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A vase is a cause because it has an effect and it is an effect because it has a cause. But vase is not cause and effect. Are cause and effect separate or the same? STUDENT: They are separate. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Is vase one or separate? STUDENT: One. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: So how can vase be cause and effect? Whatever is cause is an effect and whatever is an effect is a cause but cause and effect are separate entities. How can those that are separate entities be one? STUDENT: In a relationship of cause and effect they are separate.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Father and son are cause and effect but father is not cause and effect. They are two separate entities. STUDENT: I accept. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: C a u s e  a n d  e f f e c t  a r e  s e p a r a t e  b u t  r e l a t e d ,  t h e y  c a n n o t  e x i s t  a t  t h e  same time. Although vase can be cause and vase can be effect it is not right to say that vase is cause and effect.

28 (2) Sense direct valid cognizers The definition of the second, [a sense direct valid cognizer,] is: a new incontrovertible knower, free from conceptuality, that arises in dependence upon a physical sense power that is its uncommon empowering condition.  When [sense direct valid cognizers] are divided, there are five: (1-5) sense direct valid cognizers apprehending forms and so forth.  Thus, there are five sense direct valid cognizers that apprehend the five sense objects. (3) Mental direct valid cognizers The definition of the third, [a mental direct valid cognizer,] is: a new incontrovertible knower, free from conceptuality, that arises in dependence upon a mental sense power that is its uncommon empowering condition.  When [mental direct valid cognizers] are divided, there are six: (1-6) mental direct valid cognizers apprehending forms and so forth.   The six mental direct valid cognizers respectively apprehend forms, sounds, odors, tastes, tangible objects, and phenomena (dharmas).  Friday morning, 16 September 2005 (4) Yogic direct valid cognizers The definition of the fourth, [a yogic direct valid cognizer,] is: an other-knowing exalted knower in the continuum of a Superior that, in dependence upon a meditative stabilization that is a union of calm abiding and special insight and is its [uncommon] empowering condition, newly and directly realizes either subtle impermanence or the coarse or subtle selflessness of persons.  When [yogic direct valid cognizers] are divided there are three:  (1) valid cognizers directly realizing subtle impermanence,  (2) valid cognizers directly realizing the coarse selflessness of persons, and  (3) valid cognizers directly realizing the subtle selflessness of persons.   This subject was covered earlier on in the context of yogic direct perceivers. At that time the empowering condition (a meditative stabilization that is a union of calm abiding and special insight) was described and yogic direct perceivers were said not to have a division of awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained and so forth.  (C) Facsimiles of a direct perceiver  The explanation of facsimiles of a direct perceiver has two parts: (1) definition and (2) divisions. 1. Definition First, the definition of a facsimile of a direct perceiver is: a knower that is mistaken with regard to its appearing object.  [Facsimile of a direct perceiver] and mistaken consciousness are synonyms.   A facsimile, literally appearing like (ltar snang), is that which appears to be something that it is not. Thus, a facsimile of a direct perceiver would be something that looks like a direct perceiver but is 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 12-16 September 2005 (01)  
29 not. In fact a facsimile of a direct perceiver is the opposite of a direct perceiver. Likewise, in the context of reasoning there are said to be correct signs and facsimiles of correct signs, which are not correct signs but appear to be them.  2. Divisions Second, when [facsimiles of a direct perceiver] are divided, there are seven because there are: (1-6) six conceptual facsimiles of a direct perceiver and  (7) one non-conceptual facsimiles of a direct perceiver. (1-6) Conceptual facsimiles of a direct perceiver The first six [conceptual facsimiles of a direct perceiver] exist because there are: (1) mistaken conceptions,  (2) conventional conceptions,  (3) inferential conceptions,  (4) conceptions arisen from inference,  (5) memory conceptions, and  (6) wishing conceptions.  Illustrations are, respectively,  • of the first, [a mistaken conception], a conceptual consciousness apprehending sound as permanent;  • of the second, [a conventional conception], an inferential cognizer that realizes sound to be impermanent;  • of the third, [an inferential conception], a conceptual consciousness that is a mind apprehending a sign;  • of the fourth, [a conception arisen from inference], a conceptual consciousness that arises after an inferential cognizer;  • of the fifth, [a memory conception], a conceptual consciousness that today remembers an object of the past, and,  • of the sixth, [a wishing conception], a conceptual consciousness that today wishes for an object of the future.  A mistaken conception is for example, a conceptual consciousness apprehending sound as permanent. Such a conception is mistaken with respect to its object of engagement and is therefore both a  w r ong consciousness and a mistaken c o n s c i o u s n e s s. It is mistaken w i t h  r e s p e c t  t o  i t s  appearing object because a l l  c o n c e p t u a l  c o n s c i o u s n e s s e s  a r e  m i s t a k e n  w i t h  r e s p e c t  t o  t h e i r  appearing objects, b u t  in this case it is also mistaken w i t h  r e s p e c t  t o  i t s  o b j e c t  of engagement, sound as permanent, since sound is impermanent, due to which it is a wrong consciousness.   A conventional conception, for example, an inferential cognizer that realizes sound to be impermanent, is mistaken with respect to its appearing object. Although it realizes sound as impermanent, i t  i s  s t i l l  a  m i s t a k e n  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  b e c a u s e  i t  i s  m i s t a k e n  w i t h  r e s p e c t  t o  i t s  appearing object, a meaning generality. The fifth, a memory conception (dran pa’i rtog pa), is, for example, a conceptual consciousness that today remembers an object of the past. In other words, it is just memory. The eye consciousness has the clear appearance of two moons and in that respect the consciousness is valid, but are there two moons? There are not. This is the Prasangikas’ assertion, not the Sautrantikas.  The sixth, a wishing conception, is a conceptual consciousness that today wishes for an object of the future. (7) Non-conceptual facsimiles of a direct perceiver There are many non-conceptual facsimiles of a direct perceiver.   From among the two, [non-conceptual facsimiles of a direct perceiver]  (1) that are mental consciousnesses and  (2) that are sense consciousnesses,  an illustration of the first was explained above.   The text says “an illustration of the first was explained above” but where was it explained above? While conceptual facsimiles of a direct perceiver were explained directly above, I cannot find an earlier illustration of non-conceptual facsimiles of a direct perceiver that are mental consciousnesses. Thus, it is difficult to find an explanation above unless it is to be understood to be deduced from the explanation of conceptual facsimiles of a direct perceivers. However, later on Purbujog says: “The first, a mental non-conceptual wrong consciousness is, for example, a dream consciousness that clearly sees as blue the blue of a dream.” This would seem to be an example of a non-conceptual facsimile of a direct perceiver that is a mental consciousness because a dream consciousness is a mental consciousness. So perhaps there is just a mistake in the Tibetan that says “an illustration of the first was explained above” and instead it should say that it is explained below.

32  With regard to the second, [non-conceptual facsimiles of a direct perceiver that are sense consciousnesses], there are four:  (1) the cause of error existing in the basis,  (2) the cause of error existing in the abode,  (3) the cause of error existing in the object, and  (4) the cause of error existing in the immediately preceding condition.   The divisions of non-conceptual facsimiles of a direct perceiver that are sense consciousnesses are based on the cause of the error, which can be in the basis (the sense power), the abode, the object, or the immediately preceding condition.   The first, [the cause of error existing in the basis], is, for example, an eye impaired by obscuring disease, for a sense consciousness that sees one moon as two is produced in dependence upon that.   Also due to a certain disease that affects the eye, one sees hairs falling in front of one.  The second, [the cause of error existing in the abode], is, for example, sitting in a boat, for a sense consciousness that sees trees as moving is produced in dependence upon that.   When sitting in a moving car, train, and so forth, it appears that it is the trees and so forth outside that are moving.  The third, [the cause of error existing in the object], is, for example, a quickly whirling firebrand, for a sense consciousness that sees a firebrand as a wheel is produced in dependence upon that.   In this case the cause of the error is in the object itself, for example, when o n e  wh i r l s  a  s t i c k  o f  incense quickly in the dark a circle of fire appears.   The fourth, [the cause of error existing in the immediately preceding condition], is, for example, a mind disturbed by hatred, for a sense consciousness that sees the earth as red is produced in dependence upon that.  When one is disturbed by hatred things appear to be red. Here the cause of the error is the immediately preceding condition that precedes the eye consciousness.   Non-conceptual wrong consciousness, non-conceptual facsimile of a direct perceiver, and consciousness that has a clear appearance of a non-existent are synonymous.  Here the term “clear appearance” refers only to non-conceptual consciousnesses.  STUDENT: Why are these facsimiles of direct perceivers set out under valid cognizers since they are not valid cognizers? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Having first explained direct perceivers which are necessarily non-mistaken, now their opposites are explained. Just because something is included under a particular section of a text, i t  d o e s  n o t  necessarily have to be that s u b j e c t  u n d e r  d i s c u s s i o n ,  T h u s ,  these facsimiles of direct perceivers a r e  n o t  v a l i d  c o g n i z e r s  b u t  a r e  i n c l u d e d  i n  t h e  s e c t i o n on valid cognizers. There is another t e x t  t h a t  e x p l a i n s  d i r e c t  v a l i d  c o g n i z e r s  a n d  i m m e d i a t e l y  a f t e r w a r d  there comes an explanation of facsimiles of direct perceiver. We need to understand what appear to be direct perceivers but are not in order to thoroughly understand direct perceivers. Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 12-16 September 2005 (01)  
33 GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The etymology of the word “facsimile” means to appear like, but it is not that that which a p p e a r s  t o  b e  a  d i r e c t  p e r c e i v e r  h a s  t o  b e  exactly like a direct perceiver. A conceptual consciousness of something has a similarity to a direct perceiver, for example, seeing a vase and thinking about a vase are similar. Perhaps it is because when one thinks about vase it is as if one is seeing it. (D) Inferential valid cognizers The definition of an inferential valid cognizer is: a new incontrovertible determinative knower that is directly produced in dependence on the correct sign that is its basis.  An inferential valid cognizer is new, incontrovertible, and a determinative knower that is directly produced in dependence on a correct sign or reason. The words “determinative knower” indicate that it is a conceptual consciousness and thereby exclude an inferential valid cognizer from being a non-conceptual consciousness. H o w e v e r ,  this definition may not be quite right in terms of the words “directly produced.” For example, the syllogism “Sound is impermanent because it is a product” contains the three modes and the person hearing it understands t h a t  w h a t e v e r  i s  impermanent is a product and so forth. However quite a lot of time can pass between hearing that correct sign or reason and the generation of an inferential valid cognizer apprehending sound to be impermanent. An inferential valid cognizer must arise subsequent to a correct sign or reason since it does not arise at the same time, but it does not necessarily arise immediately after it. However the words “directly produced” would seem to mean that an inferential valid cognizer arises in the very next instant after t h a t  c o r r e c t  s i g n, w h e r e a s  i t  i s  n o t  n e c e s s a r i l y  l i k e  t h a t  a s  t h e  i n f e r e n t i a l  valid cognizer can follow a long thought process. Therefore, it would seem to be incorrect to say “directly” although this word is also present in the Tibetan text. Therefore, it is best to just delete the word “directly” in the definition of an inferential valid cognizer so that it just reads: “a new incontrovertible determinative knower that is produced in dependence on the correct sign that is its basis.” This definition is correct.   To aid the generation of an inferential valid cognizer an illustration is often given such as in the syllogism: “Sound is impermanent because it is a product, for example, a vase.” This is because it is easier to understand the impermanence of the vase than the impermanence of sound. In short, the process of examining a correct reason can take a long time before it leads to the generation of an inferential valid cognizer. It is not that just because someone sets out a reason there arises an immediate understanding. That there exists “a perfect person to whom a reason is to be presented” (a fully-fledged other party) indicates t h at it cannot just be presented to anyone. Such a perfect person to whom a reason is to be presented can be oneself or someone else. If we take the syllogism “Sound is impermanent because it is a product,” the perfect person to whom this reason is to be presented is someone who understands (1) that sound is a product and (2) that a product is impermanent, but he does not yet understand that sound is impermanent. This person should be presented with the statement “Sound is impermanent because it is a product, like a vase,” whereby he then understands that sound, l i k e  vase, i s  i m p e r m a n e n t .  M a n y  y e a r s  c a n  p a s s  b e f o r e  o n e  becomes the perfect person to whom this reason is to be presented (a fully-fledged other party).   THE END

34 Monday afternoon, 19 September 2005  [Note: All indented text is the translation Explanation of the Presentation of Objects and Object-Possessors as well as Awarenesses and Knowers by Elizabeth Napper.]  There is a prayer that is to be recited before one begins to give teachings in which one says “May I be able to teach in all languages,” but as I need a translator I better be careful when I say that as otherwise the translator may get up and go!  We are talking about inferential valid cognizers.  When [inferential valid cognizers] are divided, there are three:  (1) inferential cognizers by the power of the fact,  (2) inferential cognizers through renown, and  (3) inferential cognizers through belief.   An illustration of the first, [an inferential cognizer by the power of the fact], is an inferential cognizer that realizes that sound is impermanent through the sign of being a product.   Inferential cognizers by the power of the fact indicates that this inferential cognizer is generated in dependence on logic, that is, it is not based on scriptural citations and so forth. For example, the generation of the inferential valid cognizer realizing sound is impermanent by the sign “because it is a product” is generated by the power of the fact. Thus this inferential cognizer differs from inferential cognizers through renown and inferential cognizers through belief.  An illustration of the second, [an inferential cognizer through renown], is an inferential cognizer that realizes that it is suitable to express the rabbit-possessor by the term “moon” from the sign of its existing among objects of conception.   When we look at the moon from India there appears to be the shape of a rabbit in it, due to which it is commonly called “the rabbit-possessor.” This is what is meant by the power of renown. In other words, it is a common conception, that is, it exists among objects of conception. Thus, the term “moon” is suitable to refer to the rabbit-possessor because it exists among objects of conception.   An illustration of the third [an inferential cognizer through belief], is an inferential cognizer that realizes that the scripture, “From giving, resources; from ethics, a happy [migration],” is incontrovertible with respect to the meaning indicated by it by the sign of its being a scripture free from the three contradictions.  An inferential cognizer through renown is necessarily an inferential cognizer by the power of the fact. Also whatever is a direct perceiver is not necessarily a direct valid cognizer because the second moment of a sense direct perceiver apprehending a form is not a valid cognizer. That follows because that [i.e., the second moment of a sense direct perceiver apprehending a form] and the second moment of an inferential cognizer that realizes that sound is impermanent are subsequent cognizers. This is because Dharmottara’s The Correct s a y s ,  “ T h e  t w o ,  t h e  f i r s t  moment of a direct perceiver and the first moment of inferential cognizer, are valid cognizers, but subsequent moments in the continuums of those because of being non-different in establishment and abiding, have forsaken being valid cognizers.”  Inferential cognizers through belief are not based on logic, that is, they do not arise from logical reasoning but arise in dependence on scripture. Phenomena that are existents are of three types: manifest phenomena, slightly hidden phenomena, and very hidden phenomena. Manifest phenomena are those that are perceived by sense direct perceivers and thus consist of the five sense objects. Slightly hidden phenomena are emptiness or, in this context, the no-self of persons, impermanence, and so forth. These are not very hidden phenomena because they can be understood by the power of reasoning. Very hidden phenomena are those that can be understood 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 19-23 September 2005 (02)  
35 only by the power of the reasoning of scripture. However, the scripture cannot just be any scripture, it has to be one that is free from the three contradictions.   In the text Signs and Reasons, three correct signs or reasons are set out: a correct sign of nature, a correct sign of result, and a correct sign of non-observation. A correct sign of nature is, for example, “Sound is impermanent because it is a product.” In this case, sound is proved to be impermanent by mentioning something in the sign that is the same nature as impermanent. In other words, impermanent and product are the same nature and product. The person to whom this sign is stated has understood that impermanent and product are one nature and that sound is a product. Thus, a correct sign of nature refers to stating two things that are the same nature.   A correct sign of result is a sign that makes use of the power of the result to prove the existence of the cause. An example is: “There is fire on the mountain pass because there is smoke.” In this case, the existence of fire is proved based on the relationship of cause and result because smoke is the result of its cause, fire.   There are many correct signs by non-observation. The example given in Dharmakirti’s Commentary on [Dignaga’s] “Compendium on Valid Cognition” is that it is not suitable for a person who doubts the existence of a flesh-eating spirit (pishacha, sha za) to say “In front of me there is flesh-eater” because that person doubts the existence of flesh-eaters. This is because that person generally and specifically has not validly cognized the existence of flesh-eaters. This sign is based on not observing something, that is, not having validly cognized the existence of something.   STUDENT: What would an example be of applying a correct sign of non-observation? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: For example, if one has not correctly realized or does not know whether a person has or does not have certain qualities, then one cannot say that he or she has or does not have them. In short, if one has not actually perceived qualities, one should refrain from saying that another person has or does not have them.  STUDENT: It seems that the first two, a correct sign of nature and a correct sign of result, exclude another possibility (that is, it has to be that), but the third does not, whereby it leaves things open, the flesh-eater may or may not be there. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e  f i r s t  c o r r e c t  s i g n  n e g a t e s  p e r m a n e n c e  a n d  e s t a b l i s h e s  impermanence, and the second one establishes the existence of fire and negates the non-existence of fire. The third correct sign uses a negative as a reason “because it is not validly cognized.”   There are many types of correct signs but these three are set out specifically because they correspond with the three types of beings of the stages of the path (lam-rim). A correct sign of result proves a cause by means of the result which is useful when meditating upon the path of lower beings that mainly sets out actions and their results through establishing that suffering comes from non-virtue and happiness from virtue. The way to prove the validity of this teaching is to use results to understand their hidden causes.   On the other hand, a correct sign of nature, which says that something is something else because it has the same nature, is useful for understanding the nature of phenomena. In this case, wrong concepts of how phenomena exist are eliminated by using reasonings such as “Sound is impermanent because it is product” to understand that sound is not permanent. This corresponds with the path of middling beings which mainly focuses on understanding the nature of phenomena in order to achieve freedom from cyclic existence.   The third correct sign, that of non-observation, is mainly used to train in not judging and disparaging others by coming to understand that we cannot perceive the qualities in others’ minds, whereby we come to value others and to wish to work for their welfare. In this way, these three correct signs are said to correspond with the three paths of practitioners of the lam-rim.   An inferential valid cognizer through belief or trust is generated through the power of scripture. However, in order to use statements such as “From giving, resources; from ethics, a happy [migration]” to perceive hidden phenomena, one has to first understand that the person who said that is a valid being. Based on that, one will then understand that what that person said is valid. So how can one come to know that a person is valid as that too is a hidden phenomena? One needs to use an inferential valid cognizer through the power of the fact and an inferential valid cognizer through the power of renown, by means of which one examines whether the person who said “From giving, resources, from ethics, a happy [migration]”is valid or not. Having done this, one 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 19-23 September 2005 (02)  
36 will come to be more and more certain that what he said is valid. Thereby, one will eventually come to validly cognize by means of belief or trust that what he said is valid.   In short, he should be found to be pure by means of the three analyses. One of the reasons that prove that this person is valid is that there is no internal contradiction among the various things he said. Thus, there should be no contradiction with respect to the manifest phenomena that are directly perceived by the sense consciousnesses. There should also be no contradiction with respect to inferential valid cognizers through the power of the fact, which are used to understand slightly hidden phenomena. Then one uses an inferential valid cognizer by the power of belief in order to prove that the person is valid. In this way, one uses experience, logic, and trust.   Thus, if one wants to examine, for example, the words of the Buddha, one first uses experience to understand that what he says is not harmed by a sense direct perceiver. Then one checks whether what he says is harmed by logic, for example, one checks whether sound actually is impermanent. Then one uses trust to prove his validity by looking at what that person said in order to see whether there is any internal contradiction. These are the three kinds of analyses or tests that can be used to determine whether or not someone is a valid person. While initially the use of a inferential valid cognizer by the power of belief is not very powerful, due to not finding contradictions in what someone said, the belief gradually becomes stronger and stronger.   While the subject of karma, a very hidden phenomenon, is included in the path of lower beings, the understanding of it becomes more and more subtle as one progresses through the paths of middling beings and great beings. We can understand in general that generosity brings wealth, morality brings a good rebirth, patience brings beauty, and so forth, but it is much more difficult to point to the precise action of generosity that led to a specific result of wealth and s o  f o r t h .  F o r  example, while in general it is said that patience results in beauty, actions actually have more than one type of result such as fully-ripened results, results similar to the cause, and so forth. In fact there are people who are very beautiful but very angry, and there are others who are very ugly but very patient. These things happen and they are difficult to understand. In fact it is said that the working of karma is inconceivable. Presently we cannot directly perceive the subtle results of karma which mainly occur in the mind. In fact one type of ignorance is the ignorance that does not understand actions and results. In addition, our mind is under the control of afflictions. So, since we do not have a valid cognition of karma but have a mind that is under the control of afflictions, we are in a rather difficult situation.  STUDENT: With respect to an inferential valid cognizer by the power of belief, our date of birth, for example, is a hidden phenomenon so how should we arrive at a belief that we were born on that date? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: We can in general trust our parents when they say that we were born on a certain date. ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: My father believed for many years that he was born on a certain date but, when he eventually went to apply for a birth certificate, he discovered that he was ten days younger than he had been told by his parents!  For a buddha there are no slightly hidden or very hidden phenomena but only manifest phenomena. In order to understand emptiness, we can use a inferential valid cognizer by the power of the fact and apply reasonings and read scriptures, but for us emptiness remains a very hidden phenomena. However, this is not the case for an arya being. Therefore, for some people a very hidden phenomena is only a slightly hidden phenomena. Then, for a buddha all phenomena are manifest phenomena. But, in short, all phenomena in general can be divided into these three types of phenomena.  STUDENT: These three approaches of experience, logic, and belief seem to me to be useful for any type of meditation. When we meditate, we have to make use of these three types of inferential valid cognizers. First we do an analytical meditation using logic and then, when we come to an understanding of it, we focus on it, this being the second type of meditation, stabilizing meditation. There are three types of wisdom: the wisdom arisen from hearing, the wisdom arisen from thinking, and the wisdom arisen from meditation. One initially listens to teachings, whereby the wisdom arisen from hearing is generated. Then one thinks about or contemplates what one has heard. Then, when a valid cognition arises with respect

37 to that one focuses on it and holds it in the mind, whereby the wisdom arisen from meditation is produced.   STUDENT: The text says “three contradictions,” what are they? STUDENT: What is the purpose of the correct sign of non-observation in the example of the flesh-eater? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is that one cannot definitely say that there is a flesh-eater there. Tuesday morning, 20 September 2005 (E) Terminological divisions of valid cognizers  (1) Inference for oneself and for another Furthermore, when [valid cognizers] are terminologically divided there are two:  (1) inference for oneself and  (2) inference for another.   The first [inference for oneself] and inferential cognizer are synonyms.  The second [inference for another] and correct proof statement are synonyms.  A “terminological division” is an etymological division, not an actual division because, although inference for oneself is a valid cognizer, inference for others is not. Inference for oneself is an inference that increases one’s own understanding; it is synonymous with inferential cognizer. Inference for another, on the other hand, is a correct proof statement that is set out to someone else, such as “Whatever is a product is impermanent” and “Sound is a product.” It is speech that proves or establishes something. Thus it is a case of giving the name “inference” to that which is in fact speech and is not actually an inferential valid cognizer.   (2) Ancillarily, minds apprehending a sign  Ancillarily, with respect to explaining minds apprehending a sign, there are two parts: (1) definition and (2) divisions. 1. Definition The definition of something’s being a mind apprehending a signErrore. Il segnalibro non è definito. in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign product is: it is a knower – in the continuum of a full-fledged other party for whom sound is being proved to be impermanent by the sign product – that is a common locus of: • being incontrovertible with respect to either that sound is a product or that whatever is a product is necessarily impermanent and also  • being the cause of an inferential cognizer realizing that sound is impermanent by the sign product and arises in dependence upon its acting as a causal condition.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 19-23 September 2005 (02)  
38 The topic of minds apprehending signs occurs here because we are d i s c u s s i n g  i n f e r e n t i a l  v a l i d  cognizers that arise in dependence on a correct sign or reason. It is important to understand what it means to apprehend signs or reasons since they are the cause for the arisal of an inferential cognizer.   There is a lot to be said about signs but in this particular context we will focus on correct signs. A sign indicates the existence of something else, for example, smoke indicates the existence of fire. In the statement “Sound is impermanent because it is a product,” “product” is the sign or the reason.  If we take, for example, the statement “Sound is impermanent because it is a product” there are three modes or processes that are necessary for it to be a correct sign: (1) the property of the subject, (2) the pervasion, and (3) the reverse pervasion. When these three modes are complete it is a correct sign. However, in addition to these three modes, the person to whom the logical reasoning is presented must be a particular type of person, that is, he must be a full-fledged of fully-qualified other party. In other words, while the statement “Sound is impermanent because it is a product” is in general a correct sign, in order for it to become a correct sign in particular it must be presented to a suitable person. This person has to understand that (1) sound is a product and (2) that whatever is a product is impermanent, but he still has doubts about whether or not sound is impermanent. Such a person is a full-fledged other party with respect to the statement “Sound is impermanent because it is a product.”  For such a full-fledged other party the three modes or processes are complete. The first mode, the property of the subject, is that he ascertains that sound is a product but doubts whether sound is impermanent. The second mode, t h e  p e r v a s i o n ,  i s  t h a t  h e  u n d e r s t a n d s  t h a t  w h a t e v e r  i s  a  product is pervaded by the predicate “impermanent.” The third mode, the reverse pervasion, is that he understands that if it is empty of being product, it is empty of being impermanent. Whatever is a product is the concordant side of impermanent and whatever is a product cannot be the non-concordant side of impermanent in this particular reason. When these three modes are complete, it is a correct sign.   In this particular syllogism “correct sign” only refers to “product.” Since the definition of a correct sign is “that which is the three modes,” the property of the subject, the pervasion, and the reverse pervasion refer only to “product.” However, in addition to this there is the mental process that occurs in the mind of the full-fledged other party.   The three modes or processes are that product i s  t h e  p r o p e r t y  o f  t h e  s u b j e c t ,  a s  w a l l  a s  t h e  pervasion and reverse pervasion that establishes sound to be impermanent. Therefore, although in general it can be said that “product” is the three modes, this is not actually true unless it is presented to a full-fledged other party. Such a person is someone who understands that sound is a product and that whatever is a product is impermanent but doubts whether s o u n d  i s  impermanent. In other words, he understands these singly but has not yet connected them to each other. To do this, he needs to be presented with a correct proof statement: “Whatever is a product is pervaded by being impermanent, for example, a vase. Sound is also product.” This correct proof statement can produce an inferential valid cognizer realizing that sound is impermanent because it is a product. When a correct proof statement presents these reasonings simultaneously, the person is empowered t o  r e a l i z e  t h a t  s o u n d  i s  i m p e r m a n e n t .  I n  o t h e r  w o r d s ,  w h e n  t h a t  c o r r e c t  p r o o f  statement is presented, all these single understandings arise in his mind at the same time.   Therefore, first of all with respect to the correct sign “product,” the full-fledged other party understands that whatever is a product is impermanent, then he is presented with a correct proof statement, and then he realizes by way of an inferential valid cognizer that sound is impermanent. Hence, contrary to what was said earlier on in the text in the context of the definition of inferential valid cognizer, an inferential valid cognizer is not “directly produced” in dependence on a correct sign. This is because between the correct sign and the inferential valid cognizer there is the presentation of a correct proof statement. For this reason it is incorrect to say in the definition of an inferential valid cognizer that it is “directly produced in dependence on a correct sign” The word “directly” implies that there is nothing between the cause and the result, whereas here there is an intervening correct proof statement. The inferential valid cognizer is produced through a continuity, such that the cause (a correct sign) and its result (an inferential valid cognizer) are distant from each other. In short, the conclusion is that an inferential valid cognizer is not directly produced from the correct sign.  STUDENT 1: What is the correct proof statement in this case?

39 GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Whatever is a product is pervaded by being impermanent, for example, a vase. Sound is also a product.  STUDENT: Is it a syllogism? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is similar to a syllogism. STUDENT 2: A n  e x a m p l e  o f  a  s y l l o g i s m  i s :  “ A l l  h u m a n s  a r e  m o r t a l .  S o c r a t e s  i s  a  h u m a n .  Therefore, Socrates is mortal.”  There is two statements in this context:  • “Sound is impermanent because it is a product” and • “Whatever is a product is pervaded by being impermanent, for example, a vase. Sound is also a product.”  The first is not a correct proof statement. While it shows the three modes, it is not what makes the three modes arise simultaneously in the mind of a full-fledged other party. For this to happen such a person needs to consider the second statement “Whatever is a product is pervaded by being impermanent, for example, a vase. Sound is also a product.” Only by considering this latter statement are all three modes produced simultaneously. Thus, the second is a correct proof statement  In this context one is striving to prove to another person that sound is impermanent. To do this it is not enough just to make the statement “Sound is impermanent” because the other person has a doubt that is wavering between sound being permanent and sound being impermanent. So just to baldly state “Sound is impermanent” is not sufficient to prove that it is impermanent. To do this, one must destroy his doubt by proving that sound is impermanent through using the procedure just explained. A meaning generality is the superimposition of a conceptual consciousness. Therefore, it is odd to say that a meaning generality is created by the mind. Whenever there is an appearance to a conceptual consciousness, that concept arises through the medium of a meaning generality. It is not that the conceptual consciousness has an object, which is a manifest phenomenon, that is the meaning generality that appears to it.

Tuesday afternoon, 20 September 2005  Someone who is a full-fledged other party, that is, a suitable or qualified other party, of this context must have perceived by means of a valid c o g n i z e r  t h a t  s o u n d  i s  a  p r o d u c t  a n d  t h a t  whatever is a product is pervaded by being impermanent, but still has a doubt regarding sound being impermanent. For him to understand that sound is impermanent it is not enough for him to merely hear a correct proof statement. There are many steps that lead to that understanding.   This person could, for example, be someone who validly cognizes that sound is a product and that whatever is a product is necessarily impermanent but still believes that sound is permanent. Although he has also perceived that whatever is permanent is pervaded by not being a product, he still believes that sound is permanent. One debates with such a person saying: “It follows that sound is not a product because it is permanent.” He cannot argue with this statement because he believes that sound is permanent and that whatever is permanent is not a product. The only consequence is that he may come to wonder whether sound is perhaps impermanent. If this happens, one can say “Sound is impermanent because it is a product.” However, it is not that there

42 immediately arises an understanding of sound as impermanent, this can come about after a lot of thinking.   When one says “It follows that sound is not a product,” one is presenting a correct or perfect consequence. Doubt has two points whereas the view that sound is permanent is one pointed. A correct consequence can destroy the one-pointed certainty that sound is permanent and produce a two-pointed mind that doubts whether sound is impermanent or p e r m a n e n t .  T h e n ,  i n  o r d e r  t o  resolve this doubt, one presents either a correct proof statement or a correct sign, “Sound is impermanent because it is a product.” However, because we cling strongly to our opinions, it is not that the person’s doubt is immediately removed and he generates an inferential valid cognizer realizing that sound is impermanent. Thus, there is a lot of debate with respect to correct signs and correct proof statements, but this is the essence of it. Correct signs are set forth in great detail by Gyeltsab Je in his commentary on Dharmakirti’s Commentary on [Dignaga’s] “Compendium on Valid Cognition.”  STUDENT: Why do these statements have to be presented to a full-fledged other party? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: They not necessarily have to be presented only to a full-fledged other party since you can also present them to yourself as well as to any other person. Even someone who has no understanding of debate presents himself with many correct proof statements in order to generate an inferential valid cognizer, such as the inferential valid cognizer realizing that there is fire over there because there is smoke. He understands that naturally without need for anyone to set out a syllogism.  Minds apprehending a sign have the following divisions. 2. Divisions  When [minds apprehending a sign] are divided, there are two:  (1) minds apprehending a sign – in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign “product” – that comprehend that sound is a product, and  (2) minds apprehending a sign [in the p r o o f  that s o u n d  is impermanent by the sign “product”] that comprehend that whatever is a product is necessarily impermanent.  (1) The definition of the first [something’s being a mind apprehending a sign – in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign “product” – that c o m p r e h e n d s  t h a t  s o u n d  i s  a  product] is: it is a knower – in the continuum of a full-fledged other party for whom sound is being proved to be impermanent by the sign “product” – that is a common locus of: • being incontrovertible with respect to sound as a product and also  • being the cause of an inferential cognizer that realizes that sound is impermanent by the sign “product” and arises in dependence upon its acting as a causal condition.  (2) The definition of the second [something’s being a mind apprehending a sign – i n  t h e  proof that sound is impermanent by the sign “product” – that comprehends that whatever is a product is necessarily impermanent] is: it is a knower – in the continuum of a full-fledged other party for whom sound is being proved to be impermanent by the sign product – that is a common locus of: • being incontrovertible with respect to whatever is a product necessarily being impermanent and also  • being the cause of an inferential cognizer that realizes sound as impermanent by the sign product and arises in dependence upon its acting as a causal condition.  (1) When the first, [minds apprehending a sign – in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign “product” – that comprehend that sound is a product,] are divided, there are three:  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 19-23 September 2005 (02)  
43 (a) direct perceivers that are minds apprehending a sign – in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign “product” – that comprehend that sound is a product,  (b) inferential cognizers that are minds apprehending a sign – in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign “product” – that comprehend that sound is a product, and  (c) subsequent cognizers that are minds apprehending a sign – in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign “product” – that comprehend that sound is a product.   (a) From among these, an illustration of the first, [a direct perceiver that is a mind apprehending a sign – in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign “product” – that comprehends that sound is a product,] is a direct perceiver comprehending t h a t  s o u n d  i s  a  p r o d u c t i n  t h e  continuum of a full-fledged other party for whom it is being proved that sound is impermanent by the sign “product.”  (b) An illustration of the second, [an inferential cognizer that is a mind apprehending a sign – in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign “product” – that comprehends that sound is a product,] is an inferential cognizer comprehending that sound is a product in the continuum of [such a person].  (c) An illustration of the third [a subsequent cognizer that is a mind apprehending a sign – in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign “product” – t h a t  c o m p r e h e n d s  t h a t  s o u n d  i s  a  product] is the second moment and so forth of an inferential cognizer comprehending that sound is a product, in the continuum of [such a person].  To say that there is “a direct perceiver comprehending that sound is a product” is rather strange because for ordinary beings to understand that sound is impermanent it is necessary to rely on reasoning in that only aryas realize impermanence by means of a direct perceiver. When Gedun Drub sets out correct signs of nature and correct signs of result in his Ornament for Knowledge, he says that in the case of correct signs of results there are direct perceivers, for example, the eye consciousness directly perceives smoke and as a result one infers the existence of fire. But it is rather strange that Purbujog’s text says that there are direct perceivers that are minds apprehending a sign – i n  t h e  p r o o f  t h a t  s o u n d  i s  i m p e r m a n e n t  b y  t h e  s ign “product” – t h a t  comprehend that sound is a product. This is because a full-fledged other party is an ordinary being due to which, in order for him to understand that sound is a product, he needs to rely on a correct sign. Perhaps it can be understood to mean that when an ordinary being realizes sound to be a product, although his valid cognizer realizing sound to be a product must be an inferential valid cognizer, the self-knower that experiences that inferential valid cognizer realizing sound to be a product is a direct perceiver. A self-knower is pervaded by being a direct perceiver and it establishes that sound is a product because it experiences the inferential valid cognizer realizing sound to be a product. This is perhaps what is meant here by direct perceivers that are minds apprehending a sign that comprehend that sound is a product. The self-knower establishes sound to be a product but it does not necessarily ascertain sound to be product, although later on it can remember that sound is a product. Because a self-knower only experiences consciousness, it does not validly cognize the object of the consciousness, however it does establish it. Thus, a self-knower does not ascertain sound to be a product since that is not the function of a self-knowing consciousness. In short, the division of direct perceivers that are minds apprehending a sign that comprehend that sound is a product is rather difficult to understand.  STUDENT: Why is there not a direct perceiver comprehending sound to be a product but there is a direct perceiver comprehending the ability to perform a function? For example, when one sees that fire is burning fuel, it is possible to understand that the function of fire is to burn fuel. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Because the impermanence of cup is a slightly hidden phenomenon, in order to perceive it one must rely on reasoning. Yet if one drops the cup and it breaks into pieces, one will understand that it is impermanent. However, that is coarse impermanence, whereas subtle impermanence has to be understood by relying on a sign. Likewise, in order to understand that sound is a product it is necessary to rely on a sign or reason. That sound is a product is also a slightly hidden phenomenon, whereas that a cup functions to hold water can be seen. But by depending on a coarse function as a basis for reasoning, one can go on to understand 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 19-23 September 2005 (02)  
44 the more subtle nature of phenomena. However, do not just believe everything I say, you need to work this out for yourselves. I came to the conclusion myself that this division is incorrect but you need to verify it for yourselves.   (2) Also, with respect to the second [minds apprehending a sign – i n  t h e  p r o o f  o f  s o u n d  a s  impermanent by the sign product – that comprehend that whatever is a product is necessarily impermanent], there are three:  (a) direct perceivers that are minds apprehending a sign – in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign product – that comprehend that whatever is a product is necessarily impermanent,  (b) inferential cognizers that are minds apprehending a sign – in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign product – that comprehend that whatever is a product is necessarily impermanent, and  (c) subsequent cognizers that are minds apprehending a sign – in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign product – that comprehend that whatever is a product is necessarily impermanent.  (a) From among these, an illustration of the first [a direct perceiver that is a mind apprehending a sign – in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign product – that comprehends that whatever is a product is necessarily impermanent] is a direct perceiver comprehending that whatever is a product is necessarily impermanent in the continuum of a full-fledged other party for whom it is being proved that sound is impermanent by the sign product.  (b) An illustration of the second [an inferential cognizer that is a mind apprehending a sign – in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign product – that comprehends that whatever is a product is necessarily impermanent] is an inferential cognizer comprehending that whatever is a product is necessarily impermanent, in the continuum of [such a person].  (c) An illustration of the third [a subsequent cognizer that is a mind apprehending a sign – in the proof that sound is impermanent by the sign product – t h a t  c o m p r e h e n d s  t h a t  w h a t e v e r  i s  a  product is necessarily impermanent] is the second moment, and so forth, of an inferential cognizer comprehending that whatever is a product is necessarily impermanent, in t h e  continuum of [such a person].  Do you have any questions?  STUDENT: T h e  p e r s o n  i s  s a i d  t o  b e  a  non-associated c o m p o s i t i o n a l  f a c t o r  b u t  t h e  p e r s o n  is associated with mind. So what would be a better translation of this word? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e  w ord Tibetan term den m i n  d u  j e (ldan min 'du byed) literally means “compositional factors that do not have,” which refers to their not having form and consciousness. Since a person has consciousness and has form, how can it be a non-associated compositional factor? A person is not by nature consciousness or form, so in this sense the person does not have them or is not them. The term “to not have” (ldan min) can also mean “to not be.” For example, while a direct perceiver is said to be free from conceptuality, in the mind of a person with a direct perceiver there still exists conceptual consciousnesses. Therefore, what this actually means is that from the point of view of its very nature a direct perceiver is not conceptual. So “to not have” can mean to not be something. It is necessary to examine the specific meaning of such words. The tathagata essence, for example, is said to be that which is suitable to transform into the nature body of a buddha and to exist in a mind that has defilements, yet an arya in meditative equipoise directly perceiving emptiness or suchness does not have defilements. Therefore, “to have” can mean either to possess or to be.  ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: Therefore, den min du je c o u l d  b e  t r a n s l a t e d  a s  a  non-possessing compositional factor, meaning that it does not by nature possess or have form and consciousness. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: You are right, there is no difference for Prasangika but they make this distinction based on the Sautrantika view. Thus, for Prasangika a valid cognizer does not have to be a knower that realizes what has already been realized, whereas a subsequent cognizer has to be a knower that realizes what has already been realized. STUDENT: So on what basis is it said to be valid? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is valid just because it realizes, it does not have to be new in order to be valid. In the Prasangika system whatever is a consciousness is a valid cognizer with respect to its own appearing object or with respect to its appearance. STUDENT: Is there a difference between appearing object and appearance? ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: Appearing object and appearance are the same. STUDENT: Subtle impermanence appears to a sense consciousness but is it the appearing object of a sense consciousness? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It appears but it is not the appearing object. STUDENT: Therefore, there is no pervasion that if it appears it is the appearing object. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is not a valid cognizer with respect to all aspects of what appears. For example, true existence appears to a sense consciousness but it does not mean that a valid cognizer is valid with respect to every aspect that appears. It is said that a valid cognizer is valid with respect to its appearance and when this is argued it seems to be the same as saying that it is valid with respect to its appearing object.  STUDENT: Because true existence appears to a sense consciousness it must be a functioning thing. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: B e c a u s e  a  b l u e  s n o w  m o u n t a i n  a n d  s o  f o r t h  a p p e a r  t o  a  s e n s e  consciousness, it does not mean that they are functioning things.   STUDENT: In order to be a valid cognizer it must be incontrovertible with respect to its appearing object but it seems that it does not have to be incontrovertible with respect to every aspect that appears. With respect to what is it incontrovertible if it does not have to be incontrovertible with respect to every aspect that appears? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: When vase appears to eye consciousness, the impermanence of vase also appears but is not realized, therefore the eye consciousness cannot be an incontrovertible knower with respect to impermanence. STUDENT: The impermanence of sound appears to my ear consciousness. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is not valid with respect to every aspect that appears to it. It is only valid with respect to sound. For example, although one perceives existents, one does not perceive everything that exists.  STUDENT: So the ear consciousness is not valid with respect to the impermanence of sound? ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: That is right. A valid cognizer is an incontrovertible knower with respect to what appears, but it is not an incontrovertible knower with respect to every aspect that appears. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Just because it appears does not mean that it is validly cognized. The eye consciousness apprehending a blue snow mountain is a wrong consciousness because there is no blue snow mountain yet the blue snow mountain still appears to the eye consciousness. To that appearance the mind is valid and incontrovertible but that does not mean that it realizes it. (3) Valid cognizers that induce ascertainment by themselves and valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another When valid cognizers are divided [in another way], there are two: 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 19-23 September 2005 (02)  
46 (1) valid cognizers that induce ascertainment by themselves and  (2) valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another.   With respect to each of those, there are two parts: (1) definitions and (2) divisions.   There are valid cognizers that rely on themselves to ascertain something and those that rely on other valid cognizers to ascertain something.   Our own system is as follows. Gen-dun-drup’s Ornament for Knowledge, the General Meaning [of Dharmakirti’s Commentary on (Dignaga’s) “Compendium on Valid Cognition”] p o s i t s thus: 1. Definitions The definition of a valid cognizer that induces ascertainment by itself is: • it is a valid cognizer;  • it is able to induce ascertainment through its own power with respect to its own non-arising if the nature of its object of comprehension did not abide with the object.  This valid cognizer is able to ascertain by its own power that if the nature of its object of comprehension did not exist on the object, it itself would not arise.  The definition of a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another is: • it is a valid cognizer;  • ascertainment must be induced through the power of another [valid cognizer] with respect to its own non-arising if the nature of its object of comprehension did not abide with the object.  It is correct to posit these in this way.  This valid cognizer is not able to ascertain by its own power that if the nature of its object of comprehension did not exist on the object, it itself would not arise. These two definitions are cited from Gedun Drub’s Ornament for Knowledge.  Wednesday morning, 21 September 2005  With respect to the divisions of valid cognizers that induce ascertainment by themselves and valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another there is the following argument. 2. Divisions  With respect to the second, the explanation of the divisions, someone might say that among both valid cognizers that induce ascertainment by themselves and valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another there exist both direct valid cognizers and inferential valid cognizers.  [We respond that] that is incorrect because although both those [i.e., direct and inferential valid cognizers] exist among valid cognizers that induce ascertainment by themselves, whatever is a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another must be a direct valid cognizer.  Moreover, that is incorrect because, although both of those [i.e., valid cognizers that induce ascertainment by themselves and when it is induced by another] exist among direct valid cognizers, whatever is an inferential cognizer must be a valid cognizer that induces ascertainment by itself.  The assertion that both valid cognizers that i n d u c e  a s c e r t a i n m e n t  b y  t h e m s e l v e s  a n d v a l i d  cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another can be divided into direct valid cognizers and inferential valid cognizers is incorrect because while valid cognizers that induce ascertainment by themselves can be either direct valid cognizers or inferential valid cognizers, valid cognizers when 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 19-23 September 2005 (02)  
47 ascertainment is induced by another can only be direct valid cognizers. In other words, direct valid cognizers can be either valid cognizers that induce ascertainment by themselves or valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another, but there are no inferential valid cognizers that are valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another valid cognizer.   Someone might say, “A sense direct perceiver that apprehends from far away a reddish color that is, in fact, the color of fire and with respect to which a conceptual consciousness wonders whether or not it is the color of fire is a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another.” This is not correct because there is no time when conceptual consciousness wonders whether or not something that is, in fact, the color of fire is the color of fire. That follows because a conceptual consciousness that wonders whether or not something is the color of fire does not exist. This is so because whatever is the color of fire is necessarily realized as the color of fire by conceptual consciousness.  The point of the argument is to establish that valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by themselves can be either direct valid cognizers or inferential valid cognizers, whereas valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another can only be direct valid cognizers. Thus, a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another has to be a direct valid cognizer. The challenger says “A sense direct perceiver that apprehends from far away a reddish color that is, in fact, the color of fire and with respect to which a conceptual consciousness wonders whether or not it is t h e  c o l o r  o f  f i r e  i s  a  v a l i d  c o g n i z e r  w h e n  a s c e r t a i n m e n t  i s  i n d u c e d  b y  a n o t h e r . ”  T h i s  i s  incorrect because conceptual consciousness wondering whether or not the reddish color of fire is the color of fire does not exist. This is because there is no time when conceptual consciousness wonders whether or not something that is, in fact, the color of fire is the color of fire. This is so because whatever is the color of fire is necessarily realized as the color of fire by conceptual consciousness. This is because all existents are realized by conceptual consciousness, whereas whatever is a conceptual consciousness does not realize all existents. Therefore, whatever is the color of fire is necessarily realized as the color of fire by conceptual consciousness. A conceptual consciousness does not wonder whether or not something that is, in fact, the color of fire is the color of fire. However, there can be doubt regarding whether something that is the color of fire is or is not the color of fire.   All existents are realized by direct valid cognizers or inferential valid cognizers or conceptual consciousnesses. However, that does not mean that whatever is a direct valid cognizer realizes all phenomena, that whatever is an inferential valid cognizer realizes all phenomena, and that whatever is a conceptual consciousness realizes all phenomena. For this reason, there is no conceptual consciousness that doubts because all existents are necessarily realized to exist by conceptual consciousness.  STUDENT: How does it follow that there is no conceptual consciousness that doubts that the color of fire is the color of fire GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: While there exists doubts that are conceptual consciousnesses, there do not exist conceptual consciousnesses that doubt. This is because whatever exists is necessarily realized by conceptual consciousness. Similarly, in the case of a full-fledged other party who still wonders whether or not sound is impermanent, that doubt is a conceptual consciousness, yet conceptual consciousness realizes sound to be impermanent. Sound as impermanent is realized by a conceptual consciousness, but in the mind of that person there is a conceptual consciousness that doubts whether sound is impermanent. In the same way there is no time that conceptual consciousness doubts whether that which is the color of fire is the color of fire.   Is there a time when emptiness is not realized by a valid cognizer? If one says that there is a time when emptiness is not realized by a valid cognizer, when is that? If one says that it is in the mind of a particular person, then it would be right that there is a time when the mind of a particular person does not validly cognize emptiness. However, there is no time when emptiness is not validly cognized because whatever exists is pervaded by being validly cognized. This is because the definition of an existent is “that which is validly cognized.”   Valid cognizers, conceptual consciousnesses, and so forth are generalities but with respect to every existent there are valid cognitions and conceptual consciousnesses since they are existents. ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: Whose valid cognizer is that?  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is the valid cognizer of a person. ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: What about a distant star that none of us have ever seen?

Therefore, if it is not validly cognized it does not exist.  STUDENT: Does it not say “suitable to be validly cognized”? ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: It says “is validly cognized.” GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: However, in general “suitable to be validly cognized” and “is validly cognized” are synonymous. I n  o t h e r  w o r d s ,  i f  t h e  nature did not abide in the fire, that eye consciousness would not arise.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I n  t h e  c a s e  o f  a n  e y e  s e n s e  d i r e c t  p e r c e i v e r  s e e i n g  a  r e d d i s h  c o l o r  from afar, it apprehends the reddish color of fire but does not know it to be fire. The definition of a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another says “ascertainment must be induced through the power of another [valid cognizer] with respect to its own non-arising if the nature of its object of comprehension did not abide with the object.” In this case the object of comprehension is the reddish color, that is, the eye consciousness only comprehends reddish color. T h e  n a t u r e  o f  t h a t  o b j e c t  o f  c o m p r e h e n s i o n  i s  f i r e ,  b u t  t h e  e y e  consciousness does not know that. However, if that nature of fire did not abide in the reddish color, an eye consciousness apprehending reddish color could not arise because reddish color and fire are one nature. However, this particular eye consciousness does not ascertain that if that nature of fire did not exist in the reddish color, that it itself would not arise. In other words, the eye consciousness ascertaining a reddish color from afar does not induce the ascertainment of it being the color of fire. Hence, another valid cognizer is needed in order to induce the ascertainment of the reddish color as the color of fire.  STUDENT: The object ascertained is the nature of the object of comprehension, thus it is not that the consciousness ascertains that it itself would not arise if the nature of its object of comprehension did not abide in the object, as this would involve an elimination. The definition is difficult to understand when it says “with respect to its own non-arising” but there must arise a fault in the definition if it is not mentioned. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: An eye consciousness apprehending reddish color in the distance is a valid cognizer that induces ascertainment by itself because it ascertains reddish color by itself. For example, when one sees something in the distance but it is not clear whether or not it is a person, in order to ascertain that it is a tree one needs to depend on another valid cognizer, therefore one approaches the object and is thereby able to ascertain the nature of the object. Wednesday afternoon, 21 September 2005 2. Divisions (1) In our own system, when valid cognizers that induce ascertainment by themselves are divided, there are five:  (1) sense direct valid cognizers to which the ability to perform a function appears,  (2) sense direct valid cognizers that have a familiar object,  (3) self-knowing direct valid cognizers,  (4) yogic direct valid cognizers, and  (5) inferential valid cognizers.   Whatever is one of those five is necessarily a valid cognizer that induces ascertainment by itself.  Illustrations are as follows:  • the first, [a sense direct valid cognizer to which the ability to perform a function appears,] is, for example, a sense direct perceiver that apprehends fire as able to perform the function of burning firewood;  • the second, [a sense direct valid cognizer having a familiar object,] is, for example, a sense direct perceiver in the continuum of a son apprehending his father’s form;  • the third, [a self-knowing direct valid cognizer,] is, for example, a self-knowing direct perceiver that experiences an eye consciousness;  • the fourth, [a yogic direct valid cognizer,] is, for example, an omniscient consciousness that is an other-knowing mental consciousness;  • the fifth, [an inferential valid cognizer,] is, for example, an inferential consciousness that realizes sound to be impermanent.  The illustration of a sense direct valid cognizer to which the ability to perform a function appears is an eye sense direct perceiver apprehending fire as able to burn firewood.   The illustration of a sense direct valid cognizer having a familiar object is a sense direct perceiver in the continuum of a son apprehending his father’s form. The recognition of one’s parents in general occurs due to familiarity with them. However it is not always the case that an eye consciousness apprehending the form of one’s father is based on familiarity, for example, when a child once again sees his father who has been away for a long time. In this case, does he have a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by itself even though there is no familiarity with the form of his father?   The first moment of that sense direct perceiver in the continuum of a child apprehending the father’s form that is imbued with familiarity is a valid cognizer. However, while the second moment is also imbued with the same familiarity, that second moment is a subsequent cognizer and not a valid cognizer. Thus, although in setting out the illustration of the second the Tibetan text just says “a sense direct perceiver in the continuum of a son apprehending his father’s form,” it should specifically say “a sense direct valid cognizer in the continuum of a son apprehending his 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 19-23 September 2005 (02)  
50 father’s form” in order to rule out the possibility that a subsequent cognizer, which is not a valid cognizer, could be an illustration of “sense direct valid cognizers that have a familiar object.”   Another doubt that could arise with respect to sense direct valid cognizers that have a familiar object is that a valid cognizer must by definition be a new realizer, but if it is a new realizer how can it have a familiar object? To resolve this problem, one could perhaps posit that the father’s form is impermanent and therefore changing momentarily such that the father’s form of yesterday is not the father’s form of today, whereby when the child sees it today it is not the form that he saw yesterday and therefore it is newly realized. However, there are flaws in this reasoning.  STUDENT: Then it would be a subsequent cognizer. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is absolutely different. A subsequent cognizer realizes that which has already been realized, meaning that it realizes that which was realized in the first moment by the valid cognizer that preceded it. It is not that the object and the consciousness are simultaneously changing. In other words, it is not that the first moment of the object is perceived by the first moment of the consciousness and the second moment is perceived by the second moment of the consciousness, which is a subsequent cognizer, and so forth. In this case cognition would always be new and there would not exist subsequent cognizers. Hence, this is not the meaning of subsequent cognizer.  It is true that the father’s form of today is not the same as that of yesterday but the change is not noticeable. If a son has not seen his father for many years he will recognize that he has changed but otherwise not. According to Buddhist philosophy, aging begins in the second moment after birth but this momentary change is not seen by an eye consciousness.   The illustration of the third, a self-knowing direct valid cognizer, is said to be “a self-knowing direct perceiver that experiences an eye consciousness.” However, also in this case the text should specifically state “a self-knowing direct valid cognizer that experiences an eye consciousness.”   The illustration of the fourth, a yogic direct valid cognizer, is said to be “an omniscient consciousness that is an other-knowing mental consciousness.” However, it is not necessary to specifically mention an omniscient consciousness a s  a n  i l l u s t r a t i o n  o f  a  y o g i c  d i r e c t  v a l i d  c o gnizer because it would also be correct to posit the consciousness of an arya in meditative equipoise on the path of seeing. Therefore, this point is also subject to debate.   When one debates, one looks for things that do not appear to be quite right in a text such as the absence of the word “valid cognizer” in the illustration of the second type of valid cognizer that induces ascertainment by itself, which allows for the possibility that a subsequent cognizer could also be a suitable illustration. In other words, because direct perceivers have three divisions (direct valid cognizers, direct subsequent cognizers, and direct perceivers that are awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained), if the word “valid cognizer” is left out and only “direct perceiver” is mentioned, the illustration could be any of the three. For example, it could be a direct perceiver that is an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained because the father’s form could appear to the child but not be ascertained by him if he is distracted to something else.   In short, because the term “valid cognizer” is mentioned in divisions of valid cognizers that induces ascertainment by themselves but not in the illustration of the second, one asks whether this means that the illustration could also be a direct subsequent cognizer or a direct perceiver that is an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained. Likewise, in the case of the third illustration, a self-knowing direct perceiver that experiences an eye consciousness, because it is not specified that it is a direct valid cognizer there is the possibility that it could be a valid cognizer, a subsequent cognizer, or an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained. For example, a self-knowing direct perceiver that is an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained is the self-knower experiencing the awareness to which the father appears but is not ascertained. On the other hand, the illustration of a yogic direct perceiver, “an omniscient consciousness that is an other-knowing mental consciousness,” does not need this specification because an omniscient consciousness is pervaded by being a valid cognizer.  STUDENT: How does a sense direct perceiver apprehend the ability of fire to burn? This seems to be the scope of a conceptual consciousness.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: An eye consciousness sees burning, which is the function of fire. STUDENT: But the text says “the ability to perform a function.”

GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e  t h o u g h t  “ F i r e  h a s  t h e  a b i l i t y  t o  b u r n ”  i s  a  c o n c e p t u a l  consciousness, but the eye consciousness does see fire burning.  STUDENT: In the case of a sense direct valid cognizer having a familiar object, it would seem that familiarity with the father’s form is induced by memory, so how is the sense direct perceiver apprehending the father’s form induced by memory? In other words, if one has not seen one’s father for a long time, what brings about the recognition of a form as that of one’s father? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I t  a r i s e s  d u e  t o  h a v i n g  s e e n  h i m  p r e v i o u s l y .
