However, later on when we study the fifty-one mental factors there will be more to be said about memory or mindfulness, which is one the five ascertaining mental factors.   An illustration of the fifth, an inferential valid cognizer, is an inferential consciousness that realizes sound to be impermanent.  (2) When valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another are divided terminologically there are three:  (1) initial direct perceivers,  (2) inattentive direct perceivers, and  (3) direct perceivers having a cause of error.   Because this is a terminological d i v i s i o n  t h e r e  i s  n o  p e r v a s i o n  t h a t  a l l  t h r e e  d ivisions are valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another.  Illustrations are as follows:  • the first, [an initial direct perceiver,] is, for example, a sense direct perceiver in t h e  continuum of a person who has not previously experienced seeing an utpala that apprehends the color of an utpala.  • The second, [an inattentive direct perceiver,] is, for example, a sense direct perceiver that apprehends a sound in the continuum of a person whose mind is especially attracted to a beautiful form;  • the third, [a direct perceiver having a cause of error,] is, for example, a sense direct perceiver apprehending the color of a mirage that directly generates a superimposition apprehending the mirage as water.  An illustration of an initial direct perceiver is “a sense direct perceiver in the continuum of a person who has not previously experienced seeing an utpala that apprehends the color of an utpala.” This sense direct perceiver apprehends the blue that is the color of an utpala, not blue in general.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 19-23 September 2005 (02)  
52  An illustration of a direct perceiver that is an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained is “a sense direct perceiver that apprehends a sound in the continuum of a person whose mind is especially attracted to a beautiful form.”   An illustration of a direct perceiver having a cause of error i s  “a sense direct perceiver apprehending the color of a mirage that directly generates a superimposition apprehending the mirage as water.”  Because this is a terminological division not all three divisions are valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another, so which one of the three is not a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another? Since a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another is pervaded by being a valid cognizer, is the first a valid cognizer? Is the second? Is the third?  STUDENT: The third is a not a valid cognizer because it has a cause of error. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: All three illustrations are sense direct perceivers and for Sautrantika if it is a sense direct perceiver it cannot be a mistaken consciousness.  STUDENT: What is “the cause of error”? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Although the sense consciousness apprehending the color of a mirage has a cause of error, it is still a direct perceiver. This illustration is specifically t h a t  o f  “ a  s e n s e  direct perceiver apprehending the color of a mirage that directly generates a superimposition apprehending the mirage as water.” A sense consciousness apprehending a mirage is a wrong consciousness, but a sense direct perceiver cannot be a mistaken consciousness. So this third illustration is not a mistaken consciousness.   So which one is not a valid cognizer? Are all three valid cognizers? All three are sense direct perceivers. The second is a sense direct perceiver but it is an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained and is therefore not a valid cognizer. If it is not a valid cognizer, it cannot be a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another. For this reason, it is a terminological division of valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another.   The third, a sense direct perceiver apprehending the color of a mirage that directly generates a superimposition apprehending the mirage as water, realizes the color of a mirage. In fact it is a sense direct valid cognizer that realizes its object, the color of a mirage. However, it is not a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another because it itself ascertains the color of a mirage. Likewise, the sense direct perceiver apprehending the color of a mirage that directly induces a superimposition apprehending the mirage as water i s  n o t  a  v a l i d  c o g n i z e r  w h e n  a s c e r t a i n m e n t  i s  induced by another because there is no valid cognition of a mirage as water.   Thus, the first, “a sense direct perceiver in the continuum of a person who has not previously experienced seeing an utpala that apprehends the color of an utpala,” is a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another because the person needs to rely on another valid cognizer in order to ascertain the color of an utpala as the color of an utpala. However, the other two divisions are not actually valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another.  STUDENT: When we say “a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another,” the word another r e f e r s  t o  a n o t h e r  c o n s c i o u s n e s s . GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right, the other cognizer arises later on. STUDENT: If that cognizer does not arise, is the first moment still incontrovertible? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is still a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another and so it is a valid cognizer. In general when a person who has never seen an utpala is told later on that what he saw was the color of an utpala, that does not change the original eye consciousness apprehending the color of an utpala. While that eye consciousness apprehending the color of an utpala is a valid cognizer, with respect to what object is it valid? It is valid with respect to the color of utpala, therefore it must realize the color of utpala. So how does it realize the color of an utpala if it has never seen it before? STUDENT: Doe that mean that there are no valid cognizers with respect to objects that one sees for the first time and does not know what they are? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is not the case. If someone who I have never seen before were to walk into this room, my eye consciousness would apprehend his form. While my eye consciousness is a valid cognizer with respect to the form of the person, it is not an eye consciousness with respect to the form as being the form of a particular person s u c h  a s  J a m p a .  Therefore, even though it the first time I see that object, it is still a valid cognizer.

53 STUDENT: But another valid cognizer is needed to ascertain the form as the form of Mr. Jones. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right. If he introduces himself to me, then I will know who he is.  STUDENT: Is seeing a person for the first time not similar to seeing the color of an utpala for the first time and not knowing that it is the color of an utpala? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is the same. A characteristic of sense consciousnesses is that they do not depend upon names and terms. An eye consciousness apprehending the color of an utpala apprehends the color of an utpala without needing to know it to be “the color of an utpala.” Only later on when someone tells that person that it is the color of an utpala does a conceptual consciousness apprehending the color of an utpala arise. Such a conceptual consciousness depends on names and terms. In short, the eye consciousness apprehending the color of an utpala does not have to know it to be the color of an utpala in order to apprehend it.   The reason for debate is to bring up doubts and then resolve them.  Thursday morning, 22 September 2005  When [valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another] are terminologically divided [in another way], there are three:  (1) valid cognizers when ascertainment of the appearance is induced by itself but of the truth by another,  (2) valid cognizers when ascertainment of the generality is induced by itself but of the particular by another, and  (3) valid cognizers when ascertainment of even the mere appearance is induced by another.   The first is, for example, a sense direct perceiver apprehending in the distance a reddish color which is in fact the color of fire and with respect to which there is doubt, wondering, “Is that the color of fire or not?”   The second is, for example, a  s e n s e  d i r e c t  p e r c e i v e r  a p p r e h e n d i n g  a  t r e e  h a v i n g  l e a v e s  a n d  branches which is in fact an Ashoka tree and with respect to which there is doubt, wondering, “Is that an Ashoka tree or not?”   The third is, for example, a sense direct perceiver apprehending blue that induces a doubting consciousness that thinks, “Did I see blue or not?”  Since this is also a terminological division of valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another, among the three divisions there must be one or more that are not actually valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another.  There is a difference between those [valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another] as to whether they are actual or imputed, for the first and second are actual [valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another] whereas the latter one [a valid cognizer when ascertainment of even the mere appearance is induced by another] is an imputed one.  The first two are actually valid cognizers when ascertainment is induced by another, but the third is not, it is only an imputed valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another.   Also, from amongst those, the first, [a valid cognizer when ascertainment of the appearance is induced by itself but of the truth by another,] is both:  (i) that which induces ascertainment by itself and also  (ii) a valid cognizer that induces ascertainment by itself  with respect to a reddish color in the distance which is in fact the color of fire but with respect to which there is doubt, wondering, “Is that the color of fire or not?”  It is also both: (i) that when ascertainment is induced by another and  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 19-23 September 2005 (02)  
54 (ii) a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another  with respect to such a reddish color being the color of fire.  However, it is not a valid cognizer with respect to that [reddish color being the color of fire].  An eye sense direct perceiver apprehending the reddish color of fire is a valid cognizer with respect to the reddish color, that is, to its appearance. Hence, it is a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by itself. However, with respect to the reddish color being t he  c ol or  of  fire, it is a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another. Therefore, with respect to the appearance of reddish color it is a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by itself, whereas with respect the truth, the reddish color being the color of fire, since that has to be ascertained by another cognizer, it is a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another. Therefore, in this example there is a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by itself that is the eye consciousness apprehending reddish color, which does not need another valid cognizer to ascertain reddish color. However, the eye consciousness itself is also a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another because in order for it to know t h e  t r u t h  o r  n a t u r e  o f  t h e  reddish color it needs to rely on another valid cognizer. Therefore, it is a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by itself with respect to its appearance, reddish color, and a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another with respect to the truth, the reddish color being the color of fire. With respect to the reddish color, the eye consciousness induces ascertainment by itself a nd i s  a  v a l i d c o g ni z e r  t ha t  i nduc e s  a s c e r t a i nme nt  b y  i t s e l f .  o n t he  o t he r  hand, with respect to the reddish color being the color of fire it is an eye consciousness when ascertainment is induced by another and a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another. Thus, it is not a valid cognizer with respect to that reddish color being the color of fire.  In brief, whatever is a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another is necessarily a valid cognizer. However, whatever is a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another with respect to a particular phenomenon is necessarily not a valid cognizer with respect to that phenomenon. This is because whatever is a valid cognizer with respect to a particular phenomenon is necessarily a valid cognizer that induces ascertainment by itself with respect to that phenomenon. Just because the color of fire appears does not mean that the color of fire appears to the eye consciousness. This is a subject of debate. In conclusion, there is a valid cognizer with respect to reddish color that does not need to rely on another valid cognizer, but to ascertain the reddish color as being the color of fire another valid cognizer is necessary. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The eye consciousness apprehending reddish color is a valid cognizer with respect to reddish color, however it is not useful to the person because it is unable to ascertain its object as being the color of fire. For a person who does not approach the reddish color, the doubt remains as to whether the reddish color is the color of fire or not. For a person who approaches it and sees that it is the color of fire, he can remember the previous eye consciousness apprehending reddish color and understand that it was an eye consciousness apprehending the color of fire. For this person the doubt has been removed.  The second, valid cognizers when ascertainment of the general ity is induced by itsel f but of the particular by another, is, for example, a sense direct perceiver apprehending a tree having leaves and branches which is in fact an Ashoka tree and with respect to which there is doubt, wondering, “Is that an Ashoka tree or not?” It seems that an Ashoka tree is a certain type of tree that grows in breadth more than in height. What is apprehended by the sense direct perceiver is a tree with leaves and branches, but this is together with a doubt as to whether or not it is an Ashoka tree. Thus, it is a valid cognizer with respect to the generality, tree, but not with respect to the particularity, Ashoka tree. This division is very similar to the previous.   Since the object is an Ashoka tree, Ashoka tree appears to that eye consciousness. However, what is apprehended is the generality “a tree with leaves and branches.” That eye consciousness apprehending tree is a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by itself with respect to tree. However, in order to know whether or not that tree is an Ashoka tree, another valid cognizer is needed. Therefore, with respect to ascertaining the particularity, Ashoka tree, that eye sense direct perceiver is a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another. ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: It ascertains Ashoka tree but does not ascertain Ashoka tree as being Ashoka tree. 57 STUDENT 2: In the first moment the eye consciousness ascertains the Ashoka tree but oneself does not ascertain Ashoka tree. When later on one comes to know that it is an Ashoka tree, the eye consciousness ascertains Ashoka tree and oneself ascertains Ashoka tree. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: An eye consciousness ascertaining Ashoka tree induces a conceptual consciousness ascertaining “This is an Ashoka tree,” whereby the doubt is resolved. When one sees a tree in the distance, although the tree is an Ashoka tree, one just ascertains tree with leaves and so forth. Then, having approached it, one comes to ascertain “It is an Ashoka tree.” Thus, there is very short gap between the eye consciousness ascertaining Ashoka tree and the conceptual consciousness ascertaining Ashoka tree. But both before that and afterward, the object is an Ashoka tree.  STUDENT: I s  t h e  o t h e r  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  t h a t  i s  n e e d e d  t o  i n d u c e  a s c e r t a i n m e n t ,  a  c o n c e p t u a l  consciousness? STUDENT: Therefore, a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another is necessarily a valid cognizer with respect to its appearing object, but it is not a valid cognizer with respect to its object to be ascertained because the ascertainment of that has to be induced by another. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right. It is a valid cognizer with respect to reddish color but not a valid cognizer with respect to reddish color being the color of fire. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: H o w  a r e  o n l y  t h o s e  t h r e e  p h e n o m e n a  p e r m a n e n t  a c c o r d i n g  t o  Prasangika? They assert many permanent phenomena and in many respects are similar to the Sautrantika, their main difference being with respect to the subtlety of the object of negation. The lower schools set out, for example, the subject of the different types of minds in great detail such when one comes to study the higher schools only the differences are pointed out. 58 STUDENT: In what sense is a mental image permanent?  ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: Perhaps we should not use the term “mental image” as it is misleading but use the more literal translation of the Tibetan “meaning generality.” Alternatively perhaps “abstract” or “concept” are better words. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is not that there exists an object and a mind apprehending it and that when the object changes the mind changes with it. A meaning generality is the appearing aspect of a conceptual consciousness. In other words, it is a superimposition or an imputation of a conceptual consciousness. It is the appearance of the opposite of non-vase.  STUDENT: When I think of Geshe-la, for example, it is not a meaning generality that appears to my conceptual consciousness because a permanent phenomenon cannot give rise to a conceptual consciousness. So what is the observed object of my conceptual consciousness apprehending Geshe-la? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is better to say that the observed object condition of the conceptual consciousness apprehending Geshe-la is Geshe-la, just as the observed object condition of a conceptual consciousness apprehending vase is the vase itself. STUDENT: It cannot be Geshe-la as when you have passed away or are in Tibet, I can still think of you. For example, due to someone saying to you “I saw a rabbit with horns on its head,” a conceptual consciousness apprehending the horns of a rabbit arises.  To return to the subject of a valid cognizer w h e n  a s c e r t a i n m e n t  o f  t h e  g e n e r a l i t y  i s  i n d u c e d  b y  itself but of the particular by another a n d  i t s  i l l u s t r a t i o n .  W h a t  i s  t h e  g e n e r a l i t y  t h a t  i s  apprehended? It is a tree with leaves and branches. What is the particularity? It is an Ashoka tree. The relationship between generalities and particularities is that whatever is a particularity is a specific of a generality, thus an Ashoka tree is a particularity of tree. When the eye consciousness apprehends the generality, a tree with leaves and branches, it is also apprehending an Ashoka tree. How does it apprehend an Ashoka tree? STUDENT: An Ashoka tree is pervaded by being a tree. So when one apprehends the particularity, an Ashoka tree, one apprehends the generality, tree. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: When one apprehends an Ashoka tree, one also apprehends tree. If one apprehends wood does one apprehend a tree with leaves and branches? ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: Here there is a problem for translation because the word for “wood” (shing) and the word for “tree” (shing) are the same in Tibetan. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: H e r e  t h e  t e x t  j u s t  s a y s  shing, a general word for “ w o o d , ”  w h e r e a s  there is a more specific word for “tree” (shing sdog).   There can arise debate with respect to this illustration because the generality is “tree” and the particularity is “Ashoka tree,” but the text actually says “a tree with leaves and branches” and an Ashoka tree is also “a tree with leaves and branches.” Therefore, it could be argued that one apprehends the generality that is “an Ashoka tree with leaves and branches.” For this reason, it would be better if the text were just to say “tree” rather than “a tree with leaves and branches.” In other words, if one just says “tree,” it definitely refers to a generality, but if one adds the words “with leaves and branches,” it becomes similar to a particularity.  The third, a valid cognizer w h e n  ascertainment of even the mere appearance is induced by another, is, for example, a sense direct perceiver apprehending blue that induces a doubting consciousness that thinks, “Did I see blue or not?” This is not actually a valid cognizer when ascertainment is induced by another. Although it seems a bit strange, perhaps it can happen that one actually sees blue but later on comes to wonder whether or not one saw blue. When we see someone who we have never seen before and yet there arises the doubt wondering “Have I seen him before?”, that eye consciousness apprehending the person’s form is a valid cognizer but the doubt that is induced by it is not.  (4) Valid cognizers that are persons, speech, and consciousnesses When valid cognizers are terminologically divided [in another way] there are three:  (1) valid cognizers that are persons,  (2) valid cognizers that are speech, and  (3) valid cognizers that are consciousnesses.   The first [a valid cognizer that is a person] is, for example, the teacher Buddha.   The second [a valid cognizer that is speech] is, for example, the wheel of doctrine of the four noble truths.   The third [a valid cognizer that is a consciousness] is, for example, a direct perceiver or an inferential cognizer.  ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: The word tshad ma, which has been translated up to this point as “valid cognizer,” is not right in this context. This is because the word tshad ma in Tibetan and pramana in Sanskrit do not include the word “cognizer” but just mean “valid” or “prime.”  Since these too are terminological divisions of valid cognizers or validities, they do not have to be valid cognizers.  What makes a person valid? A person is determined to be valid or not by examining his speech. If his speech is valid, then the person can be said to be valid. Therefore, it is not that because the person is valid his speech is valid. What is valid speech? It means that that which is actually expressed is correct. By using valid consciousnesses, such as direct perceivers and inferential cognizers, speech can be determined to be valid or not.   STUDENT: S o  w h e n  t h e  T i b e t a n  word tshad ma o r  t h e  S a n s k r i t  w o r d  pramana i s  u n d e r s t o o d  b y  Sautrantika to mean “new,” how are persons and speech new? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A valid person is not a valid cognizer. “Valid” in this context means a valid source. STUDENT: I n  o n e  c o n t e x t  t h e  word that has been translated as “valid” was said to mean new whereas now it is said to just mean valid. This is confusing. Is there a better way to translate this word?

GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A generality can be either impermanent or permanent, for example, the generality of functioning things is impermanent. However, existents as a generality is a permanent phenomenon.  (F) Definite enumeration of valid cognizers With respect to the enumeration being definite, valid cognizers are divided into two: (1) direct valid cognizers and  (2) inferential valid cognizers.   That more than these are unnecessary and fewer would not be inclusive is the meaning of the enumeration of valid cognizers being limited to two for valid cognizers are limited to those two.  With respect to differences in substantial entity, direct perceiver and inferential cognizer are one substantial entity because direct perceiver is one substantial entity with inferential cognizer.  ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: Perhaps rather than “substantial entity” it would be better to translate the Tibetan word rdzas as “substance.”  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Because direct perceiver and inferential cognizer are one substance, so too are the following pairs one substance.  In dependence on this reasoning, [all of the following] are established as one substantial entity:  • the two, conceptual consciousness and non-conceptual consciousness;  • the two, mistaken consciousness and non-mistaken consciousness;  • the two, sense consciousness and mental consciousness;  • the two, mind and mental factor; and  • the two, valid cognizer and non-valid cognizer.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 19-23 September 2005 (02)  
61 The essence of the reasoning that establishes that direct perceiver and inferential cognizer are one substance is as follows. At the same time that there exists an inferential cognizer, there also exists the self-knowing direct perceiver that experiences it. That self-knowing direct perceiver is of the same substance as the consciousness that it experiences. Because that self-knowing direct perceiver is a particularity or specific of direct perceiver, it is a direct perceiver. In other words, direct perceiver is a generality and self-knowing direct perceiver is a particularity of it. Because of this it can be said that an inferential cognizer is the same substance as direct perceiver. In short, if something is the same substance as self-knowing direct perceiver, it must be the same substance as direct perceiver because self-knowing direct perceiver is a particularity or specific of direct perceiver.  STUDENT: Could Geshe-la please explain in more detail what is the meaning of “one substance” or “one substantial entity”? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It means that two phenomena are one in establishment and abiding in terms of object, time, and nature. In other words, they are produced, abide, and destroyed at the same time in terms of object, time, and nature. In this context the two that are one substance are an inferential cognizer and the self-knowing direct perceiver experiencing it.  STUDENT: Yet they have different objects. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That “they are produced, abide, and destroyed at the same time in terms of object” does not mean that they have the same object. In fact the object of an inferential cognizer is an external object whereas the object of a self-knowing direct perceiver is consciousness. In this case “object” refers to their support or basis. That they are “one in establishment and abiding” means that they are produced, abide, and destroyed at the same time.  STUDENT: While these are examples of conceptual and non-conceptual consciousnesses that are one substance, there are many examples of them that are not one substance. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: In fact when conceptual and non-conceptual consciousnesses are said to be one substance, it does not mean that every c o n c e p t u a l  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  a n d  every n o n-conceptual consciousness are one substance. It just means that a conceptual consciousness and the self-knower experiencing it are one substance. That self-knowing direct perceiver is a particularity of direct perceiver and is therefore a direct perceiver. It is one substance with the conceptual consciousness that it experiences.   Based on direct perceiver and inferential cognizer being one substance in the sense of an inferential cognizer and the self-knowing direct perceiver experiencing it being one substance, these other pairs of consciousnesses are also said to be one substance. However, this statement can be debated in many ways.  Friday morning, 23 September 2005  Returning to the subject of different minds being one substance or one substantial entity, it says here in the text that in dependence on that reason the following pairs of consciousnesses are the same substantial entity: • the two, conceptual consciousness and non-conceptual consciousness;  • the two, mistaken consciousness and non-mistaken consciousness;  • the two, sense consciousness and mental consciousness;  • the two, mind and mental factor; and  • the two, valid cognizer and non-valid cognizer.  A self-knower that experiences a conceptual consciousness. is a direct perceiver. For example, while the self-knower that experiences an inferential cognizer in the mind of a Charvaka (a non-Buddhist, literally meaning “those who have thrown far away”) is an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained, it is also a direct perceiver.   STUDENT: When the text says that a conceptual consciousness and the self-knower experiencing it are one substantial entity, it appears that they are treated as two different consciousnesses. However, this surely cannot be the case because they are two aspects of the same consciousness, just as “table” and “the wood it is made of” are the same substantial entity.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Are you saying that they are of the same nature but different isolates? STUDENT: Yes.

62 GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: When you say that a conceptual consciousness and the self-knower experiencing it are the same nature, do you mean that they are one consciousness? STUDENT: They cannot be the same consciousness because they have different objects! GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: W h i l e  i t  c o u l d  b e  a s s e r t e d  t h a t  f r o m  o n e  a s p e c t  t h e r e  i s  a  consciousness that apprehends the object itself and on the other hand it apprehends consciousness, this is not what is meant here. It cannot be said that a vase is one nature or one substance because in this context we are talking about two things being one nature. For example, the impermanence of a table and the table itself are of one nature but different isolates. Thus, they are different yet they are the same nature.   STUDENT: According to Sautrantika, is a self-knowing direct perceiver a main mind? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I t  i s  n e i t h e r  a  m a i n  m i n d  n o r  a  m e n t a l  f a c t o r ,  i t  i s  a  m e n t a l  consciousness. There are many different texts on the subject of Awarenesses and Knowers (lo-rig) and, by reading them all, you will come to a better understanding and will be able to develop your own personal assertions of what is right. STUDENT: Is a mental consciousness not pervaded by being a main mind? ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: The problem lies in the English language. “Main mind” (gtso sems), “mind” (sems), “mentality” (yid), and “consciousness” (rnam shes) are synonymous. The text sets out four possibilities between the two, mentality and mental consciousness.  Therefore, the definition of free from conceptuality is: free from being a determinative knower that apprehends a sound [generality] and a meaning [generality] as suitable to be mixed.  Next comes the subject of non-valid cognizers. To summarize, we began with a d i s c u s s i o n  o f  objects and the different divisions of objects. Then we looked at object-possessors, which are divided into persons, awarenesses, and expressive sounds. Then we examined the first division of awarenesses, valid cognizers and how these in turn are divided. Now we have come to the next division of awarenesses, non-valid consciousnesses.    STUDENT: Could Geshe-la please explain what is meant by “mode of apprehension” and “object of the mode of apprehension? What does the word “apprehension” mean in “object of the mode of apprehension”?  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The object itself is the object of the mode of apprehension. “Mode” means “way.” It refers to the way in which the consciousness apprehends its object. For example, in the case of a sense direct perceiver apprehending vase, what is it that apprehends vase? It is the eye consciousness itself, an eye sense direct perceiver. The object is the vase. Its mode of apprehension is the way it apprehends it, i.e., it apprehends it directly, free from the medium of a meaning generality. Thus, the word “mode of apprehension” means “how it apprehends,” which in this case is “directly.” In the case of a conceptual consciousness apprehending vase, the apprehender is the conceptual consciousness apprehending vase. How does it apprehend vase? It apprehends it by way of a meaning generality.   STUDENT: U p  t o  n o w  w e  h a v e  b e e n  s a y i n g  t h a t  t h e  a p p e a r i n g  o b j e c t  o f  a  c o n c e p t u a l  consciousness is a meaning generality, but what if the appearing object is a sound generality? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: There is a lot to be said about this and it will be dealt with later on in the text.  STUDENT: It is said that a conceptual consciousness engages its object in an eliminative manner. Is this also true for the determined object, appearing object, and object of engagement? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It mainly refers to the object of the mode of apprehension.  STUDENT: An not to all three? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is not clear whether this is said with respect to the appearing object or not, but it is the case with respect to the other two. All three objects of a conceptual consciousness apprehending vase are vase. The way in which a conceptual consciousness apprehends vase is in an eliminative way. In other words, it eliminates everything t h a t  i s  t h e  opposite of non-vase.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 19-23 September 2005 (02)  
63  STUDENT: Geshe-la said that meaning generalities are not included in the five aggregates. Could Geshe-la please explain this further? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: This is because meaning generalities are permanent, whereas all five aggregates are impermanent. STUDENT: Is this just according to Sautrantika? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The other schools also assert that meaning generalities are permanent.   STUDENT: C o u l d  G e s h e-la please explain once more the distinction between determined object and object of engagement? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e y  a r e  m u t u a l l y  i n c l u s i v e  i n  t h e  c a s e  o f  c o n c e p t u a l  consciousnesses. For example, in the case of a conceptual consciousness apprehending vase, the vase is both the determined object and the object of engagement. On the other hand, direct perceivers do not have a determined object, but only have an object of engagement, because determined object is a special feature of a conceptual consciousness. The word zhen i n  zhen yul, determined object, actually means “to cling” or “to adhere,” therefore it refers to that to which the consciousness clings. The function of conceptual consciousness is to distinguish between this and that.   The word ‘jug of ‘jug yul, object of engagement, means that the mind goes into or engages its object. That is true whether the consciousness is conceptual or a direct perceiver. The word “determined,” on the other hand, indicates that a conceptual mind engages its object by way of determination. They are similar, but they refer to two ways of knowing.  STUDENT: Could Geshe-la please give an example to illustrate the difference between an engaged object and an appearing object? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Why is vase the appearing object of a direct perceiver? Because it, the functioning phenomenon that is a vase, is the object that actually appears. The object of the mode of apprehension is also the vase because the eye consciousness apprehends vase directly without the need for a meaning generality. With regards to the engaged object, what does the eye consciousness engage? What does it go to? It engages the vase, which is therefore also the object of engagement. However, we have to be careful here because when we come to study the four tenet systems we will find that there are different interpretations regarding the objects of an awareness.  STUDENT: I f  w e  w e r e  s t a n d i n g  o n  t o p  o f  a  h i l l ,  f o r  e x a m p l e , could we say that the appearing object is the entire valley, whereas the engaged object is one detail, such as a house? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e  e y e  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  i s  j u s t  o n e .  I f  t e n  h o u s e s  a p p e a r  t o  t h e  e y e  consciousness, while it is possible for there to arise a conceptual mind at the same time that focuses on a particular house thinking “Oh, I like the color of that house,” that is not eye consciousness. It is a conceptual consciousness that focuses on one particular house. Thus, the ten houses are the appearing object of that eye consciousness. If one then focuses on one house, there will arise a different eye consciousness.  STUDENT: I still do not understand why there are different categories such as appearing object and object of engagement if they refer to the same thing. ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: They have different functions.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e s e  t h r e e  o b j e c t s  d o  n o t  o c c u r  s e p a rately. They are one and the same. For example, if I focus on Thomas, although there are other people around him, they are not clear, and only Thomas is clear. At that time, the object of the mode of apprehension, the appearing object, and the object of engagement are the same.  STUDENT: Are those three ever different?  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Do you think that when I focus on Thomas, that Thomas is the object of engagement whereas all the other people around him are the appearing objects? It is not like that! In fact the text says that these three are synonymous for a direct perceiver. STUDENT: Are they ever different? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I n  t h e  Ornament for C l e a r  R e a l i z a t i o n s t w o  t y p e s  o f  d i s c i p l e s  a r e  mentioned: those who follow faith and those who follow reason. The latter do not follow anything unless it is based on reasoning. That is good because this happens in the case of those of sharp faculty, whereas those of duller faculty follow faith. Therefore, you are doing the right thing by not blindly believing the text.

64 (2) Non-valid consciousnesses  With respect to the second, the explanation of non-valid consciousnesses, there are two parts: (1) definition and (2) divisions.  In our own system the definition of a non-valid consciousness is:  a knower that is not newly incontrovertible. 2. Divisions  Secondly, when non-valid consciousnesses are divided, there are five:  (1) subsequent cognizers,  (2) correctly assuming consciousnesses,  (3) awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained,  (4) doubting consciousnesses, and  (5) wrong consciousnesses.  A non-valid consciousness is defined as “a knower that is not newly incontrovertible,” of which there are five types. Previously valid cognizers where divided into two: direct valid cognizers and inferential valid cognizers. These two plus the five above are the seven main divisions of Awarenesses and Knowers.  (A) Subsequent cognizers With respect to the first, the definition of a subsequent cognizer is: a knower that realizes what has already been realized.  When subsequent cognizers are divided, there are three:  (1) directly perceiving subsequent cognizers,  (2) conceptual subsequent cognizers, and  (3) subsequent cognizers that are neither of those two. (1) Directly perceiving subsequent cognizers With respect to the first, [directly perceiving subsequent cognizers,] there are five:  (1) directly perceiving subsequent cognizers that are sense direct perceivers,  (2) directly perceiving subsequent cognizers that are mental direct perceivers,  (3) directly perceiving subsequent cognizers that are self-knowing direct perceivers, (4) directly perceiving subsequent cognizers that are yogic direct perceivers, and  (5) directly perceiving subsequent cognizers that are none of those four.  Illustrations are as follows:  • the first, for example, is that second moment of a sense direct perceiver apprehending blue;  • the second is, for example, the second moment of a clairvoyance knowing another’s mind;  • the third is, for example, the second moment of a self-knowing direct perceiver experiencing an eye consciousness and so forth;  • the fourth is, for example, the second moment of the uninterrupted path of the path of seeing;  • and the fifth is, for example, the second moment of a direct perceiver.  A subsequent cognizer is defined as “a knower that realizes what has already been realized,” of which there are three divisions: (1) directly perceiving subsequent cognizers, (2) conceptual subsequent cognizers and (3) subsequent cognizers that are neither of those two. D i r e c t l y  perceiving subsequent cognizers are further divided into five types. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 19-23 September 2005 (02)  
65  STUDENT: Would Geshe-la please posit an example of a directly perceiving subsequent cognizer that is not any of the four divisions? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The division into “directly perceiving subsequent cognizers that are none of those four” is mentioned for the purpose of eliminating debate. This is because if it were not included when someone who says that a directly perceiving subsequent cognizer has to be one of those four is asked “Which one of the four is the second moment of a direct perceiver?” he has to answer that it is none of the four because “the second moment of a direct perceiver” is the general category of directly perceiving subsequent cognizer.  STUDENT: However, when direct perceivers were divided, only four divisions were given. Why was a fifth one not included? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It does not have to be one of the four. STUDENT: Is it not true that it must be one of the four? ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: No, for instance, the second moment of a direct cognizer. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: This is a way of increasing the sharpness of you mind. For instance, if someone were to ask “Is mind valid or not?”, you could not reply. STUDENT: Is a direct perceiver necessarily one of its four divisions? ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: No. STUDENT: Posit! ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: I cannot. STUDENT: You have contradicted yourself. ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: You are right. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: If you accept what is said in the text, then a direct perceiver has to be one of the four. Why is there a fifth division in the case of directly perceiving subsequent cognizers but not in the case of direct perceivers? To understand this, it is necessary to understand the different ways of debating.  STUDENT: Is there a general category that is “none of the four”? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: If there were, then it would not be a fifth division. This fifth division is a category that is not any of the four.  STUDENT: This seems to me to be a general category within which are included the four, like, for instance, “color” which can be red and so forth. ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: This fifth division is a separate category that excludes all the other ones. STUDENT: So when we search for this fifth one, it is not described in any one of the other four?  ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: If it is a generality, it is none of its particularities. If it is a particularity, it has to be one of the four divisions. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right. Specifically speaking, any of the four are the fifth, but it does not work the other way around (that is, the fifth is none of the previous four). In other words, the first four have to be the fifth, but the fifth is “none of the four.” There are direct perceivers and subsequent cognizers. When a direct perceiver is a new incontrovertible knower it is a valid cognizer, whereas its second moment is a subsequent cognizer because it is a knower that realizes something that has already been realized. If you accept that whatever is a directly perceiving subsequent cognizer is that because it is a direct perceiver that realizes something that has already been realized, then you have to say that a directly perceiving subsequent cognizer is the result of a direct valid cognizer. Thus, it follows that whatever is a directly perceiving subsequent cognition has to be the result of a previous direct valid cognizer.   So then it follows that the first four divisions of directly perceiving subsequent cognizers must be the result of a previous direct valid cognizer. Does it follow that a directly perceiving subsequent cognizer is a result? It is a result. If it is a result, it must have a cause. Its cause is a previous direct valid cognizer such as yogic direct valid cognizer, a sense direct valid cognizer, and so forth. In this case, that direct perceiver must be a subsequent cognizer because its cause is a previous direct valid cognizer.  STUDENT: What is the substantial cause of the first moment of a valid cognizer? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: There are different kinds of causes, it does not necessarily have to be a substantial cause. For example, a yogic direct perceiver comes from a previous stream of valid cognizer. In other words, that moment of a yogic direct perceiver has a cause, a previous valid cognizer.

67 Monday afternoon, 26 September 2005  The definition of a subsequent cognizer is “a knower that realizes what has already been realized.” However, w e  d o  n o t  k n o w  w h e t h e r o r  n o t  w e  a re studying something that we have already realized! (2) Conceptual subsequent cognizers  When the second, conceptual subsequent cognizers, are divided there are two: (1) conceptual subsequent cognizers that are induced by direct perceivers and  (2) conceptual subsequent cognizers that are induced by inferential cognizers.   The first is, for example, a factually concordant ascertaining consciousness ascertaining blue that is induced by a sense direct perceiver apprehending blue.   The second is, for example, the second moment of an inferential cognizer realizing sound to be impermanent.  To these two types of conceptual subsequent cognizers – subsequent cognizers that are induced by direct perceivers a n d  s u b s e q u e n t  c o g n i z e r s  that are induced by inferential cognizers. – c a n  b e  added a third type: subsequent cognizers that are induced by neither of the two. An illustration of a subsequent cognizer that is induced by neither of these two is a conceptual subsequent cognizer. However, in this case this third division is not mentioned. It is similar to the fact that there are two types of valid cognizers, direct valid cognizers and inferential valid cognizers, but when it is asked whether a valid cognizer is a direct valid cognizer or an inferential valid cognizer, it is found to be neither of the two.   Likewise, when it is asked whether a subsequent cognizer is a directly perceiving subsequent cognizer or a conceptual subsequent cognizer, it is found to be neither of the two. When subsequent cognizers are divided into directly perceiving subsequent cognizers and conceptual subsequent cognizers, the basis of division is subsequent cognizer itself. Subsequent cognizer is the generality and directly perceiving subsequent cognizer and conceptual subsequent cognizer are particularities or specifics of it. In general, a generality has a greater pervasion than a particularity. Therefore, a directly perceiving subsequent cognizer is a subsequent cognizer and a conceptual subsequent cognizer is a subsequent cognizer, but a subsequent cognizer itself does not fit into either of these two divisions because a generality cannot be included in one of its particularities. There is nothing that is a subsequent cognizer that is not one of the two, either a directly perceiving subsequent cognizer or a conceptual subsequent cognizer. But is there something that is not a directly perceiving subsequent cognizer but is a subsequent cognizer? There is, it is a conceptual subsequent cognizer. Hence, the basis of division is a generality and its divisions are particularities. While the particularities are always the generality, the generality is none of the particularities. For example, subsequent cognizer itself is neither a directly perceiving subsequent cognizer nor a conceptual subsequent cognizer. Is that always the case? There are some exceptions. For example, existent has two particularities – p e r m a n e n t  p h e n o m e n a  a n d  t h i n g s  ( f u n c t i o n i n g  phenomena). Whatever is a thing is an existent. However, while it would normally be that existent is neither a permanent phenomenon nor thing, it is in fact a permanent phenomenon.   Another example is contradiction, of which there are two types – direct contradiction, such as permanent and impermanent, and direct contradiction of one destroying the other (direct contradiction of abiding in mutual abandonment3), such as hot and cold which cannot exist simultaneously. Since these two are divisions of contradiction, they must be the generality, contradiction. But is that which is a direct contradiction, a contradiction? It cannot be as otherwise there would be nothing that is it. If two things are direct contradictions there is nothing that is it, permanent-impermanent. For two things to be contradictory, they must lack a common locus. Contradiction itself is not a contradiction.                                                           3 Kamalashila’s Illumination of the Middle Way (cited in Chandrakirti’s Supplement to the Middle Way) says: “The definition of abiding in mutual abandonment is ‘A pair, such that if something’s being one is eliminated while its being the other is not affirmed, it does not exist.’”

Perhaps in tantra it can be said to travel to enlightenment, but also in sutra to say that it does not travel to enlightenment has faults. In order to achieve pure appearance one needs to train the mind, which is useful in seeing everyone as pure and not some as good and some as bad. However, the subject of Awarenesses and Knowers according to the Sautrantika presentation does not discuss this type of training. We tend to judge people as good and bad and as a consequence treat them well or generate anger and so forth toward them. However, that is just how we as an individual see someone, it is not that a person who we see as bad will be seen to be bad by everyone. It all depends on what we think of someone. For example, by training in patience with respect to a certain person, we will come to realize that there is no point in getting angry with him. If we train in pure appearance, no one will make us angry. It is only we ourselves who make ourselves angry. This comes from not having meditated enough on patience. There exists a truly existent self but not a self-supporting substantially existent self.  (B) Correctly assuming consciousnesses  With regard to the second [of the non-valid consciousnesses], correctly assuming consciousnesses, there are [two parts]: (1) definition and (2) divisions. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
70 1. Definition First, the definition of a correctly assuming consciousness is: a factually concordant determinative knower that is controvertible with regard to determining its object.  ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: The Tibetan word yid dpyod d o e s  n o t  h a v e  t h e  l i t e r a l  m e a n i n g  o f  “correctly assuming.”  A correctly assuming consciousness is a consciousness that focuses on its object and is getting close to it, but has not yet realized its determined object. It is “factually concordant” in the sense that it is correct, that is, it is not wrong with respect to its object. It is a determinative knower because it is conceptual. That it is “controvertible” means that it does not realize its object.  2. Divisions  When [correctly assuming consciousnesses] are divided there are five:  (1) correctly assuming consciousnesses that do not have a reason,  (2) correctly assuming consciousnesses that have a contradictory reason,  (3) correctly assuming consciousnesses for which the reason is indefinite [or lacks pervasion],  (4) correctly assuming consciousnesses for which the reason is not established, and  (5) correctly assuming consciousnesses for which a reason exists but is not settled.  The first [a correctly assuming consciousness that does not have a reason] is, for example, an awareness that apprehends sound to be impermanent in dependence upon the mere words, “Sound is impermanent.” [This is a suitable example] because the words “Sound is impermanent” express a thesis that sound is impermanent, but not a reason.  Just by hearing the statement “Sound is impermanent,” one apprehends sound to be impermanent but one does so without a reason. This is similar to blind faith in that one believes something just because someone said it is so.  The next three divisions of correctly assuming consciousnesses are related to logical reasoning and are important to understand.  Tuesday morning, 27 September 2005  One of the biggest problems of people who are studying is anxiety or worry. However, anxiety is of absolutely no benefit or help, instead it hinders understanding. When we begin to worry, everything appears to be difficult and this blocks our understanding. Therefore, it is best to have a relaxed mind. When the mind becomes tight and tense, problems arise and we become tired. We experience pain and tightness in the shoulders and we sigh a lot. It is normal that when we study something new, we will understand some things and not others. However, just by going over the material again and again, we will slowly come to a better understanding of the subject.   When studying Sautrantika, we might tend to think that their presentation does not accord with how things actually are, however that is not the right way to study. We need to understand and establish what the Sautrantika position on certain topics is. When we go on to study t h e  f o u r  schools in the subject of Tenets and arrive at the Sautrantika school, you will recognize it and find it easy to understand. So relax, be confident, and study!  There are five divisions of correctly assuming consciousnesses: (1) correctly assuming consciousnesses that do not have a reason,  (2) correctly assuming consciousnesses that have a contradictory reason,  (3) correctly assuming consciousnesses for which the reason is indefinite [or lacks pervasion],  (4) correctly assuming consciousnesses for which the reason is not established, and  (5) correctly assuming consciousnesses for which a reason exists but is not settled.  The first is to arrive at a correct assumption that sound is impermanent based on just hearing someone state “Sound is impermanent.” 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
71  The second [a correctly assuming consciousness having a contradictory reason] is, for example, an awareness that apprehends sound to be impermanent from the sign of being empty of being able to perform a function. [This is a suitable example] because empty of being able to perform a function is contradictory with impermanence.  All these illustrations or examples of correctly assuming consciousnesses involve sound being impermanent. There are, of course, other illustrations for correctly assuming consciousnesses but here sound being impermanent is used. It is said that when one debates one should not bring the teacher’s desk with one, meaning that one should leave what the teacher has said behind. This means that when one debates, one should find many other illustrations than those given in the text that one is studying.   In the statement “Sound is impermanent because it is empty of being able to perform a function,” that it is “empty of being able to perform a function” means that it is not able to perform a function. The reason “because it is empty of being able to perform a function” is given to establish that sound is impermanent, however it is a contradictory reason because sound is able to perform a function.   The third [a correctly assuming consciousness for which the reason is indefinite] is, for example, an awareness that apprehends sound to be impermanent from the sign of being an object of comprehension. [This is a suitable example] because object of comprehension is a reason that is indefinite in the proof of that [i.e., whatever is an object of comprehension is not necessarily impermanent].  In the case of the statement “Sound is impermanent because it is an object of comprehension,” the pervasion is indefinite. Therefore, an indefinite reason is used to prove that sound is impermanent. An object of comprehension is an existent. Sound is an object of comprehension, therefore object of comprehension exists in the subject, sound. However, it is not definite that sound is impermanent because it is an object of comprehension because object of comprehension is not pervaded by being impermanent.   The fourth [a correctly assuming consciousness for which the reason is not established] is, for example, an awareness that apprehends sound to be impermanent from the sign of being an object of apprehension by an eye consciousness. [This is a suitable example] because object of apprehension by an eye consciousness is a reason that is not established in the proof of that.  In the case of the third statement “Sound is impermanent because it is the object of apprehension of an eye consciousness,” there is the pervasion that whatever is an object of apprehension of an eye consciousness is impermanent, however sound is not an object of apprehension of an eye consciousness. Therefore, the reason is not established, that is, it is not established with respect to the subject, sound. There do exist objects of apprehension of eye consciousness and therefore this can be used as a reason in general but it cannot be used as a reason to establish that sound is impermanent. Therefore, if someone says “Sound is impermanent because it is the object of apprehension of an eye consciousness,” one should respond “The reason is not established.”  The point here is that in a logical statement proving sound to be impermanent: • the reason should not contradict what is being proved,  • the reason should be definite, and  • the reason should be established.  We might wonder who would set out such absurd reasons as those given above, but in actuality there are no limits to our wrong ideas. Therefore, even though these particular statements might not arise, we do tend to make many such statements. In India there was a well-known text that established that crows have teeth. It came about because the author’s wife had a lot of jewelry that was carried away by a crow, due to which the author came to the conclusion that crow’s have teeth. This text is often cited as an example of a text that has no purpose.   The statement “Sound is impermanent because it is a functioning thing” is presented to someone who already knows that functioning things are impermanent. If the other person does not know this, one has to set out other reasons.   
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
72 The fifth [a correctly assuming consciousness for which the reason exists but is not settled] is, for example, an awareness that apprehends sound to be impermanent from the sign of being a product, without its having been ascertained by valid cognition that sound is a product and that whatever is a product must be impermanent. [This is a suitable example] because although “product” is a correct sign in the proof of sound as impermanent, that person has not yet settled it.  The statement “Sound is impermanent because it is a product” contains a correct sign or reason. But for whom is it a correct sign? It is a correct sign for someone who knows that sound is a product and that whatever is a product is impermanent but wonders whether or not sound is impermanent. For such a person “product” is a correct sign, but for someone without these three characteristics it is not a correct sign. Hence, if the other person is not a full-fledged other party possessing these three characteristics, the reason exists but is not settled. In general it is a correct sign but it is not a correct sign for that particular person. Therefore, the reason is not settled.   Hence, in order for something to be a correct reason, two conditions are necessary:  • one from the point of view of the reason itself – it must not be contradictory, it must be definite, and it must be established, and  • one from the point of view of the person to whom it is stated – he or she must be a full-fledged other party possessing the three characteristics.  A person who is not yet fully fledged does not know that sound is a product and that whatever is a product is impermanent. However, we should not think that because we do not understand this completely that we are not a suitable person for this statement. Instead we should think about the statement whereby we will gradually become a full-fledged other party who is suitable for this reason.  STUDENT: I s  t h e  s e c o n d  d i v i s i o n  o f  c o r r e c t l y  a s s u m i n g  c o n s c i o u s n e s s e s  p e r v a d e d  b y  b e i n g  t h e  third and fourth divisions? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is not. In the case of the third statement “Sound is impermanent because it is an object of comprehension,” an object of comprehension is not necessarily impermanent but it can be impermanent, and likewise while it is also not necessarily permanent it can be p e r m a n e n t .  T h e r e f o r e ,  “ b e c a u s e  i t  i s  an object of comprehension” is not a contradictory reason.   On the other hand, in general whatever is the second division is also the fourth. However, this depends upon the person to whom the statement is presented, for example, it could be presented to a person who believes that sound is permanent and empty of being able to perform a function. To such a person one has to make statements that gradually lead him to realize that sound is impermanent and able to perform a function. In short, the second division, that in which the reason is contradictory, could also be the fourth division if the reason is also not established.  When the reason is indefinite, it is not just that there is no pervasion. For example, because an object of comprehension does not have to be impermanent nor does it have to be permanent, the pervasion is not definite. It is not that there is or is not pervasion.  STUDENT: Could Geshe-la please give a correct reason that it is possible to attain enlightenment and all good qualities? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The  r e a s on a c c or di ng t o Sa ut r a nt i ka  i s  “ be c a us e  a l l  s e nt i e nt  be i ngs  have the seed or potential to develop uncontaminated wisdom.” In this school we cannot posit the fact that all sentient beings have the tathagata essence as the reason, but we can posit it as the seed or potential to develop uncontaminated wisdom. STUDENT: What is that seed? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is a latency deposited on the mental continuum.  Some of what we are discussing here may be easy to understand and some may be difficult. When one has gained a good understanding of something, one should look at other texts on the subject of Awarenesses and Knowers in order to see what they have to say about that particular subject. However, if, without having understood something, one were to look to see what other texts say about it, one will only become more confused and not be able to arrive at a clear understanding of the first text’s position. It is recounted that in Tibet when some people would set out to look for cow dung to use as fuel, they would go to one place and, not finding it, put down their basket and 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
73 go to another place. Again not finding it, they would return to where they had left their basket to discover that it had been stolen. In this way they would find themselves without cow dung and without a basket! We should not be like such people!  If someone says, “The meaning generality of pot is all four objects of the conceptual consciousness apprehending pot,” [we will respond], “It [absurdly] follows that the subject, the meaning generality of pot, is the determined object of the conceptual consciousness apprehending pot because of being all four objects of that [conceptual consciousness apprehending a pot]. The reason has been asserted.”  If the consequence [that the meaning generality of pot is the determined object of the conceptual consciousness apprehending a pot] is accepted, “It [absurdly] follows that that subject, [the meaning generality of pot,] is the object of comprehension of the conceptual consciousness apprehending pot because of being the determined object of that [conceptual consciousness apprehending pot].”  If this consequence [that the meaning generality of pot is the object of comprehension of the conceptual consciousness apprehending pot], is accepted, “It [absurdly] follows with respect to the subject, the conceptual consciousness apprehending pot, that it is an awareness that is not mistaken with respect to the meaning generality of pot.”  If this consequence is accepted, ” It [absurdly] follows w i t h  r e s p e c t  t o  t h a t  s u b j e c t ,  [ t h e  conceptual consciousness apprehending pot,] that the meaning generality of pot does not appear to it as pot.”  Someone might say that the meaning generality of pot is all four objects – the appearing obj ect,  engaged object, determined object, and object of the mode of apprehension – o f  a  c o n c e p t u a l  consciousness apprehending pot. However, if one says that the meaning generality of pot is the determined object of a conceptual consciousness apprehending pot a fault occurs because the conceptual consciousness apprehending pot is mistaken with respect to its appearing object, the meaning generality of pot. On the other hand, it is not mistaken with respect to its determined object, pot. Therefore, the meaning generality of pot i s  n o t  a l l  f o u r  o b j e c t s  o f  a  c o n c e p t u a l  consciousness apprehending pot. (C) Awarenesses to which the object appears without being ascertained With respect to the third [of the five types of non-valid consciousnesses, awarenesses to which the object appears without being ascertained], the definition of something’s being an awareness to which an object appears without being ascertained is: a knower that is a common locus of: • having clear appearance of the specifically characterized phenomenon that is its engaged object and  • being unable to induce ascertainment with respect to the specifically characterized phenomenon that is its engaged object.  For Sautrantika a specifically characterized phenomenon is a functioning thing, an impermanent phenomenon, and an ultimate truth. An awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained is a knower that has a clear appearance of the specifically characterized phenomenon that is its engaged object. However, it is unable to induce ascertainment with respect to the specifically characterized phenomenon that is its engaged object.   However, one should know the manner in which not t o  p o s i t  t h e  d e f i n i t i o n  o f  “ s o m e t h i n g ’ s  being an awareness to which the object appears without being ascertained” as “that which is a common locus of (1) having clear appearance of the specifically characterized phenomenon that is its object a n d  ( 2 )  b e i n g  u n a b l e  t o  i n d u c e  a s c e r t a i n m e n t  w i t h  r e s p e c t  t o  t h e  s p e c i f i c a l l y  characterized phenomenon that is its object.” This is because a sense consciousness that sees snow mountains as blue sees as blue the white color of the snow mountains, which is its engaged object, and therefore does not see clearly the specifically characterized phenomenon which is its engaged object. Still, since its appearing object, the white color of snow mountains, appears clearly as blue whereas it does not exist [as blue], there is clear appearance of the 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
74 specifically characterized phenomenon which is its object. Also it is unable to induce ascertainment with respect to that because it engages that [object] perversely.  “Engaged object” must be specifically mentioned in the definition because if the definition just said “object” there would be a fault. This is a difficult topic when debated. In the case of an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained, the engaged object has to clearly appear although it is not ascertained. If the word “object” alone is mentioned in the definition, there would occur a fault in the definition. If we take the eye consciousness apprehending a blue snow mountain as an example, the engaged object is the white snow mountain appearing as blue. Therefore, the engaged object does not clearly appear to it because if it did clearly appear it would appear as a white snow mountain. The engaged object is a white snow mountain appearing as blue, while the blue snow mountain clearly appears to that awareness, the actual phenomenon, the white snow mountain, remains hidden and does not clearly appear. Although this consciousness does not have the ability to induce ascertainment with respect to its object, it is not an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained b e c a us e  t he  t hi ng  i t s e l f ,  t he  s pe c i f i c a l l y  c ha r a c t e r i z e d phenomenon, the white snow mountain, does not clearly appear to it because a blue snow mountain appears to it.   Its appearing object, the white snow mountain, clearly appears as blue although it does not exist as blue. Hence, there is the clear appearance of the specifically characterized phenomenon that is its object. In other words, the specifically characterized phenomenon that is the color blue clearly appears to it. However, that consciousness is unable to induce ascertainment with respect to its object because it engages its wrongly. In other words, it is a wrong consciousness. “Unable to induce ascertainment” means that the eye consciousness apprehending a white snow mountain as blue cannot induce ascertainment of a white snow mountain. However, another consciousness can induce that ascertainment, for example, an eye consciousness that arises the next day that sees it as white.   STUDENT: All this is based on the assumption that something exists as itself with a specific color. However, this discussion would be rather strange from the point of view of Prasangika because color arises in dependence on the meeting of the eye consciousness, eye sense power, and object. For example, the sky is blue but at sunset it is red and at night it is black, therefore there is no wrong consciousness seeing a white snow mountain as blue because due to the light at a certain time of the day a white snow mountain does appear as blue.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Using your own reason, the sky would not be black or red. It is just that without sun it appears to be dark and at sunset, due to the rays of sunlight, it appears to be red. The sky in fact does not have a color.  STUDENT: T h e  w o r d  “ s p e c i f i c a l l y  c h a r a c t e r i z e d  p h e n o m e n o n ”  i n  t h e  d e f i n i t i o n  s e e m s  t o  i m p l y  that an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained must be a direct perceiver and that therefore there are no conceptual consciousnesses that are awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained. Is that correct? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h a t  s e e m s  t o  b e  r i g h t  a c c o r d i n g  t o  S a u t r a n t i k a  s i n c e  a l l  t h e  illustrations are of non-conceptual consciousnesses.   The main point regarding awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained is that the their engaged object must clearly appear but they are not able to induce ascertainment later on. On the other hand, in the case of the white snow mountain appearing as blue, it does not clearly appear and does not induce ascertainment because it is a wrong consciousness that engages its object wrongly or perversely.   STUDENT: T h e  t e x t  s a y s  “ S t i l l ,  s i n c e  i t s  a p p e aring object, the white color of snow mountains, appears clearly as blue whereas it does not exist as blue, there is clear appearance of the specifically characterized phenomenon that is its object.” This was said to mean that the specifically characterized phenomenon that is the blue snow mountain clearly appears. However, a specifically characterized phenomenon is an existent and a blue snow mountain does not exist. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The blue is what clearly appears. The blue color is not a specifically characterized phenomenon, however it is a specifically characterized phenomenon that is its object. In itself it is not a specifically characterized phenomenon, but it is a specifically characterized

75 phenomenon that is the object of that eye consciousness. However, this statement is not quite comfortable and needs to be examined further.  Tuesday afternoon, 27 September 2005  Illustrations of awarenesses to which an object appears without being ascertained are, for example: • a sense direct perceiver apprehending blue that induces the doubt that wonders, “Did I see blue or not?”;  • mental direct perceivers in the continua of ordinary beings apprehending the five objects – forms and so forth; and  • self-knowers experiencing those [mental direct perceivers].  In t h e  c a s e  o f  a  s e n s e  d i r e c t  p e r c e i v e r  a p p r e h e n d i n g  b l u e  t h a t  i n d u c e s  t h e  doubt t h a t  w o n d e r s ,  “Did I see blue or not?”; the engaged object, blue, which is a specifically characterized phenomenon, clearly appears to the eye consciousness but the eye consciousness is unable to induce ascertainment by its own power and instead induces the doubt that wonders, “Did I see blue or not?”   Mental direct perceivers in the continua of ordinary beings that apprehend forms, sounds, and so forth are awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained. Initially there is, for example, an eye sense direct perceiver apprehending a form, which then induces a mental direct perceiver apprehending a form. However, the mental direct perceiver apprehending a form only lasts one sixty-fifth of the duration of a fingersnap made by a healthy person and is so short that the mental direct perceiver is unable to induce ascertainment of the object, a form, by its own power. Thus, although the object appears to a mental direct perceiver, it is not ascertained because it appears for too short a time.  Likewise, the self-knower that experiences a mental direct perceiver is also correspondingly short and therefore unable to induce ascertainment. Hence, a mental direct perceiver apprehends its object for a very short time due to which it is unable to induce ascertainment by its own power, and similarly the self-knower that experiences that mental direct perceiver, being correspondingly short in duration, is also unable to induce ascertainment.  STUDENT: Do aryas have awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A yogic direct perceiver cannot be an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained: Although there could be awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained apart from that, I would say that they do not have them. STUDENT: When they are not in meditative equipoise, why would they not have awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Although they might have an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained, I cannot think of any example.  STUDENT: Whe n an ar ya pe r c e i ve s  an obj e c t  by me ans  of  a s e ns e  di r e c t  pe r c e i ve r  doe s  he  al s o perceive it by means of a mental direct perceiver? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The mental direct perceiver that follows a sense direct perceiver in the continuum of an arya is not as short as it is in ordinary beings. While ordinary beings are unable to make use of mental direct perceivers, aryas can make use of them by the power of their practice. A yogic direct perceiver is an example of a mental direct perceiver.  STUDENT: D o  t h e y  a l s o  m a k e  u s e  o f  m e n t a l  d i r e c t  p e r c e i v e r s  w h e n  t h e y  a r e  n o t  i n  m e d i t a t i v e  equipoise? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: When they are not in meditative equipoise, a sense direct perceiver apprehending form induces a mental direct perceiver apprehending form that lasts longer than in ordinary beings. According to the author of Sera Je Monastery’s textbooks, Jetsun Chogyi Gyeltsen, arya beings when not in meditative equipoise have a realization of emptiness but it is not manifest, whereas according to the author of Drepung Loseling Monastery’s textbooks, Panchen Sonam Dragpa, at that time they do not have a realization of emptiness but still it is there. There are many such differences, for example, according to Jetsun Chogyi Gyeltsen, Maitreya is a buddha, whereas according to Panchen Sonam Dragpa, he is a tenth-ground bodhisattva. Also, according to Panchen Sonam Dragpa, a bodhisattva in meditative equipoise does not have the mind of

76 enlightenment, whereby there exists a bodhisattva without a mind of enlightenment. Similarly, according to this reasoning there can exist a monk without vows.   STUDENT: What is the difference between the mind of an arya who is not in meditative equipoise and the mind of a person on the path of preparation who has a union of calm abiding and special insight? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: F o r  e x a m p l e ,  a  m e n t a l  d i r e c t  p e r c e i v e r  r e a l i z i n g  s u b t l e  impermanence on the path of preparation lasts one sixty-fifth of a fingersnap, whereas on the arya path it lasts a lot longer. While subtle impermanence appears to a mental direct perceiver in the continuum of an ordinary being, it is not ascertained. If that subtle impermanence were ascertained then that person would be said to perceive subtle impermanence but he does not.   STUDENT: Panchen Sonam Dragpa says that a yogic direct perceiver apprehending an omniscient mind as being impermanent is an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained because the omniscient mind appears to it but is not ascertained because omniscient mind is only realized by a buddha. Perhaps a yogic direct perceiver directly perceives the impermanence of omniscient mind but does not directly perceive omniscient mind.
