If a yogic direct perceiver directly realizes omniscient mind, it would have to be said that it realizes all aspects of omniscient mind, whereby there would not be much difference between a yogic direct perceiver and omniscient mind. STUDENT: According to Panchen Sonam Dragpa the omniscient mind appears to a yogic direct perceiver but it is not ascertained because it can only be ascertained by an omniscient mind. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I  a c c e p t  t h a t  P a n c h e n  S o n a m  D r a g p a  s a y s  t h a t ,  b u t  t h e r e  a r e  differences between the assertions of the different monasteries. Jetsun Chogyi Gyeltsen was one of the first to write textbooks. Among his disciples were Panchen Sonam Dragpa and Tendawa, who is the author of the Sera Me Monastery’s textbooks. Their assertions are sometimes different from their teacher’s. The textbooks of Sera Je, Drepung Gomang, and Ganden Jangtse have similar assertions, and those of Sera Me, Drepung Loseling, and Ganden Shartse have similar assertions. However, each of them have their respective textbooks.  (D) Doubting consciousnesses Our own system is that the definition of a doubting consciousness is: a knower that has qualms two-pointedly by its own power.  A mind that possesses similarity with doubt and the feelings and so forth that accompany that [mind possessing similarity with doubt] have qualms by the power of doubt, but do not have qualms two-pointedly by their own power.  The main mind that is concomitant with doubt as well as the feelings and so forth in its retinue have qualms by the power of doubt but not by their own power.   When doubting consciousnesses are divided there are three:  (1) doubt tending toward the factual,  (2) doubt tending toward the non-factual, and  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
77 (3) equal doubt.   The first is, for example, doubt that thinks that sound is probably impermanent.   The second is, for example, doubt that thinks that sound is probably permanent.   The third is, for example, doubt that wonders whether sound is permanent or impermanent.  Doubt tending toward the factual i s ,  f o r  e x a m p l e ,  t h e  d o u b t  t h a t  t h i n k s  t h a t  s o u n d  i s  p r o b a b l y  impermanent; it tends toward the factual because sound is in fact impermanent. Doubt tending toward the non-factual is, for example, the doubt that thinks that sound is probably permanent; it tends toward the non-factual because sound is not permanent. Equal doubt i s ,  f o r  e x a m p l e ,  t h e  doubt that wonders whether sound is permanent or impermanent; it is equal because it is equally divided between the two possibilities.   Someone might say that wrong consciousness and doubting consciousness are contradictory.  [To this we reply], “It [absurdly] follows that whatever is a doubting consciousness is necessarily not a wrong consciousness because these two are contradictory. The reason has been asserted.”  If this consequence is accepted, “It [absurdly] follows that the subject, a doubting consciousness that thinks that sound is probably permanent, is not a wrong consciousness because of being a doubting consciousness. This is because it is doubt tending toward the non-factual.”  If the consequence [that a doubting consciousness that thinks that sound is probably permanent is not a wrong consciousness] is accepted, “It follows that the subject, [a doubting consciousness that thinks that sound is probably permanent,] is a wrong consciousness because of being a conceptual wrong consciousness. This is because it is a wrong thought.  “The pervasion [that whatever is a wrong thought is necessarily a conceptual wrong consciousness] exists because wrong thought and conceptual wrong consciousness are synonyms. This is because [Kay-drup’s] Clearing Away Darkness of Mind With Respect to the Seven Treatises says, ‘Wrong thought and conceptual wrong consciousness are synonyms.’”  Furthermore, “It follows that wrong consciousness and doubting consciousness are not contradictory because wrong thought and doubting consciousness are not contradictory. This is because the doubt that thinks that sound is probably permanent is both a wrong thought and a doubting consciousness. In accordance with that Kay-drup’s Clearing Away Darkness of Mind With Respect to the Seven Treatises says, “The assertion that all wrong thoughts possess an aspect that is definite as a one-pointed mode of apprehension is incorrect because it would follow that the conceptual consciousness thinking that sound is probably permanent would not be a wrong thought,” and [continues] “Therefore, wrong thought and doubt are not contradictory.”  Someone might say that doubt and wrong consciousness are contradictory or mutually exclusive, that is, that there is nothing that is both a doubt and a wrong consciousness. However, the doubt that thinks that sound is probably permanent is both a doubt and a wrong consciousness.   STUDENT: Can doubt tending towards the factual be a correctly assuming consciousness? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Good question. It would seem that doubt tending towards the factual could be a correctly assuming consciousness in that it fulfills the definition of correctly assuming consciousness of being controvertible, factually concordant, and a determinative knower. However, a correctly assuming consciousness does not doubt or wonder, whereas doubt tending towards the factual wonders whether something is this or that. Therefore, doubt tending towards the factual is not a correctly assuming consciousness. Thus, even though it would seem that doubt tending towards the factual fits the definition of a correctly assuming consciousness, it is not a correctly assuming consciousness because even though a correctly assuming consciousness does not realize its object it is very close to that and it lacks the element of doubt. When the definition of correctly assuming consciousness says that it is a “factually concordant determinative knower,” this excludes it being a doubt. For example, there is no doubt or wondering in the case of a correctly assuming consciousness that arises from hearing “Sound is impermanent.” A correctly assuming consciousness concords with the fact of sound being impermanent and does not wonder whether or not sound is impermanent.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
78 (E) Wrong consciousnesses With regard to the fifth [of the five non-valid consciousnesses], wrong consciousnesses, there are two parts: (1) definition and (2) divisions. 1. Definition Our own system is that the definition of a wrong consciousness is: a knower that engages its object erroneously. 2. Divisions  Second, when wrong consciousnesses are divided, there are two:  (1) conceptual wrong consciousnesses and  (2) non-conceptual wrong consciousnesses.   Examples of the first are a conceptual consciousness apprehending sound as permanent and a conceptual consciousness apprehending the horns of a rabbit.  With regard to the second, [non-conceptual wrong consciousnesses], there are two: (1) mental consciousnesses [that are non-conceptual wrong consciousnesses] and  (2) sense consciousnesses [that are non-conceptual wrong consciousnesses].   The first, [a mental non-conceptual wrong consciousness] is, for example, a dream consciousness that clearly sees as blue the blue of a dream. This subject is a mental consciousness, a non-conceptual consciousness, and a wrong consciousness. Respectively,  • [it is a mental consciousness] because of being a dream consciousness;  • [it is a non-conceptual consciousness] because of being a consciousness that is free from being a determinative knower that apprehends a sound generality and a meaning generality as suitable to be mixed; and  • [it is a wrong consciousness] because of being a consciousness that apprehends its object, a form which is a phenomenon source, as blue, whereas it does not exist as blue.   The reason why a dream consciousness is non-conceptual is that, when one falls asleep, all the coarse sense consciousnesses are withdrawn and only a subtle consciousness remains, and there are no conceptual subtle consciousnesses. However, it cannot be said that all the dream consciousnesses of ordinary beings are wrong consciousnesses because there are many types of ordinary beings including those on the path of accumulation and path of preparation. For example, sometimes when one goes to sleep with a doubt, the doubt is resolved in a dream. That would not be a wrong consciousness. However, the text says that a dream consciousness of the blue of a dream clearly appearing as blue is a mental consciousness, a non-conceptual consciousness, and a wrong consciousness respectively • because of being a dream consciousness;  • because of being a consciousness that is free from being a determinative knower that apprehends a sound generality and a meaning generality as suitable to be mixed; and  • because of being a consciousness that apprehends its object, a form which is a phenomenon source, as blue, whereas it does not exist as blue.  The blue of a dream is a phenomena source, an object of mental consciousness, whereas actual blue is a form source, an object of eye consciousness.   STUDENT: Then, if the dream consciousness cognizes the blue of a dream as the blue of a dream it is not a wrong consciousness, whereas if it cognizes the blue of a dream as a form source it is a wrong consciousness. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right. STUDENT: Then in the case of the meditation on the earth covered with skeletons, the skeletons are form that is a phenomena source and the consciousness apprehending them is not a wrong consciousness. In other words, if a yogi directly realizes the skeletons as a phenomena source he 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
79 has a correct consciousness, whereas if he thinks that they are a form source he has a wrong consciousness. Is that right? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right.  STUDENT: W i t h  r e s p e c t  t o  t h e  s t a t e m e n t  t h a t  t h e r e  a r e  n o  d r e a m  c o n s c i o u s n e s s e s  t h a t  a r e  conceptual consciousnesses, I once had a dream in which I was convincing my fellow students that there is no difference between the Chittamatra and Prasangika positions. This was a very conceptual dream, so why can a dream consciousnesses not be a conceptual consciousnesses? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e r e  i s  a  d i f f e r e n c e  b e t w e e n  s a y i n g  t h a t  t h e r e  a r e  c o n c e p t s  i n  a  dream and that the dream consciousness is a conceptual consciousness.  STUDENT: T h e r e  a r e  m a t h e m a t i c i a n s  w h o  s o l v e  c o m p l i c a t e d  c a l c u l a t i o n s  i n  t h e i r  dreams and when they wake up they have the answer to them. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Generally speaking there are three reasons that prove that a dream consciousness is a mental consciousness, a non-conceptual consciousness, and a wrong consciousness. It is mental consciousness because it is a dream consciousness, it is non-conceptual consciousness because it does not apprehend a sound generality and a meaning generality as suitable to be mixed, and it is a wrong consciousness because its object appears to be something that it is not. In this sense a dream consciousness is said to be non-conceptual. Whatever appears to a dream consciousness, for example, blue, is the object of a mental consciousness. It is not the object of a sense consciousness and therefore it is not coarse blue. Also since the blue clearly appears without a meaning generality, a superimposition of the mind, a dream consciousness is a non-conceptual consciousness. Although something clearly appears in a dream, we are not always able to remember it later on.   Wednesday morning, 28 September 2005  However, we say that for the person who is dreaming that [dream consciousness that clearly sees the blue of a dream as blue] is a factually concordant sense consciousness.  The blue of a dream clearly appears to a dream consciousness, which is a wrong consciousness. However, for the person w h o  i s  d r e a m i n g ,  i t  i s  a  f a c t u a l l y  c o n c o r d a n t  s e n s e  c o n s c i o u s n e s s .  Although in general a dream consciousness is not a sense consciousness but a mental consciousness, for the person who is dreaming it is a factually concordant sense consciousness. Therefore, even though it is a wrong consciousness it does not appear as such to the person who is dreaming. It is similar to the fact that Jetsun Chogyi Gyeltsen asserts that Maitreya is a buddha, whereas Panchen Sonam Dragpa asserts that he is a tenth-ground bodhisattva based on what is said in a particular prayer. Jetsun Chogyi Gyeltsen says that in the retinue of the Buddha there were many disciples including Maitreya and that for the disciples of that time he manifested as a tenth-ground bodhisattva, although he was in fact a buddha. Likewise, while a dream consciousness is a wrong consciousness, for the person who is dreaming it is not a wrong consciousness. However, this can be debated since this is not a very convincing conclusion.  STUDENT: To the person a wrong consciousness always appears to be correct. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The Svatantrika Madhyamaka and Prasangika Madhyamaka say that in the case of the conception of true existence, a pot is false, however to the conception of true existence it appears as true, therefore it is a conventional truth or a false truth. To the conception of true existence the pot appears to exist truly, whereas it is false and is therefore known as a conventional truth. There is a lot to be said about this subject.  Examples of the second, wrong consciousnesses that are sense consciousnesses, are a sense consciousness that sees snow mountains as blue and a sense consciousness that sees a white conch as yellow.  The examples of wrong consciousnesses that are sense consciousnesses are: • a sense consciousness to which a snow mountain appears as blue and  • a sense consciousness to which a white conch appears as yellow.   Next there is a division of awarenesses and knowers into three. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
80 (3) Threefold division of awarenesses and knowers With respect to the threefold division of awarenesses and knowers there are three parts:  (1) explanation of conceptual consciousnesses that t a k e  a  m e a n i n g  g e n e r a l i t y  a s  their apprehended object,  (2) explanation of non-conceptual non-mistaken consciousnesses that take a specifically characterized phenomenon as their apprehended object, and  (3) explanation of non-conceptual mistaken consciousnesses that take a clear appearance of a non-existent as their apprehended object. (A) Conceptual consciousnesses that take a meaning generality as their apprehended object With respect to the first, [conceptual consciousnesses that take a meaning generality as their apprehended object] there are two parts: (1) definition and (2) divisions. 1. Definition First, the definition of a conceptual consciousness is: a determinative knower that apprehends a sound [generality] and a meaning [generality] as suitable to be mixed.  The [term] “sound” of “sound and meaning”, [refers] to a sound generality and the “meaning” [refers] to a meaning generality. That which apprehends those two as mixed apprehends a collection of those two.  There is a purpose for saying “suitable” [to be mixed]; it is because it is necessary to include conceptual consciousnesses in the continuum of a person who has not trained in nomenclature. This is because a conceptual consciousness in the continuum of such [a person] does not apprehend sound and meaning generalities as mixed, but merely as suitable to be mixed. 2. Divisions Second, when conceptual consciousnesses are divided, there are three:  (1) conceptual consciousnesses that apprehend only a sound generality,  (2) conceptual consciousnesses that apprehend only a meaning generality, and  (3) conceptual consciousnesses that apprehend both a sound and a meaning generality.  An illustration of the first [a conceptual consciousness that apprehends only a sound generality] is a conceptual consciousness in the continuum of a person who does not know that a bulbous flat-based thing that is able to perform the function of holding water is a pot which, generated in dependence on merely the sound “pot” apprehends pot.  An illustration of the second [a conceptual consciousness that a p p r e h e n d s  only a meaning generality] is a conceptual consciousness in the continuum of such a person [who does not know that a bulbous flat-based thing able to perform the function of holding water is a pot] which, generated in dependence on merely seeing a bulbous thing apprehends a bulbous thing.  An illustration of the third [a conceptual consciousness that apprehends both a sound and a meaning generality] is a conceptual consciousness – in the continuum of a person who knows pot – apprehending a pot.  “What are the sound and meaning generalities of pot?”  That appearance which is an appearance as pot to the first conceptual consciousness [in the above three illustrations] is just a sound generality.  That appearance which is the appearance of a bulbous t h i n g  t o  t h e  s e c o n d  c o n c e p t u a l  consciousness [in the above illustrations] is just a meaning generality. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
81  When either a pot or a bulbous thing appears to the third conceptual consciousness [in the above illustrations] there is the appearance of both a sound and a meaning generality.  The definition of a conceptual consciousness says that it is “a determinative knower that apprehends a sound generality and a meaning generality as suitable to be mixed.” “Suitable to be mixed” indicates that for some conceptual consciousnesses a sound generality and a meaning generality are not mixed; for example, for conceptual consciousnesses in the continuum of a person who is not trained in nomenclature, that is, (i) someone who does not know the name for a pot although they apprehend a pot, or (ii) someone who apprehends the sound “pot” but does not know what a pot is.  STUDENT: Conceptual consciousnesses apprehend a sound generality and meaning generality as suitable to be mixed. Does “mixed” mean that the sound generality and the meaning generality are mixed or that the sound generality and the meaning generality are mixed with the object? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: In the case of a person who knows what a pot is, that is, knows that it is a bulbous flat-based thing that is able to perform the function of holding water, for his or her conceptual consciousness apprehending pot, the meaning generality and sound generality are one. In other words, they do not appear separately but are indistinguishable. However, in the case of someone who hears the sound “pot” but does not know what a pot is, he has a conceptual consciousness based only on the sound “pot.” At that time sound generality alone is the appearing object. However, it still cannot be said that there is no meaning generality because all conceptual consciousnesses have a meaning generality. When later on that person learns what a pot is, for the conceptual consciousness of that person there is both a meaning generality and a sound generality. Likewise, in the case of someone who sees a flat-bottomed bulbous-bellied water container but does not know its name, he has a conceptual consciousness for which there is only a meaning generality. “That which apprehends those two as mixed apprehends a collection of those two,” means that it apprehends these the meaning generality and sound generality together. Thus, for a conceptual consciousness apprehending pot in someone who knows what a pot is, the meaning generality and sound generality are not separate. This is the meaning of “mixed.”  In general the appearing object of all conceptual consciousnesses is a meaning generality. This is a general statement but in the case of a particular conceptual consciousness apprehending pot, the sound generality and meaning generality can be discussed separately, whereby there can be a sound generality alone, a meaning generality alone, or both a meaning generality and a sound generality. Thus, when someone who does not know what a pot is hears the sound “pot,” there is only a sound generality of pot. When someone apprehends pot but does not know what it is, there is only a meaning generality of pot.  ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: The word “meaning” in meaning generality refers to the thing itself.   A person who develops a conceptual consciousness apprehending a bulbous flat-based thing that is able to perform the function of holding water but does not know that it is a pot has a meaning generality, but because he does not know its name he does not have a sound generality.  ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: The word “sound” in sound generality means “term” or “name.”   STUDENT: I s  t h e  s o u n d  g e n e r a l i t y  a l w a y s  l i n k e d  w i t h  l a n g u a g e  s u c h  t h a t  s o m e o n e  w i t h o u t  language, for example, someone who grew up in a forest, would not have a sound generality? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A l t h o u g h  someone does not have a language, he would have a system of grunts and so forth that serve to communicate. But probably such people do not have pots! STUDENT: Do animals have meaning generalities and conceptual consciousnesses? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e y  d o h a v e  c o n c e p t u a l  c o n s c i o u s n e s s e s  b e c a u s e  t h e y  r e c o g n i z e  their mother, flee when frightened, come when they are called, and so forth. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Science is based on direct perception therefore it must be accepted. For example, in the Treasury of Manifest Dharma the world is said to be flat and so forth but this cannot be accepted anymore because it contradicts the direct perception of the world as round. However, at the time of the Buddha the description of the world with Mount Meru was useful for his listeners. When we offer the mandala with the four continents and so forth it is an appearance to the mind. However, while there is no difficulty in saying that the four continents do not exist, there still remains some difficulties in saying whether or not Mount Meru exists. Just as something can be so small that we cannot see it, perhaps it is also possible that something can be so big that we cannot see it. 83  STUDENT: I do not want to insist on the existence of Mount Meru but while our perception of the world certainly cannot confirm the existence of Mount Meru it is not clear whether or not it can confirm its non-existence. One of Marpa’s disciples once offered him a lot of gold, which he took and threw on the ground. The disciple was upset with this but Marpa told him that he should not be so miserly because the mountain on which they were standing was itself formed of gold. Therefore, things can change due to the degeneration of merit, for example, jewel mountains can become stone mountains. Likewise, in the case of a god who perceives nectar, a human being who perceives water, and a hungry ghost who perceives pus and blood in relation to the same liquid, which one is right? These things are not easy to understand.   If someone asks, “Is whatever appears to a conceptual consciousness that [is produced in] dependence on just sound necessarily only a sound generality?”, there is no [such] pervasion. This is because the appearance of a prominent rabbit horn to a conceptual consciousness apprehending the horns of a rabbit is an appearance to a conceptual consciousness which [is generated in] dependence on just sound but is not only a sound generality. That [it is not only a sound generality] follows because it is both the sound and the meaning generality of the horns of a rabbit.  Just because a conceptual consciousness arises in dependence on sound alone, it does not mean that such a conceptual consciousness has a sound generality alone. For example, in the case of the horns of a rabbit, the horns that clearly appear arise in dependence on sound alone, however there is not just a sound generality because there is both a sound generality and a meaning generality of the horns of a rabbit. There is a meaning generality even though the thing itself, the horns of a rabbit, does not exist.  Wednesday afternoon, 28 September 2005  We have discussed many types of minds. Now we should examine our own mind to see how many of them we have. Among valid cognizers there are direct valid cognizers and inferential valid cognizers. Among direct valid cognizers, there are sense, mental, self-knowing, and yogic direct valid cognizers. A self-knower is a unique assertion of Sautrantika, it is not asserted by other schools. Yogic direct valid cognizers are found in arya beings, whereas sense and mental direct valid cognizers are also found in ordinary beings.   Among inferential valid cognizers there are inferential cognizers by the power of the fact, inferential cognizers through renown, and inferential cognizers through belief. The third is difficult to recognize while the first two can be found in our minds. For example, when we infer the existence of fire due to the presence of smoke, we have a inferential valid cognizer by the power of fact. We also have inferential cognizers through renown. We also have conceptual 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
84 consciousnesses that a p p r e h e n d  a  s o u n d  g e n e r a l i t y  a n d  a  m e a n i n g  g e n e r a l i t y  a s  s u i t a b l e  t o  b e  mixed. There are conceptual consciousnesses that apprehend only a sound generality, conceptual consciousnesses that apprehend only a meaning generality, and conceptual consciousnesses that apprehend both a sound and a meaning generality.  Among non-valid cognizers there are subsequent cognizers, correctly assuming consciousnesses, awarenesses to which an object appears but is not ascertained, doubt, and wrong consciousness. We have all of them, but in particular we have wrong consciousness, the worst of which, as it interferes with our practice of Dharma, is the apprehension of permanence. This is because even though intellectually we understand that we will die, we do not want to think about or accept this unpleasant fact. Death is considered to be coarse impermanence. We deny death and want to believe that we will not die. This mind stops us from practicing the Dharma. This apprehension of permanence is a wrong consciousness that must be abandoned. This is one reason for trying to understand these different types of minds. We should not study them just to gain knowledge, but rather to be able to identify good minds and strive to strengthen them and to identify bad minds and strive to eliminate them. In addition to identifying the various types of mind, we should try to understand how they apprehend their objects. We should not study these minds from afar, as if they were “over there.” Instead we should look inside ourselves at our own mind and strive to identify these different kinds of minds and to understand how they apprehend their objects. Milarepa, when talking about logicians who can skillfully debate the impermanence of sound, said that it makes him laugh that they do this while still holding to their own permanence. Since this is of no use whatsoever, we need to apply our understanding to ourselves.  When conceptual consciousness are divided [in another way], there are two:  (1) conceptual consciousnesses that affix names, and  (2) conceptual consciousnesses that affix meanings.   A conceptual consciousness that apprehends [its object within] thinking “This bulbous thing is a pot,” is both [a conceptual consciousness that affixes a name and one that affixes a meaning]. Respectively, • [it is the first] because of being a determinative knower that apprehends [its object] within affixing the name “pot” to the object [the bulbous thing]; • [it is the second] because of being a determinative knower that apprehends [its object] within affixing attributes to a substratum.  A conceptual consciousness that apprehends its object within thinking “This bulbous thing is a pot,” is both a conceptual consciousness that affixes a name and one that affixes a meaning. It is the first because to a bulbous flat-based thing that is able to perform the function of holding water it affixes the name “pot.” It is the second because it affixes an attribute, a name, to a substratum, the bulbous flat-based thing.   Whatever is a conceptual consciousness that affixes a meaning is not necessarily one that affixes a name, for a conceptual consciousness that apprehends [its object within] thinking, “This person has a stick,” is a conceptual consciousness that affixes [only] a meaning. It is a conceptual consciousness that apprehends [its object] within affixing an attribute – stick – to a substratum - person.  The conceptual consciousness that thinks “This person is holding a stick” is a consciousness that affixes a meaning because the substratum is the person and the attribute is the stick. It is not that the person is a  s t i c k ,  b u t  t h a t  t h e  p e r s o n  has a  s t i c k .  T h e  p e r s o n  i s  t h e  s u b s t r a t u m  t h a t  h a s  t h e  attribute, stick. On the other hand, when one conceives of a bulbous flat-based thing that is able to perform the function of holding water as a pot, the name “pot” is affixed to the thing itself and therefore it is a conceptual consciousness that affixes both a name and a meaning. The first thinks “The person has a stick,” whereas the second thinks “This bulbous flat-based thing is a pot.”   Also, whatever is a conceptual consciousness is not necessarily either of those two, for a conceptual consciousness that apprehends merely the substratum pot is neither of those two.

85  Whatever is a conceptual consciousness is not pervaded b y  b e i n g  e i t h e r  a  c o n c e p t u a l  consciousness that affixes a name or a conceptual consciousness that affixes a meaning, for example, a conceptual consciousness apprehending the substratum pot is neither of the two.   STUDENT: W h a t  i s  t h e  d i f f e r e n c e  b e t w e e n  a c o n c e p t u a l  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  t h a t  a f f i x e s  n a m e s  a n d  meanings and a conceptual consciousness that apprehends a sound generality and a meaning generality? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: They are not the same. The example of a conceptual consciousness that neither affixes names or meaning is a conceptual consciousness apprehending pot, yet this is still a conceptual consciousness that apprehends a sound generality and a meaning generality as suitable to be mixed. The word “affix” means, for example, to affix the name “pot” to the thing that is pot. In the case of both a conceptual consciousness apprehending “The person has a stick” and a conceptual consciousness apprehending “This bulbous flat-based thing i s a  p o t , ”  t h e r e  i s  a  meaning generality. There is no pervasion that w h a t e v e r  i s  a  c o n c e p t u a l  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  i s  a  conceptual consciousness affixing a name or a conceptual consciousness affixing meaning, but whatever is a conceptual consciousness is pervaded by apprehending a sound generality and meaning generality as suitable to be mixed.   When we hear the sound or term “pot,” a conceptual consciousness apprehending pot arises that has a sound generality of pot as its object. When later on one sees a pot and understands it to be a pot, a sound generality and a meaning generality are mixed together. When we hear the sound “pot” without knowing what a pot is, there is no affixing of the name “pot.”   STUDENT: When not having seen a pot, a pot is described to us and we develop a mental image of pot, is that a sound generality of pot? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It depends on the person. If that person is able to develop an accurate mental image of pot, both a sound generality of pot and a meaning generality of pot are present. However, when a pot is just described in words, it is possible to develop a very strange image of pot!  STUDENT: With respect to the conceptual consciousness affixing meanings does the object have to be an existent? Is, for example, a conceptual consciousness apprehending the horns of a rabbit a conceptual consciousness affixing meanings? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I t  i s  n o t  c l e a r  h e r e  i n  t h e  t e x t  w h e t h e r  o r  n o t  a  c o n c e p t u a l  consciousness affixing a meaning has to be factually concordant, but it is probably pervaded by being factually concordant.   When conceptual consciousnesses are divided [in another way] there are two:  (1) factually concordant conceptual consciousnesses and  (2) factually discordant conceptual consciousnesses.   The definition of a factually concordant conceptual consciousness is: a factually concordant determinative knower that apprehends a sound generality and a meaning generality as suitable to be mixed.  If something is an established base, the conceptual consciousness apprehending it is necessarily a factually concordant conceptual consciousness.   An established base is an existent. If it is an established base, the conceptual consciousness apprehending it is pervaded by being a factually concordant conceptual consciousness.   The definition of a factually discordant conceptual consciousness is: a factually discordant determinative knower that apprehends a sound generality and a meaning generality as suitable to be mixed.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
86 If something is not an established base, the conceptual consciousness apprehending it is necessarily a factually discordant conceptual consciousness.  That which is not an established base is a non-existent. If it is not an established base, the conceptual consciousness apprehending it is necessarily a factually discordant conceptual consciousness. In other words, it is a wrong consciousness.  STUDENT: With respect to conceptual consciousnesses, can they only be factually concordant or factually discordant or are there those that are indeterminate? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e y  h a v e  t o  b e  o n e  o r  t h e  o t h e r ,  u n l e s s  w e  p o s i t  c o n c e p t u al consciousness itself. STUDENT: For  e x a mpl e ,  t he r e  a r e  c onc e pt ua l  c ons c i ous ne s s  t ha t  pr oj e c t  qua l i t i e s  on ot he r s  t ha t  may or may not exist. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It cannot be said that such a conceptual consciousness is right or that it is wrong. STUDENT: So what kind of consciousness is it that says “This person is stupid” or “This person is intelligent”? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I  c a n n o t  s a y .  O n e  c o n c e p t u a l  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  i s  f a c t u a l l y  d i s c o r d a n t  and one is factually concordant but which one is which depends on whether the person really is stupid or really is intelligent.   STUDENT: What about beauty, which is in the eye of the beholder, in which case there is nothing that is objectively beautiful? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: We should keep to the Sautrantika position which says that beauty exist from its own side because it is a specifically characterized phenomenon.   STUDENT: What woul d a Sautrantika say about African women who put things in their l ips to enlarge them? While African men might find this attractive, we find it very ugly. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The way things appear to us is not pure. Therefore, that we believe in what appears to us as beautiful does not conflict with the Sautrantika position. STUDENT: It conflicts with the Sautrantika position that beauty exists in the object and not in the eye of the beholder. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Just because a beautiful object exists from its own side does not mean that it is in reality beautiful because our appearances are not pure. For example, when the Miss World or Miss Italy contests take place, who can say which woman is the most beautiful? Our minds are covered with ignorance, which affects our perception. The eye consciousness apprehending an object does not perceive beauty, beauty is determined by other consciousnesses, latencies, and so forth. However, this does not conflict with the fact that everything exists from its own side. Our thoughts affect our sense consciousnesses. For example, if we think badly of someone, when we see him everything about him appears to be unpleasant, his body, voice, and so forth. Likewise, when we think good about someone, everything about that person appears to be attractive, his body, his smile (ha-ha!), and so forth.   According to Prasangika if beauty existed from its own side, there could not be a projection or imputation of beauty by thought. That is in fact how they refute the Sautrantika position that phenomena exist from their own side. However, Sautrantika says that things exist from their own side, yet as long as a conceptual consciousness is factually concordant it is correct whereas if it is factually discordant it is wrong. A conceptual consciousness that is factually concordant sees that which is beautiful as beautiful and that which is ugly as ugly. STUDENT: P r e v iously you said that beauty is not perceived by the eye consciousness but that would not be a Sautrantika position. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Beauty is seen by the eye consciousness, but it is not determined to be beauty, as this is done by other minds. Is beauty shape and color? STUDENT: It is a mere judgment, nothing more. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: W h e r e  i s  b e a u t y ?  F o r  e x a m p l e ,  i n  t h e  c a s e  o f  M i s s  I t a l y ,  w h i l e  t h e  contestants put a lot of make-up on, if they were really beautiful they would not need any make-up at a l l !  I t  i s  d i f f i c u l t  t o  b e  c e r t a i n  a b o u t  w h a t  b e a u t y  a c t u a l l y  i s  s i n c e  i t  d e p e n d s  o n  v a r i o u s  factors. For me beauty is not in the skin, the facial features, and so forth, rather someone is beautiful when it makes other happy to be around him or her. The best beauty is a beautiful mind!  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
87 (B) Non-conceptual non-mistaken consciousnesses that take a specifically characterized phenomenon as their apprehended object With regard to the second [of the threefold division of awarenesses and knowers, non-conceptual non-mistaken consciousnesses that take a specifically characterized phenomenon as their apprehended object], there are two parts: (1) definition and (2) divisions. 1. Definition The definition of something’s being a non-conceptual non-mistaken consciousness is: a knower having clear appearance that is non-mistaken with regard to its appearing object.  The two, [non-conceptual non-mistaken consciousness] and directly perceiving awareness are synonyms.  2. Divisions When [non-conceptual non-mistaken consciousnesses] are divided, there are four:  (1) sense direct perceivers,  (2) mental direct perceivers,  (3) self-knowing direct perceivers, and  (4) yogic direct perceivers.   Since they were already explained above one should know this.  These minds have all been explained before in the context of direct perceivers. (C) Non-conceptual mistaken consciousnesses that take a clear appearance of a non-existent as their apprehended object With regard to the third [of the threefold division of awarenesses and knowers, non-conceptual mistaken consciousnesses that take a clear appearance of a non-existent as their apprehended object], there are two parts: (1) definition and (2) divisions. 1. Definition First, the definition of something’s being a non-conceptual mistaken consciousness is: a knower having clear appearance that is mistaken with regard to its appearing object. 2. Divisions When [non-conceptual mistaken consciousnesses] are divided, there are two:  (1) sense consciousnesses that are [non-conceptual mistaken consciousnesses] and (2) mental consciousnesses that are [non-conceptual mistaken consciousnesses].   The definition of the first [a sense consciousness that i s  a  n o n-conceptual mistaken consciousness is: that which is a common locus of  • being a non-conceptual mistaken consciousness and  • being produced in dependence upon a physical sense power that is its uncommon empowering condition.  The definition of the second [a mental consciousness that i s  a  n o n-conceptual mistaken consciousness] is: that which is a common locus of  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
88 • being a non-conceptual mistaken consciousness and  • being produced in dependence upon a mental sense power that is its uncommon empowering condition.  Examples of sense consciousnesses that are non-conceptual mistaken consciousnesses were given before in the context of non-conceptual facsimiles of a direct perceiver that are sense consciousnesses in which the cause of error exists in the basis, in the abode, the object, or in the immediately preceding condition. An example of a mental consciousness that is a non-conceptual mistaken consciousness is a dream consciousness that clearly sees as blue the blue of a dream.  (4) Twofold division of awarenesses  Furthermore, with regard to awarenesses, there are two:  (1) self-knowers and  (2) other-knowers.   The first of these, [self-knower,] and consciousness that is directed only inward are synonyms.   The second, [other-knower,] and consciousness that is turned outward are synonyms.   Sense, mental, and yogic direct perceivers, as well as conceptual consciousnesses are illustrations of the second, [other-knowers].   Whatever is any of those, [a sense direct perceiver, mental direct perceiver, yogic direct perceiver, or conceptual consciousness,] must be an other-knower.  That a self-knower looks inward means that it is directed only inward at a consciousness and does not apprehend forms, sounds, and so forth. An other-knower, on the other hand, is directed to objects, such as forms, sounds, and so forth, that are not included in the mental continuum. Hence, other-knowers include sense direct perceivers, mental direct perceivers, yogic direct perceivers, and conceptual consciousnesses. Sense direct perceivers focus on forms, sounds, and so forth. Mental direct perceivers also focus on these objects but for a very short time. Yogic direct perceivers focus on impermanence, the no-self of persons, and so forth, which are objects that are other than consciousness. Conceptual consciousnesses also apprehend things that are other than consciousness.   A self-knower is not a sense direct perceiver, a mental direct perceiver, a yogic direct perceiver, or a conceptual consciousness, but it is a mental consciousness. It is a direct perceiver and it is a mental consciousness, so why is it not a mental direct perceiver? I think that a self-knower is a special case. Our own system, Sautrantika, says that self-knower and other-knower are mutually exclusive.   STUDENT: I s  a  s e l f-knower not excluded from being a mental direct perceiver just because the definition of mental direct perceiver includes the specification that it is “an other-knower”? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right.  Thursday morning, 29 September 2005  The difference between self-knowers and other-knowers is that the former only focus inward, that is, on consciousness itself, and do not focus on other objects, whereas the latter focus outward, that is, on external objects such as forms and so forth. This is our position, however there is the following debate with respect to it.  With regard to self-knowers and other-knowers being contradictory, someone might say, “It follows that the subject, a self-knower in the continuum of a buddha superior, is not an other-knower because of being a self-knower.” 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
89  If someone says that the reason is not established, “It follows that a self-knower in the continuum of a buddha superior is a self-knower because there exists a self-knower in the continuum of [a buddha superior]. This is because a self-knowing direct perceiver that experiences a mental consciousness in the continuum of [a buddha superior] is a self-knower in the continuum of [a buddha superior].”  If someone says that the reason is not established, “It follows with regard to the subject, a buddha superior, that the self-knowing direct perceiver that experiences a mental consciousness in his continuum is a self-knower in his continuum because he is a person possessing form.” That self-knowers and other-knowers are contradictory is stated in accordance with the first chapter of Jay-dzun-ba’s (rJe-btsun-pa) Analysis of the Limits of Pervasion in the First Chapter [of (Dharmakirti’s) Commentary on (Dignaga’s) “Compendium on Valid cognition”] and is not my own fabrication.  If the basic consequence [that a self-knower in the continuum of a buddha superior is not an other-knower] is accepted, [someone might say], “It follows that that subject [a self-knower in the continuum of a buddha superior] is an other-knower because of being a consciousness turned outward. This is because [a self-knower in the continuum of a buddha superior] takes external phenomena, forms, and so forth, as its object.”  [To this we respond] there is no pervasion.  If someone says that the reason is not established, “It follows that the subject, [a self-knower in the continuum of a buddha superior] is a consciousness that takes external phenomena, forms, and so forth, as its object because of being an exalted knower that takes all phenomena as its object. This is because [a self-knower in the continuum of a buddha superior] is an omniscient consciousness.  There exists a self-knower in the continuum of an omniscient buddha because he is a being and therefore has consciousness. With respect to this, the opponent says that that self-knower cannot be an other-knower since it is a self-knower. The point here is that since all the consciousnesses of a buddha are pervaded by being omniscient consciousnesses, also a self-knower in his continuum must know all phenomena and not just consciousness. However, there is no pervasion that just because a self-knower is an omniscient consciousness that takes forms and so forth as its object that it is not a self-knower. Here the point of the debate regards the statement that a self-knower is synonymous with a consciousness that is directed only inward, with respect to which the opponent says that it follows that a self-knower cannot be an other-knower because it is a self-knower and therefore it follows that the self-knower in the continuum of a buddha does not know external phenomena.   Since a self-knower is a  c o n s c i o u s n ess that is directed only inward, this problem must be resolved in another way. It is resolved by saying that “directed only inward” must be understood to mean that a self-knower takes consciousness as its main object, but it is not that there is no self-knower that takes external objects as its object. In other words, while even the very name “self-knower” implies that it knows consciousness, it does not mean that it exclusively takes consciousness as its object. This resolution is not found in the text but c o m e s  a b o u t  a s  t h e  conclusion of a debate. It is said that if everyone were to agree, there would be no gaining of wisdom. For this reason a true scholar examines all points of view through taking different positions.  STUDENT: I n  L a t i  R i n p o c h e ’ s  b o o k ,  Mind in Tibetan Buddhism, it says that a buddha’s sense consciousnesses are not omniscient consciousness because they are not yogic direct perceivers. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Our position is not the same as that of Ganden Shartse!  STUDENT: Then, according to our tradition the sense consciousnesses of a buddha are omniscient? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: According to us all the consciousnesses of a buddha are omniscient. STUDENT: So every mind of a buddha would also know consciousness? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Although every mind of a buddha realizes consciousness, that does not mean that every mind of a buddha is a self-knower. A self-knower is a consciousness that mainly takes consciousness as its object. However, all objects appear directly to the mental consciousness of a buddha because it is omniscient. STUDENT: So a buddha would be an exception to the definition of a self-knower? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T o  s a y  t h a t  t h e  d e f i n i t i o n  o f  a  s e l f-knower only applies to sentient beings and not to buddhas is another way of resolving the problem regarding the self-knowers in the continuum of a buddha.

Let us go back to where it says in the text:  If someone says that the definition of “appearing object” is “that which is known through having appeared”, [we will respond], “It [absurdly] follows that the definition of ‘appearing object of a certain mind’ is ‘that which is known through having appeared to that mind’ because the definition of ‘appearing object’ is ‘that which is known through having appeared’. You have explicitly accepted the reason.”  If the consequence [that the definition of “appearing object of a certain mind” is “that which is known through having appeared to that mind”] is accepted, “It [absurdly] follows that the subject, the meaning-generality of permanent sound, is that which is known through having appeared to a conceptual consciousness apprehending sound to be permanent, because of being the appearing object of a conceptual consciousness apprehending sound to be permanent.”  If he says the reason is not established, “It follows with respect to the subject, permanent sound, that its meaning-generality is the appearing object of the conceptual consciousness apprehending it because it is selfless.”  If the above consequence [that the meaning-generality of permanent sound is that which is known through having appeared to a conceptual c o n s c i o u s n e s s  a p p r e h e n d i n g  s o u n d  t o  b e  p e r m a n e n t ]  i s  accepted, “It [absurdly] follows that the subject, the meaning-generality of permanent sound, is that which is known by a conceptual consciousness apprehending sound to be permanent because of being that which is known through having appeared to [such a conceptual consciousness].”  If that consequence is accepted, “It [absurdly] follows that the subject, [the meaning-generality of permanent sound,] is that which is realized by [such a conceptual consciousness].”  If this consequence is accepted, “It [absurdly] follows that there exists realization by a conceptual consciousness apprehending sound to be permanent.”  If this is accepted, “It follows with respect to the subject, a conceptual consciousness apprehending sound to be permanent, that realization by it does not exist because it is a wrong consciousness; this is so because it is a conceptual wrong consciousness. 91  If it is accepted [that the definition of ‘the object of a certain awareness’ is ‘that which is known by that awareness’], “It [absurdly] follows that the subject, a single moon, is that which is known by a sense consciousness that sees a double moon because of being the object of a sense consciousness that sees a double moon.”  If someone says that the reason is not established, “It follows that the subject, [a single moon,] is that [which is known by a sense consciousness that sees a double moon] because of being the appearing object of that [sense consciousness that sees a double moon].”  If someone says that the reason is not established, “It follows that the subject, a single moon, is the appearing object of that [sense consciousness that sees a double moon] because of appearing to that [sense consciousness that sees a double moon].”  If someone says that there is no pervasion, [we respond] “It follows with respect to the subject, a sense consciousness that sees a double moon, that whatever appears to it must be its appearing object because it is a non-conceptual consciousness.  If someone says that the reason is not established, “It follows that the subject, a sense consciousness that sees a double moon, is a non-conceptual consciousness because of being a sense consciousness.”  If the above consequence [that a single moon is that which is known by a sense consciousness that sees a double moon] is accepted, “It [absurdly] follows that the subject, a single moon, is realized by a sense consciousness that sees a double moon because of being that which is known by a [sense consciousness that sees a double moon]. The reason has been accepted [although it should not have been.]”  This debate is about the definition of appearing object. If someone says that the definition of appearing object is “that which is known through having appeared,” we can take as an example the appearing object of the conceptual consciousness apprehending sound to be permanent which is the meaning generality of permanent sound. Based on the opponent’s definition of appearing object, it would follow that the meaning generality of permanent sound is known through having appeared. However, the meaning generality of permanent sound is not known because it is not realized. It is not realized by this conceptual consciousness because it is a wrong consciousness. According to the Sautrantika school, if something is known, it is realized. The definition of mind is that which is clear and knowing. If something is known, it is realized. Because ignorance is a mind, it knows.  STUDENT: What does ignorance realize? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Its appearance clearly appears to it. From the point of view of it being a knower, it knows its appearance. But does it know? It does not know because it is non-knowing (ma rig pa).  STUDENT: Then it would follow that the definition above of appearing object is not wrong. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e  a p p e a r i n g  o b j e c t  o f  i g n o r a n c e  – a  m e a n i n g  g e n e r a l i t y  o f  permanence or a self – does not fulfill the definition of appearing object as being “that which is known t h r o u g h  h a v i n g  a p p e a r e d . ”  T h e  a p p e a r i n g  o b j e c t  o f  i g n o r a n c e  c l e a r l y  a p p e a r s  t o  i t  a n d  from this point of view ignorance is a knower with respect to its appearing object, however it does not know its appearing object in the sense of “that which is known through having appeared.” STUDENT: Then why is this definition incorrect? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e  m e a n i n g  g e n e r a l i t y  o f  p e r m a n e n t  s o u n d  a p p e a r s  t o  t h e  conceptual consciousness apprehending sound to be permanent but is not realized because that consciousness is a wrong consciousness. According to Prasangika all consciousnesses realize their respective appearing object, but not according to this school.  STUDENT: I s  t h a t  c o n c e p t u a l  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  a p p r e h e n d i n g  s o u n d  t o  b e  p e r m a n e n t  a  w r o n g  consciousness because its engaged object does not exist?  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e r e  i s  a  l o t  o f  d e b a t e  a b o u t  w h e t h e r  o r  n o t  a n  i n f e r e n t i a l  v a l i d  cognizer realizes its appearing object, although it definitely realizes its engaged object. However, a conceptual consciousness apprehending sound to be permanent does not realize either its appearing object or its engaged object. It does not realize anything at all because it is a wrong consciousness.

92 This afternoon there will be an ordination, so I would like to talk a bit about the robes worn by monks and nuns. While in India a zen (shawl) was used to catch hair cuttings or to wipe sweat away, in Tibet it became an actual part of the monastic robes. In the past the lower garment or shamtab had three separate folds, two on each side as well as two in the front. The two folds on the right represent the first two truths, true sufferings and true origins, which are to be abandoned and therefore the folds open backward. The two folds on the left represent the last two truths, true cessations and true paths, which are to be realized and therefore the folds open forward. The two folds in the front represented method and wisdom. Nowadays the shamtab is made of less material and therefore there are less folds.  The dongka, the upper vest, has two pointed flaps over the shoulders that are called “the lion’s shoulders,” a vertical insert on both sides of the back that are called “the elephant’s tusks,” and a blue cord around the edge of the armholes. The lion’s shoulders represent fearlessness that is confident and brave, given that lions do not fear other animals. The elephant’s tusks represent the fangs in the mouth of the Lord of Death and should remind the monk or nun of impermanence when he or she puts the dongka over his or her head, it being as if he or she were being devoured in the mouth of the Lord of Death.   The blue cord around the armholes goes back to the time of the Tibetan king Langdharma who in the ninth century set out to destroy Buddhism. At that time three fully-ordained monks fled central Tibet and hid themselves in Amdo. When the king was murdered and the Dharma once again began to flourish, they returned to Tibet. However, in order to grant ordination four fully-ordained monks are necessary. Due to being joined by a Chinese monk, a hashang, they were able to give ordination to others. Since Chinese monks wore blue, the blue cord is in memory of the kindness of that Chinese monk.  There is also a yellow hat with a fringe or mane of hair along its curved top that is still used in some ordination ceremonies. The hat has a red edging along the edges and inside it is lined with white cloth. It has a tail at the back that is blue inside and has two sets of six lines that are sewn into it. The tail has three mala head beads tied to it and a piece of leather cord. The yellow body of the hat represents the pure land of a buddha. The fringe along the top represents the thousand buddhas. The red edging represents Manjushri, the white lining represents Avalokiteshvara, and the blue inside t h e  t a i l  r e p r e s e n t s  V a j r a p a n i .  T h e  t w o  s e t s  o f  s i x  l i n e s  r e p r e s e n t  t h e  t w e l v e-fold division of the scriptures. The three mala head beads represent the three baskets, Sutra, Vinaya, and Abhidharma. The leather cord is a reminder of death. In this way, this h a t  b e c o m e s  v e r y  important!  Monks also wore particular boots in the past that represent the three poisons. The toe of the boot curves upward and points inward, forming a point like the nose of a pig, which represents ignorance. These boots have a piece of multicolored cloth along the top and front of the foot that is like a snake, which represents anger. There are protruding pieces on the side of the foot that are like the wings of a rooster, which represents attachment. Therefore, wearing these boots represents trampling on the three poisons.   In Tibet and also in Dharamsala monks wear a heavy cloak in the winter. Its lapels are shaped like a lotus. The material it is made of is different outside and inside and halfway down the back the material is cut and turned inside out. It is hemmed all the way around with yellow cloth. All of this has a particular symbolism. The hem that goes all the way around the cloak is like gold which cannot tarnish and represents the doctrine of the Buddha. The lotus-shaped lapels represent the fact that in the next life a monk will be miraculously born from a lotus in a pure land. The piece at that back that is turned inside out represents turning one’s back on cyclic existence. The two pieces of cloth that meet together is an a u s p i c i o u s  s i g n  t h a t  w h e n  t h e  B u d d h a ’ s  t e a c h i n g s  d i s a p p e a r ,  Maitreya’s doctrine will immediately appear (although it is usually said that the Buddha’s teachings will gradually disappear and only after some time has passed will Maitreya’s doctrine arise).  STUDENT: Who is it that can wear the yellow fringed hat? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is worn by the Gelugpas, this being the Tibetan order founded by Lama Tsongkhapa who himself was a Sakyapa and had studied under many Sakya lamas. It is recounted that during his time the great pandits wore red hats and that one time, when red cloth had been offered by a benefactor to the lamas to make hats, Lama Tsongkhapa was the last to get a hat and because the red cloth had all been used a hat was made for him out of yellow cloth.   Thursday afternoon, 29 September 2005 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
93  The number of monks and nuns having grown since this morning (six Basic Program students took ordination today), I would like to give some advice to the new sangha. There are two ways of wearing the zen, draped over the arm and draped over the shoulder. When going somewhere one can wear it up on one’s shoulder, but when approaching a place of Dharma or a Dharma teacher, as a sign of respect, one should pull it down over one’s arm. We need to have respect for our elders. While in general the word “elder” means someone who is older than oneself, in the case of monks and nuns, those who took ordination earlier are the elders of those who took ordination later on, and therefore it has nothing to do with the age o f  t h e  p e r s o n .  I t  i s  s a i d  t h a t  s h o w i n g  respect for one’s elders is a way of showing respect for oneself because one benefits from doing so, whereas due to a lack of respect one harms oneself.   When monks and nuns sit or squat down, they should do so slowly while tucking their shamtab in behind their legs. Otherwise they will show everything off!   Monks and nuns should ideally carry a wooden rosary or mala, however they can be hard to find these days. The mala can be made of any kind of wood. They should not carry a mala made of bodhiseeds or rudraksha. While those who are practicing tantra can use malas made of these substances, they should not show them to others. Monks and nuns also should not have malas made of turquoise or coral beads, although this is fine for lay people. They should also not use bone malas, although the head bead on a mala can be made of bone so as to remind them of death-impermanence. The best kind of string is one made of leather, but if that cannot be found any strong thread or string is fine. The beads should not be made of bodhiseeds, rudraksha, or bones because these substances are used to make wrathful malas, whereas the Vinaya is peaceful.  During assemblies, the sangha who are older in ordination should sit in the front and the younger sangha behind. When pujas are done, such as Lama Chopa on the 10th and 25th or Tara Puja on the 8th, the people who are serving the drinks should not cut across the rows of sangha but go down one row and up the other. This is because the sangha, who are seated begi nni ng wi th those who are older in ordination down to those who are younger, represent a stream or continuity, whereby, if one cuts across a row, it is an inauspicious gesture.   Monks and nuns should not wear long shorts, long johns, or pants under their shamtab as they are considered to be signs of a lay person. However, they can be worn if it is really cold and a risk that their health may suffer. It is recounted that one time a Mongolian monk was reading the Vinaya and when he reached the section about not being allowed to sit on animal skins, he pulled his animal skin cushion out from under him. Then, when he reached the section about this being allowed in exceptional circumstances, he pulled it back under him! There was another Mongolian monk who, when he came to study the subject of Vinaya, realized that he no longer possessed vows as he had broken them previously. So at the time of vows he had no Vinaya, and at the time of Vinaya he had no vows! Therefore, it is important to know the essence of the Vinaya.  STUDENT: Why is Vinaya taught in the monastery after the other subjects? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: In Tibet one could not become a fully-ordained monk, a gelong, until one had studied the Vinaya. However, this system has since changed in India. It also used to be a common practice before taking full ordination to ask one’s teacher for advice.  Please, new monks and nuns, keep what I have said in mind. Strictly speaking when someone who knows the Vinaya becomes a fully-ordained monk, he s h o u l d  s e r v e  f o r  a  p e r i o d  o f  f i v e  y e a r s  a  “lama of the place.” This lama is like a parent to whom the monk must ask permission to do certain things. For example, if a monk wanted to go to Cecina, he would have to ask this lama for permission to so whereupon the lama would ask him why he wanted to go and then tell him when to return. On the other hand, a monk who does not know the Vinaya had to serve such a lama for a period of ten years.   When we monks had to go to Dharamsala, Varanasi, and so forth, our teachers would advise us to always wear our robes. This is because the robes keep one aware of being a monk and also stop other people from engaging one in meaningless talk. In this way, wearing the robes can protect monks and nuns from breaking their vows, whereas when we do not wear them it is becomes easy to lapse into inappropriate behavior. Of course, we have to look at each particular situation. Sometimes when I travel around Italy, people think that I am a Hare Krishna and come up to me saying “Hare Ram, Hare Krishna”! However, one day I was standing by the road in Padova with my zen draped over my head and my mala in my hand and some ladies came up to me and asked me if I was a Muslim! 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
94  STUDENT: Is the yellow dongka only worn by high lamas or certain monks or can it be worn by anyone? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The yellow dongka is not just for lamas as all the monks of the Lower Tantric College (Gyu Me) and Sera Je Monastery can wear them, although in general it is the geshes who actually wear them. The monks of the Upper Tantric College (Gyu To) all wear maroon dongkas. You can see that in the picture of Trijang Rinpoche he is wearing a maroon dongka although he was a very high lama.   Fully-ordained monks have 253 vows, while novice monks have 36. However, in both cases the most important vows are the four root vows and the vow to abandon drinking alcohol. Drinking alcohol is a small infraction in itself but it is in fact extremely dangerous. This is because it can lead to greater infractions. There is a story about a monk who was given a choice by a king to either have sexual intercourse with a woman, to kill a goat, or to drink a pitcher of alcohol. The monk decided that since he would commit a root downfall by having sexual intercourse, it was best to not to do that. Since by killing the goat he would create an infraction he also decided against that, and so chose to drink the alcohol whereby he would commit only a small infraction. Therefore, he drank the alcohol but as a result had sexual intercourse with the woman and killed the goat! For this reason, alcohol is strictly forbidden in the monasteries.   There is a difference between the vow to refrain from sexual conduct of a monk and the sexual misconduct that is included in the ten non-virtuous actions. For a monk sexual conduct of any type is forbidden, whereas for a lay person only certain types of sexual conduct are forbidden. In order for a monk to commit the action of stealing it is not necessary for him to actually physically take something from someone. For example, since the cushions that we are sitting on belong to the monastery, when monks and nuns sit on them they should always cover them with a dingwa, a particular type of cushion cover, because by sitting on them without a dingwa, the cushions over time will become damaged and have to be replaced at the expense of the monastery, whereby this becomes a type of stealing. Since stealing means to take what has not been given, by damaging the cushions a monk or nuns is taking money from the monastery and thereby stealing. All the vows have subtle points and therefore monks and nuns need to be conscientious in order to avoid breaking their vows.   When monks and nuns carry a chogo, the yellow shawl, it should be folded vertically and then folded in half and placed over the shoulder. Only abbots, umdzes, disciplinarians, and high lamas are permitted to wear it without folding it in half. Alternatively it can be carried in a bag. When monks and nuns do sojong, the bimonthly ceremony for purifying their vows, the chogo should not be thrown over the left shoulder but draped over the arm. I am saying all this here today because maybe here there are more people in the Basic Program who will become monks and nuns in the future!  When monks and nuns take too much food and then, unable to eat it all, throw it away, this is not right. Although it is not exactly stealing, it is similar. If they are traveling by train, they should always buy a ticket. When they speed in a car and are not caught, strictly speaking they should still pay a fine! This is something to think about. If the fine is excessive, perhaps one does not need to pay it but one should think about this and be careful of one’s vows.  Lying involves saying that one has spiritual attainments that one does not have, such as having realized emptiness although one has not, having visions of deities although one does not have them, having attained concentrations although one has not attained them, and so forth. Monks and nuns must avoid telling such lies. It is not necessary to lie by means of speech, one can also do so by means of body language.   There are many subtle points to these five main vows. The basis of killing is a human being, however it is not necessary to have a coarse intention to kill in order to break this vow. One can do so by setting a house or forest on fire while not caring that there might be someone there. If one does not care that there is someone inside, there exists a subtle motivation to kill and this is sufficient to break the root vow of abandoning killing. In short, we should be careful to keep the four root vows and to abandon alcohol.   You new monks and nuns should not think too much about what to do in the future, but just think “Now I am a monk” or “Now I am a nun” and “I will practice the Dharma.” If we compare ordained and lay people in Tibet, generally most monks and nuns would take ordination at an early age and then spend the next years memorizing and studying whereby, by the time they were 18 or 19 years of age, they would have understood many things and have gained independence. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
95 Lay people, on the other hand, were quite free until they were about the age of 20, after which they had to take more and more responsibilities as they married, had children, found a job, and so forth.   STUDENT: If someone gives us a chocolate or candy with alcohol in it, should we accept it or not? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: We can take it but not eat it and instead give it to someone else.   Strictly speaking monks and nuns should not eat in the evening but again this depends on one’s health. If lay people want to offer food to the sangha, they should not offer them an evening meal because there is no merit in this and only a fault. Therefore, they should offer either breakfast or lunch to the sangha, but not dinner.   When someone becomes a monk or nun and thinks about its great meaning, ordination becomes a very happy experience. Otherwise, it may come to feel like being put in prison! When we associate with lay people, it may seem as if we are different from them and this may make us feel unhappy. However, we should notice that when we talk with lay people most of what we say is not useful, and instead increases our pride, attachment, anger, and so forth. If we find that this happens, it is better not to spend time talking to them.   On the other hand, sometimes when lay people stay in a monastery for some time they can come to think that it is monks who are rather strange. When I myself recently stayed in my monastery for several months, I found that the monks tended to say very stupid things when talking among themselves, but that when they were with lay people they were very kind. When I asked them why they did this, they told me that it is because monks needs to practice patience, whereas lay people do not have patience and so we need to be kind to them. I thought this was rather strange! It is not always easy to understand monks!   STUDENT: When we monks are offered food after lunch, is it good to refuse it and to say to the person that it would be better to offer us lunch? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: This is difficult to say as the other person may think that it is rather strange that we refuse the offer of a meal. Therefore, if someone invites us to their house for dinner, perhaps it is better to go and to pray for and develop compassion for the person and eat the food that is offered.  STUDENT: It is almost impossible to avoid touching money and precious substances in our daily life as we need to do so even just to buy a cup of coffee. What would Geshe-la advise us to do? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A monk should not handle fire, money, not sing, and so forth but if these actions cannot be avoided, we can ask the Buddha for permission to engage in them. When we wake up in the morning, we should wash, do three prostrations, recite the prayer “Buddha, out of your great compassion, please listen to me,” and ask for permission to touch fire, money, and so forth. Then in the evening, if we have done any of these actions, we should bring them to mind and ask that they be absolved. Everyone, whether lay or ordained, who has taken a tantric empowerment in any of the four classes should say “May whatever I eat, whatever I wear, and so forth be used in the service of others.”   STUDENT: I have seen that when monks and nuns sneeze, they put their zen over their head and bend low. Is there a reason for this? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: This is done out of respect. It is considered to be rude to loudly blow one’s nose while sitting upright.  Since it is difficult to keep all the vows purely, the four main vows and the vow of abandoning alcohol are emphasized in particular.   Friday morning, 30 September 2005 (5) Another twofold division of awarenesses and knowers  Furthermore, with respect to awarenesses and knowers there are two:  (1) minds and  (2) mental factors. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
96 (A) Minds  The definition of a main mind is: a main knower that is posited by way of apprehending the entity of its object.  Main mind (gtso sems), mind (sems, chitta), mentality (yid, manas), and consciousness (rnam shes, vijnana) are mutually inclusive and synonymous.4  When [minds are] divided by way of entity there are six:  (1-6) from eye consciousness up to mental consciousness.   If condensed, they are included in two:  (1) sense consciousnesses and  (2) mental consciousnesses.  A main mind is a knower that apprehends the entity of its object. For example, an eye consciousness apprehending cup just apprehends the cup itself. The feelings, mindfulness, attachment, and so forth that accompany that main mind are mental factors. They apprehend the attributes of an object. For example, what is it that makes a main mind into doubt? This is the function of the doubt that is in the retinue of that main mind.   When a main mind apprehends an object it is concomitant with feelings and so forth. Although it appears to us that the main mind itself is feeling, in reality it is not like that. The feeling is in the retinue of the main mind, that is, it surrounds it. Thus, there exists feeling but the main mind is not feeling.   There are four possibilities between the two, mentality (yid, manas) and mental consciousness (yid shes, manovijnana). This is because  • an eye consciousness is a possibility that is mentality but not a mental consciousness;  • the feeling that occurs in the retinue of a mental consciousness is a possibility that is a mental consciousness but is not mentality;  • a mental consciousness is a possibility that is both mentality and a mental consciousness; and  • the feeling that occurs in the retinue of an eye consciousness is a possibility that is neither mentality nor a mental consciousness.   The relationship between two things can be that of being mutually inclusive, being mutually exclusive, or of there existing various possibilities between them. The latter is discussed in detail in the fourth chapter of the Ornament for Clear Realizations, which we will study next year.   Between mentality (which is synonymous with main mind, mind, and consciousness) and mental consciousness, there are four possibilities: 1. that which is mentality but not mental consciousness, 2. that which is mental consciousness but not mentality, 3. that which is both mentality and mental consciousness, and 4. that which is neither mentality or mental consciousness.  An illustration of the first is an eye consciousness, the second is the feeling that occurs in the retinue of a mental consciousness, the third is mental consciousness, and the fourth is the feeling that occurs in the retinue of an eye consciousness. Why is an eye consciousness a mentality or a main mind but not a mental consciousness?  STUDENT: Because it is a sense consciousness. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right. Why is the feeling that occurs in the retinue of a mental consciousness a mental consciousness but not a mentality or main mind?                                                          4 Editor’s note: Throughout this section on Mind the words “sentience” (yid) and “perceiver” (rnam shes) that occur in E. Napper’s translation h a v e  b e e n  r e s p e c t i v e l y  c h a n g e d t o  “ m e n t a l i t y ”  a n d  “consciousness.”
