STUDENT: When main minds are divided there are six and one of them is mental consciousness. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Mentality is synonymous with main mind.  STUDENT: What is the feeling that occurs in the retinue of a sense consciousness? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Because it is in the retinue of a main sense consciousness, it is a sense consciousness, however it is not a main sense consciousness.  STUDENT: Is the feeling in the retinue of an eye consciousness not an eye consciousness? Does it not see? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is not a main eye consciousness, nor does it see its object, yet it is a sense consciousness. Feeling has its own object. However, this can be debated.  The feeling that occurs the retinue of an eye consciousness is neither mentality nor mental consciousness. It is not a mentality or main mind because it is a mental factor. It is not a mental consciousness because it is a sense consciousness.  STUDENT: W h a t  i s  t h e  d i f f e r e n c e  b e t w e e n  a  f e e l i n g  i n  t h e  r e t i n u e  o f  a n  e y e  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  apprehending, for example, a beautiful flower and a feeling in the retinue of a mental consciousness? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: W e  h a v e  t o  d i s t i n g u i s h  b e t w e e n  m e n t a l  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  a n d  m a i n  mental consciousness. Feelings that do not depend on a sense consciousness are feelings in the retinue of a main mental consciousness.  STUDENT: But the happy feeling that arises when I see a beautiful flower is the same as the happy feeling that arises when I just feel happy. ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: That is right, the feeling is the same GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: For example, when the sun strikes us in the eyes, we experience suffering feeling, but at other times when the sun shines we experience happy feeling. From these feelings in the retinue of sense consciousness, come feelings in the retinue of mental consciousness.   One should know similarly the way of positing four possibilities between the two, mentality and sense consciousness (dbang shes, indriyajnana) and four possibilities between the two, exalted wisdom (ye shes, jnana) and mental consciousness (yid shes, manojnana).  There are also four possibilities between mentality and sense consciousness, and four possibilities between exalted wisdom and mental consciousness. With respect to the possibilities between mentality and sense consciousness, what is an illustration of something that is mentality but not sense consciousness?  STUDENT: A mental consciousness, GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A mental consciousness is not mentality.  STUDENT: The mind of enlightenment  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right. A main mental consciousness is a mental consciousness but a mental consciousness is not necessary a main mental consciousness. What is an illustration of something that is sense consciousness but not mentality? STUDENT: A feeling in the retinue of an eye consciousness. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right. It is not a mentality or main mind because it is a mental factor. It is a sense consciousness because it is in the retinue of a sense consciousness. Why is a feeling in the retinue of a sense consciousness pervaded by being a sense consciousness?  STUDENT: They share a common basis, observed object, aspect, time, and substance.  ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: You have been doing your homework! GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: You could just say that it is a sense consciousness because it is an eye sense consciousness. STUDENT: It seems strange to say that a feeling is an eye consciousness. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I t  i s  a n  e y e  s e n s e  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  b u t  i t  i s  n o t  a  m a i n  e y e  s e n s e  consciousness.

STUDENT: Because it is omniscient mind or perhaps because it is a yogic direct perceiver? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is exalted wisdom because it is a path. What is an illustration of something that is mental consciousness but not exalted wisdom? STUDENT: A mental consciousness in an ordinary being. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e r e  i s  n o  p e r v a s i o n  b e cause it cannot be said that the mental consciousness of an ordinary being is definitely not an exalted wisdom. This is because exalted wisdom exists in the continua of ordinary beings on the path of accumulation and path of preparation. STUDENT: What kind of exalted wisdom exists before the path of seeing? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A path in the continuum of someone on the path of accumulation or the path of preparation is exalted wisdom. For example, the conventional mind of enlightenment in such a person’s continuum is both exalted wisdom and mental consciousness. STUDENT: D i d  G e s h e-la agree that a mental direct perceiver in the continuum of an ordinary being is a main mental consciousness but not exalted wisdom.

STUDENT: In fact I have a doubt about that statement. For example, the mental direct perceiver in the continuum of an ordinary being who has entered a path that follows a sense consciousness is a mental direct perceiver that is conjoined with renunciation so it is an exalted wisdom. The definition of a path is “an exalted knower conjoined with uncontrived renunciation.” GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A mind that is conjoined with uncontrived renunciation is a path, but there is no pervasion that the particular mind of your example is conjoined with renunciation. STUDENT: According to Jetsun Chogyi Gyeltsen it is conjoined with renunciation, but according to Panchen Sonam Dragpa it is not. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Where is that written? STUDENT: J e t s u n  C h o g y i  G y e l t s e n  s a y s  t h a t  s u c h  a  m e n t a l  d i r e c t  p e r c e i v e r  i s  c o n j o i n e d  w i t h  renunciation in a non-manifest manner. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Y o u  s h o u l d  n o t  c o n f u s e  t h e  m e a n i n g  o f  “ m a n i f e s t ”  a n d  “ n o n-manifest” with “conjoined” and “not conjoined,” as they are not the same. For example, is the conception of a self, which is ignorance, in the continuum of an ordinary being who has entered a path conjoined with renunciation? STUDENT: It could be. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: NO, it is not! That the mind realizing selflessness is conjoined with uncontrived renunciation means that these minds mutually support each other. The mind realizing selflessness supports the mind of renunciation, and the mind of renunciation supports the mind realizing selflessness. However, because the conception of a self and the mind of renunciation do not mutually support each other, the conception of a self is not conjoined with the mind of renunciation. There is a lot of discussion about the meaning of manifest and non-manifest, but a mind that is conjoined with something is a mind that is supported by it.  STUDENT: Is the first instant of renunciation whereby one enters a path, a direct valid cognizer? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is a conceptual consciousness, not a direct valid cognizer. A mental direct perceiver that is induced by a sense consciousness in the continuum of an ordinary being is an awareness to which an object appears but is not ascertained, therefore it is difficult to say that it is conjoined w i t h  r e n u n c i a t i o n .  I f  i t  i s  a n  a w a r e n e s s  t o  w h i c h  a n  o b j e c t  a p p e a r s  b u t  i s  n o t  ascertained it is not much support to the mind of renunciation and vice versa. That mental direct perceiver lasts for such a short time that it is not much use. On the other hand, a conceptual consciousness that is induced by a sense direct perceiver can support renunciation. However, is a conceptual consciousness apprehending pot in the continuum of a person who has entered a path an exalted wisdom? When the mind is conjoined with renunciation, it becomes very beneficial in that, for example, everything that is seen is perceived to be in the nature of suffering, and in this sense t h e  e y e  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  c a n  b e  s a i d  t o  s u p p o r t  t h e  m i n d  o f  r e n u n c i a t i o n .  H o w e v e r ,  i t  i s  difficult to say that the eye consciousness is exalted wisdom. STUDENT: P e r h a p s  w e  c a n  p o s i t  c l a i r v o y a n c e  c o n j o i n e d  w i t h  r e n u n c i a t i o n ?  I s  t h e r e  n o t  a  clairvoyance conjoined with renunciation that is a mental direct perceiver? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Why? STUDENT: B e c a u s e  t h e  c l a i r v o y a n c e  k n o wi n g  t h e  a f f l i c t i o n s  i n  a n o t h e r  p e r s o n ’ s  mi n d  t o  b e  t h e  cause of suffering can be conjoined with renunciation. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: There is no problem in saying that a clairvoyance is a path, and that the eye consciousness of a sentient being ( i n c l u d i n g  t h a t  o f  a  t e n t h-ground bodhisattva) is not exalted wisdom. However, since an eye consciousness can be conjoined with renunciation perhaps it can also be a path. In general, the eye consciousness of a sentient being, even an arya being, is not a path and is not exalted wisdom, but in dependence on this reasoning it could be posited as a path because it supports the mind of renunciation and so forth. There is no reason why that eye consciousness is not a path. There is no pervasion that an eye consciousness is not an exalted wisdom for the reason that it is a sense consciousness. Exalted wisdom, exalted knower, and path are synonymous. STUDENT: Is the assertion similar in tantra that the eye consciousnesses of sentient beings are not a path? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h a t  i s  n o t  c l e a r  b u t  w e  c o u l d  s a y  t h a t  i t  i s  n o t  a  p a t h .  A  s e n s e  consciousness in the continuum of a sentient being is not a path because it is not exalted wisdom? STUDENT: What makes a sense consciousness into an exalted wisdom in the case of a buddha? Is it because a buddha no longer has a conception of true existence?

100 GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I t  i s  b e c a u s e  i t  i s  a  f i n a l  c o n s c i o u s n e s s t h a t  d i r e c t l y  p e r c e i v e s  a l l  phenomena. The sense consciousness in the continuum of a sentient being is not a path because it is a sense consciousness of someone who has not attained enlightenment! STUDENT: W h a t  m a k e s  s o m e t h i n g  a n  e x a l t e d  w i s d o m  a n d  w h a t  precludes it being an exalted wisdom? It is not that there is no longer a conception of true existence nor is that all phenomena are necessarily directly perceived because aryas still have a conception of true existence and do not perceive all phenomena directly, yet they have exalted wisdom. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e  c o n s c i o u s n e s s e s  o f  o r d i n a r y  b e i n g s  a n d  t h o s e  o f  b u d d h a s  a r e  vastly different. The exalted wisdom of the wisdom truth body is the mind of a buddha, and this is what arises as the form body of a buddha complete with all the sense powers. Our body, on the other hand, is the result of true origins and is a true suffering. The form body of a buddha is also not a mental body. That very mind that perceives all phenomena directly is what arises as the form body of a buddha. Thus, there is no distinction between the exalted wisdom and the form body of a buddha.  STUDENT: T h e  s e n s e  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  o f  a  b u d d h a  i s  i n c l u d e d  i n  w h i c h  o f  t h e  f o u r  – m e n t a l i t y ,  sense consciousness, exalted wisdom, or mental consciousness? There are a lot of points that remain unresolved regarding the sense consciousnesses of those who have entered and those who have not entered a path. However, is the thought to go to the toilet in the mind of an arya being a path? STUDENT: It is a path to the toilet! GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Ha-ha!!! What makes a mind a path? We need to think about this. So to return to the subject, what is an illustration of something that is neither exalted wisdom nor mental consciousness? STUDENT: A sense consciousness of an animal. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right. STUDENT: But an animal could be a bodhisattva., therefore, one would have to say “an animal who has not entered a path”! GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Then the eye consciousness in the continuum of a bodhisattva who has entered a path is not an exalted wisdom?  STUDENT: There could be that doubt. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is usually said that sense consciousnesses in the minds of ordinary beings are NOT paths.  (B) Mental factors The definition of a mental factor is: a knower that apprehends any of the features of its object and occurs in the retinue of whatever main mind is concomitant with it.  A main mind apprehends the entity of the object and not its features, whereas mental factors, such as wisdom, mindfulness, and so forth, apprehend its features or attributes. These mental factors arise in the retinue of the main mind with which they are concomitant. The main mind is like the king who is the principal person, and the mental factors are like the ministers who are in the king’s retinue. The mental factor of stabilization, for example, has the function of enabling the main mind to focus on its object, mindfulness has the function of enabling the main mind to not forget its object, and so forth. These functions are not functions of the main mind, but functions of the mental factors in its retinue. Therefore, what enables a main mind to function is the mental factors in its retinue. If there were no stabilization, mindfulness, wisdom, and so forth, a main mind would not be able to function.   A mind and the mental factors that occur in its retinue possess the five aspects of similarity because of having a similar: (1) basis,  (2) observed object,  (3) aspect,  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 26-30 September 2005 (03)  
101 (4) time, and  (5) substance.   This is so: • because the mental factor that occurs in its retinue depends on the sense power, as its basis, on which the main mind depends;  • because the mental factor that occurs in its retinue is produced from that observed object in dependence on which the main mind is produced;  • because the aspect of any object appears to the mental factor that occurs in its retinue just as it appears to the main mind;  • because the mental factor that occurs in its retinue is also produced at the same time as the main mind is produced;  • because a main mind and the mental factor that occurs in its retinue are produced qualified by [being] one type of substance and are not produced as different types of substances.   Vasubandhu’s Treasury of Knowledge (Abhidharmakosha) s a y s :  “ . . . s y n o n y m o u s .  M i n d  a n d  mental factors have five aspects of concomitance.”  A main mind and the mental factors in its retinue are concomitant by way of five similarities because they have the same: (1) basis,  (2) observed object,  (3) aspect,  (4) time, and  (5) substance.  These five are explained by Vasubandhu in his Treasury of Manifest Dharma (Abhidharmakosha) and by Asanga in his Compendium of Manifest Dharma (Abhidharmasamucchaya), however, there are differences between them. They are also explained in Yeshe Gyeltsen’s Necklace.   STUDENT: Yesterday in a dream I had a long conversation with a Zen master about the benefits of flu vaccinations for his disciples. This dreams seems to me to have been very conceptual. So I am still wondering why it is asserted that conceptual consciousnesses are withdrawn while dreaming? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: How do you know that concepts arose in your mind? STUDENT: It is hard to imagine that they were not concepts. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Just because there were concepts in your dream there is no pervasion that the dream consciousness is a conceptual consciousness. STUDENT: So I can think without a conceptual consciousness? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: D r e a m s  a r e  n o t  c o n c e p t s .  Wh e n  y o u  w a k e  u p  a n d  t h i n k  a b o u t  t h e  dream, then there are concepts. STUDENT: So just as the subtle mental consciousness of a dream can take on the aspect of sense consciousnesses but is not sense consciousness, so too can it take on the aspect of conceptual consciousnesses but not be conceptual consciousness? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right. Just as there are no sense consciousness while dreaming but for the person who is dreaming there are sense consciousnesses, so too there are no conceptual consciousnesses while dreaming but for the person who is dreaming there are conceptual consciousnesses. Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
102 Monday afternoon, 3 October 2005  How does this text relate to actual practice? Presently we are under the control of our minds and our minds are under the control of afflictions. Although the afflictions are not other than mind, as long as we remain under their control there can be no happiness, only suffering. Why is this? It is because suffering arises from actions (karma) and karma is created by the mind. Therefore, it follows that the mind is the source of all suffering as well as the source of all happiness. The mind is also the place of practice.   There are different kinds of afflictions are concerned, those that are coarser and those that are subtler. The coarser afflictions are those that give rise to actions of body and speech, such as killing, stealing, sexual misconduct, lying, divisive speech, harsh words, and idle talk. While those of you who are monks and nuns have eliminated coarse negative actions of body and speech, that alone is not enough. As I said last week, if having become ordained you live your life in accord with your vows, you will be happy, otherwise not.   There is an order to eliminating the afflictions, in that one begins with the coarse and then gradually progresses to the subtle. This is done by practicing the three higher trainings in morality, concentration, and wisdom. The higher training in morality is the base in that it by training in morality one eliminates coarse negative actions of body and speech. Then one progresses to the higher training in concentration, which involves the development of calm abiding, that suppresses the coarser afflictions. Then one trains in wisdom by developing special insight unified with calm abiding, whereby one abandons even the latencies of the afflictions. Thus, without the basis, morality, one will not be able to develop concentration and wisdom.   Although it is not necessary to become a monk or nun in order to possess the basis that is morality, there is a difference between lay people and those who are ordained. This is because due to having vows to not engage in certain actions, the ordained tend to be more conscientious of their behavior. Of course, if one has vows but does not guard them, one only creates more difficulties for oneself. On the other hand, without vows, one lacks conscientiousness.   When monks live in a monastery and nuns in a nunnery, there is less risk of breaking one’s vows, but here we live together with lay people. For this reason, it is important for those with vows to take special care. When I say this it is based on my own personal experience. I took ordination at the age of nine but my father insisted on sending me to an ordinary Indian school in Mysore. Although there were a few other Tibetans in the school, I was the only monk. There were kids in the school who were older than me, some of them fourteen and fifteen years of age, who would talk about girls and so forth, which made me feel rather strange. I asked my father to let me go to a monastery but he only allowed me to do so when I was seventeen.   Our negative actions of body and speech tend to be very strong because we are very familiar with them. If we allow our mind to come under the control of the afflictions, we engage in negative actions that take control of us. They can control us due to our familiarity with them, they rob us of our independence. For this reason, a person who is able to take control of his actions is a real hero.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Regarding the modes of these five similarities, there are two explanations – o n e  from the Treasury of Knowledge and one from [Asanga's] Compendium of Knowledge (Abhidharmasamucchaya).   From among these two, the explanation from the Treasury of Knowledge (stanza 2.35a) is as follows:  (1) similar support (2) similar object of observation (3) similar [subjective] aspect (4) similar time (5) similar substance  Their individual meanings are as follows:  (1) Since a mental factor is also supported by that sense power upon which the mind is supported, their supports are similar. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
103 (2) Since a mental factor also observes that object which the mind observes, their objects of observation are similar. (3) Since, when a mind is generated having an aspect, for instance, blue, its mental factor is also generated in that aspect, blue, their [subjective] aspects are similar. (4) Since a mind and its mental factor are simultaneous in regard to the three – p r o d u c t i o n ,  abiding, and cessation – their times are similar. (5) Just as minds of a similar type are distinct substances, likewise, mental factors, such as feelings, of a similar type are also distinct substances, therefore their substances are similar.  The Treasury of Knowledge and Compendium of Knowledge set out somewhat different explanations of the five similarities. The Treasury of Knowledge says that mind and the mental factors in its retinue have the same support, that is, the same sense power. They also have the same observed object as well as the same aspect. In addition, they are similar in being produced, abiding, and disintegrating at the same time. They also are similar in substance, meaning that when there is one particular main mind there is only one feeling in its retinue and not different feeling. In other words, the individual mental factors in the retinue of one mind, for example, the wisdom included in the five object-ascertaining mental factors, is only one, there being no other wisdoms in its retinue.   In the case of an eye consciousness apprehending pot, the mental factors such as feeling in its retinue share the five similarities with it. Thus, they have the same support, the eye sense power; the same observed object, pot; and the same aspect, pot. However, while the aspect of the eye consciousness itself is pot, does the feeling in its retinue not have the aspect of experience? What does it mean that the eye consciousness and the feeling in its retinue have the same aspect? According to this statement, the feeling in the retinue of the eye consciousness also has the aspect of pot because the eye consciousness and the mental factors in its retinue share the same aspect. It would follow that it is suitable to say that feeling is e y e  c o n s c i o u s n e s s  b ecause it arises in the aspect of pot. If one says that feeling is eye consciousness, how can it be in the retinue of eye consciousness? There is some debate about this. Would you say that it is comfortable to assert that the feeling in the retinue of eye consciousness is eye consciousness? STUDENTS: No. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: W h e n  t h e  s u n  s t r i k e s  y o u  d i r e c t l y  i n  y o u r  e y e s  a n d  t h e r e  a r i s e s  a  sharp feeling of pain, is that feeling, eye consciousness? STUDENT: It is body consciousness. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: We can say that it is bodily or physical feeling since it is related the body, but it cannot be said that it is body consciousness. So do you agree that it is not very comfortable to say that feeling is eye consciousness? STUDENT: F e e l i n g  i s  t o  f e e l  s o m e t h i n g. If it feels the color and shape of pot, the feeling is eye consciousness.  STUDENT: What does the word “substance” mean in this context? It usually refers to something physical and tangible.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: According to Sautrantika, a substance is a real phenomena, that is, a functioning thing or a specifically characterized phenomenon. For this school anything that is ultimately able to perform a function is a substance, therefore it does not have to be tangible or touchable. Minds and mental factors are real phenomena. They say, for example, that the mental factor feeling and the mental factor mindfulness are different substances. STUDENT: T o  s a y  t h a t  t h e y  a r e  c o n c r e t e  o r  r e a l  j u s t  b e c a u s e  t h e y  a r e  f u n c t i o n i n g  t h i n g s  s e e m s  rather strange. If “substance” means functioning, then this similarity is included in the previous four. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: When one looks at something, mental factors such as feeling, contact, mindfulness, and so forth arise in the retinue of that eye consciousness. They are a part of the eye consciousness and cannot be separated from it. It is not that eye consciousness exists alone without any mental factors in its retinue. In this sense the mental factors are as if they were a part of the consciousness and therefore one substance with it.  STUDENT: If there were no mental factors, the eye consciousness would be like a video camera, without life.  STUDENT: So in conclusion, is feeling eye consciousness or not? 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
104 GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I do not know. I cannot decide. Ha-ha! My teacher used to ask me what conclusion I had come to one day and then again the next day he would ask me again, but rarely would he tell me that something was a certain way. instead, more often he acted to increase my doubts! This is because if something were settled beyond doubt, one could set it aside without thinking any further about it. Honestly speaking, there is no way to eliminate all doubts. When one debates, one sees different sides of the subject. If we were just told how it is, there would be no room for discussion. However, here we do not have time to debate these things at length so we can say that the feeling in the retinue of eye consciousness is eye consciousness, but not a main eye consciousness. But this statement can be argued with, just as there would be argument if someone were to say that it is not eye consciousness. We need to resolve our own doubts by means of our own thought processes. There is not much benefit in someone telling us that something is definitely like this or like that.  The explanation in the Compendium of Knowledge regarding the five similarities is as follows:  (1) similar substance (2) similar object of observation and [subjective] aspect (3) similar entity (4) similar time (5) similar realm and level  (1) Similar substance refers to the following. In the retinue of a single main mind, there can arise only one mental factor, for instance, feeling, of a similar type; two [mental factors] of a similar type but of different substances cannot occur.  (2-3) Similar object of observation and [subjective] aspect a n d  s i m i l a r  entity r e f e r  t o  t h e  following.  [A mind and the mental factors in its retinue] are similar in observing one object – the object of observation – and they are similar in being afflicted or non-afflicted in terms of the object of observation and the [subjective] aspect.  If a main mind is afflicted, then the mental factors in its retinue are also afflicted, whereas if a main mind is uncontaminated, the mental factors in its retinue are also uncontaminated. (4) Similar time refers to the following. A main mind and the mental factors in its retinue are simultaneous in regard to the three – production, abiding, and cessation. (5) Similar realm and level refers to the following. If a main mind is a mind of the desire realm, then it is impossible for a mental factor of the form or formless realms to arise in its retinue, and it is impossible for a mental factor of the desire realm to arise in the retinue of a mind of the form realm. That realm in which the main mind is included is also necessarily the realm in which a mental factor in its retinue is included.  According to the Compendium of Knowledge, there is no similarity of support, the similarities of observed object and aspect are condensed in one similarity; there is a similarity of entity; and there is a similarity of realm and level. The explanation of substance is similar to that given in the Treasury of Knowledge. Also, in this case the mind and mental factors in its retinue have the same observed object and aspect, meaning that when the main mind has a certain observed object and aspect, also the mental factors, such as attachment, in its retinue have the same observed object and the same aspect. Because of this, when there is, for example, the apprehension of a self (self grasping) that is a main mind and not the ignorance of the six root afflictions, all the mental factors in its retinue, mindfulness and so forth, also become afflicted. Likewise, when the main mind is unafflicted, also all the mental factors in its retinue are unafflicted.   STUDENT: I f  t h a t  a p p r e h e n s i o n  o f  a  s e l f  i s  n o t  t h e  i g n o r a n c e  t h a t  i s  t h e  v i e w  o f  t h e  t r a n s i t o r y  collection, why do we not just say “a mind perceiving the mere I”? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Tha t  a ppr e he ns i on of  a  s e l f  t ha t  i s  a main mind does not have the definition of the ignorance included in the six root afflictions, there being many different types of ignorance. This apprehension of a self is not the ignorance apprehending a self because the latter is a mental factor. The mental consciousness that apprehends a self has the aspect of a self-supporting self but it is not ignorance because it is not a mental factor. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
105 STUDENT: If we abandon the mental factor that is ignorance by means of the Hinayana realization of emptiness, the main mind should remain untouched. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I f  t h e  i g n o r a n c e  a p p r e h e n d i n g  a  s e l f  i s  a b a n d o n e d ,  a l s o  t h e  apprehension of a self that is a main mind is abandoned. By definition the ignorance apprehending a self is a mental factor and therefore it must share five similarities with a main mind. Therefore, by affecting the mental factor, one affects the mind. STUDENT: A c c o r d i n g  t o  S a u t r a n t i k a  t h e  m e n t a l  f a c t o r  i g n o r a n c e  h a s  t h e  a s p e c t  o f  a  s e l f-supporting substantially existent self, but what is the aspect of the main mind apprehending a self? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is a self-supporting substantially existent self. The main mind and the mental factor ignorance in its retinue have the same observed object and aspect. If there is a mental factor it must be concomitant with a main mind. The definition of mental factor is: a knower that apprehends any of the features of its object and occurs in the retinue of whatever main mind is concomitant with it. The word for “mental factor” in Tibetan sems byung literally means “mind-arise,” meaning that around a main mind there arise various other minds, these being what are usually called “mental factors.”   That they are of the same realm means that if the main mind is included in the desire realm, all the mental factors in its retinue are also included in the desire realm. The same is true for the minds and mental factors of the form and formless realms. However, while the mind of calm abiding is included in the form realm, it being a preparation of the first concentration or first dhyana, the person is of the desire realm. Thus, although at this time the person is still of the desire realm, his mind is of the form realm. When one attains calm abiding, the mind is of the level of the preparation of the first concentration, then when the afflictions of the desire realm, mainly attachment, are suppressed, one attains an actual first concentration. In short, by suppressing the manifest afflictions of the desire realm, in particular attachment, one gradually progresses through the four concentrations. Then one successively progresses through the four formless absorptions from Infinite Space up to the Peak of Existence. Yet in all cases the person is still of the desire realm.   Tuesday morning, 4 October 2005  The mental factors belong to same realm or level as the main mind with which they are concomitant. There are three realms: the desire realm, the form realm, and the formless realm. The desire realm is very coarse in terms of its afflictions and the sufferings caused by them. It is not that there is no happiness in the desire realm, but the happiness here is actually the suffering of change, which does not remain happiness for long. We can see this reality with our own eyes. With the recognition that in the desire realm everything is uncertain and in the nature of suffering, one develops repulsion toward it and the desire to become free from it. As a result, one looks down upon the desire realm and looks upward at the four concentrations. It is not that there is no suffering in the four concentrations and four formless absorptions, but rather that it is more subtle than the suffering of the desire realm. For this reason, one looks at the four concentrations as happiness and aims to achieve these minds and to leave behind the mind of the desire realm. Then, when one attains the first concentration, one achieves the subtler happiness of the first concentration. After that one looks at the happiness of the first concentration as something to be abandoned and at the even more subtle happiness of the second concentration as something to be obtained. Having attained the second concentration, one looks at as that to be abandoned and at the third concentration as that to be obtained and so forth. In this way, one gradually attains the fourth concentration, in which there is no happiness feeling but only equanimity feeling, which is more subtle or refined. Then, one progresses through the four formless absorptions, Infinite Space, Infinite Consciousness, Nothingness, and Without Discrimination Not Without Discrimination, in which there is only equanimity feeling. In short, first one looks at the desire realm as coarse, and the form realm as peace, and then one looks at the form realm as coarse, and the formless realm as peace.   The greatest obstacles in the desire realm to controlling the mind are the coarse afflictions of attachment and anger. On the other hand, the teachings on calm abiding say that the two main obstacles to obtaining calm abiding are excitement and laxity. In either case, in order to achieve a mind included in the first concentration, one has to free the mind of the coarse afflictions. By suppressing the coarse manifest afflictions of the desire realm, one attains an actual first concentration, even though these afflictions have not been completely abandoned. Then one looks at the first concentration as coarse and the second concentration as peace, and continues 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
106 meditating until one attains the second concentration. Then one looks at the second concentration as coarse and the third concentration as peace, and meditates until one attains the third concentration. At that time one looks at the happiness feeling of the third concentration as something to be abandoned and attains the fourth concentration in which there is only equanimity feeling. After that, in the four formless absorptions, there is only equanimity feeling. However, at all times the person who cultivates the concentrations and formless absorptions is of the desire realm. But if one wants to be reborn in the form realm, one must achieve an actual concentration. Likewise, if one wants to be reborn in the formless realm, one must achieve an actual formless absorption.   STUDENT: In order to take rebirth in the form realm in our next life, is it necessary to obtain an actual concentration in this life or is it possible to take rebirth there due to the seeds of having achieved an actual concentration in a previous life? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Generally speaking in order to be reborn in the form realm, we need to have achieved an actual concentration of the form realm either in this life or in a past life. However, there is one exception to this rule. When the world is destroyed at the end of the eon, all the beings of the desire realm are naturally reborn in the form realm. In other words, at that time the beings do not need to have attained an actual concentration in order to be reborn in the form realm. However, there is debate regarding this statement because it can be argued whether this means that when the w o r l d  i s  d e s t r o y e d ,  a l l  t h e  b i l l i o n  w o r l d  s y s t e m s  a r e  s i m u l t a n e o u s l y  destroyed. If they are not, the beings could just be reborn in another world. On the other hand, if they are all simultaneously destroyed, then the beings would have to be naturally reborn in the form realm.  A Buddhist does not develop calm abiding and then the concentrations and formless absorptions for the purpose of being reborn in the form realm and formless realm. This is because we have already been reborn in those realms countless times, and therefore the purpose of achieving these states of mind is to attain nirvana or enlightenment. Hence, the cultivation of these states of mind is accompanied either by a mind of renunciation or a mind of enlightenment.   In the context of the similarity of realm and level, the Necklace says: “That realm in which the main mind is included is also necessarily the realm in which the mental factor in its retinue is included.” The word “included” indicates that when the first concentration is attained, the mind is no longer included in the desire realm but is included in the form realm. The mind is no longer included in the desire realm because, when the form realm is attained, the coarse afflictions of the desire realm no longer increase in that mind. On the other hand, it is included in the form realm because the afflictions of the form realm can increase in it, but not those of the desire realm. A mind of the first concentration is one in which the coarse afflictions of the desire realm, as well as excitement and laxity, have been suppressed. Because of this, when this mind observes the desirable objects of the desire realm, the afflictions of the desire realm do not increase. On the other hand, when the mind observes the desirable objects of the form realm, the afflictions of the form realm do increase. For this reason the mind is said to be “included” in the form realm, and not in the desire realm. However, the person with a body of the desire realm is still included in the desire realm, no matter what concentration or formless absorption he attains. There are many such combinations, for example, a person of the desire realm can attain any of the four concentrations and all four formless absorptions but still be of the desire realm; a person reborn in t h e  f i r s t  concentration can cultivate a formless absorption, whereby the person is of the form realm but his mind is included in the formless realm, and so forth.   When someone who has attained calm abiding but not an actual first concentration dies, he is reborn once again in the desire realm because his mind is still included in the desire realm and he has not yet attained an actual concentration of the form realm. However, it is possible that his calm abiding does not degenerate even though he experiences the coarse desire realm.   STUDENT: I am a little bit confused because yesterday it was said that calm abiding is included in the form realm. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Just because one has attained calm abiding, which is a mind included in the form realm, it does not follow that one necessarily attains an actual first concentration in that life.

107 STUDENT: I t  w a s  s a i d  t h a t  o n  t h e  b a s i s  o f  a  h u m a n  b o d y ,  i t  i s  p o s s i b l e  t o  a t t a i n  a l l  t h e  m i n d s  included in the form realm and formless realm. But how would someone who has been reborn in the third concentration ever develop the wish to attain the fourth concentration since they do not hear teachings on the fact that this state is happier nor do they have the clairvoyance that sees that the fourth concentration is happier. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A  b e i n g  o f  t h e  t h i r d  c o n c e n t r a t i o n  i s  a b l e  t o  u n d e r s t a n d  t h a t  t h e  happiness of the third concentration is not a final happiness and that there exists a superior happiness. It is similar to the fact that we human beings can see that the happiness we have is not a final happiness and that there exists a higher happiness. The minds of the beings of the third concentration are very clear, unlike our own, whereby they can understand that there are different levels of happiness, those that are coarse and those that are more subtle.  STUDENT: The beings of the Peak of Existence, the fourth formless absorption, must have a very subtle clear mind, so why do they fall back down to lower states? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e  b e i n g s  i n  t h e  P e a k o f  E x i s t e n c e ,  w h i c h  i s  a l s o  c a l l e d  W i t h o u t  Discrimination Not Without Discrimination, only have two discriminations: they discriminate having been born there and they discriminate their imminent death. Between these two discriminations, they remain in meditative absorption for an extremely long time. Then, when power of the merit that brought about this rebirth is exhausted, one of the myriad karmic latencies that they created in the past ripens, bringing about rebirth in the hell realm, hungry ghost realm, and so forth. This happens because while these beings are in meditative absorption they do not create any new merit, and so when their karma for them to abide in the Peak of Existence finishes, another latency ripens and they take rebirth in another realm.   A bodhisattva who has entered the Mahayana path trains in order to achieve the meditative absorptions of the form realm and formless realm, that is, the concentrations and formless absorptions. However, he does not do this for the purpose of taking rebirth there. While bodhisattvas who have attained the path of seeing have attained all four concentrations and four formless absorptions and can move up and down through them, they do not use them to take rebirth there. However, this does not mean that they do not take rebirth in the form and formless realms, rather it means that they do not train in the meditative absorptions for this purpose. Thus, although they can progress successively from the first concentration up through the fourth formless absorption, can jump from the first concentration to the third concentration, and so forth, they do so for the sole purpose of training or “exercising” their mind.   It is said that the mind of the desire realm cannot become a path. Therefore, the mind that attains the path of accumulation is not a desire realm mind. However, it is also said that the desire realm mind of a bodhisattva on the eighth ground can become a path by the power of his meditative stabilization. There are nine realms, the desire realm, the four form realms, and the four formless realms. A bodhisattva on the path of meditation can use any of these minds to develop the path.   STUDENT: H o w  c a n  a  b o d h i s a t t v a  o n  t h e  e i g h t h  g r o u n d  h a v e  a  d e s i r e  r e a l m  m i n d  i n  t h a t  a n  eighth-ground bodhisattva has abandoned the attachment of the desire realm? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A bodhisattva is an exception to the general rule. When we ordinary beings attain calm abiding, our mind is not included in the desire realm and all the coarse afflictions of the desire realm are suppressed. However, although eighth-ground bodhisattvas have attained the actual concentrations and so forth, because they are motivated by compassion they are not controlled by these afflictions but use them to work for the welfare of others.  STUDENT: Are we really talking about an eighth-ground bodhisattva on the path of meditation? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h a t  i s  r i g h t .  W h i l e  a c c o r d i n g  t o  P r a s a n g i k a  a n  e i g h t h-ground bodhisattva has abandoned the afflictions, this is not the case according to Svatantrika and below.   STUDENT: H o w  d o e s  p r o g r e s s  t h r o u g h  t h e  f o u r  c o n c e n t r a t i o n s  a n d  f o u r  f o r m l e s s  a b s o r p t i o n s  correspond to the five paths? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: They do not correspond. For example, there are those who, having attained the first concentration, then attain the path of accumulation, but it is not necessary to first attain the first concentration since it is sufficient to have attained a preparation of the first concentration in order to attain the path of accumulation. However, to attain the path of preparation one must have attained at least an actual first concentration. Thus, there are those who 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
108 attain the path of accumulation having previously attained all eight meditative absorptions, and there are those who attain it having attained just a preparation of the first concentration. It is said that if a person enters a path having already attained the concentrations, it is easier to progress if he has previously trained in moving through them because he has more freedom or independence. On the other hand, if one has attained even the fourth concentration, but has not trained in moving through the concentrations it is more difficult to make progress. By the eighth ground, one has gained dexterity in progressing through the concentrations. However, even prior to entering the path, one can train in doing this. To attain dexterity in moving through the concentrations, it is necessary to train in doing so. In short, although one can enter the path having attained any of the four concentrations, if one has not gained dexterity in moving through the concentrations it is best to enter the path with a fourth concentration as this mind is more powerful. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Tantra has a special ability to develop a union of calm abiding and special insight through the practice of deity yoga. However, since that is very difficult, it is better to develop calm abiding first and then go on to practice tantra. When primary consciousnesses are divided, there are the six groups of primary consciousnesses. Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
109 RESPONSE: It is an individual knower that is supported by the body and observes tactile objects.   QUESTION: What is mental primary consciousness?  RESPONSE: It is an individual knower that is supported by the mind and observes phenomena.   An eye primary consciousness is an individual knower, meaning that it knows its individual or specific object, form. It is supported by the eye sense power. It observes form, that is, colors and shapes. Likewise, the mental factors in its retinue are also supported by the eye sense power, observe form, and have the same aspect as the eye consciousness. Therefore, while feeling is not an eye primary consciousness, it is an eye consciousness.   Tuesday afternoon, 4 October 2005  Some parts of this subject are difficult to understand and some are not. In order to understand, for example, inferential cognizers, one needs to understand reasoning and to do so one really needs to have studied Signs and Reasonings, otherwise the subject of inferential cognizers remains difficult. Likewise, in order to understand the relationship between the main mind and mental factors of sharing a similar realm or level, it is necessary to understand the form and formless realms, how they are attained, and so forth. Therefore, only by gradually adding supplementary material to the study of Awarenesses and Knowers will one reach a good understanding of the subject.  When one studies it is important not to be tense and worried. One should avoid having a tight mind that wants to understand everything immediately. Such a mind is harmful to the development of knowledge and may also eventually lead one to abandon one’s studies. It is not always easy to decide that “this” is this, for example, to decide that a certain person is valid. To say that something is LIKE THIS can be like trying to carve something into stone, and therefore it is not easy to assert this is this and that is that.   When we start something, we should carry it through to its conclusion. Having completed our studies, we will be able to rejoice. In the monasteries, there are monks who begin their studies with great energy but then give everything up and there are those who plod steadily ahead through the difficulties and eventually complete their studies. Thus, it all depends upon effort and perseverance. Thus, we need to know how to persevere by pacing ourselves and by knowing our limits. We should not push too much or go too fast, as this can bring problems of sickness and so forth. I myself at first was overly zealous in my studies but I soon ended up with pain in my back and shoulders, and so I understood that I was going to have to pace myself in order to complete the fourteen or fifteen years of study. There are people who can study with great energy and maintain that energy over time, but not everyone can do this. Therefore, we need to check ourselves, our health, and so forth, and determine what we can and cannot do. When we study hard, we naturally focus and narrow the mind, due to which we can easily get lung or even life-lung (sog lung). There are those who become very learned but have a lot of lung. However, the best is to become learned while remaining relaxed and happy. This is the voice of experience speaking! Then it briefly mentions that the texts of Asanga and Vasubandhu set out eight consciousnesses (the usual six plus "mind-basis-of-all" and "afflicted intellect"), whereas the texts by Nagarjuna and Aryadeva set out six. When these mental factors are divided, there are fifty-one:  (1-5) five omnipresent mental factors, feeling and so forth (6-10) five object-ascertaining mental factors, aspiration and so forth (11-21) eleven virtuous mental factors, faith and so forth (22-27) six root afflictions, attachment and so forth (28-47) twenty secondary afflictions, belligerence and so forth (48-51) four changeable mental factors, regret and so forth  These are the divisions of the fifty-one mental factors.  [A] The five omnipresent mental factors (kun 'gro)  The five omnipresent mental factors are:  (1) feeling  (2) discrimination (3) intention (4) contact (5) attention  [A1] Feeling (tshor ba) The entity of feeling is just as it has been taught in the Compendium of Knowledge:   QUESTION: What is the defining characteristic of feeling?  RESPONSE: It has the characteristic of experience; through the entity of experience, it experiences individually the ripening results o f  v i r t u o u s  a n d  n o n-virtuous actions.  The definition of feeling is experience. It experiences the ripening of the results of virtuous and non-virtuous karma.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Here, that which is experienced by feeling is taught to be a fruition; this is taught for the purpose of making known that all feelings that arise within us, whether pleasant or suffering, are solely the result of the ripening o f  a c t i o n s  (karma). Moreover, the Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path says:   The manner in which actions are definite is as follows. Regardless of whether we are ordinary beings or superiors, all happiness in the aspect of pleasant feelings – even as slight as that produced in dependence on a cool breeze when one is born in hell – arises from virtuous actions accumulated in the past. Happiness cannot arise from a non-virtuous action. Also, all suffering in the aspect of painful feelings – even as slight as that produced in the mental continuum of a foe-destroyer – arises from non-virtue accumulated in the past. Suffering cannot arise from a virtuous action.  The Precious Garland (stanza 1.21) says:   From non-virtue comes all sufferings  And likewise all bad migrations. From virtue comes all happy migrations And the happiness within all rebirths.   
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
111 Whatever happiness there is, it arises from virtue. Whatever suffering there is, it arises from non-virtue. For example, rebirth in the hot hells is the result of non-virtue, but even the slightest cool breeze there is the result of virtue. Thus, all happiness from the tiniest pleasant breeze upward is the result of virtue. Likewise, all suffering, even the slight suffering of foe-destroyers who although free from cyclic existence experience a headache and so forth, is the result of non-virtue created prior to becoming a foe-destroyer.   We need to understand actions and their results. However, we cannot definitely say that a particular happiness is the result of a certain virtue nor that a particular suffering is the result of a certain non-virtue. Although we often hear such statements as, “From virtue, happiness. From non-virtue, suffering,” when we look around us we see people who live very virtuous lives but are often sick, have many problems and little success, and so forth, and other people who, although they kill, lie, and so forth, never get ill, are successful in their activities, and so forth. Seeing this, we may come to doubt the fact that happiness comes from virtue and suffering comes from non-virtue. However, this is because those who lead a very virtuous life but experience pain, lack success, and so forth are not experiencing the result of virtue that they have created in this life, but are experiencing the result of non-virtue that they accumulated in previous lives. Similarly, those who kill, fight, steal, and so forth in this life but never get sick and so on are experiencing the result of virtue created in the past, and not the result of non-virtue created in this life. The working of karma is inconceivable. It is even more subtle and more difficult to understand than emptiness., due to which it is included in the category of very hidden phenomena.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   When feelings are divided, there are three: (1) pleasant feelings (2) suffering feelings (3) neutral feelings  Furthermore, it is just as it has been said in A Discussion of the Five Aggregates:  QUESTION: What is feeling?  RESPONSE: It is the three types of experience – p l e a s a n t ,  s u f f e r i n g ,  a n d  t h a t  which is neither suffering nor pleasant.  • Pleasant [feeling] is that which, when it ceases, you wish to meet with again.  • Suffering is that which, when it arises, you wish to be separated from.  • That which is neither pleasant nor suffering is that which, when it arises, neither of the two wishes occurs.  The difference between the three types of feeling is that when happiness arises and then ceases, one wants to have it again; when suffering arises, one wants to be separated from it; and when neutral feeling arises, one neither wants to meet it again nor to be separated from it.   Do we really have feelings of happiness? Even when we laugh and enjoy ourselves, there is always seems to be some unpleasantness just waiting to happen! It is said that being in cyclic existence is like sitting on the tip of a needle. The suffering of change is likened to having a tiny piece of hair in the palm of the hand that is not felt and not experienced as suffering, whereas for an arya being it is like having a stone thrown in their eye! This is because the aryas directly understand our happiness to be suffering, whereas for us it is like a drug. Not recognizing suffering, we see it as happiness and because of this want more of it. Arya beings, on the other hand, directly perceive suffering as suffering. While we recognize the coarse sufferings of headaches and so forth to be suffering, there are many other sufferings that we do not recognize as suffering. The Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path sets out many types of sufferings which we need to strive to recognize.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   When these three types of feeling are differentiated in terms of the supports of body and mind, there are six types – three bodily feelings and three mental feelings.   
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
112 First, the three bodily feelings are:  (1) bodily pleasant feelings  (2) bodily suffering feelings (3) bodily neutral feelings  Second, the three mental feelings are:  (1) mentally pleasant feelings (2) mentally suffering feelings  (3) mentally neutral feelings  What is the difference between bodily and mental feelings?  Feelings that arise in the retinue of the five sense consciousness are called "bodily feelings"; feelings that arise in the retinue of the mental consciousness are called "mental feelings."   QUALM: Well then, what is the reason for calling the feeling that arises in the retinue of an eye consciousness a "bodily feeling" [even though the eye and the body are not the same]? RESPONSE: Treatise of Four Hundred Stanzas (stanza 6.10ab) says:  Just as the body sense power [pervades] the body, Likewise, ignorance abides in all.  Just as it has been said above, the body sense power pervades one's entire [body], from the crown down to the soles of the feet. Thus, any feeling arising in the retinue of the other four sense consciousnesses, such as the eye consciousness, is also called a "bodily feeling."   There are bodily feelings and mental feelings. Why is the feeling in the retinue of a sense consciousness called “bodily feeling”? It is because the body sense power pervades the entire body from the crown of the head to the soles of the feet. All the sense consciousnesses arise in dependence on their respective sense power, which is a subtle form. Therefore, just as an eye consciousness arises in dependence on the eye sense power, so too does the feeling in the retinue of the eye consciousness arise in dependence on the eye sense power. For this reason, the feeling in the retinue of the eye consciousness is called “bodily feeling.” Likewise, because the mental consciousness arises in dependence on the mental power, which is mind, the feeling in its retinue is called “mental feeling.”  Is there any doubt regarding the difference between bodily feelings and mental feelings? The body feelings are those that arise in the retinue of sense consciousnesses, whereas mental feelings are those that arise in the retinue of mental consciousness. However, there is a doubt with respect to the difference between them. For example, the feeling of happiness, suffering, and so forth in the retinue of any of the main sense consciousnesses can make the mental consciousness happy, unhappy, and so forth. What is that mental happiness or unhappiness? Is it bodily or mental feeling? Because it is developed in dependence on a sense power, it should be bodily feeling but because it is in the retinue of the mental consciousness it is mental feeling. Likewise, when the mind experiences suffering, there can also arise bodily suffering, and when there is mental happiness, such as the happiness of pliancy, it produces physical or bodily happiness. This happens because there is a strong and close relationship between the body and mind in that the mind and the wind travel together, like a horseback rider and a horse. Therefore, even though the mental consciousness affects the body, that feeling of unhappiness in the body is a bodily feeling. These two are separate even though one produces the other. Likewise, although the mental consciousness is affected by a sense consciousness, the feeling in the retinue of the mental consciousness is not bodily feeling. What experiences feeling? When the mind affects the body, the sense consciousness experiences happiness or suffering and therefore it is a bodily feeling. Likewise, when the body affects the mind, the mental consciousness is what experiences suffering or happiness and therefore the feeling is mental feeling.   STUDENT: Is the explanation of the mind and wind traveling together also present in sutra? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: In the context of developing meditative stabilization, it is said that the mind and wind travel together. When one develops calm abiding, one attains a pliancy of body 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
113 because, by the power of the mind, the wind is made to flow freely in the body. While the subtle mind and wind are a tenet of tantra, this does not mean that they do not exist according to sutra. However, the particularity of the subtle mind and wind being conjoined with the realization of emptiness occurs only in tantra. We know that when we are angry, we feel its effect in our body. Likewise, when we are happy, our entire body feels relaxed. So there definitely is a relationship between body and mind:  STUDENT: If we say that feelings are the result of karma, what about the bodily feelings that arise as a result of meditating on calm abiding? Are they the result of karma? In the Treasury of Manifest Dharma i t  s a y s  t h a t  o n l y  b o d i l y  f e e l i n g s  a r e  d e f i n i t e l y  t h e  r e s u l t  o f  k a r m a ,  b u t  n o t  a l l  m e n t a l  happiness or suffering is the result of karma.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Mental feelings are feelings of happiness, suffering, and neutral, so how can they not be the result of karma? Karma can ripen in this life, in the next life, or a future life. The karma that ripens in this life is called “karma that is a seen phenomena” because its result is seen in this very life.   STUDENT: The Treasury of Manifest Dharma says that mental happiness and mental unhappiness are not fruitional results.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right. They are not fruitional or ripened results, but they are results. The body is a fruitional result.  STUDENT: If all of us put the same amount of effort into meditating, we should all experience the same result of happiness. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is true, we would all experience the same amount of happiness.  STUDENT: But that does not correspond with the previous explanation. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h a t  i s  b e c a u s e  s o m e  p e o p l e  d o  n o t  k n o w  h o w  t o  m e d i t a t e ,  s o m e  meditate with effort but mistakenly, and so forth, therefore not everyone experiences the same result. In fact, s o m e  p e o p l e  m e d i t a t e  a n d  e x p e r i e n c e  s u f f e r i n g  a n d  s o m e  p e o p l e  m e d i t a t e  a n d  experience happiness. All feelings of happiness and suffering are the result of the ripening of karma, but not all happiness and suffering is a ripened result (also translated as “fruitional result”). For example, there can be a future ripening in the form of the path of accumulation and path of preparation, but are the path of accumulation and the path of preparation ripened results? They are not. Thus, while they are the future ripening of particular actions, they are not ripened results. Etymologically the word “ripen” means to ripen in a particular way and in this sense the path of accumulation and path of preparation do ripen, but they cannot be said to be ripened results. In general, a ripened result occurs only once since it refers to a rebirth. However, there are examples of beings who have changed bodies, for example, it is recounted that due to insulting his teacher, the disciple’s body changed into the body of a hell being in that very same life.   Wednesday morning, 5 October 2005  Feelings can be divided in different ways, for example, they can be divided into bodily feelings and mental feelings, which are each further divided into three: (1) pleasant bodily feelings  (2) suffering bodily feelings (3) neutral bodily feelings and (1) pleasant mental feelings (2) suffering mental feelings  (3) neutral mental feelings  The following is another way of dividing feelings. Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:    Also, when these six feelings are differentiated into the two – the materialistic and the non-materialistic – [feelings] are of twelve types.   QUALM: What do “materialistic” and “non-materialistic mean”? 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
114 RESPONSE: Feelings that are concomitant with the craving for the contaminated appropriated aggregates are called "materialistic f e e l i n g s . "  F e e l i n g s  t h a t  a r e  c o n c o m i t a n t  w i t h  t h e  e x a l t e d  wisdom directly realizing selflessness are called "non-materialistic feelings."   The three bodily feelings and three mental feelings can each be further divided into materialistic and non-materialistic feelings for a total of twelve types of feelings: (1) materialistic pleasant bodily feelings  (2) non-materialistic pleasant bodily feelings  (3) materialistic suffering bodily feelings (4) non-materialistic suffering bodily feelings (5) materialistic neutral bodily feelings (6) non-materialistic neutral bodily feelings (7) materialistic pleasant mental feelings (8) non-materialistic pleasant mental feelings (9) materialistic suffering mental feelings  (10) non-materialistic suffering mental feelings  (11) materialistic neutral mental feelings (12) non-materialistic neutral mental feelings  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   QUALM: Well then, if one were to explain the above-mentioned "non-materialistic suffering feeling," how can a suffering feeling be present in those who possess the exalted wisdom directly realizing selflessness?"  RESPONSE: There are many such cases. For example, the Scripture on Discipline (Vinayagama) says t h a t  e v e n  f o e-destroyers who have abandoned the conception of self experience suffering feelings such as headaches due to the fruition of past actions.   Someone asks how can there be a non-materialistic suffering feeling, that is, a suffering feeling that is concomitant with the exalted wisdom realizing selflessness? However, there are many examples given in the Vinaya of foe-destroyers who still experience suffering such as headaches and so forth.   There are three vehicles: the hearer vehicle, solitary realizer vehicle, and great vehicle (the Mahayana). Those who follow the Mahayana are said to be sharp facultied, solitary realizers are said to be middling facultied, and hearers are said to be dull facultied. However, although hearers in general are said t o  b e  d u l l  f a c u l t i e d ,  t h e y  c a n  b e  f u r t h e r  s u b d i v i d e d  i n t o  t h o s e  o f  s h a r p ,  middling, and dull faculties. Sharp-facultied hearers can attain the state of foe-destroyer in the same life, in the intermediate state, or upon taking rebirth. Those of middling faculty attain the state of foe-destroyer in two or three lives, and those of dull faculty attain the state of foe-destroyer in fourteen lives. The number of lives in which the state of foe-destroyer is attained is calculated from the time the hearer enters the path up to the attainment of foe-destroyer. But, indifferent of whether a hearer attains foe-destroyer in that same life or in fourteen lives, he attains it on a support that is a body projected by karma and afflictions, due to which he experiences the sufferings of birth, aging, sickness, and death. For this reason, foe-destroyers experience the suffering of headaches and so forth.  Although the example of a non-materialistic feeling is a feeling that is concomitant with the direct realization of selflessness, it is not pervaded by being that. For example, a headache in a foe-destroyer is a non-materialistic feeling, but that feeling is not in the retinue of the direct realization of selflessness. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It refers to when they enter the path of accumulation.  STUDENT: What is the feeling like that is in the retinue of the direct realization of selflessness? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I don’t know! I don’t have a direct realization of selflessness! ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: It is probably a good feeling!

An example of a non-materialistic suffering bodily feeling is a headache in a foe-destroyer. However, since it is difficult to posit a non-materialistic suffering mental f e e l i n g  i n  t h e  c o n t i n u u m  o f  a  f o e-destroyer, it is perhaps more feasible to understand this in relation to aryas who are not yet foe-destroyers. In any case, it cannot be the coarse suffering feeling that arises in ordinary beings.   STUDENT: I s  i t  j u s t  a  H i n a y a n a  t e n e t  t h a t  f o e-destroyers experience suffering or is this also asserted by the Mahayana? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A c c o r d i n g  t o  t h e  M a h a y a n a ,  M a h a y a n a  a r y a s  a r e  f r e e  f r o m  t h e  suffering of birth, aging, sickness, and death due to having attained a mental body, whereas the Hinayana aryas do experience suffering.  STUDENT: Does that refer to someone who is a Mahayana arya by tenet, who lacks bodhichitta, or to someone who is a Mahayana arya by method? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A  M a h a y a n a  a r y a  i s  p e r v a d e d  b y  b e i n g  a  b o d h i s a t t v a  b e c a u s e  a  Mahayana arya has attained the Mahayana path of seeing.  STUDENT: So according to the Mahayana tenets, Hinayana aryas still experience suffering? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A c c o r d i n g  t o  b o t h  t h e  H i n a y a n a  a n d  M a h a y a n a  t h e y  e x p e r i e n c e  suffering.  STUDENT: Does that not contradict the four seals that all contaminated phenomena are suffering and nirvana is peace? This would seem to mean that all uncontaminated phenomena are not suffering. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: So your question regards how nirvana can be peace if foe-destroyers who have attained nirvana still experience suffering? However, a nirvana is not a body, rather it is a cessation, the third noble truth. Although foe-destroyers have attained lasting, permanent happiness, because of having a body projected by karma and afflictions, temporarily they still experience their results. STUDENT: According to the Mahayana, the buddhas eventually exhort Hinayana foe-destroyers to rise from their meditative absorption and return. When they return, do they still experience suffering?  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Foe-destroyers who have attained a nirvana with remainder, which, according to this school, is a body projected by karma and afflictions, still experience the suffering of headaches and so forth. When such foe-destroyers die, they take a mental body that is a nirvana without remainder. In that state there are no headaches, just eternal happiness until they are exhorted to waken and enter the path.   In short, the division into materialistic and non-materialistic feelings is quite clear with respect to bodily feelings but not so clear with respect to mental feelings.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Furthermore, when feelings are divided by way of their supports, there are six:  (1) feeling arising from contact upon the aggregation of a visible form, an eye [sense power], and an eye consciousness;  (2) feeling arising from contact upon the aggregation of a sound, an ear [sense power], and an ear consciousness;  (3) feeling arising from contact upon the aggregation of an odor, a nose [sense power], and a nose consciousness;  (4) feeling arising from contact upon the aggregation of a taste, a tongue [sense power], and a tongue consciousness;  (5) feeling arising from contact upon the aggregation of a tactile object, a body [sense power], and a body consciousness; and  (6) feeling arising from contact upon the aggregation of a phenomenon, a mental [sense power], and a mental consciousness.   Further, these six are each divided into three – pleasant, suffering, and neutral – giving eighteen types of feeling. Fearing wordiness if I were to describe in detail their individual identities, I shall leave them out. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
116  Here feelings are divided into eighteen types.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Moreover, when feelings are divided by way of object of abandonment and antidote, there are two:  (1) feelings that support adherence and  (2) feelings that support deliverance.   (1) Feelings that support adherence are feelings that are concomitant with the craving for the attributes of the desire realm.  (2) Feelings that support deliverance are, for example, feelings that are concomitant with the mental consciousness that, having turned away from the attachment to the attributes of the desire realm, is included within the actual first concentration.   ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: “Adherence” means to cling to or grasp to something, whereas “deliverance” means to emerge from something, and therefore they are opposites.  The difference between these two feelings is that the first, a feeling that supports adherence, is a feeling that is concomitant with craving for the attributes of the desire realm, whereas the second, a feeling that supports deliverance, is a feeling that is concomitant with a mental consciousness included in the actual first concentration that has turned away from attachment to the attributes of the desire realm.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Such a twofold division of feelings is taught in order to make known:  the manner in which craving is induced through the force of feeling and  the manner in which one can become free of attachment to feeling in dependence on an actual concentration.   This is the reason for dividing feelings into the two: feelings that support adherence and feelings that support deliverance. As long as one has craving for the desirable objects of the desire realm, the mind is not included in the first concentration, second concentration, and so forth. Only by developing calm abiding can the mind become separate from attachment to the desire realm and come to be included in the form realm.   This division of feelings is made in terms of an object of abandonment and an antidote. But can the feeling that is concomitant with a mind of the first concentration be said to be an antidote? We need to examine this division carefully. An antidote is generally the opposing force to an affliction, an object of abandonment. A mental consciousness that is included in the first concentration looks at the desire realm as undesirable and looks at the first concentration as desirable. In the retinue of that mental consciousness, there is a feeling. This feeling is said to be an antidote, but to what is it an antidote? Is it an antidote to the attachment? It could be, however the mental consciousness looks upon the first concentration as peace, and therefore desirable, and is attached to it. In order to attain that state of foe-destroyer, that attachment has to be abandoned. So how can the feeling that is attached to the first concentration be an antidote? In short, the feeling concomitant with a mental consciousness included in the form realm is both an antidote and an object of abandonment. Compared to the first concentration, the second concentration is something to desire, and compared to the second concentration, the first concentration is something to be abandoned. Therefore, the feeling concomitant with a mental consciousness of the first concentration is an object of abandonment in relation to the second concentration, and an antidote in relation to the desire realm. However, this may seem rather strange because antidote and object of abandonment are usually mutually exclusive.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:

Also, one should learn from the Stages of the Path to Enlightenment how not to let the three – pleasant, suffering, and neutral feelings – become causes of the three poisons.  When we feel dreadful, we should not concentrate on this feeling by telling everyone about it. If, on the other hand, we tell others that we are fine, even though we are not, we may actually start to feel better.  STUDENT: But that is lying. STUDENT 2: Also I don’t like when others always say that they are fine, although I know that they are not fine  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: In general, we can never truly say that we are fine because we are always under the control of the afflictions and consequently experience suffering. However, we can say that we are fine or not fine compared to someone else. So next time someone asks you how you are, you can reply “Compared to who?”! Or even without saying that, we can just think like that. STUDENT: This is also a cultural thing because Americans always say that they are fine, whereas Europeans appreciate always hearing that. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e  q u e s t i o n  “ H o w  a r e  y o u ? ”  i s  n o t  a  Dharma q u e s t i o n ,  b u t  generally is a question about how the other person feels physically. If we have a headache or some other problem, we say that we are not fine. On the other hand, if it were a Dharma question, we would always have to say that we are not fine. In worldly conventional terms to be well and to be unwell refers to how one feels physically and mentally in that moment.   Awarenesses and Knowers says:  When mental factors are divided, there are fifty-one:  (1) five omnipresent factors,  (2) five determining factors,  (3) eleven virtuous factors,  (4) six root afflictions,  (5) twenty secondary afflictions, and  (6) four changeable factors. (1) Five omnipresent factors The first five: (1) feeling,  (2) discrimination,  (3) intention,  (4) mental engagement, and  (5) contact accompany all minds and thus are explained as “omnipresent.” This is so because whenever any one among these five is not complete, enjoyment of the object is not complete:  • without feeling, the experiencing of pleasure, pain, and so forth, does not arise;  • without discrimination, designation of verbal conventions does not occur;  • without intention, engagement of the object does not occur;  • without mental engagement, directing the mind to the object of observation does not occur;  • without contact, feeling – pleasure, pain, and so forth – is not generated. However, it is not definite that these [omnipresent mental factors] must exist manifestly, because at times such as the occasion of the subtle mind of death, or the time of just having made the connection [to one’s next rebirth, i.e., having just entered the womb] or when
