118 absorbed in [an equipoise of] cessation, some of these – feelings and so forth – merely engage [their object] in a dormant manner. (A) Feeling With respect to the first from among these, feeling, there are two parts: (1) definition and (2) divisions. 1. Definition First, the definition of feeling is: a knower that is distinguished by being that which experiences.   Feeling and feeling aggregate are synonymous. 2. Divisions Second, when [feelings] are divided, there are three:  (1) two-fold division, (2) three-fold division, and  (3) five-fold division.   With respect to the first, [the two-fold division of feeling], there are the two: (1) physical feeling and  (2) mental feeling.   The first, [physical feeling], feeling that i s  a  s e n s e  c o n s c i o u s n e s s ,  a n d  e x t e r n a l  f e e l i n g  a r e  synonyms.   The second, [mental feeling], feeling that is a mental consciousness, and internal feeling are synonyms.  Compared to the mental consciousness the five sense consciousnesses are external, and compared to the five sense consciousnesses the mental consciousness is internal. Therefore, “external feeling” does not refer to a feeling that is located outside or external to a being.   Awarenesses and Knowers says:  Also, there is a two-fold [division of feeling] into: (1) materialistic feeling and  (2) non-materialistic feeling.   The first, [materialistic feeling,] and contaminated feeling are synonyms.   The second, [non-materialistic feeling,] and uncontaminated feeling are synonyms.  The three-fold division [of feelings] is into the three: (1) happy feelings,  (2) suffering feelings, and  (3) equanimity feelings.   Moreover, although whatever is pleasure or pain is necessarily feeling, whatever is equanimity is not necessarily feeling. This is because, with regard to equanimity, there are three:  (1) equanimity feeling [or neutral feeling],

119 (2) equanimity of [that is to say, desisting from] application, which is included among the eleven virtuous [mental factors], and  (3) immeasurable equanimity [or a sense of equality devoid of attachment and hatred]. Awarenesses and Knowers says:  The five-fold division [of feeling] is into the five:  (1) happiness feeling,  (2) mental happiness feeling,  (3) suffering feeling,  (3) mental unhappiness feeling, and  (4) equanimity feeling.5  Whatever is mental happiness is necessarily happiness but is necessarily not the happiness that is within the five-fold division of feeling. This is because whatever is happiness within the five-fold division of feeling must be a physical feeling of happiness. Similarly, whatever is mental unhappiness is necessarily suffering, but is necessarily not the suffering that is within the five-fold division of feeling. This is because whatever is pain within the five-fold division of feeling must be a physical feeling of suffering.  Whatever is mental happiness is pervaded by being happiness but it is not the happiness included in the fivefold division of feeling because the happiness of this context is specifically a physical feeling of happiness. Likewise, whatever is mental unhappiness is pervaded by being suffering but is not the suffering that is included in the fivefold division of feelings because the suffering of this context is specifically a physical feeling of suffering. Therefore, happiness feeling is specifically a physical feeling of happiness, whereas mental happiness feeling is specifically a mental feeling of happiness. Likewise, suffering feeling is a physical f e e l i n g  o f  s u f f e r i n g ,  w h e r e a s  m e n t a l  unhappiness feeling is a mental feeling of suffering. These divisions are made for the purpose of debate as otherwise it would be sufficient to say physical feeling of suffering, physical feeling of happiness, mental feeling of suffering, and mental feeling of happiness. Debate is a medium for sharpening the mind.   Likewise, although there is a physical feeling of equanimity and a mental feeling of equanimity, the text mentions equanimity feeling alone, without dividing it into two. Again this is for the purpose of encouraging debate.  STUDENT: I fi nd i t di ffi cul t to posi t a mental  happi ness or mental  unhappi ness that i s separate from a physical feeling. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is true that physical feeling of happiness and suffering affect the mental consciousness whereby there quickly arise mental feelings of happiness and suffering. Likewise, it is true that happiness and suffering in the retinue of mental consciousness respectively bring about physical feelings of happiness and suffering. But the difference lies in the fact that bodily feelings of suffering and happiness arise in the retinue of sense consciousnesses, whereas mental feelings of suffering and happiness arise in the retinue of mental consciousness. Therefore, the difference between mental and physical feelings is determined by the main consciousness in whose retinue they arise. Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [A2] Discrimination ('du shes) Regarding the entity of discrimination, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is the defining characteristic of discrimination [Tibetan: 'du shes, literally, aggregation-knowing]?  RESPONSE: It has the characteristic of knowing upon aggregation. It has the entity of apprehending a sign and apprehending a mark, through which one designates an expression to objects of perceptions, hearing, differentiation, and knowledge.  Just as it has been said above, it is a knower that, upon the aggregation of the three – object, sense power, and primary consciousness – apprehends the uncommon sign of an object. Also, A Discussion of the Five Aggregates says:  QUESTION: What is discrimination?  RESPONSE: It apprehends the sign of an object.   When discrimination is divided, there are two:  (1) the apprehension of a sign and (2) the apprehension of a mark.   (1) The apprehension of a sign i s  a n  a p p r e h e n s i o n  o f  a n  u n c o mmo n  s i g n  o f  a n  o b j e c t  wh i c h  appears to a non-conceptual consciousness.  (2) The apprehension of a mark is an apprehension of an uncommon sign of an object which appears to conceptual thought.   The bases of engagement for these two types of discrimination are perceptions, hearing, differentiation, and knowledge. Their meanings are as follows.  Perceptions involve designating an expression to objects manifestly perceived.  Hearing involves designating an expression in dependence on hearing credible words. Differentiation involves designating an expression to objects ascertained in dependence on signs.  Knowledge involves designating an expression to objects ascertained directly.   
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
121 Discrimination is that which apprehends signs and marks. It is easier to understand if we take the discrimination in the retinue of a conceptual mental consciousness, which is what gives names to things, as an example. When we consider discrimination in relation to an eye consciousness apprehending an object, although in its retinue there is the discrimination that apprehends the color and shape of the object, there is no discrimination that gives names. That type of discrimination is found only in the retinue of a conceptual mental consciousness.   STUDENT: F e e l i n g s  a r e  d i v i d e d  i n t o  h a p p i n e s s ,  s u f f e r i n g ,  a n d  n e u t r a l  f e e l i n g s .  W h i l e  discrimination determines blue, red, and so forth, does it also determine whether an object is pleasant, unpleasant, or neutral? If it does, how are feeling and discrimination differentiated? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: When an eye consciousness apprehends an object, it apprehends its various colors such as yellow, red, blue, and so forth. However, the naming of these colors as “This is yellow” and so forth is done by a conceptual consciousness on the basis of discrimination which functions to recognize yellow and so forth. Thus, while the eye consciousness apprehends the different colors, that which actually discriminates them as being yellow and so forth is discrimination. When the eye consciousness apprehends blue, yellow, and so forth it also apprehends the object as pleasant, unpleasant, and so forth, but the thoughts “It is pleasant” and so forth are not the function of eye consciousness. Thus, when the eye consciousness apprehends a pleasant object there is a feeling of happiness and a discrimination of it as pleasant. Although the eye consciousness apprehends colors and pleasantness or unpleasantness, it does not have the same function as the feeling and discrimination in its retinue. Discrimination gives the eye consciousness the ability to distinguish between colors. In other words, that the eye consciousness can differentiate between various colors depends on discrimination.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   The Treasury of Knowledge6 explains discrimination as being twofold: (1) the apprehension of a sign with regard to an object and  (2) the apprehension of a sign with regard to a convention.   (1) The apprehension of a sign with regard to an object m e a n s  t o  a p prehend it through individually distinguishing an object's particularities, such as blue, yellow, and so forth. (2) The apprehension of a sign with regard to a convention m e a n s  t o  a p p r e h e n d  i t  t h r o u g h  individually distinguishing a convention's particularities, as when one thinks, "This is a man; that is a woman."   Feeling does not have the function of distinguishing or separating particularities; it is experience. Discrimination, on the other hand, has the function of distinguishing particularities.  [A3] Intention (sems pa)  Regarding the entity of intention, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is intention?  RESPONSE: It is a compositional mental action of the mind. It has the function of engaging the mind in virtue, non-virtue, or the unspecified.   Just as it has been said above, the mental factor which rouses and moves the mind that is concomitant with it to an object is called "intention." This is said to be the principal among all mental factors; it is taught that any mind or mental factor engages an object due to the power of this mental factor. For example, just as iron is helplessly drawn by a magnet, the mind helplessly engages an object due to this mental factor intention.   Intention is mental action or mental karma. It has the function of moving or urging the mind toward an object, which can be either virtuous, non-virtuous, or unspecified. Intention is the most important mental factor because it is responsible for moving all main minds and mental factors to their objects. It is also responsible for actions of body and speech because it precedes them.                                                          6 See stanza 1.14cd 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
122  STUDENT: That fact that intention moves the mind toward an object would seem to imply that intention knows an object before the mind knows it. However, if it were like that, the main mind and the intention in its retinue would not have the five similarities. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: No, no, you should not think like that. Intention is not like a whip that makes a horse move. Rather, when the main mind moves toward an object, that which moves it is the mental factor intention. It is not that when the five omnipresent mental factors are present, mind arises. Rather, when there is a main mind, that which enables it to experience an object, that which enables it to discriminate an object, that which enables it to move toward an object, that which enables it to abide on an object, and so forth are these mental factors. Without these five omnipresent mental factors, what would a main mind do? These mental factors make up the main mind, they are parts of it. The Tibetan term sems byung, which is translated literally as “arising from mind,” indicates that they are parts of the mind. Just as when we walk along a road, we mainly look ahead but also see things to each side, likewise, when we meditate, we mainly focus the mind on its object but mindfulness functions to remind the mind of the object and what to do, introspection functions to recognize excitement and laxity, and so forth. These mental factors of mindfulness and introspection exist in different strengths in different people.   STUDENT: With respect to the omnipresent mental factors, I have difficulty understanding how the mental factor intention arises at the same time as the other four mental factors since it seems to precede or motivate them. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I t  i s  n o t  t h a t  i n t e n t i o n  a r i s e s  f i r s t  a n d  t h e n  t h e  o t h e r  f o u r  m e n t a l  factors arise. ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: Perhaps the translation of the Tibetan word sems pa a s  “ i n t e n t i o n ”  i s  misleading since it seems be related to motivation, which precedes something else and inspires or drives one to do something. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I n  T i b e t a n  t h e r e  a r e  t w o  k i n d s  o f  m o t i v a t i o n s ,  a  c a u s a l  m o t i v a t i o n  and a time motivation. A causal motivation precedes an action, but there is also a motivation that arises simultaneously with an action, this being the “time motivation” or “motivation of the time.” So perhaps intention can be likened to the time motivation. Thus, in this context intention is not like the causal motivation that precedes an action, but more like the simultaneous time motivation. From the point of view of a main mind moving toward on object, what is it that makes it move? It is intention. Intention rouses and moves the mind to an object. That is its function. However, it can also act as a motivation in terms of producing actions of body and speech. In this sense intention can act as a motivating force or like a causal motivation. However, that is not what is meant by intention in this context.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   This intention is mental action. In general, there are two types of actions: (1) actions that are intentions  (2) intended actions   It is just as it has been said in the Treasury of Knowledge (stanza 4.1cd):   They are intention and that produced by it. Intention is mental action. That produced by it are actions of body and speech.   What are actions? Actions are intention and that which is produced by it. Intention is mental action. That which is produced by it are actions of body and speech.  STUDENT: Do the five omnipresent mental factors have the same object?  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The five omnipresent factors in the retinue of a single main mind have the same object. The intention in the retinue of an eye consciousness, the feeling in the retinue of an ear consciousness, the discrimination in the retinue of nose consciousness, the contact in the retinue of tongue consciousness, and the attention in the retinue of body consciousness do not have the same object.

123  STUDENT: Can we say that intention is never the result of karma? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Intention is not the result of karma, but there is no pervasion that it is necessarily not karma. STUDENT: So when is it the result of karma? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A n  i n t e n t i o n  t h a t  i s  t h e  r e s u l t  o f  k a r m a  i s  the intention that is the result of karma. Ha-ha!!! STUDENT: Please explain that! GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is a like the chicken and the egg in that which comes first, intention or karma? Because intention is a result, it must have a cause that is karma, therefore it is the result of karma. It is rather like functioning thing and impermanent phenomenon, which are synonymous and mutually inclusive. Thus, whatever is a functioning thing is an impermanent phenomenon, and whatever is an impermanent phenomenon is a functioning thing. Yet there is a functioning thing that is the result of an impermanent phenomenon and an impermanent phenomenon that is the result of a functioning thing, although they are mutually inclusive and exist simultaneously. Likewise, whatever is intention in the mind of Kundrol is mental action, but it cannot be said that mental action is the intention in the mind of Kundrol. That mental action in the mind of Kundrol is produced by previous actions and produces future actions.   Debate is useful to gain clear understanding of a subject and to resolve doubts concerning it. Only when we are questioned about something, do we understand how well or how little we understand something.   STUDENT: Can we make a decision, such as that to enter a path, that is not due to karma? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Just because things arise from causes, you should not think that we cannot have desires and wishes and so forth. Any wish or desire to do something must come from a previous cause. [A4] Contact (reg pa)  Regarding the entity of contact, the Compendium of Knowledge says:   QUESTION: What is contact?  RESPONSE: It distinguishes the transformation of the sense power upon the aggregation of the three. It has the function of acting as a support for feeling.  Contact is the support of feeling since without contact there would be no feeling. Again there could be a doubt about which one comes first. When a main or primary mind, a sense power, and an object come together, there is contact. From that contact, comes feeling. Therefore, it would seem that contact arises first. Is that right? If it were, the main mind and the mental factors in its retinue could not share the similarity of time among the five similarities. Therefore, it cannot be right. When there is the aggregation of a main mind, a sense power, and object, there is contact. If contact is the support of feeling, it cannot arise before the feeling. When the main mind and object meet, there is feeling. That feeling is produced, abides, and disintegrates together with the main mind. Therefore, the statement that “contact is the support of feeling” does not mean that from contact comes feeling, but rather that they mutually depend on each other. In other words, because there is contact, there is feeling, not in terms of cause and effect but in terms of mutual dependence.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Just as it has been said above, contact is a knower that, upon aggregation of the three – object, sense power and primary consciousness – distinguishes an object in accordance with whatever feeling, pleasant and so on, that is to be experienced. Here, "transformation of the sense power" [in the above citation] is stated for the following reason. For example, due to meeting with an attractive form, the eye sense power transforms into a cause of attachment to the pleasant feeling. Likewise, contact, by distinguishing an attractive form, transforms into a cause of a pleasant feeling.   
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
124 It is correct that due to the eye sense power meeting an attractive object, it transforms into a cause of attachment to happy feeling, but it is strange to say that contact transforms into the cause o f  pleasant feeling. When there is a main mind that meets an attractive object and the contact that makes that happen, there is also feeling. Thus, the main mind, contact, and feeling exist at the same time. However, there are other feelings that arise later, so the subsequent feeling must refer to this and not to the feeling that exists at the time of the main mind and contact. In the retinue of any primary mind, in addition to contact, there is feeling, whether happiness, suffering, or neutral feeling. By the power of that contact, there arises subsequent feeling. In other words, by the causal condition of contact, future feelings are produced.   Thursday morning, 6 October 2005  The fifth omnipresent mental factor is attention. [A5] Attention (yid la byed pa)  Regarding attention, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is attention?  RESPONSE: It is an engagement of the mind. It has the function of holding the mind to the object of observation.  Just as it has been said above, it is a knower that focuses the mind which is concomitant with it on a particular object of observation. What is the difference between intention and attention? Intention moves the mind to general objects, whereas attention directs the mind to a particular object.  Attention has the function of holding the mind on a particular observed object. The difference between intention and attention is that i n t e n t i o n  mo v e s  t h e  mi n d  t o  o b j e c t s  i n  g e n e r a l ,  wh e r e a s  attention directs the mind to a particular object. For example, intention functions to, for example, move the mind to look at a group of people, whereas attention directs the mind to one particular person in the group. Similarly, intention moves the mind to look at a cup, whereas attention causes it to focus on one particular color of the cup. Thus, the functions of discrimination, intention, and attention can be said to be that intention moves the mind to an object in general, for example, a cup; discrimination differentiates the various parts or colors of the cup; and attention enables discrimination to function by holding the mind on that particular object, the cup. This is one way of thinking about these three mental factors, however it is quite difficult to distinguish them. You need to check your own mind in order to determine how they actually function.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [Discussion of the category of omnipresent mental factors] What is the reason for calling these five mental factors – feeling and so forth – "omnipresent" [Tibetan: kun 'gro, literally, go all]? They are so-called because they are mental factors that accompany [Tibetan: rjes su 'gro ba, literally, go after] all minds. Furthermore, if any of the five omnipresent mental factors are missing, then the utilization of an object will not be complete:  if feeling is absent, then there will be no experience;  if discrimination is absent, then there will be no apprehension of the uncommon signs of the object;  if intention is absent, then[the mind] will not be directed to an object;  if attention is absent, then [the mind] will not be focused on a particular object of observation;  if contact is absent, then there will be no support [for feeling].   Therefore, in order for the utilization of an object to be complete, all five omnipresent mental factors must be present without exception.   
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
125 These five mental factors are called “go-all” or omnipresent because they are mental factors that go with all minds. In fact, in order for a main mind to fully utilize its object, it must have these five mental factors in its retinue. Without feeling, there would be no experience. Without discrimination, there would be no ability to d i s t i n g u i s h  t h e  f e a t u r e s  o f  a n  o b j e c t .  W i t h o u t  intention, the mind would not go toward an object. Without attention, the mind would not remain on an object. Without contact, there would be no support for feeling.   STUDENT: I t  s e e m s  t o  m e  t h a t  i t  w o u l d  b e m o r e  l o g i c a l  f o r  t h e s e  m e n t a l  f a c t o r s  t o  a r i s e  sequentially, first contact, then attention, and so on. What does Prasangika say about them? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: All four schools have the same position on these mental factors, that is, that they are present in the retinue of all main minds and hence are not sequential. Although there are differences regarding how the four schools assert ignorance and wrong view, otherwise they are very similar in terms of how they posit the mental factors.  Awarenesses and Knowers says: (2) Five object determining mental factors  The second [group of mental factors], the five: (1) aspiration,  (2) belief, (3) mindfulness,  (4) stabilization, and (5) wisdom, individually ascertain objects and thus are called “ascertainers [or determiners] of objects.”   Because these five mental factors individually ascertain their objects, they are called “object ascertaining” or “object determining” mental factors.   Awarenesses and Knowers says:  • Aspiration aspires to or seeks the object.  • Belief engages that object joyfully.  • Mindfulness apprehends the object of observation and the aspect.  • Stabilization abides one-pointedly.  • Wisdom analyzes individually.   The first, [i.e., that aspiration aspires to or seeks the object,] is established because aspiration (’dun pa), wish (’dod pa), and seeking (don gnyer) are synonyms.   Thus, if there exists an aspiration that seeks a certain phenomenon, effort for the sake of that phenomenon is begun.  When there is an aspiration that seeks an object, the effort to attain it begins. Thus, aspiration is the basis for effort or perseverance.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [B] The five object-ascertaining mental factors (yul nges) The five object-ascertaining mental factors are:  (1) aspiration (2) belief (3) mindfulness (4) meditative stabilization (5) wisdom 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
126 [B1] Aspiration ('dun pa) Regarding the entity of aspiration, the Compendium of Knowledge says:   QUESTION: What is aspiration?  RESPONSE: It is the very wish to be endowed with this or t h a t  [ a t t r i b u t e ]  o f  a  desired thing. It has the function of acting as a support for making effort.   Just as it has been said above, it is a knower that, upon observing some intended thing, seeks it. The way in which aspiration acts a support for effort is as follows. [Maitreya's] Discrimination of the Middle and the Extremes (stanza 4.5ab) says:   The support and that supported by it and The cause and the result.   The Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path also says:   If you cannot stop the laziness t h a t  i s  a  n o n-enthusiasm toward cultivating meditative stabilization, and that takes joy in its discordant class, then from the outset you will not be inclined to engage in meditative stabilization. Even if you do achieve it once, it will quickly degenerate since you will be unable to sustain it. Therefore, it is most crucial to put an end to laziness at the beginning. Regarding this, when you attain pliancy in which your body and mind are filled with joy and bliss, laziness will be overcome, since no weariness will arise in your application to virtue all day and night. In order to generate that [pliancy], you must be able to undertake continuous effort in meditative stabilization, the cause for generating pliancy. And in order to produce that [effort], you n e e d  a  c o n t i n u o u s  f e r v e n t  aspiration that seeks meditative stabilization. As a cause for that [aspiration], you must have the stable faith of captivation due to having seen the excellent qualities of meditative stabilization. Therefore, for a start, you should meditate again and again on faith that reflects upon the excellent qualities of meditative stabilization. Having witnessed the sequence of these through your practice, you will apprehend this excellent essential point with the clearest certainty.  As a proof for this point, [Tsongkhapa] cited the [above] passage of the Discrimination of the Middle and the Extremes, "The support…" He explains its meaning as follows:   The support i s  a s p i r a t i o n ,  t h e  s u p p o r t  o f  s t r i v i n g .  T h e  supported i s  s t r i v i n g  o r  effort. The cause of aspiration is the faith of conviction in excellent qualities. The result of striving is pliancy.   When one comes to understand the benefits of developing meditative stabilization, one develops belief or conviction in it. Due to this belief, one generates the aspiration or wish to attain it. Due to this aspiration, effort to attain it arises. From effort, comes the result of pliancy of body and mind. These mental factors are in turn accompanied by mindfulness, which does not forget its object, and wisdom, which is able to distinguish. In this way, these five object-ascertaining mental factors are necessary to attain calm abiding or samatha. Calm abiding is a meditative stabilization, however here meditative stabilization refers to that which i s  a  s e e d  o f  h i g h e r  s t a t e s  o f  m e d i t a t i v e  stabilization. Thus, although the function of the meditative stabilization included in these five mental factors is to hold the mind on its object, it is not the same as the meditative stabilization that can remain on an object for long periods of time.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [B2] Belief (mos pa) Regarding the entity of belief, the Compendium of Knowledge says:   
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
127 QUESTION: What is belief?  RESPONSE: It holds an ascertained thing to be just the w a y  i t  h a s  b e e n  ascertained. It has the function of non-captivation.   Just as it has been said above, it is a knower which holds the object that has been ascertained by its valid cognition, thinking, "It is just like this and not otherwise." Here its function is specified as "non-captivation," because when one has gained firm belief in the object that one has ascertained, an opponent cannot steer one away from it.   Aspiration and so forth are the basis for achieving greater things, whereas belief or conviction has decided that this is this. Therefore, there also exists a kind of blind faith. Belief ascertains something to be a certain way and holds it to be that. However, there also exist other kinds of belief. For example, there can be belief in that which is correct and belief in that which is wrong, for example, that which is opposite to the four noble truths. Similarly, while aspiration and so forth have their individual definitions, there is no pervasion that everything that seeks an object is aspiration and so forth.   In the presentation of the three vehicles, they are said to exist on the basis of three types of belief or conviction or disposition. This is because certain beings have a particular belief in one rather than another and based on this belief develop aspiration for a certain goal and engage in actions to achieve that result. With respect to the three vehicles, belief in the Mahayana does not fit the definition that belief ascertains something and then cannot be captivated away from it because a person who has entered the Mahayana path can t u r n  a w a y  f r o m  t h e  M a h a y a n a  a n d  e n t e r  t h e  Hinayana, and likewise someone on the Hinayana path can t u r n  a w a y  f r o m  i t  a n d  e n t e r  t h e  Mahayana. Therefore, in this context belief means that in the retinue of a ma i n  mi n d ,  t h e r e  i s  a  belief that is a liking for an object. But then it can be asked whether there is belief in the retinue of an eye consciousness seeing someone who one does not like? However, that is possible because we may like to get angry at an enemy!   STUDENT: But surely it is possible to have belief or conviction in true sufferings without liking them? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Just as we can like getting angry at an enemy, we can also like true sufferings in the sense of generating the wish to become free from them. This is possible. However, if one does not recognize suffering as suffering, this will not happen, but when one recognizes suffering as suffering there is a liking that wants to be free from it. If one does not recognize the suffering of change to be suffering because it appears to be happiness, there is liking for that happiness. However, even when one recognizes it to be suffering, there can still be a liking for that. It is necessary to think about this.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [B3] Mindfulness (dran pa) Regarding the entity of mindfulness, the Compendium of Knowledge says:   QUESTION: What is mindfulness?  RESPONSE: It is a non-forgetfulness of the mind with respect to a familiar object. It has the function of non-distraction.   Just as it has been said above, it is a knower that possesses three features. These three features are:  (1) objective feature – a familiar object  (2) [subjective] aspect feature – a non-forgetfulness upon having observed that object; and  (3) functional feature – non-distraction   (1) Since mindfulness is not produced with respect to that which has not been previously familiarized with, the objective feature is stated as "a familiar object."  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
128 (2) Even though the mind might have previously familiarized itself with an object, if it does not presently dawn as an object of the mind, mindfulness will not occur. Thus, [the subjective aspect feature] is specified as "non-forgetfulness of the mind."  (3) Since the stability of the mind is enhanced in dependence on special mindfulness, its functional feature is specified as that of "non-distraction."   STUDENT: Does mindfulness focus on a past object. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Mindfulness is said to focus on an object with which one is familiar or strongly connected. However, this is not always the case because someone who has studied a lot and has a good memory can remember it even years later. Therefore, it is not always necessary that the object of mindfulness is an object with which one is strongly familiar. Some people have very good memories in that years later they remember the name of someone whom they met for a very short time. Other people cannot remember the names of people with whom they are very familiar! Thus, while mindfulness is non-forgetfulness of the mind with respect to a familiar object, whatever is mindfulness is not necessarily a non-forgetfulness of the mind with respect to a familiar object.   ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: The Tibetan word dran pa a c t u a l l y  c o m b i n e s  t h e  m e a n i n g s  o f  b o t h  mindfulness and memory.  It is not so difficult to remember inanimate objects but it is more difficult to remember people. Even though we know many people roughly and therefore are in this sense familiar with them, it is very difficult to know them thoroughly. It is easier to know an object. Mindfulness is developed through effort, one needs to train in developing it. For example, one reads a text, thinks about it, and goes over the meaning again and again in order to develop mindfulness or memory of it. If one does not train in mindfulness, it weakens over time.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [B4] Meditative stabilization (ting nge 'dzin) Regarding the entity of meditative stabilization, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is meditative stabilization?  RESPONSE: It is a one-pointedness of the mind with respect to an imputed thing. It has the function of acting as a support for knowledge.  Just as it has been said above, it is a one-pointedness of mind that, within observing an imputed thing, is continuously set upon it. The objective feature of meditative stabilization is specified to be "an imputed thing," because, when cultivating meditative stabilization, one holds the mind to an object of observation that is imputed by the mind.   Meditative stabilization is a one-pointedness of mind that observes an imputed thing and continuously sets upon it. Normally any kind of holding to an object is stabilization, therefore it also exists in sense consciousnesses, for example, there is stabilization when an eye consciousness holds an object. However, here meditative stabilization specifically refers to the object of a conceptual consciousness because the object is an imputed thing, something that is imputed or imagined by the mind. So in this context meditative stabilization does not seem to refer to the meditative stabilization that also exists in sense consciousnesses but refers only to conceptual consciousnesses.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Furthermore, although there appear to be many enumerations of objects of observation for meditative stabilization, when condensed, it is taught that there are four [categories]:  (1) objects of observation for purifying behavior  (2) objects of observation for purifying afflictions  (3) pervasive objects of observation 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
129 (4) objects of observation for [developing] skill  Also, the objects of observation for [developing] skill are taught to be of infinite types – observation of the [five] aggregates, observation of the [eighteen] constituents, observation of the [twelve] sources, observation of [the twelve links of] dependent-arising, and so forth.  These are explained in the teachings on the stages of the path so we will not discuss them here.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:    Nowadays, it seems that there are some instructions at odds with the wording of the Conqueror's scriptures that clearly teach the method of performing staring meditation within observation of a form appearing to the eye consciousness. However, Arya Asanga clearly stated that meditative stabilization is not produced in sense consciousnesses; rather, it is only produced in a mental consciousness. Moreover, its object of observation is not form appearing to a sense consciousness; rather, it is only a mental object imputed by the mind.   There are those who assert that it is possible to develop meditative stabilization by staring at an object. However, Arya Asanga clearly states that meditative stabilization is not produced in sense consciousnesses. In order to develop meditative stabilization one initially observes a form, such as the statue of a buddha, with the eye consciousness, but one does not use that eye consciousness to develop meditative stabilization. Instead, one then closes the eyes and imagines the object. When one does this, it may seem that the object moves up and down as one moves one’s head up and down but this is only due to familiarity with seeing objects by means of the eye consciousness. Therefore, what one observes is only a mental object imputed or imagined by the mind.   When one meditates it does not matter whether or not the eyes are open or closed. Although it is usually advised to focus the eyes on the tip of the nose, this can become uncomfortable so it can be better to keep the eyes open but relaxed. However, the main point here is that the object appears to the mental consciousness and not to an eye consciousness, and that one focuses on a mental object.   STUDENT: T h e r e f o r e ,  d o e s  t h e  m e n t a l  f a c t o r  m e d i t a t i v e  s t a b i l i z a t i o n  n o t  p l a y  a  part in a yogic direct perceiver because it is present only in a conceptual consciousness? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I t  i s  n o t  q u i t e  l i k e  t h a t .  W h e n  t r a i n i n g  i n  d e v e l o p i n g  m e d i t a t i v e  stabilization, one initially focuses on a mental object. For example, when one meditates for the purpose of realizing selflessness, at first one does this by way of a meaning generality, but then gradually the object becomes clearer and clearer until there arises a direct perception of it. For this reason it could be said that the definition of meditative stabilization given here is not quite right because it says that the object is imputed by the mind, whereas in the case of a yogic direct perceiver the object is not imputed by the mind. Therefore, at the beginning, the mind focuses on an imputed object but later on the actual object is perceived directly. Therefore, what it says in this text is not completely precise but is correct only with respect to the initial training in meditative stabilization. If rather than saying “an object imputed by the mind” if we say “an object that appears to the mind and is focused upon,” it would satisfy the definition of meditative stabilization both at the beginning of training and at the end when a yogic direct perceiver is generated.   In short, “imputed by the mind” should be understood to mean that the object appears to the mind. In this way, this definition of meditative stabilization can fit both the initial training in meditative stabilization and the culmination of meditative stabilization in a yogic direct perceiver. Because at the beginning one imagines an object, it can be said to be an imputed object. However, in the case of a yogic direct perceiver the object appears and is focused upon, it is not imputed or imagined.  STUDENT: I s  t h i s  definition of meditative stabilization only found in this school? I ask this because in the Theravadin school one focuses on the breath, which is not an object of mental consciousness, in order to achieve meditative stabilization.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Who says that it is not an object of mental consciousness? How does the breath appear when one focuses on it? Is there not a conceptual consciousness that follows the 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
130 movement of the breath? When one focuses on the breath, the body consciousness experiences the sensation of the breath at the nostrils and the nose experiences the odor of the breath. The wind or breath is form and therefore can be a tangible object of the body consciousness, but can also have odor and therefore be an object of the nose consciousness. However, in order to develop meditative stabilization, one does not focus on the breath with the body or nose consciousness, rather one focuses on it with the mental consciousness. Although the object remains the breath, it is an object that is imputed or imagined by the mind.   Thursday afternoon, 6 October 2005 [B5] Wisdom (shes rab) Regarding the entity of wisdom, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is wisdom?  RESPONSE: It strongly differentiates the qualities of things that are to be examined. It has the function of overcoming doubt.   Wisdom differentiates or analyzes the qualities of things. It has the function of overcoming or turning away the three kinds of doubt:  (1) doubt tending toward the factual,  (2) doubt tending toward the non-factual, and  (3) equal doubt.  Wisdom, even in colloquial Tibetan, is synonymous with intelligence and thorough differentiation.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Just as it has been said above, wisdom is a knower that individually differentiates the faults and good qualities of things that are to be examined. Objects that are to be examined by wisdom are of three types: virtuous, non-virtuous, and unspecified7. [Wisdom] individually differentiates their faults and good qualities.   In short, wisdom is that which differentiates between the qualities and faults of things.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:    There are four ways of reasoning for differentiating faults and good qualities:  (1) reasoning of function  (2) reasoning of dependence (3) logical reasoning (4) reasoning of nature  Wisdom uses four types of reasoning to differentiate faults and qualities. The reasoning of functioning examines the function or abilities of phenomena. The reasoning of dependence examines cause and result, i.e., that from this causes comes that result. Logical reasoning is, for example, sound is impermanent because it is a product. The reasoning of nature examines the nature of phenomena, for example, that water flows downward and fire flows upward.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   When one gains certainty though analysis with these four types of reasoning, doubt will be overcome. Hence, its feature of function is specified as "overcoming doubt."   Through training in the four types of reasoning, one gains certainty, whereby doubt is overcome.   All beings of the six realms have wisdom, even animals. However, the wisdom that exists in human beings is far more powerful than that of animals and can therefore be used to great effect. Hence, if it is used for bad, such as to cause suffering and pain, there is nothing worse. If, on the                                                         7 In this context, "unspecified" has the meaning of being neither virtuous nor non-virtuous. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
131 other hand, it is used for good, there is nothing better. Therefore, wisdom, the ability to differentiate, can be equally used for good or for bad. When it is used for good, wisdom is used to think about how to benefit the world. When it is used for bad, that same wisdom is used to create havoc.   In order to increase one’s wisdom, it is said to be good to recite the mantra of Manjushri – OM A RA PA TSA NA DHIH, which we do at the beginning of every class while reciting the Praise to Manjushri. Lama Tsongkhapa recited so many of these mantras that it even spontaneously appeared carved in a rock. Also, there was a monk from Lama Zopa Rinpoche’s kangtsen, Pati Kangtsen, who was the worst in the class in that he was unable to study, to memorize, or to debate. In order to resolve this problem, he received the permission or jenang of White Manjushri and went to meditate in a cave for two or three years. One night it seemed to him that the sun was still shining and he realized that he had accomplished the meditation on White Manjushri. He returned to Sera and became a great scholar who was known as the White Manjushri of Pati. Such accomplishments are the result of effort or perseverance. It is said that at first one studies hard without thinking about food, clothing, and so forth, and then later on one becomes learned and is given more food, clothing, and so forth than one actually needs. For this reason, there is the saying “At first my mouth could not find food, but later on the food could not find my mouth”!   It is very important to develop wisdom as this makes everything easier. Although it is not easy to develop good qualities, the hardships involved must be faced and dealt with. We should not run away from them. In terms of perseverance, for those of you who are monks and nuns it is important to remain a monk or nun until the end of your life. During the lifetime of Buddha Shakyamuni, many of his disciples who became foe-destroyers. Likewise, in the future there will arise other buddhas, all of whom have pledged to lead those disciples who did not become a foe-destroyer under the previous buddha to that state. Therefore, even if we do not attain higher states in this life, when Maitreya comes in the future those of you who are monks and nuns will definitely meet him and enter the path.   Awarenesses and Knowers says: (3) Eleven virtuous mental factors  The third [group of mental factors] – the eleven: (1) faith,  (2) shame,  (3) embarrassment,  (4) non-desire,  (5) the mental factor that is non-hatred,  (6) the mental factor that is non-ignorance,  (7) effort,  (8) pliancy,  (9) conscientiousness,  (10) equanimity, and  (11) non-harmfulness – are virtues by way of turning away from their opposites, non-faith, and so forth. Thus, they are called the “eleven virtuous factors” and are a definite enumeration.  However, faith in that which is not an object of faith, such as demons, and so forth, and effort in the direction of non-virtue are imputed faith and effort, but not actual [faith and effort]. Since this is so, it is necessary to distinguish these.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [C] The eleven virtuous mental factors (dge ba) There are eleven virtuous mental factors:  (1) faith (2) shame 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
132 (3) embarrassment (4) non-attachment (5) non-hatred (6) non-ignorance (7) effort (8) pliancy (9) conscientiousness  (10) equanimity (11) non-harmfulness [C1] Faith (dad pa) Regarding the entity of faith, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is faith? RESPONSE: It is a conviction, clarity, and wishing with respect to an existent that is endowed with excellent qualities and power. It has the function of acting as a support for aspiration.   Just as it has b e e n  s a i d  a b o v e ,  f a i t h  i s  a  k n o we r  t h a t  h a s  t h e  a s p e c t  o f  c o n v i c t i o n ,  c l a r i t y ,  o r  wishing, and it serves as the direct antidote for non-faith. When divided, faith is of three types:  (1) clarifying faith,  (2) faith of conviction and  (3) wishing faith.   (1) Clarifying faith is a clear mind engendered by seeing the excellent qualities of those so endowed, such as the Three Jewels. Why is it called "clarifying"? For example, when one places a water-purifying gem in dirty water, the murkiness of the water is immediately cleared away. Similarly, when this faith is generated, the murkiness of the mind is cleared away, whereupon all excellent qualities of realization become suitable to arise in one’s continuum.  (2) Faith of conviction is the gaining of conviction through contemplating the modes of dependent-arising, cause and result, and so forth taught by the Conqueror.  (3) Wishing faith, is, for instance, having contemplated the modes of the four noble truths, ascertained true sufferings and true origins to be objects of abandonment, and true cessations and true paths to be objects of attainment, and having understood that these can be attained if one makes the proper effort, the faith that thinks, "I shall definitely obtain them."   Clarifying or clear faith a r i s e s  f r o m  s e e i n g  t h e  q u a l i t i e s  o f ,  f o r  e x a m p l e ,  t h e  Three Jewels – t h e  Buddha, Dharma, and Sangha. It is a clear or unpolluted mind that is generated by seeing their excellent qualities.   Such faith can be understood better by looking at its opposite, non-faith. For example, when someone with strong jealousy looks upon someone with qualities, he either looks down upon that person or does not recognize his qualities, or sees something that is not a quality as a quality. Clear faith is a mind that, lacking in such murkiness or unruliness, looks at good qualities and wishes to attain them.   Faith of conviction comes from thinking about the explanations of karma and so forth taught by the Buddha. Wishing or longing faith is to think about the four noble truths, to ascertain that true sufferings and true origins are to be abandoned and true cessations and true paths are to be attained, to understand that they can be obtained with effort, and as a result to think “I will obtain them.”  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Here I have merely identified some illustrations of the three types of faith; it is not that all [instances] have been exhausted here.   These are just some illustrations of the three kinds of faith, but there are many more.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
133 Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Faith is by entity a virtuous mental factor, whereas liking has both virtuous and non-virtuous factors. If this is explained in detail, there are the four possibilities:  (1) that which is liking but not faith (2) that which is faith but not liking  (3) that which is both (4) that which is neither   There are four possibilities between liking and faith. Faith by entity is virtuous, whereas liking can be either virtuous or non-virtuous. For example, rejoicing in virtue is virtuous liking, rejoicing in non-virtue is non-virtue liking.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   (1) The first, that which is liking but not faith is, for example, liking one's son, one’s wife and so forth, and liking sources of misdeeds, such as drinking alcohol and eating meals after noon [when ordained].  (2) The second, that which is faith but not liking is, for example, fear from one's depths and faith of conviction regarding the drawbacks of the sufferings of cyclic existence.  (3) That which is both faith and liking is, for example, faith from one's depths and liking due to contemplating the excellent qualities of the spiritual guide and the benefits of wholesome actions and their results.  (4) That which is neither faith nor liking is anger, suffering, and so forth.   The example of that which is liking but not faith is liking one’s son or one’s wife and liking drinking alcohol and so forth, but this is because this text was meant for the ordained. That which is faith but not liking is f e a r  o f  a n d  f a i t h  i n  t h e  d r a w b a c k s  o f  t h e  s u f f e r i n g s  o f  c y c l i c  e x i s t e n c e .  Although it is necessary to develop the faith of conviction in the drawbacks of the sufferings of cyclic existence and in how they arise from their respective causes, this is not easy to develop. The word “fear” is what makes this an example of that which is faith but not liking, as conviction in suffering alone is both faith and liking. For example, when someone meditates on patience, he welcomes suffering as a method to develop patience, whereby there is both faith in suffering and a liking of it. Also those who practice taking and giving like when suffering arises. Thus, the fact that this example specifically mentions “fear” eliminates it being a liking.  That which is both faith and liking is the faith and liking that arises due to contemplating the excellent qualities of one’s spiritual teacher and the benefits of virtuous actions. That which is neither faith nor liking is anger, suffering, and so forth.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   QUALM: Well then, are liking and respect the same or are they different?  RESPONSE: Again, in the world we speak of them as if they are the same, but in fact they are not. Liking a spiritual friend is faith, but respect for him involves contemplating his kindness, knowing shame, and valuing him highly. Hence, when [liking and respect] arise in the continuum, they are separate mental factors.   It is possible for respect to arise from liking a person, however they are different mental factors. To like someone means to enjoy seeing him, whereas respect implies a reverence for that person.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   If, in accordance with how they appear in the great treatises, you analyze these modes in detail with the wisdom of individual investigation, examining the way in which they are generated in the continuum by turning your mind inwards, then you will get to know them; you cannot know them merely through words. With these meanings in mind, the Foremost Omniscient [Tsongkhapa] repeatedly advises that, in order to perform wholehearted practice, you must rely 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
134 upon a skillful spiritual friend and acquire much hearing on the meaning of the scriptures. However, nowadays, when these great textual systems are explained to foolish beings who are deprived of the gem of intelligence and are inferior in merit, they become frightened, terrified and flee faraway, as though a poisonous snake had sensed the odor of musk, or a little child had caught sight of a whirlpool. Those who view the exalted speech of the great scholars and adepts from the Land of Superiors [i.e., India] as pith instructions seem like stars during daytime.   It seems that poisonous snakes are frightened by the odor of musk! The great treatises are difficult to understand but it is very important to try to understand them since they contain much knowledge and explain many different types of practices. If, instead of studying them, one practices calm abiding alone, for example, one will not create great virtue. The practice of calm abiding should not be done in isolation but should be cultivated in order to develop other qualities.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:    Here, the function of faith is specified as "acting as a support for aspiration," because, as explained above, the cause of all excellent qualities is effort; in order to generate effort, one needs the aspiration that seeks; in order to generate aspiration, one needs to see the excellent qualities as well as possess the faith of conviction. For this reason, faith is praised more than once as the foundation of all virtuous qualities in the scriptures and their commentaries.   Faith is important because from it comes aspiration, from aspiration comes effort, and from effort come all excellent qualities. However, this faith should not be ignorant or blind faith, rather it should be faith based on knowledge or understanding. In other words, faith should be based upon reasoning since blind faith is unstable. From the faith that is based on knowledge comes aspiration, and from that comes effort.   If one has a blind faith that believes in God or the Three Jewels, when one becomes sick or experiences other problems there is the risk that one will think that God or the Three Jewels are angry with one. This faith is ignorant because suffering is not created by God or the Buddha, but comes about as the result of one’s own negative actions. Some people request many pujas to be done to cure their illness, for them to achieve success, and so forth, but when nothing happens they come to think that it is the pujas that are useless. In this way, they transfer their own faults onto the Three Jewels. This is the result of blind faith and a sign that one does not understand how and why sufferings arise.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [C2] Shame (ngo tsha shes pa) Regarding shame, the Compendium of Knowledge says:   QUESTION: What is shame?  RESPONSE: It is an avoidance of misdeeds on account of oneself. It has the function of acting as a support for effective restraint from misconduct.   Just as it has been said above, shame is an avoidance of misdeeds on account of oneself.  [C3] Embarrassment (khrel yod pa) Regarding embarrassment, the Compendium of Knowledge says:   QUESTION: What is embarrassment?  RESPONSE: It is an avoidance of misdeeds on account of others. It has the function of just that [i.e., acting as a support for effective restraint from misconduct].   Just as it has been said above, shame is an avoidance of misconduct on account of others.   
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
135 Shame is a mental factor that prevents one from engaging in misdeeds on account of oneself, whereas embarrassment is a mental factor that prevents one from engaging in misdeeds on account of what others might think.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [Discussion of the pair – shame and embarrassment] What is the difference between shame and embarrassment? Although both of them are similar in terms of avoiding misconduct, shame is such that, when one is about to engage in misconduct, one avoids it, thinking, "This is not something I should do". In the case of embarrassment, when one comes close to engaging in misconduct, one avoids it on account of others, thinking, "If others were to despise me [as a result of my engaging in misconduct], this would not be fitting." One avoids misconduct due to concern about the displeasure of one's gurus or the Teacher – the principal objects [causing] this avoidance. Their function is specified as "acting as a support for effective restraint from misconduct" because shame and embarrassment are definitely necessary for restraining misconduct of the three – body, speech, and mind. If shame and embarrassment are absent, one will be unable to restrain oneself from any misconduct whatsoever, for, without fear about the fruition from one's own side, and lacking concern about the displeasure of one’s gurus, the Teacher, and so forth, there will be no way to cease misconduct.  Both shame and embarrassment prevent misconduct, in that when one is close to engaging in misconduct one is prevented from doing so either by feeling ashamed of oneself or by thinking about what others would think. In this case “others” mainly refer to one’s gurus and the Teacher, Buddha Shakyamuni, therefore one avoids engaging in misdeeds out of a wish to not disappoint them.   Hence, these two minds of shame and embarrassment are important in restraining misconduct of body, speech, and mind. When we are in public, we tend to be careful of our behavior but, when we are alone, we do whatever we want. This is not good, therefore we should think about what our gurus and the Buddha would think of our bad behavior and, remembering that the Buddha is watching us, refrain from negative behavior even when we are alone.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [C4] Non-attachment (ma chags pa) Regarding non-attachment, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is non-attachment?  RESPONSE: It is a lack of attachment to [cyclic] existence and the articles of [cyclic] existence. It has the function of acting as a support for non-engagement in misconduct.   Just as it has been said above, it is a knower that is disenchanted and lacks attachment.  Many faults arise because of attachment. Attachment is a wrong consciousness because it either observes a pleasant object and exaggerates is attractiveness, or is observes an unattractive object and sees it as attractive. Due to follow this wrong consciousness, due to which we engage in negative actions. We never have enough, we always want more. The nature of attachment is to always want more, due to which we are never satisfied. If we get the object, it is not enough and we want more. If we do not get it, we suffer. Therefore, attachment has great faults and we need to think about them in order to reduce it.  STUDENT: What is the difference between chags pa and ‘dod chags? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: They are the same.   Friday morning, 7 October 2005  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
136 STUDENT: It was said yesterday that the Buddha is constantly watching us, but if that were so there would not be any difference between Buddha, God, Ishvara, and so forth, especially if we are to see the Buddha as sitting there taking notes about our good and bad actions. I believe in the buddha nature within us but not in a buddha who is constantly watching us. That seems to me to be superstition. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The way in which a buddha looks upon sentient beings and the way in which we look at others is totally different. We examine other people while looking for their faults and mistakes, but a buddha does not think “Now I will check on what so-and-so is doing.” Rather, motivated by compassion, he looks at whether sentient beings are creating the causes for suffering and if they are he is saddened. But the buddhas are not like spies! At all times the buddhas gaze upon sentient beings out of compassion. The point is to think about cause and effect such that when we are alone and about to engage in a bad action, we remember that at all times the buddhas see all phenomena, even without any motivation to do so. This way of thinking is one method that is useful for restraining us from engaging in non-virtue.  STUDENT: T h e  b u d d h a s  c a n  o n l y  h e l p  u s  b y  t e a c h i n g  u s ,  t h e r e f o r e  i t  w o u l d  b e  s u p e r s t i t i o n  t o  develop fear through thinking that the buddhas are watching us. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: There is no need to be afraid of the buddhas, they never harm us. We should refrain from engaging in non-virtue out of respect for ourselves by remembering that the buddhas are also aware of what we are doing. It is not that the buddhas will punish us if we do something wrong. We alone punish ourselves.  STUDENT: I think that we should look at the buddha inside us and not at an external buddha. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e r e  i s  n o  b u d d h a  i n s i d e  u s ,  a l t h o u g h  t h e r e  i s  t h e  p o t e n t i a l  t o  become a buddha within us. The buddha lineage is not a buddha, so there is no buddha inside us.  STUDENT: It is our job to become buddha, we ourselves must do the work. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right. For this reason we need shame and embarrassment in order to develop the buddha lineage, whether we are alone or with others. STUDENT: But we do not need to feel guilty about ourselves.
