However, what is important is to judge ourselves, to look inside ourselves. If we do this and see that we have done something wrong, instead of becoming depressed or feeling guilty we should think “Until now I had not seen that this behavior is wrong but now that I understand that it is, I will be careful in the future.” We should use this as an opportunity to develop our courage and strength of mind. It does not matter what others say about us, good or bad, as people always have something to say! It is important that we judge ourselves.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [C5] Non-hatred (zhe sdang med pa) Regarding non-hatred, the Compendium of Knowledge says:   QUESTION: What is non-hatred?  RESPONSE: It is a lack of malice with regard to sentient beings, suffering, and the sources of suffering. It has the function of acting as a support for non-engagement in misconduct.   Just as it has been said above, it is a knower that conquers the generation of hatred within observation of any of the three objects that generate hatred, [i.e., sentient beings, suffering, and the sources of suffering,] and is a lack of malice.  Non-hatred is non-anger. It is a lack of malice toward sentient beings, suffering, and the sources of suffering. Hatred or anger can arise toward the animate and inanimate, for example, if we stub our toe on a rock, we get angry at the rock; if we cannot find the right key in a mass of keys, we get angry with the keys; if we are in a hurry and can’t get our clothes on right, we get angry at our clothes.   
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
137 STUDENT: I thought that hatred is defined as the wish to harm, but here it seems to be enough to become upset without having any wishing to harm. Is that right or is it that one wants to harm the rock, the keys, and so forth? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Hatred and anger are based on malice or ill-will. It is not always the case that first there is ill-will and then that develops into anger, as there can arise anger that is followed by malice.  STUDENT: So non-hatred is patience? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is right.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [C6] Non-ignorance (gti mug med pa)  Regarding non-ignorance, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is non-ignorance?  RESPONSE: It is k n o w l e d g e  [ a r i s e n ]  f r o m  f r u i t i o n ,  s c r i p t u r e ,  c o n t e m p l a t i o n ,  o r  realization, and is a wisdom of individual investigation. It has the function of acting as a support for the non-engagement in misconduct.   Just as it has been said above, it is a wisdom of individual investigation that is able to serve as the antidote to ignorance [arising] from its cause – being either acquired through birth or arisen through application.  Non-ignorance is a lack of confusion. There are, for example, people who learn very quickly due to having a clear mind, this is non-ignorance. It is an absence of mental dullness or fogginess. There are two types of non-ignorance: (1) non-ignorance that is present at birth that does not depend on study and so forth but comes about as a result of having trained in it in previous lives (this is non-ignorance arisen from fruition) and (2) non-ignorance that arises from application through studying the scriptures, contemplating them, and gaining realizations. Although one of the non-ignorances that arises from application comes from studying the scriptures, it is not always the case that people who study a lot and become very learned have a clear mind as some of them can be very angry, arrogant, contemptuous of others, and so forth. Such people were perhaps better people before they studied the scriptures! They have become wise in the words of the scriptures but not in the meanings that lie behind them. This does not mean that they do not understand the meaning of the words, but rather that they have not applied the meaning to their minds. They could be said to have the mental factor of non-ignorance with respect to the words but to not have it with respect to the meanings. However, when such people later on in life meet with difficulties, they will be better prepared to deal with them than those people who have never studied. It is said that for a foolish person even slight non-virtue becomes very heavy, whereas for a wise person even great non-virtue becomes very light. This is because for someone who does not know how to purify their negative karma, even though initially it was very little, it becomes heavy. On the other hand, someone who has studied knows how to purify their negative karma such that even their heavy non-virtue can become light. Therefore, it is better to be learned than to not have studied, but the very best is to apply one’s learning to one’s mind.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [Discussion of the three – non-attachment, non-hatred and non-ignorance] These three mental factors are like the root of all virtuous qualities, the method for ceasing all misconduct, and the essence of all the paths. All paths and stages are for the sake of abandoning the three poisons as well as their imprints, and all misconduct definitely arises from one of the three poisons. Hence, their function is specified as "acting as a support for effective restraint from misconduct"8.   The divisions that are instances of these [three mental factors] are infinite, because, taken extensively, all paths are subsumed within them.                                                         8 One might expect the text here to read "acting as a support for non-engagement in misconduct," but it does not. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
138  Furthermore, regarding non-attachment: Non-attachment to this life, one's mind having turned away from this life to seek the purpose of future lives, is the attitude of a being of small scope. Non-attachment to all the marvels of [cyclic] existence, having turned away from one's depths from adherence, is the attitude of a being of intermediate scope. Non-attachment to the extremes of both [cyclic] existence and [solitary] peace, and seeking non-abiding nirvana is the attitude of a being of great scope.  Non-attachment, non-hatred and non-ignorance are the root of all virtuous qualities, the method for ceasing all misconduct, and the essence of all paths. The purpose of the paths is to abandon the three poisons and their imprints because all misconduct arises from them. Non-attachment, non-hatred and non-ignorance are the opposites or antidotes to the three poisons. All the paths, the path of beings of small scope, the path of beings of intermediate scope, and the path of beings of great scope, are subsumed in these there mental factors.  If one lacks attachment to this life, one naturally works for the happiness of future lives; this is the path of inferior beings. If one lacks attachment to cyclic existence, one naturally works to attain liberation; this is the path of middling beings. If one is lacks to the extremes of existence and peace, one naturally works to attain the non-abiding nirvana that is enlightenment; this is the path of great beings. In addition, non-hatred, which is patience, is needed to progress on all three paths. Non-ignorance, which is intelligence and knowledge, is also necessary to travel all three paths.   Here it is said that a being of great scope strives for the non-abiding nirvana that is free from the extreme of existence and the extreme of peace. The extreme of cyclic existence is easy to understand, whereas the extreme of peace refers to remaining absorbed for eons in the meditative absorption that is nirvana, during which one does not enact the welfare of other sentient beings. Therefore, a being of great scope turns away from both the extreme of existence and the extreme of peace.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Explained in this manner, it follows that all the scriptures will need to be explained here. How could I possibly do that? Here, I merely indicate the door to the intelligent ones. Likewise, the same applies also to non-hatred and non-ignorance.   This is just a brief explanation of these three mental factors.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:    Here, non-ignorance is a wisdom of individual investigation, and is of two types: (1) [non-ignorance] acquired through birth  (2) [non-ignorance] arisen through application  From among these two, (1) [non-ignorance] acquired through birth is indicated by "[arisen] from fruition" [in the above citation], because it arises from the fruition of past actions without depending on the circumstances of this life, and (2) [non-ignorance] arisen through application refers to the three – t h e  w i s d o m  a r i s e n  f r o m  hearing, the wisdom arisen from contemplation, and the wisdom arisen from meditation.   The basis upon which one engages in hearing with the wisdom arisen from hearing is the Conqueror's scriptures as well as their commentaries. T h e  T e a c h e r ' s  s c r i p t u r e s  h a v e  t w e l v e  divisions: (1) set of discourses (2) set of melodious descriptions  (3) set of prophesies  (4) set of verses 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
139 (5) set of purposeful expressions (6) set of background accounts (7) set of expressions of realizations (8) set of legends (9) set of birth stories (10) set of the very extensive (11) set of marvels  (12) set that settles   Since it would be verbose if I were to explain in detail the identity and principal subject matter of each of them, I shall not write more here.   Acharya Chandrakirti taught that these twelve branches of scripture can be subsumed into nine branches; by taking the four – the main background accounts and its associates [i.e., (7)-(10) in the listing above] – as one, [the twelve] are condensed into nine. The nine [divisions] can in turn be condensed into three:  (1) the basket of discourses (sutrapitaka) (2) the basket of discipline (vinayapitaka) (3) the basket of knowledge (abhidharmapitaka)   The main subject matter of these three baskets is the three trainings. It is often taught that: (1) the main subject matter of the basket of discipline is the training in ethics (2) the main subject matter of the basket of discourses is the training in meditative stabilization (3) the main subject matter of the basket of knowledge is the training in wisdom  In addition, the Compendium of Knowledge teaches that: the three trainings are equally taught in the basket of discourses;  the two trainings in ethics and in meditative stabilization are taught in the basket of discipline, and  the training in wisdom is taught in the basket of knowledge.   What is the intention behind this? When one guards the stipulated boundaries of discipline by properly relying upon mindfulness and introspection, one will be able to effectively cease laxity and excitement through just that, whereupon it becomes the supreme method for generating meditative stabilization. Nowadays, forming an understanding of such stages of the path is extremely rare.   Therefore, seek much hearing on the three baskets as well as their commentaries – the basis upon which one engages in hearing, analyze again and again the meaning of what has been heard with the four types of reasoning, and generate an ascertaining consciousness. Put the entire path into practice by way of analytical and stabilizing meditation upon that very meaning which the wisdom arisen from contemplation has gained ascertainment of.  These are the assertions of the Great Trailblazers.   One can read and understand the above for oneself.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [C7] Effort (brtson 'grus)  Regarding effort, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is effort?  RESPONSE: It is a mind that is enthusiastic in regard to armoring, application, non-inferiority, irreversibility, and non-complacency. It has the function of fulfilling and accomplishing the class of virtue.  Just as it has been said above, the mind that is enthusiastic about virtue is effort.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
140  Effort, in short, is delight in virtue.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Thus the Acharya Vasubandhu says [in his A Discussion of the Five Aggregates]:   QUESTION: What is effort?  RESPONSE: It is the antidote for laziness and a mind that is enthusiastic about virtue.   Effort is the antidote to laziness, which is of three types: (1) the laziness of procrastination, (2) the laziness of adherence to unwholesome activities, and (3) the laziness of discouragement, which is the thought that one is unable to do something. However, it also includes indolence, which is the thought to not do something.  The laziness of procrastination is a bad habit. For example, although we have something that we need to do, we go on talking and chatting and instead put it off until tomorrow, but the next day again we do the same. Procrastination also happens because we sleep too much and as a result do not have enough time to do what needs to be done. This does not mean that we should deprive ourselves of sleep, but rather that we should determine what is the right amount of sleep for us and not exaggerate. Likewise, friends are important, but sometimes they can harm us in that as a result of spending time talking to them time passes and we do not get certain things done. When studying, it is important not to spend too much time chatting and therefore to set a limit to this activity. In addition, we need to examine whether our friends are Dharma friends or false friends. False friends do not appear with horns on their heads, although this would be better for us in that we would not waste so much time talking to them! However, false friends smile and are nice to us, due to which we spend a lot of time talking to them. Therefore, it is best to have Dharma friends who are agreeable to talking less and to leaving us to our studies. When we are having a good time, time flies and even two or three hours are suddenly gone. Therefore, we need to lessen the time spent in that way. There is never enough time. For example, there are twenty-four hours in a day but we spend about eight of them sleeping. Then we have breakfast, lunch, and dinner, which takes up as much as another two or three hours a day. Then we need to go to the toilet, to bathe, and so forth. Then we go here and there, do our jobs, and talk to friends. If we examine the amount of time that remains for study and practice, we will find that it is very little. For this reason, we need to spend less time talking. Time is precious.   Procrastination has as its root the clinging to our own permanence, the thought that we will live for a long time and that we therefore still have time. But due to this thought, life passes and one becomes old, whereupon one no longer has the strength to do many things. Trijang Rinpoche used to say that we spend sixty years of our life not doing anything. For the first twenty years, we are too young to want to study and so forth. Then, we spend the next twenty years thinking about doing something but not doing it. Then, we spend the next twenty years thinking that it is too late to do anything!  The laziness of adherence to unwholesome activities refers to striving to attain only the pleasures of this life. While we tend to think that people who work constantly and hard have a lot of effort, it is not what is meant by effort here. By working we can gain money, but while this gives us some temporary comfort and pleasure, it does not solve our problems in the long-term. Therefore, because money and so forth do not bring lasting happiness, striving for them is said to be a type of laziness because it is opposite to the effort that delights in virtue and brings lasting happiness. Our possessions and wealth are a cause of happiness, but they are not the main cause, this being the creation of virtue. The hardships involved in striving in the Dharma f o r  t h e  happiness of future lives are nothing compared to the hardships involved in working to attain the happiness of this life. This is because no matter how hard one works there is no guarantee that one will attain happiness in this life, whereas by practicing the Dharma it is guaranteed that we will attain happiness in our future lives. What is the practice of Dharma? To practice Dharma means to subdue or tame one’s mind. All other activities such as making offerings, doing recitations, and so forth are just secondary practices, the main practice being to subdue the mind. Hence, all virtuous 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
141 actions of body and speech, such as prostrations and so forth, are secondary to the practice of training one’s mind.   We have a mind that wants suffering and a mind that wants happiness. What is the mind that does not want suffering? Generally it is a mind that does not want to experience headaches, cold, and the ordinary sufferings of daily life. However, such a mind is generated with regard to only very coarse sufferings, and not with regard to the sufferings that pervade our body and the sufferings that pervade all our lives. Likewise, the mind that wants happiness is limited; it is a mind that wants only the temporary happiness of this life. Therefore, both these minds are very superficial. Instead, we should develop the mind that strives to attain long-term and unchanging happiness, and to avoid the more subtle pervasive suffering.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Hence it is of utmost importance to know the following in accordance with the Foremost Omniscient [Tsongkhapa]'s Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path:  the benefits of making effort;  the disadvantages of not doing so;  the way in which it stops its discordant class – the laziness of procrastination, the adherence to unwholesome activities, and indolence;  the way to practice the conditions that are favorable for effort, the four powers – the power of belief, the power of steadfastness, the power of joy, and the power of abandonment.  Since I cannot mention everything here, you should understand them by carefully perusing Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds and the Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path.   There are four powers that are favorable conditions for the development of effort– the power of belief, the power of steadfastness, the power of joy, and the power of abandonment. The power of belief refers to having the three kinds of faith since from faith comes aspiration, and from aspiration comes effort. In this sense belief is said to be the support of effort. The power of steadfastness refers to having constancy. The power of joy refers to the fact that effort is in nature joyous. The power of abandonment is to abandon laziness.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [C8] Pliancy (shin tu sbyangs pa) Regarding pliancy, the Compendium of Knowledge says:   QUESTION: What is pliancy?  RESPONSE: It is a serviceability of body and mind due to severing the continuum of unfavorable states of body and mind. It has the function of eliminating a l l  obscurations.   Just as it has been said above, it is a knower that deposits an imprint so that the mind can be applied, however one wishes, to a virtuous object of observation, and that severs the continuum of unfavorable states of body and mind.   From effort comes pliancy (shin tu sbyangs ba literally means “thorough training”).  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   When divided, pliancy is of two types:  (1) physical pliancy (2) mental pliancy  (1) Physical pliancy is such that, through the power of meditative stabilization, unserviceability of the body is purified, whereupon the body is buoyant and light, like a ball of cotton, and can be applied to virtuous activity according to one's wish.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
142 (2) Mental pliancy i s  s u c h  t h a t ,  t h r o u g h  t h e  p ower of meditative stabilization, the mind becomes free of unserviceability, whereupon it becomes amenable to application to an object of observation without hindrance.  The fact that when we meditate our legs, back, and so forth hurt and are uncomfortable is a sign that we lack physical pliancy. Pliancy overcomes unfavorable states of body and mind that prevent us from doing what we want. Such states include discomfort in the body, distraction and non-virtuous states of mind, and so forth. Until we attain pliancy, we should be careful when meditating not to push to much and, when tired, to take a break, go for a walk, rinse our face with cold water, and so forth. If instead we continue to force ourselves to meditate, the winds in our body will become unbalanced and we can become sick. When pliancy is attained, the body and mind become serviceable and we will no longer be hindered by such unfavorable states of body and mind.  Having attained pliancy, whatever virtue is brought to mind, it remains and cannot be turned away by distractions, non-virtuous minds, and so forth.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [C9] Conscientiousness (bag yod)  Regarding conscientiousness, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is conscientiousness?  RESPONSE: I t  i s  a  c u l t i v a t i o n  o f  v i r t u o u s  q u a l i t i e s  w i t h i n  a b i d i n g  i n  n o n-attachment, non-hatred, non-ignorance, as well as effort, and guards the mind against contaminations. It has the function of thoroughly completing and fully establishing all mundane and supramundane success.  Just as it has been said above, it is an awareness that accomplishes virtue within abiding in effort without coming under the influence of afflictions, and it guards the mind against contaminations.   Conscientiousness prevents us from engaging in misdeeds or non-virtue. We should be conscientiousness at all times, whatever we do, and not allow our mind to come under the control of non-conscientiousness or carelessness. To be non-conscientiousness is, for example, when washing plates to carelessly bang them together, causing them to chip and break. Instead we should be conscientious or careful in all our activities. In particular we should be conscientious with respect to property that belongs to a monastery and be careful to not damage it or misuse it. We should also be careful with our own property and cultivate this type of mind rather than allowing ourselves to develop bad habits. Just as we usually take good care of our own belongings, so too should we take care of monastic or public property.   We should apply conscientiousness to our actions of body, speech, and mind. With respect to our mind, we should not allow it to follow after useless thoughts; with respect to our speech, we should not say whatever comes to our mind; and with respect to our body, we should not engage in just any kind of action. In other words, we need to take care of what we think, say, and do. This is what is meant by conscientiousness.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [C10] Equanimity (btang snyoms) Regarding equanimity, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is equanimity?  RESPONSE: It is a discordance with afflictive states, an evenness of mind, a dwelling in a natural state, and a spontaneous abiding, within abiding in non-attachment, non-hatred, non-ignorance, as well as effort. It has the function of not allowing an opportunity for afflictions.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 3-7 October 2005 (04)  
143 Just as it has been said above, in dependence on the techniques for internally setting the mind single-pointedly on an object of observation, t h e  n i n e  m e n t a l  a b i d i n g s9 a r e  g r a d u a l l y  accomplished. When the ninth is achieved, one no longer needs to strive to use the antidotes to laxity and excitement and one attains a spontaneous abiding of the mind.   In general, there are three types of equanimity: (1) equanimity of application (2) equanimity which is feeling (3) immeasurable equanimity  From among these, this [mental factor here] is equanimity of application.   The mental factor equanimity is the equanimity of application, or compositional equanimity. When cultivating calm abiding, whenever laxity or excitement arise we need to apply their respective antidotes. On the other hand, when neither laxity or excitement have arise, we should allow the mind to abide in equanimity. This equanimity is the equanimity of this context. It is an evenness of mind and a mind abiding in its natural state.   In general when studying the subject of Awarenesses and Knowers (lo-rig), one should first get an overview of the different groups in which the fifty-one mental factors are included in order to get an idea of what they are. Then, one should look at the individual mental factors included in each group.   END

144 Monday afternoon, 10 October 2005  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [C11] Non-harmfulness (rnam par mi 'tshe ba)  Regarding non-harmfulness, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is non-harmfulness?  RESPONSE: It is a mind of compassion and is involved with non-hatred. It has the function of not inflicting injury.   Just as it has been said above, non-harmfulness is a patience that, lacking malice, observes suffering sentient beings, thinking, "May they be free of that [suffering]!" This abandoning harm to sentient beings, or non-harmfulness, is the essence of the meaning of the Conqueror's scriptures.   The opposite to non-harmfulness is harmfulness or ill-will. The Buddhist conduct is that of non-violence or non-harmfulness and the Buddhist view is that of dependent-arising. The text says “non-harmfulness is a patience that, lacking malice, observes suffering sentient beings, thinking, "May they be free of suffering!" However, strictly speaking the mind that observes suffering sentient beings and thinks “May they be free from suffering” is compassion, not patience. However, when we are angry but able to see that the other person is suffering, we develop patience toward him and from that comes an attitude of non-harmfulness. Also when someone else is angry with us, we can also think that the other person is angry because he is suffering and thereby develop patience toward him and, from that, non-harmfulness. Patience can be considered to be a type of non-harmfulness in that when we observe someone as suffering and develop patience toward him, we naturally do not harm that person.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   It is taught [in sutra]:   Patience is the supreme austerity. The Buddha said, "Patience is supreme nirvana."  An ordained one who harms or injures another Is not a trainee-in-virtue.   In general, it is not good for anyone to get angry, but it is especially negative for ordained monks and nuns to become angry. This is because those who are ordained, literally those who have gone forth from their homes, should be Dharma p r a c t i t i o n e r s ,  wh e r e a s  i f  t h e y  g e t  a n g r y  t h e y  are not creating virtue and therefore not practicing Dharma. A trainee in virtue is a person who is accumulating virtue.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Even the Conqueror's teaching in the context of bestowing a water strainer in the Vinaya procedural rite is a fine distinction of compassion. Since one must definitely turn away from harming others as well as their bases, the necessity of equipping oneself with a strainer for the sake of abandoning harm to creatures in water has been taught. And on the occasion of giving the instructions, one is cautioned about the necessity to abide in the four qualities that makes one a trainee-in-virtue:  Even when derided, do not deride in return.  Even when someone gets angry at one, do not get angry in return.  Even when hit, do not hit back.  Even when one's faults are exposed, do not expose others' faults.   
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
145 Therefore, if the intelligent analyze and understand this well, they will be able to understand that abandoning harmfulness is the essence of the teachings.   A trainee in virtue should not return criticism when criticized, should not become angry at someone who is angry, should not hit someone in retaliation, and should not expose others’ faults even though they expose one’s faults. Instead, when any of these situations arises, one should sit humbly in silence with one’s head bowed. Although others might think that one is strange for not retaliating, this is the correct behavior. If we retaliate, there is no end to the problem as then the other person replies badly or hits us back, then we do the same, then again the other, and on and on. On the other hand, if we do not retaliate, it ends there. However, it is not easy to not respond.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [Discussion of the category of virtuous mental factors] QUALM: With regard to virtue, are the eleven [virtuous mental factors] that have been explained here exhaustive? RESPONSE: In general, virtues are taught to be of five types: (1) virtues by way of entity (2) virtues by way of relation  (3) virtues by way of subsequent relation (4) virtues by way of motivation (5) virtues by way of ultimacy  (1) The eleven explained above – faith and so forth – are specified as being virtues by way of entity because they are generated in the entity of virtue from their mere establishment without relying on other factors such as motivation. Hence, here I have indicated the eleven principal virtues.  (2) "Virtues by way of relation" are minds and mental factors that are concomitant by way of the five similarities with the eleven – faith and so forth.  (3) "Virtues by way of subsequent relation" are imprints of virtue.  (4) "Virtues by way of motivation" are, for instance, physical and verbal actions motivated by faith.  (5) "Virtues by way of ultimacy" are explained to be thusnesses, because all obscurations will be purified if one meditates within observation of thusness; therefore, they are designated as "virtues" but are not actual virtues.  There are five types of virtue: (1) virtues by way of entity – the eleven mental factors,  (2) virtues by way of relation – all the minds and mental factors concomitant with any of the eleven virtuous mental factors,  (3) virtues by way of subsequent relation – t h e  i m p r i n t s  o f  v i r t u e  d e p o s i t e d  o n  t h e  m e n t al continuum, (4) virtues by way of motivation – actions of body and speech motivated by any of the virtuous mental factors,  (5) virtues by way of ultimacy – s e l f l e s s n e s s ,  b e c a u s e  b y  w a y  o f  m e d i t a t i n g  o n  i t  o n e  a t t a i n s  liberation, which is the ultimate.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   When the virtues explained above are divided from the viewpoint of circumstances, there is: (1) virtue by way of attainment at birth (2) virtue by way of application (3) virtue by way of activity [directed] in front (4) virtue by way of benefiting (5) virtue by way of bearing (6) virtue by way of acting as an antidote (7) virtue by way of pacification 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
146 (8) virtue by way of concordant cause  (1) Virtue by way of attainment at birth i s ,  f o r  i n s t a n c e ,  t h e  f a i t h  t h a t  arises through mere rebirth due to the imprints from past lives without depending on familiarization in this lifetime. (2) Virtue by way of application i s ,  f o r  i n s t a n c e ,  t h e  g e n e r a t i o n  o f  t h e  w i s h  t o  a t t a i n  buddhahood in dependence on the four conditions – (i) relying on a virtuous spiritual guide, (ii) listening to the excellent doctrine, (iii) properly taking such to mind, and (iv) practicing doctrines that are concordant with the attainment of nirvana. (3) Virtue by way of activity [directed] in front is, for instance, actions of making prostrations and offerings within observation of the special field of accumulation. (4) Virtue by way of benefiting is, for instance, actions such as ripening sentient beings by way of the four means of gathering disciples10.  (5) Virtue by way of bearing is, for instance, special wholesome actions that act as the means for attaining high status and definite goodness. (6) Virtue by way of acting as an antidote is, for instance, virtuous actions that possess the special power to directly destroy the objects of abandonment and the discordant class. (7) Virtue by way of pacification is explained to be, for instance, true cessations, just as it is said [in a stanza of homage to the Dharma]:  Purity separating from attachment, Virtue liberating from the bad migrations, Unique, ultimate, supreme –  The pacifying Dharma…  (8) Virtue by way of concordant cause is, for instance, the five clairvoyances11 a n d  t h e  t e n  powers12 that arise though the force of attaining true cessations.  These virtues explained above are either actual or designated.  Virtue by way of activity directed in front refers to visualizing the merit field in front of oneself and making prostrations, offerings, and so forth to it. V i r t u e  b y  w a y  o f  bearing, literally “thoroughly holding,” is, for instance, the special white actions that act as the cause for attaining high status, that is, high rebirth, and definite goodness, that is, liberation or enlightenment. Virtue by way of benefiting is, for instance, actions such as ripening sentient beings by way of the four means of gathering disciples. The four are: 1. giving the disciple what he needs, 2. speaking pleasantly and sweetly to him, 3. even though oneself has high attainments acting in accordance with one’s disciple and acting in accordance with what one teaches, and 4. helping one’s disciple engage in the teachings.  We usually see His Holiness the Dalai Lama, Trijang Rinpoche, and other high lamas sitting on a high throne in the distance and rarely have the opportunity to meet them more closely. 147 this, there is little danger of finding fault in them, criticizing them, and so fort, whereas there is this risk with respect to the lamas with whom we live. Therefore, it can be better for one’s lamas to remain far away from us as then their blessing is greater because we do not find fault with them, while if they are closer we may come to lose the recognition of them as our teacher. For this reason, we should be very careful when it comes to choosing a teacher and examine someone carefully over time before accepting him as a teacher. When we listen to teachings, whether given by a great lama or an ordinary person, we should think about what the person says and examine him even for a period of years. We should never immediately accept someone as our lama. In the past it was advised to take up to twelve years to choose a teacher, after which one would actually request the lama to be one’s teacher. With respect to this, there is the joke that the lama might respond “Since you have spent twelve years examining me, now I will spend twelve years examining you!” This is because the relationship between teacher and disciple is a two-way relationship, that is, it must come from both sides. When this relationship exists, it is said that the attainment of enlightenment is as easy as sculpting a buddha statue. But perhaps we are not yet ready for a guru-disciple relationship like that which existed between Marpa and Milarepa!  Awarenesses and Knowers says: (4) Six root afflictions The fourth [group of mental factors] – the six: (1) desire which is that, [i.e., a root affliction],  (2) anger,  (3) pride,  (4) ignorance,  (5) afflicted doubt, and  (6) afflicted view, – act as the root of both cyclic existence and the secondary afflictions, and thus are called “root afflictions.”  Whatever is either desire or ignorance is not necessarily a root affliction for these [occurring] in the continuum of a bodhisattva superior are not root afflictions because they are diminished in capacity, like poison overcame by medicine or mantra.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [D] The six root afflictions (rtsa nyon) The six root afflictions are:  (1) attachment  (2) anger (3) pride  (4) ignorance (5) doubt  (6) afflicted view  Regarding the general defining characteristic of afflictions, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  The definition of an affliction is a phenomenon such that when it arises, the characteristic of being very unpeaceful arises, and due to its arising, the mental continuum arises as being very unpeaceful.  Just as it has been said above, it is an awareness that causes the mental continuum to be very unpeaceful when it arises. The individual entities are as follows.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
148 An affliction is that which makes the mind to thoroughly lack peace. There are people who are constantly angry but do not recognize the suffering that their anger causes them because they are so familiar with it. If such people try to cultivate patience, it can cause a lot of hardship and frustration. This is due to their familiarity with anger and lack of familiarity with patience, and so it may seem to them that it is better to be angry rather than to practice patience. However, it is not the cultivation of patience that causes suffering, it is our lack of familiarity with patience that makes it difficult to achieve. The difficulty lies in not being able to bear something. Also, when we become angry, it can seem that we possess a kind of power that can protect us from harm. Although that itself feels good, what has really happened is that we have lost control of our mind. It is also important to keep in mind that whether we cultivate patience or not, others will still become angry with us. Although it is not the case that they no longer become angry with us, by meditating on patience we will develop more interest in cultivating it.   The general definition of an affliction is that which disturbs the mind. However, there are people who, not understanding this, welcome the afflictions.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [D1] Attachment ('dod chags) Regarding attachment, the Compendium of Knowledge says:    QUESTION: What is attachment?  RESPONSE: It is clinging13 of t he t hr ee r eal ms.  I t  has t he funct i on of pr oduci ng suffering.   Just as it has been said above, it is a mental factor that perceives a contaminated thing to be attractive by way of its own entity and thereupon seeks it. Regarding this, the Foremost Omniscient [Tsongkhapa] says in [his Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path]:  Attachment observes an external or internal object that is beautiful and attractive and then becomes attached to it. For example, just as oil that has soaked into a piece of cloth is difficult to remove, likewise [attachment] spreads and adheres to its object of observation, making it difficult to tear oneself away from it.  Attachment is likened to oil that has soaked into a piece of cloth because just as oil permeates the cloth and is difficult to remove, so too is attachment difficult to eliminate.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Attachment is of three types:  (1) attachment of the desire realm (2) attachment of the form realm (3) attachment of the formless realm  The Treasury of Knowledge14 divides it into two:  (1) attachment of the desire realm (2) attachment for [cyclic] existence  [The Treasury of Knowledge] condenses the attachment of the two upper realms [i.e., form realm and formless realm] into one, and takes that to be the attachment for [cyclic] existence.   (1) Attachment of the desire realm is aspiration for and attachment to the five qualities of the desire realm – form, sound, and so forth – within observation of them.  (2) The attachment of the two upper realms is condensed into one, known as the "attachment for [cyclic] existence," because the two upper realms are referred to as "[cyclic] existence" in order                                                         13 Tibetan: chags pa. Also translated as "attachment." 14 See stanza 5.2ab. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
149 to overcome the mistake of taking the meditative absorptions of the form and formless realms as a path of liberation.   The function of attachment is specified as "producing suffering" for the purpose of making us understand that the root of all suffering within the three realms of cyclic existence is rebirth which connects us to cyclic existence, and the principal cause for rebirth within cyclic existence is attachment, craving itself.  The Treasury of Knowledge c o n d e n s e s  t h e  a t t a c h m e n t s  o f  t h e  f o r m  a n d  f o r m l e s s  r e a l m s  i n t o  “ t h e  attachment to existence” for those who think that the meditative absorptions of the form and formless realms are a path to freedom from suffering. There is a difference between a preparation and an actual concentration and a preparation and an actual concentration that constitute a path. If one enters the path by means of a preparation or actual concentration, that preparation or actual concentration itself becomes a path to freedom. However, this does not mean that any preparation or actual concentration is itself a path. While those who attain the meditative absorptions of the form and formless realms might think that they have attained liberation, at the time of death, when they see their rebirth, they come to understand that it is not liberation. Therefore, there is the difference between entering the path on the basis of a preparation or actual concentration and the mere attainment of a preparation or actual concentration. To enter the path through a preparation or actual concentration means that the mind is conjoined either with renunciation or the mind of enlightenment, which is not the case when one merely attains a preparation or actual concentration.   We are beings of the desire realm because we have attachment to the desirable objects of the desire realm. It is very difficult to become free from this attachment. However, we need to work to lessen our desire and increase our satisfaction or contentment. If we do not actually cultivate the opponents or antidotes to attachment we will always suffer from dissatisfaction. Even if someone were to be given everything good in the entire world, it would still not be enough! Ha-ha! For this reason, we need to reduce our attachment.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [D2] Anger (khong khro) Regarding anger, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is anger?  RESPONSE: It is a malice towards sentient beings, suffering, and phenomena that are sources of suffering. It has the function of acting as a support for not abiding in contact and for misconduct.   Just as it has been said above, anger is a malice that, upon observing the three objects of observation of anger, cannot tolerate them and wishes to harm them. The three objects of observation of anger are:  (1) sentient beings,  (2) one's own sufferings, and (3) the sources from which these sufferings arise  “Not abiding in contact” refers to the fact that when one is angry at something one does not want to be near it, but wants to be away from it. However, when we are angry with someone, we sometimes actually go nearer to hit him! Perhaps it can be said that the mind distances itself from the object of anger.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Regarding the teachings on "the nine bases of malice"15, the Precious Garland ( s t a n z a  5 . 3 1 )  says:                                                          15 See the explanation on [E1] Belligerence for a listing of these nine bases of malice. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
150  Harmful intent arises from nine causes Of intending to harm others:  Bearing senseless misgivings in the three times  With regard to oneself, one's friends, and one's enemies.   The nine bases of anger are: (1-3) those who have harmed, are harming, and will harm oneself, (4-6) those who have harmed, are harming, and will harm one’s friends, and (7-9) those who have helped, are helping, and will help one’s enemies.  Both the Supplement to the Middle Way and Engaging in a Bodhisattva Deeds say that there is no evil greater than anger. Hence, it would be better to cultivate patience rather than to get angry. Patience and anger are not related to the expression on a person’s face; for example, someone can be very angry inside but not show it externally. Patience and anger exist in the mind. Therefore, when there is anger in the mind, that is where we need to develop patience. Some people get very angry and manifest it strongly, but then the anger gone. Others hold anger inside, without showing it externally, allowing it to build up until they explode. Of the two, it is better to get angry externally and get it over with. However, to not show one’s anger does not necessarily mean that one suppresses it as later on one can work with it mentally and let it go. If, on the other hand, one does not do that, that suppressed anger can even lead to heart problems. In that case, it would be better to show the anger rather than bottling it up inside.   Tuesday morning, 11 October 2005  STUDENT: In Tibetan do the words for “anger” and “wrath” have the same meaning? The word “anger” in Italian implies the wish to harm someone, whereas “wrath” means to be upset with someone but without any wish to harm him. Also in tantra the wrathful deities are said to have compassion. Therefore, the karma of anger and that of wrath should be quite different.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Although when one is angry, one may not have a conscious intention to hurt or harm, by its very nature of anger is a harmful mind. In order for there to be a harmful mind it is not necessary for the object to be another person, it can be directed at a key that does not turn in the lock, making one swear and think to throw it on the ground. In this case there is a mind of harm toward that key. Likewise, when one stubs one’s toe on a rock and swears, there is a harmful mind toward that rock.   Wrath mixed with anger is the very worst state. While wrathful deities have a wrathful expression, they do not have anger in their mind and they are motivated by compassion. Unlike us, they have control over anger; it does not control them. However, it is also possible for us to show a wrathful expression motivated by compassion in order to benefit and help someone else. In this case our actual state of mind is the wish to benefit, but our demeanor is that of wrath. Since some people do not respond to pleasant words, it may be necessary at times to show a wrathful aspect in order to make them listen to us. However, since it is easy for this wrathful aspect to become actual anger or to be mixed with anger, we have to be very careful. Thus, although it is never good to become angry, it can be useful to show a wrathful attitude in certain situations.   All of us know that anger is not good, yet still we get angry. Therefore, the mere knowledge that anger is bad is not enough to put an end to it. In addition, we need to ask ourselves questions as to why we get angry, the point of our getting angry, whether it helps us or the object of our anger, and so forth. We have to think about anger again and again until we come to clearly see its great faults. By doing that, we will eventually develop a wish to no longer get angry. Anger does not arise for any good r e a s o n ,  r a t h e r  we  g e t  a n g r y  f o r  t i n y  r e a s o n s  o r  e v e n  n o  r e a s o n  a t  a l l .  F o r  example, we can get angry just because of the expression on someone else’s face, but this is not a correct reason for becoming angry.   When Atisha went from India to Tibet he deliberately brought bad-tempered companions with him in order to be able to develop his patience. In fact it is not enough to think about past events in order to develop our patience, in addition we need actual people around us who provide us with the opportunity to practice patience. Once patience has been developed, there will not be anyone who can make us angry. This is not because other people will have changed, but because we have changed our own mind. With patience, there will be less and less and less people who make us

151 angry. Certain kinds of actions that previously made us angry will no longer make us angry because we h a v e  c h a n g e d .  F o r  e x a m p l e ,  i f  w e  h a v e  p a t i e n c e ,  w e  w i l l  n o t  g e t  a n g r y  w h e n  b a d  words are spoken to us. That will be to our own benefit or profit; it is we ourselves who will achieve peace and happiness. For this reason, Atisha chose bad tempered people as his companions. However, we do not have to purposely chose bad-tempered companions, we will naturally find them around us all the time!  STUDENT: S o  t h e  c o n c l u s i o n  i s  t h a t  s o m e o n e  c a n  s h o w  w r a t h  e x t e r n a l l y  w h i l e  r e m a i n i n g  c a l m  internally. Is it the same for the other afflictions? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: There are methods for taking attachment, anger, pride, and so forth into the path. It is possible to do this. However, unless one has gained mastery over these afflictions, this cannot be done or it will be very difficult to do. So, first of all we need to gain control over the afflictions before we can make use of them.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [D3] Pride (nga rgyal) Regarding pride, the Compendium of Knowledge says:   QUESTION: What is pride?  RESPONSE: It is a puffing up of the mind in dependence on the view of the transitory collection. It has the function of acting as a support for disrespect and suffering.   Just as it has been said above, it is a mental factor that has the aspect of a puffing up of the mind upon observing the basis for puffing up – one's own wealth, qualities, and so forth.   Here the Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path says:  Pride is based on the view of the transitory collection and is a puffing up of the mind upon observing an external or internal phenomenon that is high or low, good or bad; it involves an aspect of loftiness.   "In dependence on the view of the transitory collection" is specified because all pride is generated in dependence on the innate conception that thinks "I".   Pride is to be full of oneself. One can be proud of one’s wealth, education or skill, knowledge, qualities, attractive body, good voice, and so forth. When one is proud, one either does not listen to others or does not respect what they say because one thinks that one knows better. Therefore, the fault of pride is that it prevents one from gaining qualities. Just a s  w a t e r  p o u r e d  o n  a  l u m p  o f  metal does not enter it but falls to either side of it, likewise due to pride qualities cannot enter the mind. Thus, if a disciple is proud, he will not receive benefit even from his lama’s teachings.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Pride is of seven types:  (1) pride  (2) exceeding pride (3) pride beyond pride (4) pride of thinking "I" (5) pride of vanity (6) pride of slight inferiority (7) wrongful pride   (1) Pride is a puffing up of the mind, thinking that one is superior to lower persons. (2) Exceeding pride is a puffing up of the mind, thinking that one is superior to peers. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
152 (3) Pride beyond pride is a puffing up of the mind, thinking that one is greatly superior even to other superior persons. (4) Pride of t h i n k i n g  " I "  i s  a  p u f f i n g  u p  o f  t h e  m i n d ,  t h i n k i n g  " I "  w i t h i n  o b s e r v i n g  t h e  appropriated aggregates. (5) Pride of vanity is a puffing up of the mind, thinking that one has attained what has not been attained. (6) Pride of slight inferiority is a puffing up of the mind, thinking that one is just slightly lower than others who are actually greatly superior.  (7) Wrongful pride is a puffing up of the mind, thinking that one has attained excellent qualities when one has actually gone astray. An example would be conceit about having attained excellent qualities when one has actually been possessed by a spirit. Also, the Scripture on Discipline states, "to be made conceited by what is actually a branch of shame…" Just as it has been said, [this pride] is, for example, [an ordained person], having neglected the stipulated boundaries of the Vinaya code as if he is a lay person, conceitedly thinking that he is benefiting others out of his attachment to gain and honor.   These [definitions] are arranged according to what is taught in the Treasury of Knowledge16.   The first of these, pride, observes others who are inferior to oneself and thinks that one is better than them. However, it could in fact be true that others are inferior to oneself in some way, whereby oneself is actually superior to them. Is that pride, since it is true? However, we can never be sure that someone else is actually inferior to us since we are unable to see all aspects of the person. But even if the other person is in fact inferior to us, as a result of having become puffed up and proud we will not learn anything from him. It is not always possible to determine internal qualities from someone’s outer appearance; for example, there are people who are very elegant and present themselves well but have no inner qualities, and people who dress very poorly and so forth but have many inner qualities.   With respect to this, there is a story about a great lama from Sera Je Loba Kangtsen, Lama Langdol Rinpoche, who wore ragged and torn robes, was poor in health, and was very emaciated. There was a tradition that before beginning the study of the Perfection (Ornament), the monks would go to Lhasa to look for firewood. Having found some, Lama Langdol Rinpoche was returning to the monastery when he stopped on the side of the road to rest. At that time a wealthy woman, wearing fine clothing and covered in jewelry, passed by while mentally making a prayer to be reborn as a monk. However, upon seeing Lama Langdol Rinpoche in his pathetic state, she added the specification “but not like the monk sitting on the side of the road.” Knowing this, Lama Langdol Rinpoche told her that she need not worry because even if she prayed strongly for seven lifetimes she would not be reborn like him. She was immediately horrified by her mistake with respect to this great lama and later on engaged in practices to purify it.   A real practitioner does not look for food, clothing, or a good reputation as a result of his practice. Such practitioners do not worry about their comfort, but a r e  w i l l i n g  t o  b e a r  t h e s e  hardships so that in their future lives they will not encounter such problems again. If, on the other hand, one seeks the happiness of this life, one will definitely encounter difficulties in one’s future lives. It is better to experience some difficulties in this life and happiness in the future, rather than to experience happiness in this life and difficulties in the future.  STUDENT: Was the lama not being proud when he responded to that woman in that way? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: N o ,  h e  w a s  m e r e l y  g i v i n g  h e r  a d v i c e  t o  n o t  j u d g e  o t h e r s  b y  t h e i r  outward appearance. Because she was not actually reciting the prayer out loud, she immediately understood that he had read her mind, whereupon she understood his qualities, and recognized and regretted her mistake.  STUDENT: C o u l d  G e s h e-la please say something about the difference between pride and self-confidence? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: W e  n e e d  s e l f-confidence and do not need pride, but we ordinary beings often confuse the two. When we think “I can do it. I can understand it,” there is the risk that                                                         16 See stanza 5.10a. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
153 this thought become pride so we need to be careful. To be self-confident is good in that it means to not feel discouraged or down, but instead to have strength or courage of mind.   STUDENT: With respect to this difference, while it is important to love and encourage a person who underestimates himself, can this become an incentive to pride?  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is always possible that when we encourage another person in order to lift his spirits up that he develops pride. However, this is not necessarily the case. Pride involves feeling superior about one’s qualities and so forth, but we are not advising the other person to feel that way. In the beginning, we need compassion for ourselves, especially when we are training in the paths of lower and middling beings. At that time we work on developing the thought to attain the happiness of our future lives and liberation, but later on, when we move into the path of greater beings, we need to extend this compassion to others. If we do not have compassion for ourselves, we cannot develop it for others. We need to have compassion for ourselves that is accompanied by the motivation to develop into a better person in order to be able to help others. Pride i s  f o c u s e d  o n  o n e s e l f ,  o n e ’ s  q u a l i t i e s ,  a n d  s o  f o r t h  s i n c e  i t  i s  b a s e d  o n  t h e  v i e w  o f  t h e  transitory collection. One observes the I and thinks that oneself is superior to someone else. The Tibetan word for pride is literally “I-king” (nga rgyal). Confidence is different because it understands that while one presently does not have certain qualities, one can develop them.  STUDENT: With respect to the risk of inciting pride when we encourage others, yesterday when Geshe-la described non-ignorance as being an intelligence that is a fruition, it came to my mind that there are children who are very intelligent but are a disaster in school. This happens because they are not encouraged at home. So perhaps they have a non-ignorance that is a fruition but due to lacking good completing karma they are not encouraged to develop it. For this reason, encouragement is an important factor for their development. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: A l t h o u g h  s o m e  c h i l d r e n  h a v e  a  n a t u r a l  i n t e l l i g e n c e ,  t h e y  m a y  b e  lacking in effort. There are many monks in the monastery who are intelligent but do not put effort into their studies, and others who are not so intelligent but put a lot of effort into their studies who, in the end, do better than those who are more intelligent. Therefore, it is important for parents and teachers to encourage and stimulate children to want to study and to enjoy studying.  STUDENT: C o u l d  G e s h e-la please say something about the pride of lowliness? The Precious Garland says: Deriding oneself, saying, "I am useless," is called The pride of lowliness. Such is a brief description of them.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h i s  i s  a  r e v e r s e  k i n d  o f  p r i d e  i n  t h a t  o n e  h a s  a  t i n y  a m o u n t  o f  qualities but one looks at them and feels proud of them. There are people who lack intelligence and are proud of that, people who have nothing and are proud of that, and so forth. This kind of pride is strange in that one thinks “I don’t know anything” and is proud of that. STUDENT: When we studied the Treasury of Knowledge, Geshe Jampa Gyatso said that to feel filled with hopelessness is an example of this kind of pride. However, he did not say that there is a need to feel that being hopeless is good or wonderful.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Pride has the meaning of a lifted up or puffed up mind, so I do not see how being depressed about one’s hopeless situation can be a kind of pride.  Tuesday afternoon, 11 October 2005  Pride is necessarily a puffed up mind that is focused on I. Self-confidence, on the other hand, is not like that, without being puffed up it thinks “I can learn, I can study,” and so forth. It is a strong mind that believes in oneself. However, pride and self-confidence can, of course, be mixed together because they are not mutually exclusive.   The pride of lowliness or inferiority is not about feeling wretched due to lacking in qualities and so forth and being depressed about this. On the other hand, there are people who take pleasure in and are proud of not having anything, in not being anything, in being uneducated and unskilled, and so forth. For example, there are those who spend their life committing negative actions but

154 they take pleasure in this and are proud of themselves. Even in my own experience there were monks in my monastery who did not study nor did they want to study, who did not go to classes, debate, and so forth but they just remained in their rooms doing other things and relaxing. Some of them would even sit by the road watching the other monks going to class and expressing pity for them, proud of the fact that they did not study. It is also possible that due to their behavior some of the other monks who were studying came to doubt what they were dong and eventually gave up their studies because of them.   The remaining six of seven types of pride are: (2) Exceeding pride is a puffing up of the mind due to thinking that one is superior to one’s peers or equals. (3) Pride beyond pride is a puffing up of the mind due to thinking that one is greatly superior even to other superior persons. (4) Pride of thinking "I" is a puffing u p  o f  t h e  m i n d  d u e  t o  t h i n k i n g  " I "  w i t h i n  o b s e r v i n g  t h e  appropriated aggregates.
