(6) Pride of slight inferiority is a puffing up of the mind due to thinking that one is just slightly lower than others who are actually greatly superior.  (7) Wrongful pride is a puffing up of the mind due to thinking that one has attained excellent qualities when one has actually gone astray.   STUDENT: According to Sautrantika is it possible for several main minds to exist simultaneously or is there only one main mind at any given time? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e r e  c a n  d e f i n i t e l y  b e  several main minds at the same time. For example, when one eats, one simultaneously sees the food, smells it, tastes it, and so forth.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [D4] Ignorance (ma rig pa) Regarding ignorance, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is ignorance?  RESPONSE: It is unknowing of the three realms. It has the function of acting as a support for the arising of wrong ascertainment, doubt, and afflictions with respect to phenomena.   Just as it has been said above, it is the mental factor of unknowing that is obscured regarding the mode of abiding of all phenomena.   It is of two types: (1) obscuration that is the mental factor of unknowing  (2) an awareness that apprehends erroneously   From among these two, the explicit indication in the Compendium of Knowledge e x p l a i n s  [ignorance] to be the mental factor of unknowing. Since Acharya Vasubandhu also explained it in this way in his A Discussion of the Five Aggregates, the assertions of both Asanga and his brother agree. However, Acharya Dharmakirti asserts [ignorance] to be an awareness that apprehends erroneously. In this way, although there are two types of assertions with regard to ignorance – ( 1 )  t h e  a s s e r t i o n  t h a t  i t  i s  a  w r o n g  c o n c e p t i o n  a n d  ( 2 )  t h e  a s s e r t i o n  t h a t  it is an awareness that does not realize – [ b o t h ]  a r e  a l i k e  i n  a s s e r t i n g  t h a t  t h e  p r i n c i p a l  a n t i d o t e  t o  ignorance is the wisdom realizing selflessness.  Ignorance is of two types:  (1) ignorance that is obscuration with respect to actions and their results (2) ignorance that is obscuration with respect to the meaning of suchness  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
155  (1) The ignorance that is obscuration with respect to actions and their results causes one to accumulate actions for rebirth in the lower realms. (2) The ignorance that is obscuration with respect to the meaning of suchness causes one to accumulate actions for circling in the good migrations.   There are two main ignorances: ignorance that is obscuration with respect to actions and results and ignorance that is obscuration with respect to the suchness that is selflessness. While the ignorance that is obscuration with respect to actions and their results causes us to accumulate actions for rebirth in the lower realms and the ignorance that is obscuration with respect to the meaning of suchness causes us to accumulate actions for circling in the good migrations, it is not the case that due to the ignorance that is obscuration with respect to actions and their results that we will definitely take rebirth in the lower realms and that due to the ignorance that is obscuration with respect to the meaning of suchness that we will definitely take rebirth in the good migrations. Rather, the fact is that due to the ignorance that is obscuration with respect to the meaning of suchness, we continue t o  c y c l e  i n  c y c l i c  e x i s t e n c e .  W i t h  r e s p e c t  t o  u n d e r s t a n d i n g  a c t i o n s  a n d  results, we should study and learn the ten non-virtuous actions and their corresponding results. The seven non-virtues of body and speech are extensively explained in the Vinaya.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [D5] Doubt (the tshom) Regarding doubt, the Compendium of Knowledge says:   QUESTION: What is doubt?  RESPONSE: This is being two-minded with regard to the truths. It has the function of acting as a support for not engaging in the class of virtue.  Just as it has been said above, it is a mental factor that wavers between two points within observation of the four truths, cause and result, and so forth. This doubt hinders all virtuous activities and especially hinders the seeing of the truth. Since it will be overcome when one sees the truth, it is said to be a thorough entanglement that is an object of abandonment of the path of seeing.17  Doubt is a two-pointed mind with respect to the four noble truths and so forth. If one doubts the four noble truths, one doubts the Dharma because the Dharma is true cessations. If one doubts the Dharma, one doubts all three Jewels. If one doubts the Three Jewels, one doubts the two truths. Therefore, if one has a doubt that thinks perhaps the four noble truths do not exist, then one also doubts whether all the rest exist.   The doubt being discussed here is that which is included in the six root afflictions, therefore it is to be abandoned. However, previously three types of doubt were set out, so is the same true of the doubt tending toward the factual that thinks that sound is probably impermanent or of the equal doubt that is equally divided between the possibility of sound being impermanent and sound being permanent? The first noble truth is true sufferings and while one of their attributes is that they are impermanent, this needs to be established. When one is investigating the nature of things, such as whether sound or true sufferings are impermanent, doubts arise to which one applies reasonings. Such doubt is good doubt, whereas the doubt that is a root affliction is a lack of belief or interest in the existence of the four noble truths and so forth. Good doubt, which is generated on the basis of wanting to know whether sound is impermanent and so forth, leads to understanding. The doubt that is a root affliction is not generated by wanting to gain knowledge and does not lead to knowledge. These are my thoughts on the subject but you should examine it yourself.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:                                                          17 The three thorough entanglements are: (1) view of the transitory collection, (2) view holding [bad] morality and conduct as supreme, and (3) doubt.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
156 [D6] Afflicted views (lta ba nyon mongs can) Afflicted views are of five types:  (1) view of the transitory collection (2) view holding to an extreme (3) conception of a [bad] view as supreme  (4) conception of [bad] ethics and modes of conduct as supreme (5) wrong view [D6.1] View of the transitory collection ('jig lta) Regarding the view of the transitory collection, the Compendium of Knowledge says:   QUESTION: What is the view of the transitory collection? RESPONSE: It is any endurance, desire, intelligence, conception, or view that views the five appropriated aggregates as a real I and mine. It has the function of acting as a support for all views.   Just as it has been said above, it is an afflicted wisdom that observes the appropriated aggregates and apprehends them to be a real I and mine.   The view of the transitory collection is an afflicted wisdom that observes the appropriated aggregates of one’s own continuum and conceives them to be a self-supporting self.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   What is the reason for calling this view the "view of the transitory collection"? It is just as it has been said in the Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path:  Regarding this, since the "transitory" is impermanent and a "collection" is manifold, the basis which it views is simply phenomena that are impermanent and manifold. However, one designates the name "view of the transitory collection" in order to indicate that "a permanent and unitary person does not exist."  It is called “the view of the transitory collection” because it views the aggregates that, due to having been appropriated by causes, are transitory or impermanent. Because there are five of them, the aggregates are called a “collection.” This is the meaning of “transitory collection.”   This view of the transitory collection is ignorance, but there are ignorances that are not a view of the transitory collection, for example, the ignorance that is an unknowing with respect to actions and their results.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Here, the Compendium of Knowledge explains the synonyms18 – "endurance" and so forth [in the citation above]: since it does not fear the erroneous meaning, it is called an "endurance"; since it seeks the erroneous object, it is called a "desire"; since it thoroughly discriminates that object, it is called an "intelligence"; since it strongly adheres to that object, it is called a "conception"; since it observes that object, it is called a "view." The view of the transitory collection has the function of acting as a support for all bad views.   The view of the transitory collection is a tolerance or endurance because it is not afraid of the erroneous meaning. It is a desire because it seeks an erroneous object. It is an intelligence because                                                         18 Note that synonyms are an enumeration of names and are not necessarily mutually inclusive.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
157 it discriminates its object. It is a conception because it strongly adheres to its object. It is a view because it observes its object. It acts as a support for all bad views.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [D6.2] View holding to an extreme (mthar lta) Regarding the view holding to an extreme, the Compendium of Knowledge says:   QUESTION: What is the view holding to an extreme?  RESPONSE: It is any endurance, desire, intelligence, conception, or view that views the five appropriated aggregates as being either permanent or annihilated. It has the function of hindering definite emergence by means of the middle path.   Just as it has been said above, it is an afflicted wisdom that observes the self that is held by the view of the transitory collection and apprehends it to be either permanent or annihilated. How does it hold [that self] to be either permanent or annihilated? The Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path says:   The view holding to an extreme is an afflicted wisdom which, observing the self that is held by the view of the transitory collection, views it as either being permanent in the sense of being unchanging, or being annihilated in the sense of not transmigrating to a future life after this life.   Therefore, since this bad view causes one to fall to the extremes of permanence and annihilation, it is the principal obstacle to progressing on the middle path free from these extremes.   There is a view holding to the extreme of permanence or eternalism and a view holding to the extreme of annihilation or nihilism. The view holding to the extreme of permanence observes the self and holds it to be permanent and unchanging. The view holding to the extreme of annihilation observes the self and holds it to come to an end at death.   STUDENT: The view of the transitory collection was said to be “an afflicted wisdom that observes the appropriated aggregates and apprehends them to be a real I and mine,” but how can an afflicted wisdom be an ignorance? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: This is not contradictory because the view of the transitory collection is the type of ignorance that is obscuration with respect to suchness.  STUDENT: Are all five views included in ignorance? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Most likely.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [D6.3] Conception of a [bad] view as supreme (lta ba mchog 'dzin) Regarding the conception of a [bad] view as supreme, the Compendium of Knowledge says:   QUESTION: What is the conception of a [bad] view as supreme?  RESPONSE: It i s any endurance,  desi re,  i nt el l i gence,  concept i on,  or vi ew whi ch views:  • views and  • the locus of the views, that is, the five appropriated aggregates,  to be supreme, to be chief, to be superior, and to be excellent. It has the function of acting as a support for strongly adhering to bad views.  Just as it has been said above, it is an afflicted wisdom that observes other bad views and the aggregates, the locus in dependence on which they arise, and holds them to be supreme. Here, the synonyms – "supreme" and so forth [in the citation above] – are explained as follows:  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
158 since it is conceited about them being marvelous, it holds them to be supreme,  since it holds that there is none more outstanding than them, it holds them to be chief,  it holds them to be superior to others, and  since it holds that there is none equal to them, it holds them to be excellent. In this context the Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path says:   The conception of a [bad] view as supreme is an afflicted wisdom that observes:  • any of the three – the view of the transitory collection, the conception of a [bad] view as supreme, or wrong view – and • the aggregates of the viewer in dependence on which they [i.e., the above three views] arise,  and holds them to be supreme.  Its function is specified as acting as a support for strongly adhering to wrong views, because it deposits the imprints for not separating from bad views in this life and future lives.   The view holding a bad view as supreme is an afflicted wisdom that observes any of the three – the view of the transitory collection, the view holding a bad view as supreme, or a wrong view – and the aggregates of the viewer in dependence on which these three views arise, and holds them to be supreme.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [D6.4] Conception of [bad] ethics and modes of conduct as supreme (tshul khrims dang brtul zhugs mchog 'dzin)  Regarding the conception of [bad] ethics and modes of conduct as supreme, the Compendium of Knowledge says:   QUESTION: What is the conception of [bad] ethics and modes of conduct as supreme?  RESPONSE: It is any endurance, desire, intelligence, conception, or view that views: • [bad] ethics and modes of conduct and  • the locus of the [bad] ethics and modes of conduct, that is, the five appropriated aggregates, to be purifying, liberating, and delivering. It has the function of acting as a support for fruitless fatigue.  Just as it has been said above, it is an afflicted wisdom that observes: [bad] ethics motivated by bad views,  [bad] m o d e s  o f  c o n d u c t  t h a t  p r e s c r i b e  p h y s i c a l  a n d  m e n t a l  a c t i v i t i e s  s u c h  a s  w e a r i n g  a n i m a l  skins, and  the abode of [bad] ethics and modes of conduct – the five appropriated aggregates –  to be purifying and liberating.  Here, the Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path says:   The conception of [bad] ethics and modes of conduct as supreme is an afflicted wisdom that observes:  • [bad] ethics which [supposedly] abandon immorality,  • [bad] modes of conduct which prescribe attire, behavior, physical and verbal activities, and  • the aggregates of the viewer in dependence on which they [i.e., the above two – bad ethics and modes of conduct] arise,  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
159 and views them as purifying misdeeds, liberating one from afflictions, and definitely delivering one from cyclic existence.   It is simply to be understood that its function is "[acting as a support for] fruitless fatigue."   This view holds bad morality or bad modes of conduct to be good morality or good modes of conduct. An example of holding bad morality to be good morality is the practice of animal sacrifice in which one kills an animal and makes an offering of its blood.   Here “conduct” refers to leaving behind normal, worldly behavior and taking on a special kind of behavior. An example of such conduct is someone who, with limited clairvoyance, sees that he was a pig in his previous life and, having come to the conclusion that if he is to be reborn again as a human being he must once again act as a pig, behaves like a pig. Such conduct is a bad mode of conduct.   Also, I saw a documentary on Italian television some time ago about a temple in India overrun with rats where the rats are worshipped and taken care of. This is done because it is taught that in order to be reborn as one of those rats there is the need of merit equal to that of seven human lives. This is an example of both a view holding bad morality as supreme and a view holding a bad mode of conduct as supreme.   In Tibet, prior to the advent of Buddhism, the Bon tradition included the practice of animal sacrifice but this was gradually eliminated by Buddhism. In fact nowadays the Bon tradition is similar in many ways to Buddhism, but even nowadays in Nepal there still exists the practice of sacrificing animals. There is an annual religious festival in which many sacrifices are made, where the poor who do not own an animal break an egg as a symbolic act of killing.  STUDENT: W h a t  w o u l d  a n  e x a m p l e  b e  o f  t h i s  v i e w  i n  t h e  W e s t e r n  w o r l d ?  I t  s e e m s  t h a t  i t  i s  related to religion. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I t  s e e m s  t o  b e  that way. Since Westerners are interested in many different types of religion, it is important to examine a religion before adhering to it. In India there are many practitioners with tantric powers so, before taking such a person as one’s teacher, one should carefully examine him. I have seen Westerners who initially followed such teachers but when they later on turned away from them, they experienced a lot of mental unhappiness and difficulties.   STUDENT: The quote from the Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path with respect to the last two views say that they observe “the aggregates of the viewer in dependence on which they arise” in addition to their respective observed objects. What does that mean? The conception of a [bad] view as supreme is an afflicted wisdom which observes:  • any of the three – the view of the transitory collection, the conception of a [bad] view as supreme, or wrong view – and • the aggregates of the viewer in dependence on which they [i.e., the above three views] arise,  and holds them to be supreme.  The conception of [bad] ethics and modes of conduct as supreme is an afflicted wisdom which observes:  • [bad] ethics which [supposedly] abandon immorality,  • [bad] modes of conduct which prescribe attire, behavior, physical and verbal activities and  • the aggregates of the viewer in dependence on which they [i.e., the above two – bad ethics and modes of conduct] arise,  and views them as purifying misdeeds, liberating one from afflictions, and definitely delivering one from cyclic existence.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The view holding a bad view as supreme observes any of the three views – the view of the transitory collection, the view holding a bad view as supreme, or a wrong view – or it observes the aggregates of the viewer in dependence on which the three views arise. So it would seem that there are four views holding a bad view as supreme. STUDENT: In the case of the view holding bad ethics and modes of conduct as supreme, the text says: “It is any endurance, desire, intelligence, conception, or view, which views [bad] ethics and 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
160 modes of conduct and the locus of the [bad] ethics and modes of conduct, that is, the five appropriated aggregates, to be purifying, liberating, and delivering.” What does that mean? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The locus of this bad ethics and modes of conduct is the aggregates. For example, the view that bathing in the Ganges River is a cause of liberation is related to the body or form aggregate. It is a view that holds that such behavior purifies misdeeds, liberates from the afflictions, and definitely delivers one from cyclic existence. This is explained by the quote from the Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path t h a t  s a y s :  “ T h e  c o n c e p t i o n  o f  [ b a d ]  e t h i c s  a n d  modes of conduct as supreme is an afflicted wisdom which observes [bad] ethics which [supposedly] abandon immorality, [bad] modes of conduct which prescribe attire, behavior, physical and verbal activities and the aggregates of the viewer in dependence on which they [i.e., the above two – bad ethics and modes of conduct] arise, and views them as purifying misdeeds, liberating one from afflictions, and definitely delivering one from cyclic existence.” STUDENT: How does bathing in the Ganges purify misdeeds? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is not the aggregates that purify, it is the faith of person in what he is doing that purifies.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [D6.5] Wrong view (log lta) Regarding wrong view, the Compendium of Knowledge says:   QUESTION: What is wrong view?  RESPONSE: It is any endurance, desire, intelligence, conception, or view which deprecates cause, result or functionality, and wrongly conceives existent disintegrating things. It has the functions of severing roots of virtue, holding tightly to roots of non-virtue, and acting as a support for engaging in non-virtue and not engaging in virtue.   Just as it has been said above, it is an afflicted wisdom which views the cause and result of actions, past and future lives, and so forth as non-existent. The Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path says:   Wrong view is an afflicted wisdom which:  • deprecates, stating, "Past and future lives, actions and their results, and so forth do not exist," and  • holds that Ishvara, the fundamental nature, and the like to be the cause of migrating beings.  Wrong view holds to the non-existence of past and future lives and so forth.  Wednesday morning, 12 October 2005  To return briefly to the subject of pride. Pride is the cause of holding others in contempt, therefore it is very bad. It harms oneself, not others. Pride thinks “I am the best” and, lacking consideration for others, looks down upon them. Due to pride, one is easily upset by criticism.   Wrong view is a deprecation that denies the existence of that which exists or that which is.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
161 When wrong view is divided, there are four:  (1) wrong view that deprecates causes,  (2) wrong view that deprecates results,  (3) wrong view that deprecates functionality, and  (4) wrong view that deprecates existent things.   (1) The wrong view that deprecates causes is the view that good behavior and bad behavior and so forth do not exist. (2) The wrong view that deprecates results is the view that the fruition of virtuous actions and misdeeds does not exist. (3) The wrong view that deprecates functionality is the view that one's parents, past and future worlds [i.e., rebirths], and so forth do not exist. (4) The wrong v i e w  t h a t  d e p r e c a t e s  existent things i s  t h e  v i e w  t h a t  t h e  a t t a i n m e n t  o f  f o e-destroyer and so forth do not exist.  Although in general there are many wrong views, since the wrong view that deprecates actions and their results, past and future worlds [i.e., rebirths], and so forth severs all roots of virtue, it is taught to be the most serious among all wrong views.   There are these four types of wrong view, but in reality there are many wrong views. Among them, the worst is the wrong view that deprecates actions and their results, past and future lives, and so forth because it severs all roots of virtue.  STUDENT: With respect to wrong view, is the superimposition that holds to a self of persons not a wrong view since it superimposes a self rather than deprecates the existence of something? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e r e  i s  n o  p e r v a s i o n  t h a t  w r o n g  v i e w  n e c e s s a r i l y  d e p r e c a t e s  something, although in the case of denying actions and results, past and future rebirths, and so forth it is a deprecation. Wrong view includes both deprecation and superimposition. Although actions and results and the ignorance that is an unknowing of actions and results are important to understand this is difficult because the law of cause and effect is subtle. However, for the very reason that it is difficult to understand, we need to be very careful regarding our actions.   Thus, there are six root afflictions. However, when afflicted view is divided into five, there are ten root afflictions – five non-views and five views. All ten afflictions are included in the desire realm, whereas because there is no anger in the upper realms, the form and formless realms, there are only nine afflictions there. Anger does not exist in the upper realms because there is no non-virtue there. Although there imprints of non-virtue, which are the cause for future rebirth in the lower realms, do exist in the continua of the beings of the upper realms, they are not non-virtues included in the upper realms. The ten afflictions are explained in detail in the Grounds and Paths (sa lam). Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [E] The twenty secondary afflictions (nye nyon) The twenty secondary afflictions are:  (1) belligerence (2) resentment (3) concealment  (4) spite  (5) jealousy  (6) miserliness (7) deceit  (8) dissimulation (9) haughtiness  (10) harmfulness (11) non-shame  (12) non-embarrassment  (13) lethargy  (14) excitement  (15) non-faith  (16) laziness  (17) non-conscientiousness  (18) forgetfulness  (19) non-introspection  (20) distraction  [E1] Belligerence (khro ba) Regarding belligerence, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is belligerence?  RESPONSE: It is a malice when the cause of harm abides nearby, and is involved with anger. It has the function of acting as a support for taking up weapons, punishing, and so forth, and preparing to injure others.   Just as it has been said above, it is a malice that wishes to harm, by striking and so forth, when any of the nine bases of malice abides nearby. The nine bases of malice are: (1-3) the thoughts that this person has harmed me, is harming me, and will harm me;  (4-6) the thoughts that this person has harmed my friend, is harming my friend, and will harm my friend; (7-9) the thoughts that this person has benefited my enemy, is benefiting my enemy, and will benefit my enemy.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
163 These nine are explained to be "the nine bases of malice." The scriptural source for this is just as in a previous citation. [See the explanation on {D2} Anger.]  Belligerence is a malice that wishes to inflict harm by striking the object with a stick and so forth. It follows anger and manifests itself in physical expressions of anger, gestures, and so forth. (ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: Khro ba could also be translated as hostility or aggression.)   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [Discussion on the pair, anger and belligerence] QUALM: Since anger occurs in the context of root afflictions and belligerence in the context of secondary afflictions, what is the difference between anger and belligerence? RESPONSE: Perhaps anger is an intolerant malicious mind occurring when the three objects dawn as objects of observation, whereas belligerence is an extremely disturbed state of mind which, upon a great intensifying of anger, wishes to strike physically when the cause of anger abides nearby.  [Asanga's] Compendium of Knowledge refers to: "malice towards the three phenomena" in the context of explaining anger, and  "when the cause of harm abides nearby" and "taking up weapons and so forth" and so forth in the context of explaining belligerence.   [Vasubandhu's] A Discussion of the Five Aggregates explains:  "malice towards sentient beings" in the context of anger and  "abides in causing immediate harm" in the context of explaining the secondary affliction belligerence.  Therefore, having contemplated the presentations in these texts of Asanga and his brother [Vasubandhu], you may wonder whether they are just as they have been explained above. However, since these are very difficult to realize, the intelligent should further analyze them in detail. It is simply to be understood that anger acts as a support for harming others.   It is difficult to distinguish the secondary affliction belligerence from the root affliction anger. Anger is a malicious mind that observes any of the three sources of anger – s e n t i e n t  b e i n g s ,  suffering, or the sources of suffering, whereas belligerence is the wish to physically harm someone. It involves a strengthening or increase of anger.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [E2] Resentment (khon du 'dzin pa) Regarding resentment, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is resentment?  RESPONSE: It is a refusal to let go of thoughts of harm following that [i.e., belligerence] and is involved with anger. It has the function of acting as a support for intolerance.   Just as it has been said above, it is a mind that, refusing to let go of a continuum of anger, wishes to harm or retaliate. It is simply to be understood that resentment acts as a support for intolerance.   In regard to these two mental factors, the Precious Garland (stanza 5.3ab) explains:   Belligerence is a disturbance of mind;  Tagging along behind that is resentment. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
164  Resentment follows upon anger, it holds to the thoughts of harm and does not let them go. It is included in anger, as is belligerence. Resentment continues to think about the person or thing that harmed one and is determined to vindicate that harm.  (ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: Resentment is holding a grudge or thoughts of revenge.)  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [E3] Concealment ('chab pa) Regarding concealment, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is concealment?  RESPONSE: It is a hiding of misdeeds when one is justly accused and is involved with ignorance. It has the function of acting as a support for not abiding in contact with regret.   Just as it has been said above, it is an awareness that wishes to hide faults through the force of ignorance when another person, such as a spiritual guide, benignly points out one's faults. Refusing to accept one's own faults, this concealment causes even small faults to greatly increase. It serves as a cause of not abiding in contact with regret [for one's faults] and wi t h happiness, and has the function of impelling one into lower rebirths in future lives.   Concealment is the hiding of one’s faults. In other words, when someone points out one’s faults, one denies their existence. Due to refusing to accept one’s own faults, even small faults increase greatly. To deny that one has faults and to hide them indicates that one is not willing to work on eliminating them. Concealing one’s faults acts as a basis for not recognizing how many faults one actually has. And even though one purifies negativities by applying the four opponent powers, one will not be able to purify faults that one does not admit to having. For the ordained this attitude is especially bad because if they conceal their faults and do not admit them it will not be possible to purify them even by performing Sojong (the bimonthly purification ceremony). In the past it was a common practice that before each Sojong the monks and nuns would meet to discuss and point out each others’ faults, whereby they would become more aware of their faults so that they could confess them more effectively. However, if that were done nowadays it would probably only end up in arguments and fighting! Of course, it is not easy for monks and nuns to declare their faults, but it is important for them not to have this mind of concealment and to at least openly declare their faults to another monk or nun. In this way, the fault is brought to mind and then confessed during Sojong. If a monk or nun has not created a fault, he or she can remain silent when any degeneration of the vows is confessed. If, on the other hand, they have committed a downfall, they should confess it. If one knows that one has committed a fault but does not confess it, it is like lying. One can show the recognition of a fault even just by bowing or nodding one’s head in acknowledgement as one recites the ceremony.   STUDENT: The Western fully-ordained nuns in India would meet before Sojong and discuss the vows and admit to having broken those that they had broken.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is very, very good. According to the Vinaya one should first ask the other sangha member for permission to ask about his or her faults. Then, only if this permission is granted, should one question the other person. These days we do not do that, but it should be born in mind and when doing Sojong we can indicate by physical gestures that we have created a particular fault.  Concealment is never good because as long as we conceal them we will not be able to purify our faults, whereas until we attain the state of foe-destroyer we will continue to create faults that need to be purified. However, the Vinaya accords with the Vaibhashika tenets and with respect to it there is a lot of debate. For example, according to Vinaya, faults that have been concealed cannot be purified, but then what happens later on when one comes to regret having hidden these faults? Does it follow that one can never purify the faults that were originally concealed?  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
165 STUDENT: I s  i t  e n o u g h  t o  d e v e l o p  a  m i n d  o f  regret for one’s faults without actually verbally declaring them during Sojong? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: In the past monks and nuns did verbally express their faults to each other, but nowadays this is no longer done. It is not clear whether a mind of regret alone is sufficient to purify faults completely, but the most important thing is to not have the attitude of hiding them. Because we often have a mind of concealment due to being embarrassed to publicly express our faults, it is good to develop the habit of expressing them even to one other person, such as a good friend. However, even that is not easy. In fact it is very difficult to confess to others because it is difficult for us to accept others’ criticism.  STUDENT: In order to confess one’s faults, in general one needs to know what they are. However, while we do not normally kill and so forth, there is the danger that we may come to think that everything we do and say is wrong. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: We should not have such worries regarding our practice, study, and meditation. Since it is only ourselves who give us this anxiety, we should avoid doing that. By thinking again and again about some small mistake that we have committed, we can build up anxiety and tension. Since we naturally do not want suffering, why create additional suffering for ourselves? We already have enough. If we do not know whether what we have done is good or bad, we can leave it aside for the time being without confessing it. We should not worry about it as later on it will become clear whether it was bad or no. On the other hand, when we clearly know that something is wrong, we should confess and purify it. However, we should avoid getting into a state of anxiety as this will only stop us from thinking clearly and intelligently. Therefore, we should confess what we know to be wrong and determine to refrain from doing it again. On the other hand, if we are not sure that something is wrong, we should not turn it over and over again in our mind until we end up in a state of anxiety, but instead we should just set it aside for the time being.   One of the four opponent powers is the mind of restraint which is a determination or resolution to not commit a certain fault again. However, we need to be careful that his promise does not become a lie. For example, if having had a lifetime habit of telling lies, one day we come to regret this and immediately promise never to tell a lie again, but then the very next day lie again, we will have lied twice. Kyabje Ling Rinpoche often made the point t h a t  w e  m u s t  b e  c a r e f u l  a b o u t  pledging never to do a certain fault again. He said that it is better to make a short-term promise such as “Today I will not tell a lie” and then for the twenty-four hours put effort into not telling a lie. In this way, gradually one will be able to extend the period up to a week, a month, and so forth. This is a better way of promising to refrain from non-virtue rather than saying that one will never do a particular negative action again. If even one day is too long, one can make a promise to not do it for one hour! For these reasons, among the four opponent powers, the most difficult one to apply is the promise not to engage in the negative action again.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [E4] Spite ('tshig pa) Regarding spite, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is spite?  RESPONSE: It is a malicious mind that is preceded by belligerence and resentment, and is involved with anger. It has the function of acting as a support for rough violent speech, to increase that which is non-meritorious, and not abiding in contact [with happiness].   Just as it has been said above, it is an awareness which, for instance, lacking thoughts of regret and admission when another points out one’s faults, wishes to utter harsh speech out of hateful thoughts driven by belligerence and resentment. Regarding this, A Discussion of the Five Aggregates says:  …hurting with rough words.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
166 Explaining it as a refusal to let go due to adhering to misdeeds, the Precious Garland (5.3d) says:  Spite is adherence to misdeeds.  However, [Vasubandhu's] Explanation of the 'Treasury of Knowledge' (Abhidharmakosha-bhashya) [i.e., the self-commentary to Treasury of Knowledge] explains spite to be a tight holding to misdeeds, and that it arises from the conception of a [bad] view as supreme. Due to it, one engages in many faulty actions, such as harsh speech, and by producing many non-meritorious actions, one will not abide in contact with happiness in this life, and unpleasant fruition will be produced in future lives.  The word ‘tshig pa in Tibetan is used interchangeably with anger, but in the scriptures it has its own specific definition.   First the anger that is a root affliction arises. From anger comes belligerence. From belligerence comes resentment. From belligerence and resentment comes spite – the wish to say something that is spiteful and hurtful. Belligerence, resentment, and spite are all included in anger.   Hurtful words are not necessarily an indication of spite because they can even be said as a joke. Harsh words by definition are words that are expressed in order to hurt someone’s feelings. However, not everyone is hurt by the same words, some people might be hurt, while others are not. Harsh words can be true or untrue. For example, if we call someone “blind,” it could be true but harsh and hurtful or it could be untrue but harsh and hurtful. In either case there is no guarantee that the person to whom they are said will be hurt. For example, there was a monk in my monastery who everyone referred to as “Crazy,” but being called this only made him laugh! Likewise, there is a Tibetan-American Khampa who likes to be called “Yak” and even introduces himself in that way. However, calling people by the name of an animal, whether malicious or not, creates non-virtue. There is story recounted about the Buddha , one day when walking in the forest with his entourage of 500 monks, was offered a bowl of honey by a monkey. The Buddha accepted the offering and, in his joy, the monkey leaped from tree to tree until he suddenly fell to the ground and died. When the monks asked the Buddha what karma had been the cause of this, he told them that in a past life the monkey had been a monk who was unable to swim and that, having gone bathe in a river with other monks, he told them, without any malice, that they were like monkeys due to the way in which they jumped from rock to rock. Due to this, he created the karma to be reborn as a monkey 500 times, and in the very last of those lives was the monkey who had offered the Buddha the bowl of honey. This story shows that we need to be very careful what we say to others and what we call them. We Tibetans often call other people “Beggar,” but we need to be careful about such habits.  STUDENT: Often parents call their children by animal names, “My little rabbit” and so forth, and lovers also call each other by such pet names. Is this the cause for lower rebirth? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: If we think about this well, we will understand that it is not a good thing to do.   Wednesday afternoon, 12 October 2005  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [E5] Jealousy (phrag dog) Regarding jealousy, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is jealousy?  RESPONSE: It is a deep disturbance of mind that cannot tolerate another's marvelous attributes due to excessive attachment to gain and honor, and is involved with hatred. It has the function of [causing] mental unhappiness and of not abiding in contact [with happiness].   
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
167 Just as it has been said above, it is a deep disturbance of mind that cannot bear another's success due to attachment to gain and honor. It produces serious undesirable [consequences] in both this and future worlds [i.e., rebirths]; in this life there will be mental unhappiness, and in future lives one will be impelled into bad migrations.   Jealousy mainly observes those people whom we do not like; it is unable to bear seeing their wealth, possessions, renown, reputation, and so forth. Jealousy is a strange thing, for example, when a woman talks to another man she expects her husband to be jealous and, if he is not, she becomes upset due to thinking that it is a sign that he does not love her. Likewise, when a man talks to another woman, he expects his wife to be jealous as otherwise it would mean that she does not care. However, in order to live together harmoniously, two people need to trust each other because otherwise suspicion and jealousy will constantly arise based on thinking that the other person is interested in someone else. Without trust, there will be many difficulties in a relationship. If one sees one’s partner talking to a person of the opposite sex and wonders what is going on, it indicates that one lacks trust in him or her.   Jealousy can even be directed at a good friend who has obtained wealth, possessions, and so forth. It can bring about the end of a friendship. If one is jealous of another person’s possessions and so forth, whether he is a friend or not, in the end it only harms oneself.  STUDENT: Each of the secondary afflictions is related to a root affliction. Here this is translated as “involved with,” but is it not the case that a secondary affliction is caused by a root affliction? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Belligerence, resentment, and spite are involved with anger, meaning both that they arise from a nge r  a nd t ha t  t he y are like a nge r .  The y a r e  l i ke  t he  of f s pr i ng of  a nge r .  (ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: Char gtogs pa could also be translated as “belong to the category of” rather than “involved with.”)  STUDENT: In Italian the word “jealousy” has the meaning of being afraid of losing what one has or not wanting to share what one has. Thus, one does not want to lose or share one’s wife or husband. It comes from attachment and involves three people. Envy, on the other hand, is wanting to have what one does not have and involves only two people. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: J e a l o u s y  c a n  i n v o l v e  a  f e a r  o f  l o s i n g  o n e ’ s  w i f e  o r  h u s b a n d  b u t  i t  mainly is an inability to bear the thought of someone else having her or him. Jealousy means that one does not want others to have something.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [E6] Miserliness (ser sna) Regarding miserliness, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is miserliness?  RESPONSE: It is a thorough holding of the mind to possessions due to excessive attachment to gain and honor, and is involved with attachment. It has the function of acting as a support for the non-diminishment of possessions.  Just as it has been said above, it is a tight holding that is unable to let go of possessions through the force of attachment to gain and honor. It produces serious undesirable [consequences] in both this life and future lives.   The Moon Lamp Sutra (Candrapadipasutra) says:   This body which is rotting away,  As well as one's life-force waver helplessly. The childish are attached to Dreamlike illusions,  Thus, committing extremely evil actions.  They come under the power of misdeeds. The unwise depart, mounted on the Lord of Death and  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
168 Proceed to hell with unendurable minds.   Also, [Jnanagarbha's] Dharanis That Accomplish Infinite Doors (Anantamikhanirharadharini-vyakhyanakarika) says:   Sentient beings who get into a fight Hold tightly to the basis of the quarrel.  Thus if they were to abandon that for which they have craving, [And also] abandon craving, they will attain a dharani.   There are infinite such teachings.   Miserliness is a tight holding that is unable to let something go. While it mainly arises with respect to one’s possessions, it is also possible to be miserly with one’s knowledge. For example, one does not share all of one’s knowledge regarding something with others but only a part of it out of fear that one’s student will become as learned as oneself. Thus, if a teacher who is skilled in carpentry, painting, sewing, and so forth does not teach his students everything he knows out of fear of the students becoming better than him, he is being miserly. Someone who is teaching the Dharma should never be miserly with the teachings. We should be careful not to act like this.   To not be miserly does not mean that we should not take care of our possessions, for example, to let plates and so forth fall to the floor and break. It is probably true that we all have some degree of miserliness but, however small or great it is, it is worthwhile to work on reducing it by practicing generosity. We can start by giving away something small and then gradually increase our ability to give.   If we do not have jealousy it may seem like we have lost something with respect to our partner and so forth, but actually we only become happier by giving it up. Although it may appear that if we give up jealousy with respect to someone we do not like, his possessions will decrease, this does not happen. Jealousy only makes problems for ourselves. Our jealousy does not hurt others, it only hurts us.   When we physically give something to someone, we should always check our state of mind. for example, we should not give and then regret it later on, nor should we give so that others will think that we are generous, nor give with the thought of getting a reward in return. If one gives without these attitudes it is generosity, otherwise it is not because the mind is contaminated. Therefore, when you give, have a look at your mind.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [E7] Deceit (sgyu) Regarding deceit, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is deceit? RESPONSE: It is a display of what is not [one's] good quality due to excessive attachment to gain and honor, and is involved with attachment and ignorance. It has the function of acting as a support for wrong livelihood.   Just as it has been said above, it is a display of good qualities one does not actually possess, due to excessive attachment to gain and honor, as in the case of pretending to be peaceful and subdued with the thought to deceive others, even though one's own mind is far from being subdued. In this vein, A Discussion of the Five Aggregates says:  …displaying the unreal to deceive others.  The Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path explains it similarly.   Deceit is to pretend to have a quality that one lacks. It is mainly related to attachment. Deceit is, for example, while one’s mind is actually untamed and agitated, to pretend to be calm and subdued in order to deceive others. One does this motivated by a wish to deceive them.  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
169  While we do not spontaneously become a Dharma practitioner as this happens over the course of years, our teachers often advise us to assume the appearance of a Dharma p r a c t i t i o n e r  a s  a  method to actually becoming one. This is correct behavior, even though at the beginning it is not genuine, because one does not do it in order to deceive others, but in order to become an actual practitioner.   STUDENT: Sometimes it can seem that we Westerners are just pretending to practice the Dharma. Our compassion, for example, can be forced and artificial and this may be perceived by others who then criticize us for our false behavior. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Do not worry about what other people say! You yourself know that what you are doing is genuine and that it will eventually lead to the development of real compassion. By familiarizing ourselves with compassion, one day our compassion will become non-artificial. It is impossible to immediately and spontaneously become compassionate.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:   Deceit is specified as acting as a support for wrong livelihood, because apart from being a method to practice wrong livelihood, a pretentious attitude toward others is worthless. Wrong livelihood is of five types: (1) pretension  (2) flattery (3) hinting (4) extortion (5) seeking gain from gain  (1) Pretension i s ,  a s  e x p l a i n e d  a b o v e ,  a n  e x t e r n a l  p r e t e n s e  t o w a r d  o t h e r s ,  f e i g n i n g  g o o d  qualities one does not have.  (2) Flattery is to speak smooth words in conformity with the minds of others for the sake of gain and honor. (3) Hinting i s  t o  f i r s t  f l a t t e r  o t h e r s  a n d  t h e n  p r a i s e  t h e i r  p o s s e s s i o n s  o u t  o f  a n  a t t a c h me n t  t o  gain.  (4) Extortion is to speak about the faults of great miserliness and the like for the sake of gain.  (5) Seeking gain from gain is to seek gain by praising in the presence of another what one has previously obtained, saying, "Previously so-and-so gave me such-and-such."  In brief, without guarding the stipulated boundaries of the trainings, even wandering to ask for alms in a village out of attachment to gain and honor is taught to be wrong livelihood. Therefore, if you do not wish to be defiled by wrong livelihood, do not seek to ingratiate yourself with others, and protect without duplicity the stipulated boundaries of the Vinaya in a place of solitude.   This discussion of wrong livelihood is mainly set out for monks and nuns but it can also be applied to lay people. “Wrong livelihood” refers to a wrong way of obtaining things. There is a difference between giving that is motivated by compassion and the wish to help others and giving offerings to the sangha. This is because one makes an offering to the sangha out of faith in and devotion to them. For this reason, monks and nuns have to be careful not to transform such offerings into a means of wrong livelihood.  Pretension is to pretend to have a quality that one does not have in order to gain something. For example, a monk or nun might show that he or she has a certain quality or knowledge in order to gain something from his or her sponsor. For example, although one normally sleeps until nine or ten in the morning, when staying in one’s benefactor’s house one gets up at three or four in the morning and sits in meditation motivated by the thought to obtain food and so forth. This is wrong livelihood or a wrong way of obtaining the necessities of life.   However, there are exceptions to such behavior being considered deceit or pretension. For example, there are people who judge a religion by the people who practice it, whereby if they see someone who is not behaving well they think that the religion itself is not good. In this case it can be necessary to put on a show in order to avoid such people generating a negative mind. Thus, 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
170 when a monk or nun is staying in someone’s house, it may actually be beneficial to put on a good appearance for the sake of the Dharma and therefore to get up early, sit in meditation, and so forth. In this case, one does not do this for one’s own sake, but does so for the sake of the Dharma. If a monk or nun acts well and speaks well, it will leave good imprints on others’ minds. On the other hand, if a monk or nun acts badly it may lead others to abandon the Dharma, therefore this is to be avoided.  STUDENT: To return to the subject of spontaneous compassion, I was shocked to here that none of us here would spontaneously show compassion as I have seen many examples of Westerners who do show such compassion. So perhaps I do not understand the meaning of “spontaneous compassion.” GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is not that we do not have compassion; for example, when there is a natural disaster many people spontaneously reach out to help. But, what we do not have is great compassion, that is, compassion for everyone. We, for example, do not have compassion for people who we do not like, nor for people who are rich, nor for people who seem to have everything. This kind of compassion needs to be developed by training. It does not arise spontaneously.  STUDENT: Related to the story about the monkey who rejoiced in having made an offering to the Buddha, I was surprised to hear that an animal can rejoice. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Pe r haps  i t  i s  be t t e r  t o s ay t hat  he  was  happy t hat  t hat  t he  Buddha accepted his offering, rather than that he rejoiced in it in the sense of rejoicing in a virtuous action. The monkey was happy because the Buddha accepted the honey and offered it to the monks. We can see, for example, that dogs become happy and wag their tail when they are praised, and are sad and put their tail between their legs when they are scolded.  STUDENT: I find it reasonable to think that we should refrain from bad behavior in front of others, but I am not so sure that it is good to feign having qualities such as compassion because it is often obvious that the person is pretending and this just irritates others. In addition, it can also happen that the person himself ends up believing that he is compassionate and so forth although he does not.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: T h e r e  i s  n o  o n e  w h o  at the beginning of practice can produce a natural, non-artificial compassion, so initially it must be fabricated or created by effort. Even though that is the case, it is done with the motivation to gradually develop this contrived compassion until it becomes natural and genuine. Therefore, at the beginning our compassion is necessarily erroneous and not pure because our minds have been dominated since beginningless time by the afflictions. However, by slowly reducing our faults, it can develop into pure compassion. It would be wrong to pretend to have compassion if one does not have it.  ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: It is also wrong to use compassion as a means of getting someone to do something by saying “You should have compassion for all sentient beings.”  Flattery is to speak pleasant words to make the other person feel good with the thought to receive something in return. Without such a motivation, but instead with a good motivation, it is good to speak nicely and pleasantly to other.  Hinting is, for example, to say to one’s sponsor “That thing that you gave me last year was very useful” with the thought to receive something in return. However, without that motivation, it is important to thank someone for what one has been given. In other words, it is good to show gratitude with the right motivation.   Extortion (ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: Perhaps this is not the best translation) is, for example, with the wish to receive more, to say to someone who has recently given something small and trivial to another person, “You a r e  s o  v e r y  k i n d  a n d  g e n e r o u s ”  i n  o r d e r  t o  r e c e i v e  a  b i g g e r  offering. However, in the case that one is actually glad to receive something, it is very good to praise the other person for his generosity.   Thursday morning, 13 October 2005  The way in which “seeking gain from gain” is defined here in the Necklace seems to be very similar to hinting in that one praises what one has received in the past in order to receive something from the listener. On the other hand, Lama Tsongkhapa’s Great Exposition of the Stages of the Path says that seeking gain from gain i s  t o  g i v e  s o m e t h i n g  s m a l l  t o  s o m e o n e  m o t i v a t e d  b y  t h e  w i s h  t o  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
171 receive something bigger in return, whereas to say “Someone gave me such-and-such in the past” with the hope of receiving something similar from another person is hinting. Therefore, perhaps seeking gain from gain can be understood in either of two ways: (1) to say to one person “Such-and-such person gave me such-and-such in the past and it was very useful” in the hope of gaining something from one’s listener, or (2) to give something small to someone in order to gain something bigger from him in return.  Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [E8] Dissimulation (g.yo) Regarding dissimulation, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is dissimulation?  RESPONSE: It hides faults due to excessive attachment to gain and honor, and is involved with attachment and ignorance. It has the function of hindering the acquisition of perfect instructions.   Just as it has been said above, it is an awareness that wishes to hide one's faults, not revealing them to others through the force of attachment to gain and honor. Nowadays, hiding our faults, we act smart and clever if we can get away with it. But going by the yardstick of Dharma, we have deceived ourselves. Regarding this, Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds says (stanza 5.31-32ab):  "I am constantly dwelling in the presence Of all the buddhas and bodhisattvas  Who are always endowed  With unobscured vision."  By thinking in this way,  I shall likewise develop a sense of shame, respect, and fear.   Dissimulation is similar to concealment, however dissimulation is more clever or more cunning in that due to it one cleverly covers one’s faults up, whereas in the case of concealment one denies that one has faults when they are pointed out. In the case of dissimulation one purposely avoids revealing one’s faults to others, whereby, when someone suspects that one has a particular fault, one immediately and cleverly covers it up. While the person who does this may be considered by others to be clever, in terms of the Dharma he is being very fool ish in that he is onl y deceiving himself.   Although we do not like to reveal our faults to others and do not like to have them pointed out, it should not be the case that we are happy when we manage to hide our faults, but rather that we are happy when we do not have faults. In other words, if we are sincerely working to eliminate our faults, we should be happy with our success in doing this rather than be happy with our success in hiding them from others. Hence, if real happiness is to be free from faults, we should be happy to see them and to be able to strive to rid ourselves of them. If it makes us happy to not have faults, then we should be happy to have the opportunity to make our faults non-existent. Consequently, if we want to eliminate our faults, we need first of all to recognize them. Without recognizing our faults, we will not be able to eliminate them.   It is, of course, difficult to be honest about our faults. Dissimulation is dishonest because it hides faults that actually exist. While we do not like other people to point out our f a ul t s ,  we  a r e  v e r y  quick to point out their f a u l t s .  T h e r e  i s  t h e  e x p r e s s i o n  “ T h o s e  w h o  h a v e  a f f e c t i o n  f o r  u s  d o  n o t  speak sweetly,” meaning that if someone truly like us and compassion for us, he will frankly point out our faults by saying, for example, “You drink too much,” “You smoke too much,” and so forth. This i s  t h e  me a s u r e  o f  a  t r u e  f r i e n d .  We ,  o n  t h e  o t h e r  h a n d ,  tend to like when others say to us, “Leave aside your studies and let’s go out and relax.” Such smiling people, however, are false friends, whereas it is important for us to have true friends. However, if one has a strong mind that is not adversely influenced by such people, one can associate with them in order to benefit them. 
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
172 On the other hand, if one does not have a strong mind, one risks being adversely influenced by them.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [E9] Haughtiness (rgyags pa) Regarding haughtiness, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is haughtiness?  RESPONSE: It is a joy and mental happiness upon seeing one's own good health, youth, signs of long life, or contaminated success, and is involved with attachment. It has the function of acting as a support for all [root] afflictions and secondary afflictions.   Just as it has been said above, it is a puffing up of the mind due to joy and mental happiness upon observing one's own good health, prosperity, and so forth. Since it produces all afflictions, it acts as the root of non-conscientiousness. The Exhortation to the Exceptional Resolve says:   This haughtiness is the root of all non-conscientiousness.  Do not deride an inferior fully-ordained monk; You will not attain liberation in an eon.  This is the sequence in this teaching.   The Letter to a Friend (stanza 12cd) also says:   Look upon haughtiness on account of youth, or greatness of  Lineage, physique, learning, or power, as an enemy.   Haughtiness has arisen if one feels great and free from problems, sickness, worries, and so forth. Due to being full of oneself, one becomes non-conscientious. (ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: Perhaps this word could be translated as “vanity” or “arrogance.”) Haughtiness tends to arise in those who are young and fit who feel strong and think that they do not need or depend on anyone or anything.  STUDENT: The word “haughtiness” seems to fit the description in the citation above that says “Do not deride an inferior fully-ordained monk.” GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: In this case haughtiness seems to be very similar to pride.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [E10] Harmfulness (rnam par 'tshe ba) Regarding harmfulness, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is harmfulness?  RESPONSE: It is involved with anger. It is non-benevolence, non-compassion, and non-mercy. It has the function of harming others.   Just as it has been said above, it is a wish, bereft of benevolence, to harm sentient beings. Here the synonyms – n o n-benevolent and so forth [in the citation above] – a r e  e x p l a i n e d  i n  t h e  commentaries as follows: since it is a wish to harm by oneself, it is non-benevolence,  since it is a wish to engage others in harming, it is non-compassion, and  since it is a rejoicing upon seeing or hearing others acting [to harm], it is non-mercy. It is simply to be understood that its function [is as stated above].  
Basic Program: Commentary to Mind and Cognition by Geshe Tenzin Tenphel – 10-14 October 2005 (05)  
173 Harmfulness is the opposite to the non-harmfulness that is included in the eleven virtuous mental factors. The Compendium of Knowledge s ays that it is “non-benevolence,” “non-compassion,” and “non-mercy.” (ENGLISH TRANSLATOR: In other words, it is ruthlessness or heartlessness or hard-heartedness.) This particular state of mind is the opposite to non-harmfulness which thinks “If I do such-and-such to him it will hurt him just as if it were done to me it would hurt me. Therefore, I will not do it.” Harmfulness, on the other hand, understands that something will harm the other person and deliberately wishes him harm.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [E11] Non-shame (ngo tsha med pa) Regarding non-shame, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is non-shame?  RESPONSE: It is a non-avoidance of misdeeds on account of oneself and is involved with attachment, hatred, and ignorance. It has the function of assisting all [root] afflictions and secondary afflictions.   Just as it has been said above, it is an awareness that does not avoid misdeeds on account of either oneself or the Dharma. For example, if a fully-ordained monk encounters an intoxicant and avoids a misdeed on account of himself, thinking, "Drinking this intoxicant is not something I should do," then this is shame. The opposite of this is non-shame.   Non-shame is the opposite to the shame that is included in the eleven virtuous mental factors. Shame avoids doing something negative out of consideration for oneself, whereas non-shame is a lack of care with respect to engaging in misdeeds.   Necklace for Those of Clear Awareness says:  [E12] Non-embarrassment (khrel med pa)  Regarding non-embarrassment, the Compendium of Knowledge says:  QUESTION: What is non-embarrassment?  RESPONSE: It is a non-avoidance of misdeeds on account of others and is involved with attachment, hatred and ignorance. It has the function of assisting all [root] afflictions and secondary afflictions.   Just as it has been said above, it is an awareness that does not avoid faults on account of others. If one, when about to engage in a misdeed, avoids the misdeed on account of others, thinking, "If the Teacher and the clairvoyant gods were to become displeased and I were to be despised by others, this would be unfitting," then this is embarrassment. The opposite of this is non-embarrassment.   Non-embarrassment is the opposite to the embarrassment included in the eleven virtuous actions, which causes one to refrain from doing a negative action out of consideration for what others would think or say. Thus, in the case of non-embarrassment there is a lack of care about what others would say or think about one’s actions.  STUDENT: Are the mental factors shame and embarrassment present only if the negative action is avoided or can one engage in a negative action with a little bit of shame or embarrassment present but not enough to stop one from engaging in the negative action? Do they necessarily function to stop a negative action? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: The function of shame and embarrassment is that just on the verge of doing something wrong, one thinks that one should not do it and so does not do it. However, it is also possible that, having done an negative action, later on one comes to feel shame or embarrassment about what one has done. Therefore, these mental factors can arise either prior to or after a negative action.

174  STUDENT: T h e r e  s e e m s  t o  m e  t o  be another kind of shame and embarrassment: For example, when one does not do a virtuous action out of fear of what others will think or out fear of their disapproval, for example, not doing prostrations when one is among non-Dharma people, is this embarrassment? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: I t  i s  g o o d  t o  r e f r a i n  f r o m  d o i n g  s u c h  a c t i o n s  o u t  o f  f e a r  o f  o t h e r s  ridiculing one and creating negative karma by doing so. STUDENT: Recently at a meeting of Western teachers in Switzerland there was a discussion about prostrations due to the fact that such behavior can provoke a negative reaction in Westerners who do not normally bow down in respect before someone else.  GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: Essentially, Dharma is in the mind and therefore it is not necessary to prostrate physically or verbally in public. If one sincerely believes that actions such as prostrations will incur the disapproval or anger of others, then do not do them. It is sufficient to just prostrate mentally. It all depends on one’s motivation. In order to live in this world, outwardly one has to conform with what is acceptable to other people. However, internally one should continue to practice the Dharma.   STUDENT: I  t h i n k  t h a t  t h e r e  e x i s t s  a  k i n d  o f  s h a m e  t h a t  t h i n k s  t h a t  o n e  i s  d o i n g  s o m e t h i n g  ridiculous due to which one refrains from doing a virtuous action. This kind of shame would seem to me to be an affliction since in this case one does not engage in a virtuous action on account of oneself. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is possible to think “I will not do such-and-such because others will laugh at me or hold me in contempt.” One can feel that one will lose the esteem of others and be considered rather strange.  STUDENT: But if we always act in accordance with the people around us, how can we influence them to change? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: To act in conformity with the world does not mean that we should do everything that others do. For example, monks and nuns live in harmony with the world without drinking alcohol and so forth. We should strive to create strong and healthy relationships with others and then slowly begin to try to help those who, for example, drink too much. One should skillfully try to influence them while still living in the world. We need to accept that we can help some people but not others. It is not that we can save the entire world, that is just not possible. But if we can help even one person, then two, three, and so forth, that is very good. While our motivation should always be to help others, it is not actually possible to help everyone. But if we can help just one person by setting him on the path, that will be one less suffering person in the world. Therefore, we need a long-term view with respect to helping others, thinking to enact their benefit “as long as cyclic existence is not empty.”   STUDENT: What would Geshe-la advise us to do when people around us kill ants? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: It is difficult to tell others what not to do as they might tell us to mind our own business! They can say “This is my house, get out” or “This is my life, do not tell me what to do.” STUDENT: I specifically meant friends and family. In their case should we explain to them why they should not kill ants? GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: If one is really sure that they will listen, then one can say something. However, killing is something that happens all over the world all the time. For example, here at the moment in Pomaia every morning we hear the gunfire of hunters shooting animals, therefore it is a global issue, not just an individual one. These problems will remain until cyclic existence ends, therefore we need to develop our courage in order to face them. STUDENT 2: We could also offer to buy their ants! GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: They might refuse to give them to you! Ha-ha!

175 STUDENT: If one really wants to be helpful to others and attract them to the Dharma, it is useful to look at the kind of people who already attract others to the Dharma a n d  e x a m i n e  w h y  t h i s  happens. GESHE TENZIN TENPHEL: That is the main thing, you are right. It all depends on having a good motivation. Everything comes down to one’s motivation. However great or strong is one’s motivation to help others, that is how much one will be able to help others. The child meanwhile had picked up the paper and was about to put it in the garbage can but the father stopped him and told him to throw it on the ground. I was frozen with shock. Even just talking about it still makes me tremble. What should we do in this case? STUDENT 2: Punch him in the nose! Hee-hee!
